Applied AI Add-On Track (For SDE 2 + AI Roles)

Use this as a parallel track, not a replacement for DSA/LLD/HLD. Target outcome: Software Engineer / SDE 2 who can ship AI-powered features in production.

Time Split Recommendation
70% Core SDE 2 prep (DSA + LLD + HLD + mocks)
20% Applied AI concepts + API integration
10% AI portfolio + resume positioning

What to Learn

  • LLM basics: tokens, context window, temperature, hallucinations, prompting
  • Core AI app patterns: chat, summarization, extraction, classification, RAG
  • Tool calling / agents: structured outputs, tool invocation, orchestration
  • Embeddings + retrieval: chunking, vector search, reranking, hybrid search
  • Evaluation: prompt evals, regression sets, failure analysis, human review
  • Production concerns: latency, cost, retries, rate limits, observability, guardrails

Suggested Stack

  • Language: Python (recommended) + your main SDE stack
  • Model APIs: OpenAI / Gemini / Anthropic style APIs
  • Vector DB: pgvector, Pinecone, Weaviate, Chroma
  • Frameworks: light exposure to LangChain or LlamaIndex
  • Backend: FastAPI / Node backend, queues, streaming, auth, persistence
  • Ops: logs, traces, prompt versioning, cost tracking

  • Internal docs assistant with RAG + citations
  • Ticket / PR summarizer with action-item extraction
  • Support assistant with human approval flow
  • Developer workflow agent that calls internal tools/APIs

Interview / Resume Positioning

  • Position yourself as SDE 2 who can build AI-powered features
  • Lead with engineering impact, not "prompt engineering" alone
  • Be ready to explain quality, cost, latency, observability, and security
  • Use resume bullets that highlight backend integration and production tradeoffs
Important
Do not drop DSA, LLD, or HLD for AI prep. Most SDE 2 + AI roles still expect strong software engineering fundamentals. AI is an add-on differentiator, not a substitute for core engineering skill.

How to Approach Any Problem You've Never Seen

1. Read Signal Words

Underline keywords in the problem, then map them:

"contiguous subarray" + "sum"→ Prefix Sum
"longest/shortest subarray"→ Sliding Window
"all combinations/permutations"→ Backtracking
"minimum cost" + "choices"→ DP
"sorted array" + "find target"→ Binary Search
"connected components"→ Union-Find / DFS
"shortest path"→ BFS / Dijkstra
"pair with target sum"→ Two Pointers / HashMap

2. Ask the 5 Questions

Before writing any code, answer these in order:

Q1What am I optimizing? (min, max, count, true/false)
Q2What are my choices? (pick/skip, left/right, include/exclude)
Q3Do choices depend on future? YES → DP. NO → Greedy might work.
Q4What's the brute force? (always start here)
Q5What's repeated in brute force? (that's what your data structure eliminates)

3. Brute Force → Optimize Pipeline

Every optimization removes redundant work from brute force. Name what's redundant:

4. Practice by Category, Not Randomly

Solve 3-4 problems of the same pattern back-to-back. After 3-4, your brain sees the shape automatically. Then mix them up.

DayFocusWhat you build
Day 14 sliding window problemsWindow expand/shrink instinct
Day 24 prefix sum problems"Running sum + HashMap" reflex
Day 34 backtracking problemsChoose-explore-unchoose rhythm
Day 44 tree problemsRecursive thinking for subtrees
Day 5Mix of all abovePattern recognition

5. After Solving, Write ONE Line: "I recognized this because..."

This one sentence is more valuable than solving 5 more problems. It forces you to name the signal, not just the solution.

Sliding Window:
"I recognized this because it asked for longest substring with a constraint on characters."
DP:
"I recognized this because each position's answer depends on previous positions and there's no greedy shortcut."
Prefix Sum:
"I recognized this because it asked for subarray sum equals k — that's the complement lookup pattern."

01 / 18

Arrays & Hashing — Interview Notes (Java)

0. Building Intuition — How to Recognize the Pattern

Arrays and Hashing are the foundation of almost every interview. The challenge isn't knowing what an array or hashmap is — it's recognizing which technique to apply.

Question 1: "Am I searching for something — a pair, a complement, a duplicate?"

  • If YES → HashMap to store what you've seen so far, turning O(n²) brute force into O(n)
  • This is the single most important optimization in array problems
  • Why? A HashMap gives O(1) lookup. Instead of scanning the whole array for each element, you ask the map: "Have I seen my complement before?"
Real-world analogy: You're at a party looking for someone wearing a red shirt. You don't walk up to every person and ask — you scan the room once and remember faces. The HashMap is your memory.

Question 2: "Do I need cumulative information — sum of a range, count up to index i?"

  • If YES → Prefix Sum or Prefix Map
  • Instead of re-computing sums for every subarray (O(n²)), precompute running totals once
  • Why? sum(i, j) = prefix[j+1] - prefix[i] — any range sum in O(1)
Real-world analogy: Your bank statement shows a running balance. To know how much you spent between March and June, you don't add up every transaction — you subtract March's balance from June's. That's prefix sum.

Question 3: "Do I need to group, categorize, or count frequencies?"

  • If YES → HashMap for frequency counting or grouping by key
  • Anagrams? Group by sorted string. Most frequent? Count with map. Duplicates? Set or map.
Real-world analogy: A librarian sorts books by genre (the key) into shelves (the value). They don't compare every book with every other book — they just look at the genre and put it on the right shelf.

The "Array/Hashing smell test" — keywords in problem statements

Keyword in problemWhat it signals
"two numbers that sum to", "pair with target"Two Sum → HashMap complement
"subarray with sum K"Prefix Sum + HashMap
"contiguous subarray", "range sum"Prefix Sum
"maximum subarray sum"Kadane's Algorithm
"duplicates", "unique", "distinct"HashSet
"anagram", "permutation", "frequency"Frequency Map (int[26] or HashMap)
"group by", "categorize"HashMap with custom key
"in-place", "O(1) space"Swap-based / index marking
"product except self"Prefix + Suffix pattern
"majority element", "more than n/k times"Boyer-Moore Voting

When it's NOT just arrays/hashing

  • "Sorted array" + "find target" → Binary Search (Topic 4)
  • "Subarray" + "window of size K" → Sliding Window (Topic 3)
  • "Two sorted arrays" + "merge" → Two Pointers (Topic 2)
  • If you need O(n log n), consider sorting first then applying another technique

0.1 Decision Tree — Which Pattern to Use?

Is the problem about finding a pair/triplet with a target sum?
├── YES → Pattern 1: HashMap Complement (Two Sum)
│
├── Is it about subarray sums or range queries?
│   ├── "subarray sum equals K" → Pattern 2: Prefix Sum + HashMap
│   ├── "maximum subarray sum" → Pattern 3: Kadane's Algorithm
│   └── "range sum query" → Prefix Sum Array
│
├── Is it about frequencies, duplicates, or grouping?
│   ├── "find duplicates" → HashSet
│   ├── "group anagrams" → Pattern 4: Frequency Map Grouping
│   └── "top K frequent" → Frequency Map + Heap/Bucket Sort
│
├── Is it about transforming the array in-place?
│   ├── "move zeros", "sort colors" → Pattern 5: In-Place Manipulation
│   └── "next permutation", "rotate" → Index math
│
├── Does it involve prefix AND suffix computation?
│   └── YES → Pattern 6: Prefix-Suffix Product/Sum
│
├── Is it about finding majority element?
│   └── YES → Pattern 7: Boyer-Moore Voting
│
└── None of the above?
    → Might be Two Pointers, Sliding Window, or Binary Search instead

1. Core Concept

Arrays

  • Contiguous block of memory, O(1) random access by index
  • Fixed size in Java (int[]), dynamic with ArrayList
  • Strengths: Fast access, cache-friendly. Weakness: O(n) insert/delete in middle

HashMap

  • Key-value store with O(1) average lookup, insert, delete
  • Java: HashMap<K, V>, HashSet<K> (set = map with no values)
  • Collision handling: chaining (linked list) or open addressing
  • Worst case O(n) — but amortized O(1), interviewers accept this

▶ How HashMap Works Internally (click to expand)

The Basics

A HashMap is internally an array of buckets. When you call map.put("abc", 10):

  1. Hash: Compute "abc".hashCode() → some integer
  2. Index: index = hash & (array.length - 1) → maps to a bucket
  3. Store: Place the key-value pair in that bucket

Lookup is the reverse: hash the key, go to the bucket, find the matching key.

What is a Collision?

A collision happens when two different keys hash to the same bucket index. E.g., both "abc" and "xyz" hash to index 5.

Strategy 1: Chaining (what Java uses)

Each bucket holds a linked list of entries that hashed to the same index:

  • Insert: Hash the key, go to that bucket, append to the list.
  • Lookup: Hash the key, go to that bucket, walk the list comparing keys with .equals().
  • Java 8+: When a single bucket exceeds 8 entries, the linked list converts to a red-black tree (O(log n) instead of O(n) for that bucket).

Strategy 2: Open Addressing (Python dicts, some C++ implementations)

No linked lists. If a bucket is occupied, probe for the next empty slot:

  • Linear probing: Try index, index+1, index+2, ... (shown above)
  • Quadratic probing: Try index+1², index+2², index+3², ... (reduces clustering)
  • Double hashing: Use a second hash function for step size.

Load Factor & Rehashing

  • Java's default load factor is 0.75. When 75% of buckets are filled, the table doubles in size and rehashes everything.
  • This is why a single put() can occasionally be O(n) — but amortized over all operations, it's still O(1).
  • Initial capacity: 16 buckets. Grows to 32, 64, 128... as needed.

Why Override Both hashCode() and equals()?

Contract: hashCode() finds the bucket, equals() resolves collisions within that bucket.

If you override equals() but not hashCode(): Two "equal" objects may land in different buckets → map.get() returns null even though the key "exists".

If you override hashCode() but not equals(): Objects land in the same bucket but equals() uses reference equality → can't find the matching key.

Common Interview Questions About HashMap

QuestionAnswer
"Why is HashMap O(1) average but O(n) worst case?"All keys collide into one bucket → degrades to linked list/tree traversal
"What's the load factor?"Default 0.75. At 75% capacity, table doubles and rehashes everything
"HashMap vs TreeMap?"HashMap: O(1) unordered. TreeMap: O(log n) but keys are sorted (red-black tree)
"HashMap vs Hashtable?"Hashtable is legacy, synchronized (slower). HashMap is not thread-safe but faster. Use ConcurrentHashMap for thread safety
"What happens with null keys?"HashMap allows one null key (hashes to bucket 0). Hashtable does not allow null
Key insight: The power of arrays + hashing together: Arrays give you order and index access. HashMaps give you O(1) lookup by value. Most array problems become efficient by trading space for time — store what you've seen in a map.

2. Java Implementation Essentials

// --- HashMap ---
Map<Integer, Integer> map = new HashMap<>();
map.put(key, value);
map.get(key);                    // null if absent
map.getOrDefault(key, 0);       // default if absent
map.containsKey(key);
map.remove(key);
map.keySet();                    // iterate keys
map.entrySet();                  // iterate key-value pairs

// --- Frequency counting (most common pattern) ---
Map<Character, Integer> freq = new HashMap<>();
for (char c : str.toCharArray()) {
    freq.merge(c, 1, Integer::sum);  // cleanest way
    // OR: freq.put(c, freq.getOrDefault(c, 0) + 1);
}

// --- HashSet ---
Set<Integer> seen = new HashSet<>();
seen.add(val);
seen.contains(val);

// --- int[26] as frequency array (faster for lowercase letters) ---
int[] freq = new int[26];
for (char c : str.toCharArray()) freq[c - 'a']++;

// --- ArrayList ---
List<Integer> list = new ArrayList<>();
list.add(val);
list.get(index);
list.set(index, val);

3. Time & Space Complexity

OperationArrayHashMapHashSet
Access by indexO(1)N/AN/A
Search by valueO(n)O(1) avgO(1) avg
InsertO(n)*O(1) avgO(1) avg
DeleteO(n)*O(1) avgO(1) avg

*For arrays, insert/delete at arbitrary position requires shifting.

Important for interviews: When you use a HashMap to optimize, always state: "Trading O(n) space for O(1) lookup, bringing total from O(n²) to O(n)."

4. The 7 Core Patterns

Pattern 1 — HashMap Complement (Two Sum Family)

When to use: Find pair(s) in array that satisfy a condition (sum, difference, etc.)
How to arrive at this: You read "find two numbers that sum to target". Brute force checks every pair → O(n²). But for each number x, you know exactly what you need: target - x. Instead of scanning for it, just ask a HashMap: "Have I seen target - x before?" One pass, O(n).

The general principle: Whenever you're looking for a complement (the other half of a pair), store what you've seen so far in a map.
Single-Pass (Recommended)
// Check THEN insert
public int[] twoSum(int[] nums, int target) {
    Map<Integer, Integer> map = new HashMap<>();
    for (int i = 0; i < nums.length; i++) {
        int comp = target - nums[i];
        if (map.containsKey(comp))
            return new int[]{map.get(comp), i};
        map.put(nums[i], i); // add AFTER
    }
    return new int[]{};
}
// Self-match impossible: nums[i] isn't
// in the map yet when we check.
// Cleaner — no extra guard needed.
Two-Pass (Also Valid)
// Build map first, then check
public int[] twoSum(int[] nums, int target) {
    Map<Integer, Integer> map = new HashMap<>();
    for (int i = 0; i < nums.length; i++)
        map.put(nums[i], i);

    for (int i = 0; i < nums.length; i++) {
        int comp = target - nums[i];
        if (map.containsKey(comp)
            && map.get(comp) != i) // guard!
            return new int[]{i, map.get(comp)};
    }
    return new int[]{};
}
// Needs != i guard to prevent
// using the same element twice.
Why single-pass is preferred: "Check before insert" naturally prevents self-matching — you haven't added nums[i] yet when you look for its complement. The two-pass approach works but needs an explicit map.get(comp) != i guard. For LC 560 (Subarray Sum), single-pass is required for correctness — two-pass overcounts by matching future prefix sums.
Edge Cases:
  • Duplicate values → HashMap stores latest index; problem may ask for first occurrence
  • Same element used twice → "Can I use the same index?" Check: map.get(complement) != i
  • No solution exists → return empty/null based on problem
  • Negative numbers → no change in logic, complement math works the same
  • Single element array → can't form a pair, return empty

Variations: Two Sum II (sorted → two pointers instead), 3Sum (sort + two pointers), 4Sum, Two Sum in BST

Classic Problems: Two Sum, Two Sum II, Subarray Sum Equals K, Count Pairs With Given Sum

Pattern 2 — Prefix Sum

Why size n+1 instead of n? There are two ways to build the prefix array:

Option A (traditional, size n): pref[i] = pref[i-1] + arr[i]
Range sum: sum(i, j) = pref[j] - pref[i-1]
Problem: when i == 0, pref[i-1] is out of bounds — you need a special check.

Option B (recommended, size n+1): pref[0] = 0, pref[i+1] = pref[i] + arr[i]
Range sum: sum(i, j) = pref[j+1] - pref[i]
No edge case needed — works uniformly for all ranges including those starting at index 0.

Always prefer Option B in interviews. One fewer branch = one fewer bug under pressure. 
// Basic Prefix Sum Array (Option B — size n+1)
int[] prefix = new int[nums.length + 1];
for (int i = 0; i < nums.length; i++) {
    prefix[i + 1] = prefix[i] + nums[i];
}
// sum from index i to j (inclusive) = prefix[j+1] - prefix[i]
When to use: Subarray sum queries, count subarrays with sum K, range sum.
How to arrive at this: You need "subarray sum equals K". Brute force: check every subarray start & end → O(n²). Think differently: if prefix[j] - prefix[i] == K, then the subarray from i to j has sum K. So for each j, you need to know if prefix[j] - K exists as a previous prefix. That's a HashMap lookup!

The formula: sum(i+1, j) = prefix[j] - prefix[i]

▶ Building Intuition — How to think through LC 560 from scratch (click to expand)

Step 1: What are we actually looking for?

"Count subarrays with sum = k." A subarray is a contiguous slice. So we need all (i, j) pairs where nums[i] + nums[i+1] + ... + nums[j] = k.

Step 2: Brute force first

For every start i:
    For every end j >= i:
        Compute sum(i..j)
        If sum == k, count++

This is O(n³), or O(n²) with running sum. The question becomes: how do I avoid checking every pair?

Step 3: The key insight — reframe the question

Instead of asking "what is the sum from i to j?", ask:

"I know the total sum from index 0 to j. If I could subtract off the sum from 0 to some earlier index i, and what's left equals k — that's my subarray!"
[0 ... i | i+1 ....... j]
 ^^^^^^^^  ^^^^^^^^^^^^^
 subtract   this part = k
 this part
 (prefix[i])  (prefix[j] - prefix[i])

This is like a bank balance:

  • Your balance today (index j) is 15
  • You want to know when you spent exactly 5 (that's k)
  • So you look back: was my balance ever 10? Because 15 − 10 = 5

That "balance at each point" is the prefix sum. That "was it ever 10?" is the HashMap lookup.

Step 4: The formula falls out naturally

sum(i..j) = prefix[j] - prefix[i-1] = k

Rearrange:
prefix[i-1] = prefix[j] - k

So at each index j, you don't scan backwards. You just ask: "how many times has prefix[j] - k appeared before?" The HashMap tells you in O(1).

Step 5: Why check before insert?

Because "appeared before" is the whole point. If you add all prefix sums first, you're also matching against future indices — subarrays that go backwards in time, which don't exist.

The 3-line story to remember (not code)

  1. I'm walking left to right, keeping a running sum — that's the prefix sum
  2. At each step, I ask: "has anyone seen sum - k before?" — that's the HashMap lookup
  3. Then I record my current sum for future steps to find — that's the insert

The put(0, 1) at the start? That's saying "before I started walking, my balance was 0, and that happened once." Without it, you'd miss subarrays that start at index 0.

Self-test: Can you answer these without code?

#QuestionAnswer
1What does prefix[j] represent?Sum from index 0 to j
2If prefix[j] - prefix[i] = k, what does that mean?Subarray from i+1 to j has sum k
3So what do I need to look up?prefix[j] - k — has this appeared as a previous prefix?
4Why HashMap?O(1) lookup instead of scanning all previous prefixes
5Why check before insert?Only match past indices, not future ones

If you can answer these five, you'll reconstruct the solution every time.

Without Prefix Array (Recommended)
// Running sum + HashMap — O(n) time, O(n) space
public int subarraySum(int[] nums, int k) {
    Map<Integer, Integer> map = new HashMap<>();
    map.put(0, 1); // empty prefix
    int sum = 0, count = 0;

    for (int num : nums) {
        sum += num;
        // Check FIRST: how many previous
        // prefix sums equal (sum - k)?
        count += map.getOrDefault(sum - k, 0);
        // Insert AFTER
        map.merge(sum, 1, Integer::sum);
    }
    return count;
}
// No array needed — running sum variable
// is enough since we process left to right.
// map stores counts of all previous sums.
With Prefix Array (Explicit)
// Prefix array + HashMap — O(n) time, O(n) space
public int subarraySum(int[] nums, int k) {
    int n = nums.length;
    int[] pref = new int[n + 1]; // size n+1!
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++)
        pref[i + 1] = pref[i] + nums[i];

    Map<Integer, Integer> map = new HashMap<>();
    int count = 0;
    // SINGLE PASS: check then insert
    for (int i = 0; i <= n; i++) {
        count += map.getOrDefault(pref[i] - k, 0);
        map.merge(pref[i], 1, Integer::sum);
    }
    return count;
}
// pref[] makes the logic visible but
// is redundant — left version does the
// same with just a running sum variable.
Why MUST this be single-pass? Why not build map first, then check separately?

If you add all prefix sums to the map first and then check, you match against prefix sums at future indices — creating invalid subarrays where start > end.

Counterexample: nums = [1, -1, 0], k = 0
pref = [0, 1, 0, 0] → Two-pass map: {0:3, 1:1}
Two-pass gives 7. Correct answer is 3.

"Check before insert" ensures you only match prefix sums from indices before the current one — guaranteeing valid subarrays where start ≤ end.

▶ Why put(0, 1)? — the most common bug in prefix sum problems (click to expand)

The problem without it

Say nums = [3, 4, 7], k = 3.

The subarray [3] (just the first element) has sum 3 = k. Its prefix sum at index 0 is sum = 3.

To find it, you check: does sum - k = 3 - 3 = 0 exist in the map?

If you didn't seed the map with {0: 1}, the map is empty at this point — you miss this valid subarray.

What does put(0, 1) mean?

It represents the prefix sum before the array starts — "the sum of zero elements is 0, and that happened once."

         pref[0]=0    pref[1]=3    pref[2]=7    pref[3]=14
            |            |            |            |
array:     [ ]          [3]         [3,4]       [3,4,7]
            ↑
     "before the array"
     this is what put(0,1) captures

When sum = 3 and you look for sum - k = 0, you're asking: "is there a previous point where the running total was 0?" Yes — before the array started. That means the subarray from the very beginning up to here sums to k.

Dry run comparison

Without put(0, 1) — WRONG
nums = [3, 4, 7], k = 3
map = {}  // empty!

sum=3: check 3-3=0 in map? NO  ← MISSED!
       insert {3:1}
sum=7: check 7-3=4 in map? NO
       insert {3:1, 7:1}
sum=14: check 14-3=11? NO
        insert {3:1, 7:1, 14:1}

count = 0  ← WRONG (should be 1)
With put(0, 1) — CORRECT
nums = [3, 4, 7], k = 3
map = {0:1}  // seeded!

sum=3: check 3-3=0 in map? YES ← found!
       count=1, insert {0:1, 3:1}
sum=7: check 7-3=4 in map? NO
       insert {0:1, 3:1, 7:1}
sum=14: check 14-3=11? NO
        insert {0:1, 3:1, 7:1, 14:1}

count = 1  ← CORRECT
One-line summary: put(0, 1) is the prefix sum of the empty subarray — it lets you detect when the running sum itself equals k (subarrays starting at index 0).
Edge Cases:
  • k == 0 → still works; looking for prefix sums that appeared before with same value
  • All negative numbers → prefix sum can decrease; map handles it
  • Single element equals k → prefixCount starts with {0: 1} so this is caught
  • Empty subarray → the initial put(0, 1) handles this (subarray starting from index 0)

Classic Problems: Subarray Sum Equals K, Range Sum Query, Contiguous Array, Subarray Sums Divisible by K

Pattern 3 — Kadane's Algorithm (Maximum Subarray)

When to use: Find the contiguous subarray with the maximum (or minimum) sum.
How to arrive at this: At each index, you have a choice — either extend the current subarray, or start fresh from here. If the running sum so far is negative, it will only drag down whatever comes next. So reset. This is a greedy/DP hybrid thought.

The core idea: currentMax = Math.max(nums[i], currentMax + nums[i])
If currentMax + nums[i] < nums[i], the accumulated sum is negative → start fresh. Track the global maximum across all positions. 
// Maximum Subarray (LC 53)
public int maxSubArray(int[] nums) {
    int currentMax = nums[0];
    int globalMax = nums[0];

    for (int i = 1; i < nums.length; i++) {
        currentMax = Math.max(nums[i], currentMax + nums[i]);
        globalMax = Math.max(globalMax, currentMax);
    }
    return globalMax;
}
Edge Cases:
  • All negative numbers → Kadane's still works; returns the least negative number
  • Single element → returns that element
  • All positive numbers → returns total sum (never resets)
  • Need the actual subarray (not just sum) → track start/end indices during iteration

Variation: Minimum subarray sum → flip signs, run Kadane's, flip result. Or use Math.min.

Classic Problems: Maximum Subarray, Maximum Circular Subarray, Maximum Product Subarray

Pattern 4 — Frequency Map Grouping

When to use: Group elements by some property, find anagrams, count frequencies.
How to arrive at this: You read "group anagrams". Brute force: compare every pair of strings → O(n² × k). Think: what makes two strings anagrams? Same characters, same frequencies. If you sort both, they're identical. So use sorted string as a HashMap key, and group all strings with the same key.

The general principle: When you need to group things, find a canonical representation (the key) that all members of the group share.
Approach 1: Sort Key — O(n · k log k)
// Sort each string → anagrams get same key
public List<List<String>> groupAnagrams(
        String[] strs) {
    Map<String, List<String>> map
        = new HashMap<>();
    for (String s : strs) {
        char[] chars = s.toCharArray();
        Arrays.sort(chars);
        String key = new String(chars);
        map.computeIfAbsent(key,
            k -> new ArrayList<>()).add(s);
    }
    return new ArrayList<>(map.values());
}

// "eat" → sort → "aet"
// "tea" → sort → "aet"  ← same key!
// "tan" → sort → "ant"  ← different
Approach 2: Frequency Key — O(n · k)
// Count char frequency → same count = anagram
public List<List<String>> groupAnagrams(
        String[] strs) {
    Map<String, List<String>> map
        = new HashMap<>();
    for (String s : strs) {
        String key = getKey(s);
        map.computeIfAbsent(key,
            k -> new ArrayList<>()).add(s);
    }
    return new ArrayList<>(map.values());
}
private String getKey(String s) {
    int[] count = new int[26];
    for (char c : s.toCharArray())
        count[c - 'a']++;
    return Arrays.toString(count);
}
Approach 2 example walkthrough:

Input: ["eat", "tea", "tan"]

"eat": e=1, a=1, t=1 → key = "[1, 0, 0, 0, 1, 0, ..., 1, 0, 0, 0, 0, 0]"
"tea": t=1, e=1, a=1 → key = "[1, 0, 0, 0, 1, 0, ..., 1, 0, 0, 0, 0, 0]" ← same key! Grouped together.
"tan": t=1, a=1, n=1 → key = "[1, 0, 0, 0, 0, 0, ..., 1, 1, 0, ..., 1, 0, 0, 0, 0, 0]" ← different key.

Why faster? Sorting each string costs O(k log k). Counting frequency costs O(k). For long strings (large k), this matters. Both use the same grouping logic — only the key generation differs.
Edge Cases:
  • Empty strings → all empty strings are anagrams of each other; key = "" or all-zero array
  • Single character strings → each unique char is its own group
  • All strings identical → one group with all strings
  • Unicode characters → int[26] won't work; use HashMap for char frequency

Classic Problems: Group Anagrams, Valid Anagram, Find All Anagrams in a String, Isomorphic Strings

Pattern 5 — In-Place Array Manipulation

When to use: Modify array without extra space — move, swap, partition, mark.
How to arrive at this: You read "O(1) extra space". That means you can't use a HashMap or new array. You must work with the array itself. Techniques: swap elements to correct positions, use the array indices as a hash map (mark visited by negating), or use partitioning (like quicksort's partition).

Technique A — Find Duplicates (LC 442) — nums are in range [1, n]

Index as HashMap — O(1) space
// Use array indices as hash: mark visited
// by negating the value at that index
public List<Integer> findDuplicates(
        int[] nums) {
    List<Integer> result = new ArrayList<>();
    for (int num : nums) {
        int idx = Math.abs(num) - 1;
        if (nums[idx] < 0)
            result.add(idx + 1); // seen!
        else
            nums[idx] = -nums[idx]; // mark
    }
    return result;
}
// Key: nums[i] in [1,n] → use value as
// index. Negate to "mark" as visited.
// Already negative = duplicate.
HashMap — O(n) space
// Standard frequency count approach
public List<Integer> findDuplicates(
        int[] nums) {
    List<Integer> result = new ArrayList<>();
    Set<Integer> seen = new HashSet<>();
    for (int num : nums) {
        if (!seen.add(num))
            result.add(num); // was in set
    }
    return result;
}


// Simpler to understand but O(n) extra
// space. Interviewer will ask: "Can you
// do it in O(1) space?" → use left approach.

Technique B — Sort Colors (LC 75) — partition into 3 groups in-place

Dutch National Flag — O(1) space, 1 pass
// 3 pointers: low, mid, high
// [0..low-1]=0, [low..mid-1]=1, [high+1..n-1]=2
public void sortColors(int[] nums) {
    int lo = 0, mid = 0, hi = nums.length-1;
    while (mid <= hi) {
        if (nums[mid] == 0)
            swap(nums, lo++, mid++);
        else if (nums[mid] == 1)
            mid++;
        else
            swap(nums, mid, hi--);
    }
}
// Why mid doesn't advance on swap with hi:
// The swapped value from hi is unknown,
// needs to be checked again.
Common Bug — mid++ after swap with hi
Fails on [1, 2, 0]:
mid=1, hi=2: swap → [1, 0, 2], hi=1, mid=2 (skips the 0!)
Result: [1, 0, 2] — wrong.

Rule: Swap with lo → advance mid (lo..mid are all 1s, already seen).
Swap with hi → stay (unknown element just arrived).
Counting Sort — O(1) space, 2 passes
// Pass 1: count 0s, 1s, 2s
// Pass 2: overwrite array
public void sortColors(int[] nums) {
    int[] count = new int[3];
    for (int num : nums)
        count[num]++;

    int idx = 0;
    for (int c = 0; c < 3; c++)
        for (int i = 0; i < count[c]; i++)
            nums[idx++] = c;
}

// Simpler logic but needs 2 passes.
// Interviewer may ask for 1-pass →
// use Dutch National Flag (left).

Technique C — Move Zeros (LC 283) — two pointer in-place

// Move Zeroes (LC 283)
public void moveZeroes(int[] nums) {
    int insertPos = 0;
    for (int num : nums) {
        if (num != 0) nums[insertPos++] = num;
    }
    while (insertPos < nums.length) nums[insertPos++] = 0;
}
Edge Cases:
  • Array is already sorted/partitioned → should still work (no-op swaps)
  • All same elements → algorithm terminates correctly
  • Single element → trivially correct
  • Negative numbers when using index-marking → take Math.abs() before using as index

Classic Problems: Move Zeroes, Sort Colors, Find All Duplicates, First Missing Positive, Next Permutation

Pattern 6 — Prefix-Suffix Decomposition

When to use: Each position needs information from "everything before" AND "everything after".
How to arrive at this: You read "product of array except self — without division". For each index i, the answer is product of all elements before i × product of all elements after i. You can't compute this per-element without redundant work... unless you precompute prefix products (left to right) and suffix products (right to left), then multiply them. 
// Product of Array Except Self (LC 238)
public int[] productExceptSelf(int[] nums) {
    int n = nums.length;
    int[] result = new int[n];

    // prefix pass (left to right)
    result[0] = 1;
    for (int i = 1; i < n; i++) {
        result[i] = result[i - 1] * nums[i - 1];
    }

    // suffix pass (right to left)
    int suffix = 1;
    for (int i = n - 2; i >= 0; i--) {
        suffix *= nums[i + 1];
        result[i] *= suffix;
    }
    return result;
}
Edge Cases:
  • Array contains zeros → product of everything except the zero might be non-zero; with two+ zeros, all products are 0
  • Single element → product except self = 1 (empty product)
  • Negative numbers → math works the same
  • Integer overflow → problem usually constrains values to prevent this; mention it in interview

Classic Problems: Product of Array Except Self, Trapping Rain Water (prefix-suffix max), Candy (prefix-suffix pass)

Pattern 7 — Boyer-Moore Voting Algorithm

When to use: Find the majority element (appears > n/2 times) or elements appearing > n/k times.
How to arrive at this: You need the element appearing more than n/2 times. HashMap works in O(n) time and O(n) space. But can you do O(1) space? Think of it as an "election": the majority candidate can survive every "vote against" because it has more than half the votes. Maintain a candidate and a count — increment for same, decrement for different. The majority element will always be the last standing.

Why it works: The majority element has more than n/2 occurrences. Even if every other element "votes against" it, it can't be fully cancelled out.

▶ Building Intuition — How Boyer-Moore actually works (click to expand)

The core idea

Imagine a room full of people voting. Each person holds a card with a number. The majority number appears more than half the time.

The rule: Pick any two people with different cards — they cancel each other out and both leave. Keep doing this. The last person standing holds the majority number.

Why? If one number has more than half the votes, it can't be fully cancelled — there aren't enough "others" to cancel it.

Dry run: [2, 2, 1, 1, 1, 2, 2] — majority is 2 (4 out of 7)

StepnumcandidatecountWhat happens
1221count was 0 → new candidate = 2
2222same as candidate → count++
3121different → count-- (1 cancels one 2)
4120different → count-- (1 cancels one 2)
5111count was 0 → candidate switches to 1!
6210different → count-- (2 cancels the 1)
7221count was 0 → candidate back to 2 ✔

The "aha" moment

At step 5, the candidate switches to 1. That feels wrong — but it's fine! By that point, three 2s and two 1s have cancelled each other out. What's left is [1, 2, 2] — and 2 still wins.

The majority element can lose battles, but it can never lose the war. There simply aren't enough non-majority elements to cancel it out completely.

Think of it as a tug-of-war

Every rise = "my candidate appeared again." Every drop = "someone else appeared — one cancellation." When count hits 0, the previous candidate was fully cancelled — start fresh. The majority element always recovers because it has > n/2 copies.

Why verification is sometimes needed

ScenarioVerification needed?Why
LC 169 (n/2, guaranteed)NoProblem guarantees majority exists. Boyer-Moore always finds it.
LC 169 (n/2, NOT guaranteed)Yes[1, 2, 3] — no majority, but algorithm still returns a candidate. Second pass needed to verify count.
LC 229 (n/3)AlwaysAt most 2 candidates. Both must be verified with a second pass count.

Majority Element (LC 169) — appears > n/2 times

Boyer-Moore Voting — O(1) space
public int majorityElement(int[] nums) {
    int candidate = 0, count = 0;
    for (int num : nums) {
        if (count == 0) candidate = num;
        count += (num == candidate) ? 1 : -1;
    }
    return candidate;
}
// Think of it as an election:
// Same as candidate → +1 vote
// Different → -1 vote
// Count hits 0 → new candidate
// Majority survives because it has
// more than half the votes.
HashMap Frequency — O(n) space
public int majorityElement(int[] nums) {
    Map<Integer, Integer> freq
        = new HashMap<>();
    for (int num : nums) {
        int c = freq.merge(num, 1,
            Integer::sum);
        if (c > nums.length / 2)
            return num;
    }
    return -1;
}
// Straightforward: count everything,
// return when any count exceeds n/2.
// O(n) space. Interviewer will ask:
// "Can you do O(1) space?"
// → Boyer-Moore (left).

Majority Element II (LC 229) — appears > n/3 times (at most 2 candidates)

// Extended Boyer-Moore: track 2 candidates
public List<Integer> majorityElement(int[] nums) {
    int c1 = 0, c2 = 0, count1 = 0, count2 = 0;
    for (int num : nums) {
        if (num == c1) count1++;
        else if (num == c2) count2++;
        else if (count1 == 0) { c1 = num; count1 = 1; }
        else if (count2 == 0) { c2 = num; count2 = 1; }
        else { count1--; count2--; }
    }
    // Verify candidates (second pass — always required for n/3)
    List<Integer> result = new ArrayList<>();
    count1 = 0; count2 = 0;
    for (int num : nums) {
        if (num == c1) count1++;
        else if (num == c2) count2++;
    }
    if (count1 > nums.length / 3) result.add(c1);
    if (count2 > nums.length / 3) result.add(c2);
    return result;
}
Always verify! The algorithm finds a candidate, not the guaranteed answer. Always verify with a second pass unless the problem explicitly guarantees a majority element exists.
Edge Cases:
  • All elements are the same → candidate = that element, count never drops
  • No majority exists → algorithm still returns a candidate; you must verify (second pass)
  • n/3 variant → at most 2 candidates; need to track both
  • Single element → that element is the majority

Classic Problems: Majority Element, Majority Element II

5. Pattern Recognition Cheatsheet

Problem SignalPattern to Use
"Two numbers sum to target"HashMap Complement
"Subarray sum equals K"Prefix Sum + HashMap
"Maximum subarray sum"Kadane's Algorithm
"Group by property" / "anagrams"Frequency Map Grouping
"In-place", "O(1) space"Index marking / Swap / Partition
"Product/sum except self"Prefix-Suffix Decomposition
"Majority element" / "> n/k times"Boyer-Moore Voting
"Find missing/duplicate in [1,n]"Index as HashMap (negate)
"Longest consecutive sequence"HashSet + expand
"Contains duplicate within k distance"Sliding window HashSet

6. Common Mistakes to Avoid

  1. Off-by-one in prefix sum — Prefix array has length n+1, where prefix[0] = 0. Forgetting this causes wrong range sums.
  2. Forgetting prefixCount.put(0, 1) — In subarray sum problems, missing this means you miss subarrays starting at index 0.
  3. HashMap vs int[26] — For lowercase letter frequency, int[26] is faster and simpler. Use HashMap only when keys are unbounded.
  4. Modifying array while iterating — When using index-as-hashmap technique, always use Math.abs(nums[i]) to get the original value.
  5. Not verifying Boyer-Moore candidate — The algorithm finds a candidate, not the guaranteed answer. Always verify with a second pass unless the problem guarantees a majority exists.
  6. Using == for String comparison in HashMap — Use .equals() for String keys. == compares references, not content.
  7. Integer overflow in sum — For large arrays, use long for prefix sums.
  8. Iterating over nums instead of set (LC 128) — In Longest Consecutive Sequence, the second loop must iterate over the HashSet, not the original array. If nums has duplicates (e.g., [1, 1, 1, ..., 1]), iterating over nums starts the chain from 1 every time → TLE. The set has no duplicates, so each sequence start is visited exactly once → O(n).
LC 128 — TLE (iterates over nums)
Set<Integer> set = new HashSet<>();
for (int n : nums) set.add(n);

for (int n : nums) {  // BUG: duplicates!
    if (!set.contains(n - 1)) {
        int num = n, len = 0;
        while (set.contains(num)) {
            len++; num++;
        }
        max = Math.max(max, len);
    }
}
// nums = [1,1,1,...,1] (10^5 times)
// starts chain from 1 every time → TLE
LC 128 — Correct (iterates over set)
Set<Integer> set = new HashSet<>();
for (int n : nums) set.add(n);

for (int n : set) {   // FIX: no duplicates!
    if (!set.contains(n - 1)) {
        int num = n, len = 0;
        while (set.contains(num)) {
            len++; num++;
        }
        max = Math.max(max, len);
    }
}
// Each number visited at most twice
// (once as start, once in a chain) → O(n)

8. Quick Revision Card (1-min before interview)

HashMap = O(1) lookup. Trade space for time.
Prefix Sum = prefix[j+1] - prefix[i] = sum(i, j)
Kadane's = max(nums[i], currentMax + nums[i])
Boyer-Moore = cancel out non-majority, survivor wins

Patterns:
1. Complement search      → HashMap: seen[complement]?
2. Subarray sum = K        → Prefix Sum + HashMap (don't forget put(0,1))
3. Max subarray sum        → Kadane's: extend or restart
4. Group/categorize        → HashMap with canonical key (sorted, freq array)
5. In-place manipulation   → Index marking (negate), swap partition
6. Need prefix AND suffix  → Two-pass: left→right then right→left
7. Majority element        → Boyer-Moore Voting (verify with 2nd pass!)

Complexities:
  HashMap: O(1) avg insert/lookup, O(n) space
  Prefix Sum: O(n) build, O(1) per query
  Kadane's: O(n) time, O(1) space

Common traps:
  - prefix sum: don't forget put(0, 1) for subarrays starting at index 0
  - index marking: use Math.abs() when reading values
  - Boyer-Moore: always verify unless majority guaranteed
02 / 18

Two Pointers — Interview Notes (Java)

0. Building Intuition — How to Recognize a Two Pointers Problem

Two Pointers is not a data structure — it's a technique that eliminates unnecessary comparisons by exploiting structure in the data (usually sorted order or array properties).

Question 1: "Is the array sorted (or can I sort it without losing information)?"

  • Sorted data lets you make intelligent decisions about which pointer to move
  • If you move left pointer right → value increases. If you move right pointer left → value decreases.
  • This is why Two Sum on a sorted array is O(n) instead of O(n²) — you don't need to check every pair because the sorted order tells you which direction to adjust
Real-world analogy: You're looking for a specific weight by combining two objects from a sorted shelf. If the current combination is too light, you pick a heavier object from the right. Too heavy? Pick a lighter one from the left. You'd never randomly try pairs.

Question 2: "Am I comparing elements from the ends, or processing from one direction?"

  • Opposite ends (converging) → Two Sum II, Container With Most Water, Palindrome
  • Same direction (reader-writer) → Remove Duplicates, Partition, Merge
  • This distinction immediately tells you which sub-pattern to use
Real-world analogy (converging): Two people walking towards each other in a hallway — they start at opposite ends and meet somewhere in the middle. Each step, one or both move inward based on some condition.
Real-world analogy (same direction): A teacher grading papers (reader) and stacking good ones in a pile (writer). The reader scans every paper, but the writer only advances when a paper is worth keeping.

Question 3: "Am I trying to avoid O(n²) brute force on pairs/triplets?"

  • If brute force is "for each element, scan the rest" → Two Pointers can likely reduce it to O(n)
  • The key insight: each pointer only moves forward (or inward), never backwards → total moves ≤ 2n = O(n)

The "Two Pointers smell test" — keywords in problem statements

Keyword in problemWhat it signals
"sorted array" + "find pair"Opposite-end pointers
"palindrome", "symmetric"Opposite-end pointers
"container", "area", "trap water"Opposite-end pointers
"remove duplicates in-place"Same-direction reader-writer
"merge two sorted"Same-direction merge
"squares of sorted array"Opposite-end (negatives!)
"3Sum", "triplet"Sort + fix one + two pointers
"closest to target"Sort + opposite-end
"partition", "rearrange"Same-direction or Dutch flag

When it's NOT two pointers

  • Unsorted array + can't sort → HashMap might be better (Two Sum unsorted)
  • Need subarray of variable size → Sliding Window (Topic 3)
  • Need element at specific position → Binary Search (Topic 4)
  • Comparing elements far apart with no structure → probably not two pointers

0.1 Decision Tree — Which Pattern to Use?

Is the input sorted (or should you sort it)?
├── YES, sorted →
│   ├── Find pair with target sum/diff? → Pattern 1: Opposite-End
│   ├── Find triplet? → Pattern 3: Fix One + Two Pointers
│   ├── Merge two sorted arrays? → Pattern 4: Merge Pattern
│   └── Squares of sorted array? → Pattern 1 variant (from ends)
│
├── Is it about checking symmetry (palindrome)?
│   └── YES → Pattern 1: Opposite-End
│
├── Is it about maximizing area/container?
│   └── YES → Pattern 2: Greedy Opposite-End
│
├── Is it about removing/partitioning in-place?
│   └── YES → Pattern 5: Reader-Writer (Same Direction)
│
├── Is it about trapping water / height-based?
│   └── YES → Pattern 2 variant: Opposite-End with tracking
│
└── None of the above?
    → Might be Sliding Window, Binary Search, or HashMap instead

1. Core Concept

Two Pointers = Use two index variables to traverse data, making decisions about which to advance based on some condition.

Two fundamental setups:

Opposite-end (converging):        Same-direction (fast-slow):
[1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7]           [1, 1, 2, 2, 3, 4, 4]
 L→                ←R             S→
                                  F→
 L and R move inward              F scans ahead, S marks write position

Why it's O(n): Each pointer moves at most n times. Total work = O(n) + O(n) = O(n). No nested loops.

The invariant rule: At each step, you must be able to prove that moving a specific pointer doesn't miss any valid answers. In sorted arrays, this proof comes from monotonicity — moving left increases sum, moving right decreases it.

2. Java Implementation Essentials

// --- Opposite-end template ---
int left = 0, right = arr.length - 1;
while (left < right) {
    // compute something with arr[left] and arr[right]
    if (condition) left++;
    else if (otherCondition) right--;
    else {
        // found answer
    }
}

// --- Same-direction template ---
int slow = 0;
for (int fast = 0; fast < arr.length; fast++) {
    if (shouldKeep(arr[fast])) {
        arr[slow] = arr[fast];
        slow++;
    }
}
// slow = count of kept elements

// --- Merge template ---
int i = 0, j = 0, k = 0;
while (i < a.length && j < b.length) {
    if (a[i] <= b[j]) result[k++] = a[i++];
    else result[k++] = b[j++];
}
// copy remaining from whichever isn't exhausted

3. Time & Space Complexity

PatternTimeSpaceWhy
Opposite-endO(n)O(1)Each pointer moves at most n times
Same-directionO(n)O(1)Single pass
Fix one + two pointers (3Sum)O(n²)O(1)*Fix each of n elements, two pointers for rest
Merge two sortedO(n + m)O(n + m)*Touch each element once

*Excluding output space and sort space.

Important: Two Pointers on a sorted array is O(n). But if you need to sort first, total is O(n log n). Interviewers will ask about this — make sure to state it.

4. The 5 Core Patterns

Pattern 1 — Opposite-End Pointers (Converging)

When to use: Sorted array + find pair with target sum, check palindrome, or any problem where comparing from both ends makes sense.
How to arrive at this: You read "sorted array, find two numbers that sum to target". Brute force: every pair → O(n²). But it's sorted! Start with the smallest + largest. If sum too big → decrease by moving right pointer left. If too small → increase by moving left pointer right. Each step eliminates one candidate. You'll find the pair (or prove none exists) in O(n).

Why you never miss a valid pair: When sum > target and you move right--, you're discarding all pairs with the current right. But all those pairs have sum ≥ arr[left] + arr[right] > target (because everything to the right of left is ≥ arr[left]). So none of them could be valid. Safe to skip. 
// Two Sum II — Sorted Array (LC 167)
public int[] twoSum(int[] numbers, int target) {
    int left = 0, right = numbers.length - 1;
    while (left < right) {
        int sum = numbers[left] + numbers[right];
        if (sum == target) return new int[]{left + 1, right + 1}; // 1-indexed
        else if (sum < target) left++;
        else right--;
    }
    return new int[]{};
}
// Valid Palindrome (LC 125)
public boolean isPalindrome(String s) {
    int left = 0, right = s.length() - 1;
    while (left < right) {
        while (left < right && !Character.isLetterOrDigit(s.charAt(left))) left++;
        while (left < right && !Character.isLetterOrDigit(s.charAt(right))) right--;
        if (Character.toLowerCase(s.charAt(left)) != Character.toLowerCase(s.charAt(right)))
            return false;
        left++;
        right--;
    }
    return true;
}
Edge Cases:
  • Empty array / single element → left >= right immediately, return not found / true for palindrome
  • All elements the same → pointers converge quickly
  • Multiple valid pairs → returns first found (leftmost pair)
  • Target impossible (smaller than 2×min or larger than 2×max) → pointers cross without match
  • Palindrome with special characters → skip non-alphanumeric (inner while loops)

Classic Problems: Two Sum II, Valid Palindrome, Reverse String, Squares of a Sorted Array

Pattern 2 — Greedy Opposite-End (Area / Trapping)

When to use: Maximize/minimize something by choosing positions from both ends — container area, trapped water.
How to arrive at this: You read "container with most water" — find two lines that form the largest area. Brute force: every pair → O(n²). Key insight: area = min(height[L], height[R]) × (R - L). Starting from widest container (L=0, R=n-1), if you move the taller pointer inward, width decreases AND height can't improve (bottleneck is the shorter side). So always move the shorter pointer — that's the only way area might increase.

Why this greedy works: The shorter line is the bottleneck. Keeping it and shrinking width can only make things worse. Moving the shorter line gives a chance of finding a taller line. Moving the taller line has zero chance of improving.
Greedy Two Pointers on Unsorted Arrays — Deep Dive

Why It's Rare

Most two-pointer problems require sorting first because the greedy elimination depends on order. Unsorted two-pointer greedy only works when the structure of the problem itself creates the elimination argument, not the data order.

The Common Trait in Unsorted Cases

The array indices themselves carry meaning (like position/width), and the values at those indices are a separate dimension (like height). The greedy works on the index dimension, not the value dimension.

LC 11:  answer = f(value[L], value[R], R - L)
                   ^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^  ^^^^^
                   unsorted, don't care  THIS is what shrinks monotonically

You're not scanning values in order — you're shrinking a window, and at each step proving the shorter side has no better partner.

A Different Problem With the Same Pattern

"Maximum Distance Between Two Elements"
Given an unsorted array nums, find the maximum j - i such that nums[i] <= nums[j].

nums = [34, 8, 10, 3, 2, 80, 30, 33, 1]
answer = 6  (i=1, j=7 → nums[1]=8 <= nums[7]=33, distance=6)

Two-pointer greedy (no sorting needed):

  • Precompute leftMin[i] = min from left, rightMax[j] = max from right
  • Start i=0, j=0
  • If leftMin[i] <= rightMax[j] → valid pair, record j-i, move j++ (try wider)
  • Else → move i++ (current left is too big, shrink from left)

The greedy elimination: if leftMin[i] > rightMax[j], then i paired with anything < j is also invalid (rightMax only gets smaller). Safe to skip.

How to Spot These Problems

Ask two questions:

  1. Does position/index matter independently of value? (distance, width, subarray length)
  2. Does one dimension shrink monotonically as you move pointers? (width always decreases, window always shrinks)

If both are yes → two-pointer greedy may work on an unsorted array. If the answer depends only on values (like Two Sum), you'll need sorting or a hash map.

The honest truth: these problems are uncommon. LC 11 and LC 42 are the main ones you'll encounter. The intuition to build is — when the index gap is part of the answer, and values are just constraints, greedy elimination can work without sorting.

// Container With Most Water (LC 11)
public int maxArea(int[] height) {
    int left = 0, right = height.length - 1;
    int maxWater = 0;
    while (left < right) {
        int area = Math.min(height[left], height[right]) * (right - left);
        maxWater = Math.max(maxWater, area);
        if (height[left] < height[right]) left++;
        else right--;
    }
    return maxWater;
}
Approach 1: Prefix-Suffix Arrays — O(n) time, O(n) space
// Trapping Rain Water (LC 42)
public int trap(int[] height) {
    int n = height.length;
    int[] leftMax = new int[n];
    int[] rightMax = new int[n];

    // prefix max (left to right)
    leftMax[0] = height[0];
    for (int i = 1; i < n; i++) {
        leftMax[i] = Math.max(leftMax[i-1], height[i]);
    }

    // suffix max (right to left)
    rightMax[n-1] = height[n-1];
    for (int i = n - 2; i >= 0; i--) {
        rightMax[i] = Math.max(rightMax[i+1], height[i]);
    }

    // water at each index
    int water = 0;
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        water += Math.min(leftMax[i], rightMax[i])
                 - height[i];
    }
    return water;
}
Approach 2: Two Pointers — O(n) time, O(1) space ✓
// Trapping Rain Water (LC 42)
public int trap(int[] height) {
    int left = 0, right = height.length - 1;
    int leftMax = 0, rightMax = 0;
    int water = 0;

    while (left < right) {
        if (height[left] < height[right]) {
            leftMax = Math.max(leftMax, height[left]);
            water += leftMax - height[left];
            left++;
        } else {
            rightMax = Math.max(rightMax, height[right]);
            water += rightMax - height[right];
            right--;
        }
    }
    return water;
}

// Key insight: the shorter side is the
// bottleneck, so we can compute water there
// without knowing the exact other side's max.
Approach 1 → 2 intuition: In approach 1, water at index i = min(leftMax[i], rightMax[i]) - height[i]. We only need the smaller of the two maxes. If height[left] < height[right], then leftMax ≤ rightMax is guaranteed — so we can compute water at left using just leftMax, without needing the full rightMax array. That eliminates both arrays.
Edge Cases:
  • All heights equal → no water trapped, container area = height × (n-1)
  • Strictly increasing/decreasing → no water trapped
  • Two elements → one computation, no trapping
  • Very large arrays → O(n) time, O(1) space — optimal

Classic Problems: Container With Most Water, Trapping Rain Water

Pattern 3 — Fix One + Two Pointers (3Sum / kSum)

When to use: Find triplets (or k-tuples) that satisfy a condition.
How to arrive at this: You read "find all triplets that sum to zero". Brute force: three nested loops → O(n³). Think: if you fix one element (nums[i]), the remaining problem is "find two numbers in the rest that sum to -nums[i]". That's Two Sum on a sorted array! So: sort, then for each element, run opposite-end two pointers on the remaining subarray. O(n²).

The reduction: Any k-Sum problem can be reduced to (k-1)-Sum by fixing one element. 4Sum → fix one + 3Sum → fix two + 2Sum. Base case is always two pointers. 
// 3Sum (LC 15)
public List<List<Integer>> threeSum(int[] nums) {
    Arrays.sort(nums);
    List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();

    for (int i = 0; i < nums.length - 2; i++) {
        if (i > 0 && nums[i] == nums[i - 1]) continue; // skip duplicates

        int left = i + 1, right = nums.length - 1;
        int target = -nums[i];

        while (left < right) {
            int sum = nums[left] + nums[right];
            if (sum == target) {
                result.add(Arrays.asList(nums[i], nums[left], nums[right]));
                while (left < right && nums[left] == nums[left + 1]) left++;   // skip dupes
                while (left < right && nums[right] == nums[right - 1]) right--; // skip dupes
                left++;
                right--;
            } else if (sum < target) left++;
            else right--;
        }
    }
    return result;
}
// 3Sum Closest (LC 16)
public int threeSumClosest(int[] nums, int target) {
    Arrays.sort(nums);
    int closest = nums[0] + nums[1] + nums[2];
    for (int i = 0; i < nums.length - 2; i++) {
        int left = i + 1, right = nums.length - 1;
        while (left < right) {
            int sum = nums[i] + nums[left] + nums[right];
            if (Math.abs(sum - target) < Math.abs(closest - target)) closest = sum;
            if (sum < target) left++;
            else if (sum > target) right--;
            else return target; // exact match
        }
    }
    return closest;
}
Handling duplicates in 3Sum — the #1 interview mistake:
  • After sorting, skip nums[i] if it equals nums[i-1] (outer loop)
  • After finding a valid triplet, skip duplicate left and right values (inner loop)
  • Forgetting either causes duplicate triplets in the result
Edge Cases:
  • Array has fewer than 3 elements → return empty
  • All zeros → single triplet [0, 0, 0]
  • All positive or all negative → no triplet sums to 0 (early termination possible)
  • Many duplicates → skipping logic is critical for correctness AND performance

Classic Problems: 3Sum, 3Sum Closest, 4Sum, 3Sum Smaller

Pattern 4 — Merge Pattern (Two Sorted Arrays)

When to use: Merge two sorted arrays/lists, or compare elements from two sorted sequences.
How to arrive at this: You have two sorted arrays and need to merge them. Each array is already sorted, so the smallest unmerged element is always at one of the two current pointers. Compare, pick the smaller one, advance that pointer. Like merging in merge sort.

The invariant: At each step, everything before both pointers is already in the correct merged position. 
// Merge Sorted Array (LC 88) — merge into nums1 (has extra space)
public void merge(int[] nums1, int m, int[] nums2, int n) {
    int i = m - 1, j = n - 1, k = m + n - 1;
    while (i >= 0 && j >= 0) {
        if (nums1[i] > nums2[j]) nums1[k--] = nums1[i--];
        else nums1[k--] = nums2[j--];
    }
    while (j >= 0) nums1[k--] = nums2[j--]; // remaining from nums2
}
Why merge from the end? In LC 88, nums1 has trailing zeros as placeholder space. If you merge from the front, you'd overwrite existing elements. Merging from the end fills the empty space first — no overwrites. 
// Squares of a Sorted Array (LC 977)
public int[] sortedSquares(int[] nums) {
    int n = nums.length;
    int[] result = new int[n];
    int left = 0, right = n - 1;
    for (int k = n - 1; k >= 0; k--) {
        if (Math.abs(nums[left]) > Math.abs(nums[right])) {
            result[k] = nums[left] * nums[left];
            left++;
        } else {
            result[k] = nums[right] * nums[right];
            right--;
        }
    }
    return result;
}
Why opposite-end for Squares? Input is sorted but may have negatives. After squaring, the largest values are at the ends (large negatives or large positives). So compare from both ends and fill result from the back.
Edge Cases:
  • One array is empty → result is the other array
  • Arrays of very different sizes → smaller array exhausted first, copy remainder
  • All negatives → squares are in reverse order, opposite-end handles it
  • Merge in-place with extra space → merge from end to avoid overwrites

Classic Problems: Merge Sorted Array, Squares of a Sorted Array, Intersection of Two Arrays II

Pattern 5 — Reader-Writer (Same Direction)

When to use: Remove duplicates, remove specific elements, partition array — all in-place with O(1) space.
How to arrive at this: You read "remove duplicates from sorted array in-place". You need a "reader" that scans every element, and a "writer" that only advances when an element should be kept. The writer position marks where the next valid element should go. Reader runs ahead, writer stays behind.

The mental model: Imagine two people at a conveyor belt. The "reader" inspects each item. The "writer" places good items into boxes. The writer never moves ahead of the reader. 
// Remove Duplicates from Sorted Array (LC 26)
public int removeDuplicates(int[] nums) {
    if (nums.length == 0) return 0;
    int writer = 1; // first element always stays
    for (int reader = 1; reader < nums.length; reader++) {
        if (nums[reader] != nums[reader - 1]) {
            nums[writer] = nums[reader];
            writer++;
        }
    }
    return writer; // new length
}
// Remove Duplicates II — allow at most 2 of each (LC 80)
public int removeDuplicates(int[] nums) {
    if (nums.length <= 2) return nums.length;
    int writer = 2;
    for (int reader = 2; reader < nums.length; reader++) {
        if (nums[reader] != nums[writer - 2]) {
            nums[writer] = nums[reader];
            writer++;
        }
    }
    return writer;
}
// Remove Element (LC 27)
public int removeElement(int[] nums, int val) {
    int writer = 0;
    for (int reader = 0; reader < nums.length; reader++) {
        if (nums[reader] != val) {
            nums[writer] = nums[reader];
            writer++;
        }
    }
    return writer;
}
Edge Cases:
  • Empty array → return 0
  • No duplicates / no elements to remove → writer advances with reader, array unchanged
  • All elements are duplicates / all should be removed → writer stays at 0
  • Single element → always kept
  • "Allow K duplicates" generalization → compare nums[reader] with nums[writer - K]

Classic Problems: Remove Duplicates, Remove Duplicates II, Remove Element, Move Zeroes

5. Pattern Recognition Cheatsheet

Problem SignalPattern to Use
"Sorted array" + "pair with sum/diff"Opposite-End Pointers
"Is palindrome" / "check symmetry"Opposite-End Pointers
"Maximum area / container"Greedy Opposite-End
"Trap water"Greedy Opposite-End with max tracking
"3Sum" / "triplet" / "k numbers"Fix One + Two Pointers
"Closest to target"Sort + Opposite-End
"Remove duplicates in-place"Reader-Writer (Same Direction)
"Merge two sorted arrays"Merge Pattern
"Squares of sorted array"Opposite-End (compare absolutes)
"Partition array"Same Direction or Dutch Flag

6. Common Mistakes to Avoid

  1. Forgetting to sort first — Two Pointers on unsorted array is meaningless for sum problems. Always check if sorting is needed (and state O(n log n) cost).
  2. Not handling duplicates in 3Sum — Both outer loop (i > 0 && nums[i] == nums[i-1]) and inner loop (after finding a match) need duplicate skipping. Missing either = duplicate triplets.
  3. Off-by-one in merge from end — Starting index for merge is m + n - 1, not m + n. The last element is at index m + n - 1.
  4. Moving the wrong pointer — In Container With Most Water, always move the shorter pointer. Moving the taller one can never improve area.
  5. Using two pointers when HashMap is simpler — If the array is NOT sorted and sorting destroys needed information (like original indices), use HashMap instead.
  6. Forgetting inner while loops for duplicate skipping — In 3Sum, after finding a valid triplet, you need to advance both left and right past duplicates AND then do the normal left++; right--.
  7. Not considering negative numbers in Squares — Squares of a sorted array with negatives: the largest square could be at either end. Must compare absolute values.
