Pick any 3 you struggled with from above. Solve without looking at notes. Focus especially on 3Sum (duplicate handling) and Trapping Rain Water (hardest intuition).

8. Quick Revision Card (1-min before interview)

Two Pointers = exploit sorted order to avoid O(n²) brute force

Setups:
  Opposite-end: L=0, R=n-1, converge based on condition
  Same-direction: slow (writer), fast (reader)

Patterns:
1. Opposite-End       → sorted pair sum, palindrome, reverse
2. Greedy Opposite    → container area, trap water (move shorter side)
3. Fix One + 2P       → 3Sum: sort, fix i, two-pointer rest
4. Merge              → two sorted arrays, compare heads, pick smaller
5. Reader-Writer      → remove dupes, partition (writer only advances on valid)

Key rules:
  - Sort first (unless already sorted or sorting loses info)
  - Each pointer moves only forward/inward → O(n) total
  - Container: always move the SHORTER pointer
  - 3Sum: skip duplicates in BOTH outer and inner loops
  - Merge from END when merging in-place (avoid overwrites)
  - Squares: compare absolute values from BOTH ends

Complexities:
  Two pointer on sorted: O(n) time, O(1) space
  3Sum: O(n²) time (can't do better)
  Merge: O(n + m) time
03 / 18

Sliding Window — Interview Notes (Java)

0. Building Intuition — How to Recognize a Sliding Window Problem

Sliding Window is not a data structure — it's a technique that maintains a contiguous subarray/substring and efficiently updates its state as the window moves, avoiding redundant recomputation.

Question 1: "Am I looking at contiguous elements (subarray/substring)?"

  • Sliding window ONLY works on contiguous sequences — subarrays or substrings
  • If the problem asks about subsequences (elements don't need to be adjacent), sliding window won't work
  • If you can rephrase the problem as "find the best window of elements in a row", it's a sliding window candidate
Real-world analogy: You're looking through a train window at houses along the track. You can only see a continuous stretch of houses at any time. As the train moves, new houses appear on one side and old ones disappear from the other. You never see "house 3, then house 7, then house 12" — only consecutive houses.

Question 2: "Can I express the answer as: best subarray of size K, or best subarray meeting condition X?"

  • Exact size K → Fixed-size window (Pattern 1)
  • Smallest subarray where condition is met → Dynamic shrinkable window (Pattern 2)
  • Largest subarray where condition holds → Dynamic longest window (Pattern 3)
  • This distinction immediately tells you which template to use
Real-world analogy: Imagine adjusting a magnifying glass over a line of text. Fixed window = the magnifying glass has a fixed width. Dynamic window = the magnifying glass is elastic — you stretch it right until you capture what you need, then squeeze it from the left to find the tightest fit.

Question 3: "When I add an element on the right, can I cheaply update the window state?"

  • If adding/removing one element lets you update the answer in O(1) → sliding window works
  • If every window requires scanning all its elements from scratch → sliding window gives no benefit
  • Classic cheap updates: running sum (add/subtract), frequency map (increment/decrement), counter (check threshold)
Real-world analogy: You're computing the average temperature of the last 7 days. When a new day arrives, you add its temperature and subtract the temperature from 8 days ago. You don't re-sum all 7 days. That "add one, drop one" is the essence of sliding window.

The "Sliding Window smell test" — keywords in problem statements

Keyword in problemWhat it signals
"subarray of size K"Fixed-size window
"maximum/minimum average"Fixed-size window
"smallest subarray with sum ≥"Dynamic shrinkable (shortest)
"longest substring without repeating"Dynamic non-shrinkable (longest)
"at most K distinct"Dynamic longest + HashMap
"anagram", "permutation in string"Window + HashMap/Counter
"sliding window maximum"Window + Monotonic Deque
"contiguous subarray"Sliding window candidate
"consecutive ones"Dynamic longest
"minimum window containing"Dynamic shrinkable + HashMap

When it's NOT sliding window

  • Elements don't need to be contiguous (subsequence problems) → DP or Greedy
  • Data is sorted and you're comparing from both ends → Two Pointers (Topic 2)
  • You need to find a specific element's position → Binary Search (Topic 4)
  • Window state can't be cheaply updated (e.g., median of window) → more complex structures needed
  • Subarray products with zeros → resets break the window model

0.1 Decision Tree — Which Pattern to Use?

Is the problem about a contiguous subarray or substring?
├── NO → Not sliding window. Consider DP, Two Pointers, or HashMap.
│
├── YES →
│   ├── Is the window size fixed (given K)?
│   │   └── YES → Pattern 1: Fixed-Size Window
│   │
│   ├── Is the window size variable?
│   │   ├── Find SHORTEST/SMALLEST subarray meeting a condition?
│   │   │   └── YES → Pattern 2: Dynamic Shrinkable (expand, then shrink to minimize)
│   │   │
│   │   ├── Find LONGEST/LARGEST subarray meeting a condition?
│   │   │   └── YES → Pattern 3: Dynamic Non-Shrinkable (expand, shrink only when broken)
│   │   │
│   │   └── Not sure?
│   │       → If condition is "at least X" → shrink to minimize (Pattern 2)
│   │       → If condition is "at most X" → expand to maximize (Pattern 3)
│   │
│   ├── Does it involve character frequencies / anagram / permutation?
│   │   └── YES → Pattern 4: Window + HashMap/Counter
│   │
│   └── Does it need the max/min of each window position?
│       └── YES → Pattern 5: Window + Monotonic Deque

1. Core Concept

Sliding Window = Maintain a contiguous range [left, right] over the array. Expand right to include new elements, shrink left to remove old elements. Update window state incrementally.

Fixed-size window (size K=3):

  Step 1: [1, 3, 2] 5, 1, 4     sum = 6
  Step 2:  1 [3, 2, 5] 1, 4     sum = 6 - 1 + 5 = 10  (subtract left, add right)
  Step 3:  1, 3 [2, 5, 1] 4     sum = 10 - 3 + 1 = 8
  Step 4:  1, 3, 2 [5, 1, 4]    sum = 8 - 2 + 4 = 10

Dynamic-size window:

  "Smallest subarray with sum >= 7"
  [1, 3, 2, 5, 1, 4]

  Expand right →→→ until sum >= 7:
  [1, 3, 2, 5] 1, 4     sum = 11 >= 7, window size = 4

  Shrink left → to minimize:
   1 [3, 2, 5] 1, 4     sum = 10 >= 7, window size = 3
   1, 3 [2, 5] 1, 4     sum = 7 >= 7, window size = 2  ← best so far

Why sliding window is O(n): Each element enters the window at most once (right++) and leaves the window at most once (left++). Total pointer movements ≤ 2n.

Sliding Window vs Two Pointers — the common confusion:
  • Two Pointers: typically works on sorted data, pointers may converge from opposite ends
  • Sliding Window: works on contiguous subarrays, both pointers move in the same direction (left to right)

2. Java Implementation Essentials

// --- Fixed-Size Window Template ---
int windowSum = 0;
for (int i = 0; i < arr.length; i++) {
    windowSum += arr[i];                    // add right element
    if (i >= k) {
        windowSum -= arr[i - k];            // remove left element
    }
    if (i >= k - 1) {
        result = Math.max(result, windowSum); // window is exactly size K
    }
}

// --- Dynamic Window (Shrinkable — find SHORTEST) Template ---
int left = 0, minLen = Integer.MAX_VALUE;
for (int right = 0; right < arr.length; right++) {
    // expand: add arr[right] to window state
    while (windowMeetsCondition()) {
        minLen = Math.min(minLen, right - left + 1);  // record INSIDE while
        // shrink: remove arr[left] from window state
        left++;
    }
}

// --- Dynamic Window (Non-Shrinkable — find LONGEST) Template ---
int left = 0, maxLen = 0;
for (int right = 0; right < arr.length; right++) {
    // expand: add arr[right] to window state
    while (!windowMeetsCondition()) {
        // shrink: remove arr[left] from window state
        left++;
    }
    maxLen = Math.max(maxLen, right - left + 1);  // record OUTSIDE while
}
Key difference between the two dynamic templates:
  • Shortest (Pattern 2): Record answer inside the while loop (while condition is met, try shrinking to minimize)
  • Longest (Pattern 3): Record answer outside the while loop (fix violations first, then record the valid window)

3. Time & Space Complexity

PatternTimeSpaceWhy
Fixed-size windowO(n)O(1)Single pass, constant work per step
Dynamic shrinkable (shortest)O(n)O(1)Each element enters/leaves window once
Dynamic non-shrinkable (longest)O(n)O(1)Same amortized argument
Window + HashMapO(n)O(k)*HashMap stores at most k distinct elements
Window + Monotonic DequeO(n)O(k)Deque holds at most k elements

*k = size of character set or number of distinct elements.

Important: The inner while loop does NOT make it O(n²). Across ALL iterations of the outer for loop, the left pointer moves at most n times total. Interviewers love asking about this.

4. The 5 Core Patterns

Pattern 1 — Fixed-Size Window

When to use: The problem specifies an exact window size K. Find max sum, max average, or some property of all subarrays of size K.
How to arrive at this: You read "maximum average subarray of length K". Brute force: for each starting index, sum K elements → O(nK). But when you slide one step right, you add one new element and drop one old element. Update sum in O(1). Total: O(n).

The insight: A fixed-size window is the simplest sliding window. You don't need to decide when to shrink — the window always has exactly K elements. Just slide it one position at a time. 
// Maximum Average Subarray I (LC 643)
public double findMaxAverage(int[] nums, int k) {
    int windowSum = 0;
    for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) {
        windowSum += nums[i];  // build first window
    }
    int maxSum = windowSum;
    for (int i = k; i < nums.length; i++) {
        windowSum += nums[i] - nums[i - k];  // slide: add right, remove left
        maxSum = Math.max(maxSum, windowSum);
    }
    return (double) maxSum / k;
}
Edge Cases:
  • k == nums.length → only one window, return its sum/average
  • k == 1 → answer is max element
  • All elements equal → every window has the same value
  • Array has negative numbers → sliding still works, just track max carefully

Classic Problems: Maximum Average Subarray I (LC 643), Subarrays with Average ≥ Threshold (LC 1343)

Pattern 2 — Dynamic-Size Window (Shrinkable — Find Shortest)

When to use: Find the smallest/shortest subarray that meets a condition (sum ≥ target, contains all required characters, etc.).
How to arrive at this: You read "minimum size subarray with sum ≥ target". Brute force: try all subarrays → O(n²). Observation: if a subarray [left..right] has sum ≥ target, making it larger still satisfies the condition. So we want to shrink from the left. Strategy: expand right until condition is met, then shrink left as much as possible while condition holds. Each shrink step is a candidate for the answer.

Why the while loop works: Once the condition is met, shrinking from the left can only decrease the sum. Eventually the condition breaks, and we resume expanding right. The left pointer never goes backwards. 
// Minimum Size Subarray Sum (LC 209)
public int minSubArrayLen(int target, int[] nums) {
    int left = 0, windowSum = 0;
    int minLen = Integer.MAX_VALUE;

    for (int right = 0; right < nums.length; right++) {
        windowSum += nums[right];                  // expand

        while (windowSum >= target) {              // condition met → try to shrink
            minLen = Math.min(minLen, right - left + 1);
            windowSum -= nums[left];               // shrink
            left++;
        }
    }
    return minLen == Integer.MAX_VALUE ? 0 : minLen;
}
// Minimum Window Substring (LC 76) — the classic hard problem
public String minWindow(String s, String t) {
    if (s.length() < t.length()) return "";

    Map<Character, Integer> need = new HashMap<>();
    for (char c : t.toCharArray()) need.merge(c, 1, Integer::sum);

    int left = 0, formed = 0, required = need.size();
    int[] ans = {-1, 0, 0}; // {window length, left, right}
    Map<Character, Integer> windowCounts = new HashMap<>();

    for (int right = 0; right < s.length(); right++) {
        char c = s.charAt(right);
        windowCounts.merge(c, 1, Integer::sum);

        if (need.containsKey(c) && windowCounts.get(c).intValue() == need.get(c).intValue()) {
            formed++;
        }

        while (formed == required) {               // all characters found → shrink
            if (ans[0] == -1 || right - left + 1 < ans[0]) {
                ans[0] = right - left + 1;
                ans[1] = left;
                ans[2] = right;
            }
            char leftChar = s.charAt(left);
            windowCounts.merge(leftChar, -1, Integer::sum);
            if (need.containsKey(leftChar) &&
                windowCounts.get(leftChar) < need.get(leftChar)) {
                formed--;
            }
            left++;
        }
    }
    return ans[0] == -1 ? "" : s.substring(ans[1], ans[2] + 1);
}
Key insight for Minimum Window Substring: Use a formed counter to track how many unique characters have reached their required frequency. This avoids scanning the entire frequency map at each step — O(1) check instead of O(k).
Edge Cases:
  • No valid subarray exists → return 0 or empty string
  • Entire array is the answer → left never moves
  • Target is very small → answer might be a single element
  • t has duplicate characters (e.g., "AAC") → frequency map, not just presence
  • s shorter than t → impossible, return early

Classic Problems: Minimum Size Subarray Sum (LC 209), Minimum Window Substring (LC 76)

Pattern 3 — Dynamic-Size Window (Non-Shrinkable — Find Longest)

When to use: Find the longest/largest subarray where some condition holds (no repeating characters, at most K distinct, at most K replacements, etc.).
How to arrive at this: You read "longest substring without repeating characters". Brute force: try all substrings → O(n²). Observation: if [left..right] has no repeats, extending right might add a repeat. When it does, shrink left just enough to remove the duplicate. The window always represents a valid (no-repeat) substring. Track the maximum size.

Key difference from Pattern 2: In Pattern 2, the condition being met triggers shrinking (to minimize). In Pattern 3, the condition being violated triggers shrinking (to restore validity). You record the answer after the window is valid again. 
// Longest Substring Without Repeating Characters (LC 3)
public int lengthOfLongestSubstring(String s) {
    Map<Character, Integer> lastSeen = new HashMap<>();
    int left = 0, maxLen = 0;

    for (int right = 0; right < s.length(); right++) {
        char c = s.charAt(right);
        if (lastSeen.containsKey(c) && lastSeen.get(c) >= left) {
            left = lastSeen.get(c) + 1;  // jump left past the duplicate
        }
        lastSeen.put(c, right);
        maxLen = Math.max(maxLen, right - left + 1);
    }
    return maxLen;
}
Why lastSeen.get(c) >= left? The character might have been seen before, but outside the current window (to the left of left). Without this check, you'd incorrectly shrink the window for a "duplicate" that isn't actually in the current window. 
// Longest Repeating Character Replacement (LC 424)
public int characterReplacement(String s, int k) {
    int[] freq = new int[26];
    int left = 0, maxFreq = 0, maxLen = 0;

    for (int right = 0; right < s.length(); right++) {
        freq[s.charAt(right) - 'A']++;
        maxFreq = Math.max(maxFreq, freq[s.charAt(right) - 'A']);

        // window size - most frequent char = chars needing replacement
        while ((right - left + 1) - maxFreq > k) {
            freq[s.charAt(left) - 'A']--;
            left++;
        }
        maxLen = Math.max(maxLen, right - left + 1);
    }
    return maxLen;
}
Why we don't update maxFreq when shrinking: When we shrink, maxFreq might be stale. But that's OK! A stale (too high) maxFreq means the window might be slightly too generous, but we only care about the MAXIMUM valid window. A smaller maxFreq can only produce a shorter window, which we don't need. This subtle optimization avoids scanning all 26 frequencies each step.
Edge Cases:
  • Empty string → return 0
  • All same characters → entire string is the answer
  • k == 0 → longest run of a single character
  • k >= s.length() → entire string (can replace everything)

Classic Problems: Longest Substring Without Repeating Characters (LC 3), Longest Repeating Character Replacement (LC 424), Fruit Into Baskets (LC 904), Max Consecutive Ones III (LC 1004)

Pattern 4 — Window with HashMap/Counter

When to use: Anagram finding, permutation checking, or any problem with character frequency constraints.
How to arrive at this: You read "find all anagrams of p in s". An anagram has the same character frequencies. Brute force: for each starting index, check if the next p.length() characters are an anagram → O(n × m). But as the window slides, only one character enters and one leaves. Update frequency counts in O(1). Use a matches counter to track how many characters have the exact required frequency.

The matches counter trick: Instead of comparing the entire frequency array at each step (O(26)), maintain a counter that tracks how many of the 26 positions match. When matches == 26, the window is a valid anagram. O(1) per step. 
// Find All Anagrams in a String (LC 438) — simple version
public List<Integer> findAnagrams(String s, String p) {
    List<Integer> result = new ArrayList<>();
    if (s.length() < p.length()) return result;

    int[] pFreq = new int[26], wFreq = new int[26];
    for (char c : p.toCharArray()) pFreq[c - 'a']++;

    int windowSize = p.length();
    for (int i = 0; i < s.length(); i++) {
        wFreq[s.charAt(i) - 'a']++;                         // add right
        if (i >= windowSize) {
            wFreq[s.charAt(i - windowSize) - 'a']--;         // remove left
        }
        if (i >= windowSize - 1 && Arrays.equals(pFreq, wFreq)) {
            result.add(i - windowSize + 1);
        }
    }
    return result;
}
// Optimized version with matches counter (avoids Arrays.equals each step)
public List<Integer> findAnagrams(String s, String p) {
    List<Integer> result = new ArrayList<>();
    if (s.length() < p.length()) return result;

    int[] pFreq = new int[26], wFreq = new int[26];
    for (char c : p.toCharArray()) pFreq[c - 'a']++;

    int matches = 0;
    for (int i = 0; i < 26; i++) {
        if (pFreq[i] == 0) matches++;  // both are 0 → they match
    }

    int windowSize = p.length();
    for (int i = 0; i < s.length(); i++) {
        int idx = s.charAt(i) - 'a';
        wFreq[idx]++;
        if (wFreq[idx] == pFreq[idx]) matches++;
        else if (wFreq[idx] == pFreq[idx] + 1) matches--;

        if (i >= windowSize) {
            idx = s.charAt(i - windowSize) - 'a';
            wFreq[idx]--;
            if (wFreq[idx] == pFreq[idx]) matches++;
            else if (wFreq[idx] == pFreq[idx] - 1) matches--;
        }

        if (matches == 26) result.add(i - windowSize + 1);
    }
    return result;
}
// Permutation in String (LC 567) — same idea, return boolean
public boolean checkInclusion(String s1, String s2) {
    if (s1.length() > s2.length()) return false;

    int[] s1Freq = new int[26], wFreq = new int[26];
    for (char c : s1.toCharArray()) s1Freq[c - 'a']++;

    int windowSize = s1.length();
    for (int i = 0; i < s2.length(); i++) {
        wFreq[s2.charAt(i) - 'a']++;
        if (i >= windowSize) wFreq[s2.charAt(i - windowSize) - 'a']--;
        if (i >= windowSize - 1 && Arrays.equals(s1Freq, wFreq)) return true;
    }
    return false;
}
Edge Cases:
  • p longer than s → no possible anagram, return empty/false
  • p has duplicate characters → frequency map handles this naturally
  • Single character pattern → check if character exists in string
  • All same characters → every window of size p.length() is an anagram if all chars match

Classic Problems: Find All Anagrams (LC 438), Permutation in String (LC 567), Minimum Window Substring (LC 76)

Pattern 5 — Window with Monotonic Deque

When to use: Find the maximum or minimum of every window of size K. Brute force O(nK), heap O(n log n), monotonic deque O(n).
How to arrive at this: You read "sliding window maximum". For each window position, you need the max element. Key insight: if element a is to the left of element b in the window AND a <= b, then a can never be the maximum for any current or future window (because b will outlast a and is bigger). So discard a. Maintain a deque where elements are strictly decreasing from front to back. The front is always the current max.

Why deque beats heap: With a max-heap, removing the leftmost element takes O(n) to find or O(log n) with lazy deletion. With a monotonic deque, elements that can never be the answer are proactively removed. Each element is added and removed at most once → O(n) total. 
// Sliding Window Maximum (LC 239)
public int[] maxSlidingWindow(int[] nums, int k) {
    int n = nums.length;
    int[] result = new int[n - k + 1];
    Deque<Integer> deque = new ArrayDeque<>();  // stores INDICES

    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        // Remove indices that are out of the window
        while (!deque.isEmpty() && deque.peekFirst() < i - k + 1) {
            deque.pollFirst();
        }

        // Remove indices whose values are smaller than nums[i]
        while (!deque.isEmpty() && nums[deque.peekLast()] <= nums[i]) {
            deque.pollLast();
        }

        deque.offerLast(i);  // add current index

        if (i >= k - 1) {
            result[i - k + 1] = nums[deque.peekFirst()];  // front is the max
        }
    }
    return result;
}

Walk-through with nums = [1, 3, -1, -3, 5, 3, 6, 7], k = 3:

i=0: deque=[0]           (value 1)
i=1: deque=[1]           (3 > 1, remove 0, add 1)
i=2: deque=[1, 2]        (-1 < 3, just add)     → result[0] = nums[1] = 3
i=3: deque=[1, 2, 3]     (-3 < -1, just add)    → result[1] = nums[1] = 3
i=4: deque=[4]           (5 > all, clear & add)  → result[2] = nums[4] = 5
i=5: deque=[4, 5]        (3 < 5, just add)       → result[3] = nums[4] = 5
i=6: deque=[6]           (6 > all, clear & add)  → result[4] = nums[6] = 6
i=7: deque=[7]           (7 > 6, clear & add)    → result[5] = nums[7] = 7

Result: [3, 3, 5, 5, 6, 7]
The deque invariant: At all times, values at deque indices are in strictly decreasing order from front to back. The front is the current window maximum.
Edge Cases:
  • k == 1 → every element is its own max, return the array itself
  • k == nums.length → single window, return the global max
  • All elements equal → deque grows to size K, every window max is the same
  • Strictly increasing → deque always has size 1 (each new element clears it)
  • Strictly decreasing → deque grows to size K (nothing gets popped from back)

Classic Problems: Sliding Window Maximum (LC 239), Shortest Subarray with Sum at Least K (LC 862)

5. Pattern Recognition Cheatsheet

Problem SignalPattern to Use
"Subarray of exact size K" + max/min/averageFixed-Size Window
"Smallest subarray with sum ≥ target"Dynamic Shrinkable (Shortest)
"Minimum window containing all characters"Dynamic Shrinkable + HashMap
"Longest substring without repeating"Dynamic Non-Shrinkable (Longest)
"Longest with at most K distinct"Dynamic Longest + HashMap
"Longest with at most K replacements"Dynamic Longest + freq array
"Find all anagrams" / "permutation in string"Fixed Window + Counter/HashMap
"Sliding window maximum/minimum"Monotonic Deque
"At most K zeros you can flip"Dynamic Longest (count zeros in window)
"Fruits into baskets" (at most 2 types)Dynamic Longest + HashMap
"Exactly K distinct"atMost(K) - atMost(K-1) trick

6. Common Mistakes to Avoid

  1. Confusing sliding window with two pointers — Two pointers: sorted data, converging. Sliding window: contiguous subarrays, same direction.
  2. Forgetting to shrink — You expand but forget the shrink step. The window grows forever and you check the entire array as one "window".
  3. Recording the answer in the wrong place — Shortest: record inside while. Longest: record outside while. Swapping these gives wrong answers.
  4. Off-by-one in window boundaries — Window [left, right] has size right - left + 1. Fixed window of size K is ready when right >= k - 1.
  5. Using == vs .equals() for Integer in HashMap — Integer beyond [-128, 127] needs .equals(). Use .intValue() to be safe.
  6. Not handling "no valid window" case — If minLen stays Integer.MAX_VALUE, return 0 or "".
  7. Resetting maxFreq when shrinking in LC 424 — The stale value is intentional. Don't "fix" it.
  8. Using HashMap when int[26] suffices — For lowercase English letters, int[26] is faster.
  9. Storing values in deque instead of indices — You need indices to check if elements left the window.
  10. Forgetting "exactly K" = atMost(K) - atMost(K-1) — Can't solve "exactly K distinct" directly with sliding window.

7. Must-Solve Problems

Solve First (core pattern, do these on Day 1-2)

✔#ProblemPatternDifficulty

2Longest Substring Without Repeating Characters (LC 3)Dynamic LongestMedium

4Permutation in String (LC 567)Window + CounterMedium

Spaced Repetition Revisit (solve again after 5-7 days)

Pick any 3 you struggled with. Focus on Minimum Window Substring (hardest template), Sliding Window Maximum (deque mechanics), and Longest Repeating Character Replacement (stale maxFreq insight).

8. Quick Revision Card (1-min before interview)

Sliding Window = maintain contiguous [left, right] range, update state incrementally

Why O(n): each element enters once (right++) and leaves once (left++) → 2n total

Templates:
  Fixed:     slide window of size K, add right, remove left, process
  Shortest:  expand right, while condition MET → record + shrink left
  Longest:   expand right, while condition BROKEN → shrink left, then record

Patterns:
1. Fixed-Size         → exact size K, max sum/avg
2. Dynamic Shortest   → expand till met, shrink to minimize (LC 209, 76)
3. Dynamic Longest    → expand always, shrink only when broken (LC 3, 424)
4. Window + HashMap   → anagrams, permutations, frequency matching (LC 438, 567)
5. Monotonic Deque    → window max/min in O(n) (LC 239)

Key rules:
  - Both pointers move LEFT TO RIGHT only (never backwards)
  - Shortest: record INSIDE while loop. Longest: record OUTSIDE while loop.
  - HashMap counter trick: track "formed" count, avoid scanning map each step
  - Deque: store INDICES not values, front = current max, decreasing order
  - "Exactly K distinct" = atMost(K) - atMost(K-1)
  - int[26] > HashMap for lowercase English letters
  - LC 424: do NOT update maxFreq when shrinking (stale value is intentional)
04 / 18

Binary Search — Interview Notes (Java)

0. Building Intuition — How to Recognize a Binary Search Problem

Binary Search is not just "search in a sorted array" — it's a technique for eliminating half the search space with each comparison. Any time you have a monotonic condition over a range (false-false-false-true-true-true), binary search can find the boundary in O(log n).

Question 1: "Is the search space sorted or monotonic?"

  • It doesn't need to be a literally sorted array. Any space where the left half satisfies one condition and the right half satisfies another works.
  • Sorted array? Obviously yes. But "can we ship all packages in mid days?" is also monotonic — if we can do it in 10 days, we can do it in 11, 12, 13... days too.
Real-world analogy: You're looking up a word in a physical dictionary. You open to the middle, see if the word comes before or after, and eliminate half the pages. The dictionary works because it's sorted — but any "sorted-like" property lets you do the same thing.

Question 2: "Can I eliminate half the search space with one comparison?"

  • This is the fundamental property. If comparing the middle element tells you which half the answer is in, binary search works.
  • In a sorted array: if arr[mid] < target, the answer is in the right half.
  • In "binary search on answer": if canAchieve(mid) is true, the answer might be mid or smaller.
Real-world analogy: You're playing the number-guessing game. "I'm thinking of a number between 1 and 100." You guess 50. "Too high." Now you know it's between 1 and 49. One comparison, half the space gone.

Question 3: "Am I looking for a specific value, or a boundary/transition point?"

  • Specific value → Use while (left <= right) template. Returns exact match or -1.
  • Boundary → Use while (left < right) template. Converges to the transition point.
  • This distinction determines which template you use. Getting it wrong leads to infinite loops or off-by-one errors.
Real-world analogy: Specific value = finding a particular person's name in a phone book. Boundary = finding the first person whose last name starts with "M" — you just want where the transition happens.
Hack: Picking the wrong loop condition = infinite loop or missed answer.
while (left <= right) — search space can be empty (left > right). Use for exact match. Return inside loop or -1 after.
while (left < right) — converges to a single point (left == right). Use for boundaries (first true, last false). Answer is left after loop.
Critical pairing: With left < right, use right = mid (not mid - 1) when going left, and left = mid + 1 when going right. Mismatch causes infinite loops when right - left == 1.

The "Binary Search smell test" — keywords in problem statements

Keyword in problemWhat it signals
"sorted array" + "find target"Standard binary search
"first position", "leftmost"Lower bound (find boundary)
"last position", "rightmost"Upper bound (find boundary)
"first bad version"Lower bound (first true)
"minimum speed/capacity to achieve X"Binary search on answer
"minimize the maximum"Binary search on answer
"maximize the minimum"Binary search on answer
"rotated sorted array"Binary search on rotated
"sorted matrix"Binary search on matrix
"peak element"Binary search with condition
"square root", "kth smallest"Binary search on answer/condition

When it's NOT binary search

  • Data has no monotonic property → Linear scan
  • You need ALL elements that match → Full scan or HashMap
  • The search space is a graph or tree → BFS/DFS
  • Problem involves contiguous subarrays → Sliding Window
  • Problem needs pairs from both ends of sorted data → Two Pointers

0.1 Decision Tree — Which Pattern to Use?

Can I eliminate half the search space with one comparison?
|-- NO --> Not binary search. Consider linear scan, HashMap, or DP.
|
|-- YES -->
|   |-- Am I searching in a SORTED ARRAY for a target?
|   |   |-- Exact match needed? --> Pattern 1: Standard (left <= right)
|   |   |-- First/last occurrence? --> Pattern 2: Lower/Upper Bound (left < right)
|   |
|   |-- Is the sorted array ROTATED?
|   |   --> Pattern 3: Binary Search on Rotated Array
|   |
|   |-- Am I searching for an ANSWER VALUE (not index)?
|   |   |-- "Minimize the maximum" or "Maximize the minimum"?
|   |   |-- Can I write a feasibility function: canAchieve(mid)?
|   |       --> Pattern 4: Binary Search on Answer
|   |
|   |-- Am I searching in a 2D MATRIX?
|   |   --> Pattern 5: Binary Search on Matrix
|   |
|   |-- The search space is a RANGE where a condition flips?
|       --> Pattern 6: Binary Search with Condition Function

1. Core Concept

Binary Search = Repeatedly divide the search space in half. Each comparison eliminates half the remaining candidates.

Search for target = 9 in [1, 3, 5, 7, 9, 11, 13]:

Step 1: left=0, right=6, mid=3 → arr[3]=7 < 9 → go RIGHT
Step 2: left=4, right=6, mid=5 → arr[5]=11 > 9 → go LEFT
Step 3: left=4, right=4, mid=4 → arr[4]=9 == target → FOUND!

How the search space halves:
n → n/2 → n/4 → n/8 → ... → 1
Array of 16 elements: log2(16) = 4 comparisons

The TWO fundamental templates

Template A: "Find exact match" — while (left <= right)
  Search space: [left, right] inclusive
  Terminates when: left > right (empty)
  Each step: left = mid + 1 OR right = mid - 1 (exclude mid)

Template B: "Find boundary" — while (left < right)
  Search space: [left, right] converges to single point
  Terminates when: left == right (one candidate)
  Each step: left = mid + 1 OR right = mid (keep mid as candidate)
  Answer is at left (== right)
Why mid = left + (right - left) / 2 instead of (left + right) / 2? left + right can overflow Integer.MAX_VALUE. The safe formula avoids overflow. This is a standard interview expectation.
LEFT-BIASED vs RIGHT-BIASED mid:
  • mid = left + (right - left) / 2 → left-biased (rounds down). Pair with right = mid.
  • mid = left + (right - left + 1) / 2 → right-biased (rounds up). Pair with left = mid.
  • Left-biased mid + left = mid → INFINITE LOOP when right - left == 1!

2. Java Implementation Essentials

// --- Template A: Standard Binary Search (Find Exact Match) ---
int left = 0, right = arr.length - 1;
while (left <= right) {
    int mid = left + (right - left) / 2;
    if (arr[mid] == target) return mid;
    else if (arr[mid] < target) left = mid + 1;
    else right = mid - 1;
}
return -1;

// --- Template B: LEFT-BIASED Boundary (Find First True) ---
int left = 0, right = arr.length - 1;
while (left < right) {
    int mid = left + (right - left) / 2;       // left-biased
    if (condition(mid)) right = mid;            // mid might be answer, keep it
    else left = mid + 1;                        // mid not answer, skip it
}
// left == right == first position where condition is true

// --- Template B: RIGHT-BIASED Boundary (Find Last True) ---
int left = 0, right = arr.length - 1;
while (left < right) {
    int mid = left + (right - left + 1) / 2;   // right-biased (prevents infinite loop)
    if (condition(mid)) left = mid;             // mid might be answer, keep it
    else right = mid - 1;                       // mid not answer, skip it
}
// left == right == last position where condition is true

3. Time & Space Complexity

PatternTimeSpaceWhy
Standard binary searchO(log n)O(1)Halves search space each step
Lower/upper boundO(log n)O(1)Same halving logic
Rotated array searchO(log n)O(1)At least one half is always sorted
Binary search on answerO(log(range) × C)O(1)C = cost of feasibility check
Matrix (fully sorted)O(log(m×n))O(1)Treat as 1D array
Matrix (row-col sorted)O(m + n)O(1)Staircase search
Important: Binary search on answer = O(log(range) × C). For Koko Eating Bananas, C = O(n), so total is O(n × log(maxPile)). Break down both components for the interviewer.

4. The 6 Core Patterns

Pattern 1 — Standard Binary Search

When to use: Search for an exact target value in a sorted array. Return index or -1.
How to arrive at this: You read "given a sorted array, find target". Brute force: O(n). But the array is sorted — if arr[mid] < target, everything left of mid is also less. Eliminate the left half. Repeat. O(log n). 
// Binary Search (LC 704)
public int search(int[] nums, int target) {
    int left = 0, right = nums.length - 1;
    while (left <= right) {
        int mid = left + (right - left) / 2;
        if (nums[mid] == target) return mid;
        else if (nums[mid] < target) left = mid + 1;
        else right = mid - 1;
    }
    return -1;
}
Edge Cases:
  • Empty array → return -1 immediately
  • Single element → one comparison
  • Target smaller/larger than all elements → pointers cross, return -1
  • Duplicates → returns one (not guaranteed which)

Classic Problems: Binary Search (LC 704)

Pattern 2 — Lower Bound / Upper Bound (First/Last Occurrence)

THIS IS THE MOST IMPORTANT PATTERN. Find the first position where a condition becomes true (lower bound), or the last position (upper bound).
How to arrive at this: Standard binary search finds a match, but not the first or last. When you find a match, you can't stop — you need to keep searching in one direction. The boundary template naturally converges to the first/last position.

The mental model: Think of the array as [F, F, F, T, T, T]. Lower bound = first T. Upper bound = last T. 
Find first occurrence of 5 in [1, 3, 5, 5, 5, 7, 9]
Condition: nums[mid] >= 5

Index:    0  1  2  3  4  5  6
Values:   1  3  5  5  5  7  9
Cond:     F  F  T  T  T  T  T
               ^ first true = index 2 (lower bound)

// First Bad Version (LC 278)
public int firstBadVersion(int n) {
    int left = 1, right = n;
    while (left < right) {
        int mid = left + (right - left) / 2;
        if (isBadVersion(mid)) right = mid;     // mid might be first bad
        else left = mid + 1;                    // first bad is after mid
    }
    return left;
}

// Search Insert Position (LC 35)
public int searchInsert(int[] nums, int target) {
    int left = 0, right = nums.length;  // NOTE: right = length (can insert at end)
    while (left < right) {
        int mid = left + (right - left) / 2;
        if (nums[mid] >= target) right = mid;
        else left = mid + 1;
    }
    return left;
}

// Find First and Last Position (LC 34)
public int[] searchRange(int[] nums, int target) {
    if (nums.length == 0) return new int[]{-1, -1};
    int first = findFirst(nums, target);
    if (first == -1) return new int[]{-1, -1};
    return new int[]{first, findLast(nums, target)};
}

private int findFirst(int[] nums, int target) {
    int left = 0, right = nums.length - 1;
    while (left < right) {
        int mid = left + (right - left) / 2;         // left-biased
        if (nums[mid] >= target) right = mid;
        else left = mid + 1;
    }
    return nums[left] == target ? left : -1;
}

private int findLast(int[] nums, int target) {
    int left = 0, right = nums.length - 1;
    while (left < right) {
        int mid = left + (right - left + 1) / 2;     // RIGHT-BIASED
        if (nums[mid] <= target) left = mid;
        else right = mid - 1;
    }
    return nums[left] == target ? left : -1;
}
Why findLast needs right-biased mid: With left-biased mid and left = mid, when right - left == 1, mid = left, and left = mid doesn't change → infinite loop! Right-biased mid ensures mid = right in that case.
Edge Cases:
  • Target not in array → verify nums[left] == target after loop
  • All elements are target → first = 0, last = length - 1
  • Insert at very end → right must be nums.length (not length - 1)

Classic Problems: First Bad Version (LC 278), Find First and Last Position (LC 34), Search Insert Position (LC 35)

Pattern 3 — Binary Search on Rotated Array

When to use: Array was sorted then rotated. Search for target or find minimum.
How to arrive at this: The array isn't fully sorted, so standard binary search doesn't directly apply. Key insight: when you split a rotated array at mid, at least one half is always properly sorted. Determine which half is sorted, check if target falls in it. 
// Search in Rotated Sorted Array (LC 33)
public int search(int[] nums, int target) {
    int left = 0, right = nums.length - 1;
    while (left <= right) {
        int mid = left + (right - left) / 2;
        if (nums[mid] == target) return mid;
        if (nums[left] <= nums[mid]) {            // left half sorted
            if (nums[left] <= target && target < nums[mid])
                right = mid - 1;                    // target in sorted left half
            else left = mid + 1;
        } else {                                    // right half sorted
            if (nums[mid] < target && target <= nums[right])
                left = mid + 1;                     // target in sorted right half
            else right = mid - 1;
        }
    }
    return -1;
}

// Find Minimum in Rotated Sorted Array (LC 153)
public int findMin(int[] nums) {
    int left = 0, right = nums.length - 1;
    while (left < right) {
        int mid = left + (right - left) / 2;
        if (nums[mid] > nums[right]) left = mid + 1; // min is in right half
        else right = mid;                             // min is at mid or left
    }
    return nums[left];
}
Why compare with nums[right] not nums[left] for finding minimum? Comparing with nums[left] is ambiguous when the subarray is already sorted. Comparing with nums[right] is unambiguous in all cases.
Edge Cases:
  • Not rotated (rotation by 0) → works fine
  • Array of size 1 → returns that element
  • Duplicates (LC 81) → worst case O(n), need right-- fallback

Classic Problems: Search in Rotated Sorted Array (LC 33), Find Minimum in Rotated (LC 153)

Pattern 4 — Binary Search on Answer (VERY important for SDE 2)

When to use: "Minimize the maximum" or "Maximize the minimum". The answer space is monotonic.
How to arrive at this: You read "Koko wants to eat all bananas in H hours at speed K. Find minimum K." Brute force: try K = 1, 2, 3... But if K = 5 works, K = 6 also works. The answer space is monotonic: [F, F, F, T, T, T]. Binary search for the first T.

The template: 1) Define answer range [lo, hi]. 2) Write canAchieve(mid). 3) Binary search on the range. 4) Answer at left. 
// Koko Eating Bananas (LC 875)
public int minEatingSpeed(int[] piles, int h) {
    int left = 1, right = 0;
    for (int p : piles) right = Math.max(right, p);
    while (left < right) {
        int mid = left + (right - left) / 2;
        if (canFinish(piles, mid, h)) right = mid;
        else left = mid + 1;
    }
    return left;
}
private boolean canFinish(int[] piles, int k, int h) {
    int hours = 0;
    for (int p : piles) hours += (p + k - 1) / k; // ceil division
    return hours <= h;
}

// Capacity to Ship Packages (LC 1011)
public int shipWithinDays(int[] weights, int days) {
    int left = 0, right = 0;
    for (int w : weights) { left = Math.max(left, w); right += w; }
    while (left < right) {
        int mid = left + (right - left) / 2;
        if (canShip(weights, mid, days)) right = mid;
        else left = mid + 1;
    }
    return left;
}
private boolean canShip(int[] weights, int cap, int days) {
    int needed = 1, load = 0;
    for (int w : weights) {
        if (load + w > cap) { needed++; load = 0; }
        load += w;
    }
    return needed <= days;
}

// Split Array Largest Sum (LC 410) — "minimize the maximum"
public int splitArray(int[] nums, int m) {
    int left = 0, right = 0;
    for (int n : nums) { left = Math.max(left, n); right += n; }
    while (left < right) {
        int mid = left + (right - left) / 2;
        if (canSplit(nums, mid, m)) right = mid;
        else left = mid + 1;
    }
    return left;
}
private boolean canSplit(int[] nums, int maxSum, int m) {
    int groups = 1, sum = 0;
    for (int n : nums) {
        if (sum + n > maxSum) { groups++; sum = 0; }
        sum += n;
    }
    return groups <= m;
}
Notice the pattern: All three problems have the EXACT same structure: 1) Define [lo, hi], 2) Write greedy feasibility check O(n), 3) Binary search on the range.
Edge Cases:
  • lo == hi → only one possible answer
  • Packages must be shipped in order → greedy check respects this
  • m == 1 → total sum; m == length → max element
  • Integer overflow in sum → use long

Classic Problems: Koko Eating Bananas (LC 875), Capacity to Ship Packages (LC 1011), Split Array Largest Sum (LC 410)

Pattern 5 — Binary Search on Matrix

When to use: Sorted 2D matrix — either fully sorted or row/column sorted.
How to arrive at this: If the matrix is fully sorted (row-by-row), treat it as a 1D array: row = mid / cols, col = mid % cols. If only row-sorted and column-sorted (LC 240), use staircase search from top-right corner. 
// Search a 2D Matrix (LC 74) — fully sorted
public boolean searchMatrix(int[][] matrix, int target) {
    int rows = matrix.length, cols = matrix[0].length;
    int left = 0, right = rows * cols - 1;
    while (left <= right) {
        int mid = left + (right - left) / 2;
        int value = matrix[mid / cols][mid % cols];
        if (value == target) return true;
        else if (value < target) left = mid + 1;
        else right = mid - 1;
    }
    return false;
}

// Search a 2D Matrix II (LC 240) — row & column sorted, staircase search
public boolean searchMatrix(int[][] matrix, int target) {
    int row = 0, col = matrix[0].length - 1; // start top-right
    while (row < matrix.length && col >= 0) {
        if (matrix[row][col] == target) return true;
        else if (matrix[row][col] > target) col--;  // eliminate column
        else row++;                                   // eliminate row
    }
    return false;
}
Why start at top-right? Moving left decreases value, moving down increases. This gives a binary decision at each step. Top-left doesn't work: both directions increase.
Edge Cases:
  • One row/column → degenerates to 1D search
  • Target out of range → return false immediately

Classic Problems: Search a 2D Matrix (LC 74), Search a 2D Matrix II (LC 240)

Pattern 6 — Binary Search with Condition Function (Advanced)

When to use: Search space is a range where a condition transitions from false to true. Peak finding, square root, median of arrays.
How to arrive at this: "Find peak element" — no sorted array, but if nums[mid] < nums[mid+1], a peak MUST exist to the right (values can't keep going up forever — the array ends). Eliminate the left half. That's binary search on a condition. 
// Find Peak Element (LC 162)
public int findPeakElement(int[] nums) {
    int left = 0, right = nums.length - 1;
    while (left < right) {
        int mid = left + (right - left) / 2;
        if (nums[mid] < nums[mid + 1]) left = mid + 1; // peak is right (uphill)
        else right = mid;                                // peak is at mid or left
    }
    return left;
}

// Sqrt(x) (LC 69) — find last n where n*n <= x
public int mySqrt(int x) {
    if (x < 2) return x;
    long left = 1, right = x / 2;
    while (left < right) {
        long mid = left + (right - left + 1) / 2;  // right-biased
        if (mid * mid <= x) left = mid;
        else right = mid - 1;
    }
    return (int) left;
}

// Median of Two Sorted Arrays (LC 4) — O(log(min(m,n)))
public double findMedianSortedArrays(int[] nums1, int[] nums2) {
    if (nums1.length > nums2.length)
        return findMedianSortedArrays(nums2, nums1);
    int m = nums1.length, n = nums2.length;
    int left = 0, right = m;
    while (left <= right) {
        int i = left + (right - left) / 2;
        int j = (m + n + 1) / 2 - i;
        int maxL1 = (i == 0) ? Integer.MIN_VALUE : nums1[i - 1];
        int minR1 = (i == m) ? Integer.MAX_VALUE : nums1[i];
        int maxL2 = (j == 0) ? Integer.MIN_VALUE : nums2[j - 1];
        int minR2 = (j == n) ? Integer.MAX_VALUE : nums2[j];
        if (maxL1 <= minR2 && maxL2 <= minR1) {
            if ((m + n) % 2 == 0)
                return (Math.max(maxL1, maxL2) + Math.min(minR1, minR2)) / 2.0;
            else return Math.max(maxL1, maxL2);
        } else if (maxL1 > minR2) right = i - 1;
        else left = i + 1;
    }
    throw new IllegalArgumentException();
}
Median of Two Sorted Arrays idea: Split both arrays into left/right halves so left halves have (m+n+1)/2 elements and every left element ≤ every right element. Binary search on partition position in the shorter array.
Edge Cases:
  • Peak: single element → it's the peak. Strictly increasing → peak at end.
  • Sqrt: x = 0 or 1 → return x. Use long to prevent overflow.
  • Median: one array empty → handle with MIN_VALUE/MAX_VALUE boundaries.

Classic Problems: Find Peak Element (LC 162), Sqrt(x) (LC 69), Median of Two Sorted Arrays (LC 4)

5. Pattern Recognition Cheatsheet

Problem SignalPattern to Use
"Sorted array" + "find target"Standard Binary Search
"First occurrence" / "leftmost"Lower Bound (left-biased)
"Last occurrence" / "rightmost"Upper Bound (right-biased)
"Insert position" / "first >= target"Lower Bound
"First bad version"Lower Bound (first true)
"Rotated sorted array"Rotated Array Search
"Minimize the maximum"Binary Search on Answer
"Maximize the minimum"Binary Search on Answer
"Minimum speed/capacity to finish"Binary Search on Answer
"Sorted matrix"Matrix Binary Search or Staircase
"Peak element"Condition Function
"Square root" / "kth root"Condition Function (last true)
"Median of two sorted arrays"Partition Binary Search

6. Common Mistakes to Avoid

  1. Using (left + right) / 2 — Overflows. Always use left + (right - left) / 2.
  2. Confusing the two templates — left <= right for exact match, left < right for boundaries. Wrong template = infinite loop or missed answers.
  3. Infinite loop from wrong mid — left = mid needs right-biased mid. Left-biased + left = mid = infinite loop when right - left == 1.
  4. Not verifying after boundary search — The boundary template converges to a position, but it might not contain the target. Always check nums[left] == target.
  5. Wrong right initialization — Search Insert Position needs right = nums.length (not length - 1) since insertion can be at the end.
  6. Comparing with nums[left] in Find Minimum of Rotated — Use nums[right]. Comparing with nums[left] is ambiguous when subarray is already sorted.
  7. Not using long for Sqrt(x) — mid * mid overflows int for large values.
  8. Wrong answer range in BS on Answer — lo = max single element (not 0 or 1). hi = total sum. Getting lo wrong is the most common error.
  9. Non-monotonic feasibility function — canAchieve(mid) must be monotonic. If not, binary search gives wrong results.
  10. Confusing LC 74 with LC 240 — LC 74 (fully sorted): 1D binary search O(log(mn)). LC 240 (row+col sorted): staircase O(m+n). Different structures.

⚠️ LC 878 - Nth Magical Number - Critical Overflow Bug

Problem: A positive integer is magical if it's divisible by either a or b. Return the nth magical number modulo 10⁹ + 7.

Key Insight - Inclusion-Exclusion Principle:

For any number mid, count of magical numbers ≤ mid:

count = mid/a + mid/b - mid/LCM(a,b)
        ↑       ↑       ↑
     multiples multiples common
     of a      of b    (subtract to avoid double-counting)

Example: a=2, b=3, mid=10

  • Multiples of 2: {2,4,6,8,10} → 10/2 = 5
  • Multiples of 3: {3,6,9} → 10/3 = 3
  • Common (LCM=6): {6} → 10/6 = 1
  • Total magical: 5 + 3 - 1 = 7

❌ Bug - Fails for test case: n=1000000000, a=40000, b=40000

❌ Wrong Code: 
long high = Math.min(a,b) * n;  // INTEGER OVERFLOW!

Why it fails:

  • a, b, n are all int parameters
  • Math.min(a,b) returns int 40000
  • 40000 * 1000000000 is calculated as int multiplication
  • Result: 40,000,000,000,000 overflows int (max ~2.1 billion)
  • Garbage value assigned to high → binary search breaks

✅ Fixed Solution:

public int nthMagicalNumber(int n, int a, int b) {
    long low = Math.min(a, b);
    long high = (long)Math.min(a, b) * n;  // ← Cast BEFORE multiplication
    long ans = -1;
    long lcm = getLCM(a, b);
    
    while (low <= high) {
        long mid = low + (high - low) / 2;
        long midIndex = mid/a + mid/b - mid/lcm;
        
        if (midIndex < n)
            low = mid + 1;       // Need more magical numbers
        else if (midIndex > n)
            high = mid - 1;      // Overshot, go left
        else {                   // midIndex == n
            ans = mid;           // Potential answer
            high = mid - 1;      // Find smallest valid answer on left
        }
    }
    
    int MOD = (int)Math.pow(10, 9) + 7;
    return (int)(ans % MOD);
}

long getLCM(long a, long b) {
    return (a * b) / gcd(a, b);
}

long gcd(long a, long b) {
    return b == 0 ? a : gcd(b, a % b);
}

🔑 Key Takeaways:

  1. Cast before multiplication — (long)a * b not a * b then assign to long
  2. Three-way split is correct — midIndex == n case needs special handling to find smallest valid answer
  3. Why search left when count == n? — Multiple non-magical numbers can have same count; we need the actual magical number
  4. Overflow debugging tip — When mysterious failures on large inputs, check every arithmetic operation involving large numbers

Spaced Repetition Revisit (solve again after 5-7 days)

Pick any 3 you struggled with. Focus on Find First and Last Position (left-biased vs right-biased), Split Array Largest Sum (feasibility function), and Median of Two Sorted Arrays (partition logic).

8. Quick Revision Card (1-min before interview)

Binary Search = eliminate half the search space with each comparison
Works on ANY monotonic space, not just sorted arrays

mid = left + (right - left) / 2  ← prevents overflow

TWO Templates:
  Exact match:  while (left <= right)  → returns index or -1
  Boundary:     while (left < right)   → converges to transition point

Boundary subtypes:
  First true:  left-biased mid,  right = mid,    left = mid + 1
  Last true:   right-biased mid, left = mid,     right = mid - 1
  RIGHT-BIASED = left + (right - left + 1) / 2  ← prevents infinite loop

6 Patterns:
1. Standard         → sorted array, find target (LC 704)
2. Lower/Upper      → first/last occurrence, insert point (LC 34, 35, 278)
3. Rotated Array    → one half always sorted (LC 33, 153)
4. BS on Answer     → "minimize max"/"maximize min", canAchieve() (LC 875, 1011, 410)
5. Matrix           → fully sorted: 1D index (LC 74). Row+col: staircase (LC 240)
6. Condition Func   → peak, sqrt, median (LC 162, 69, 4)

Key rules:
  - left-biased mid + right = mid → safe (find first true)
  - right-biased mid + left = mid → safe (find last true)
  - left-biased mid + left = mid → INFINITE LOOP!
  - BS on Answer: lo = min possible, hi = max possible, canAchieve MUST be monotonic
  - Rotated: compare nums[mid] with nums[right] (not nums[left])
  - Verify nums[left] == target after boundary search
  - Use long for mid*mid in sqrt problems

BS on Answer template:
  1. Define [lo, hi] range
  2. Write canAchieve(mid) → boolean (monotonic!)
  3. if canAchieve → right = mid, else left = mid + 1
  4. Answer at left
05 / 18

Stack & Monotonic Stack

Comprehensive DSA Notes for SDE 2 Interview Prep

Section 1: Topic Overview

What is a Stack?

A Stack is a linear data structure following LIFO (Last In, First Out). Think of a stack of plates — you can only add or remove from the top.

What is a Monotonic Stack?

A Monotonic Stack maintains elements in sorted order (increasing or decreasing from bottom to top). When a new element violates the monotonic property, we pop until it's restored. This "controlled popping" gives us the answers we need.

Interview Frequency

Very High for SDE 2 at Indian product companies (Google, Amazon, Microsoft, Flipkart, PhonePe, Swiggy, Meesho). Monotonic Stack is a differentiator — knowing it signals SDE 2 readiness. Expect 1-2 stack questions in a typical interview loop.

Section 2: Core Concepts & Theory

Stack Operations (All O(1))

OperationDescription
push(x)Add element to the top
pop()Remove and return top element
peek()View top element without removing
isEmpty()Check if stack is empty

Java Implementations

// PREFERRED: ArrayDeque
Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
stack.push(10);     // pushes to front (acts as top)
stack.pop();        // removes from front
stack.peek();       // views the front
stack.isEmpty();    // check empty

// AVOID: java.util.Stack (synchronized, slower, extends Vector)
Interview tip: Always say "I'll use ArrayDeque as my stack since it's faster than the legacy Stack class." This shows awareness.

Monotonic Stack: The Big Idea

Monotonic Increasing Stack (bottom→top: small→large):

  • Pop when current < stack top
  • Finds: Next Smaller, Previous Smaller

Monotonic Decreasing Stack (bottom→top: large→small):

  • Pop when current > stack top
  • Finds: Next Greater, Previous Greater

Quick Decision Table

You need to find...Stack TypePop condition
Next Greater ElementMonotonic Decreasingcurrent > top
Next Smaller ElementMonotonic Increasingcurrent < top
Previous Greater ElementMonotonic Decreasingcurrent >= top
Previous Smaller ElementMonotonic Increasingcurrent <= top
Analogy: Think of a monotonic decreasing stack like a row of people standing in line, where only taller people are visible from behind. When a very tall person arrives, all shorter people "lose" — the tall person is their "next greater element."

Section 3: Pattern Recognition & Intuition Building

Pattern 1: Basic Stack (Matching / Validation)
How to Arrive at This: Ask yourself: "Am I matching things in a nested or last-opened-first-closed manner?" Matching opening/closing brackets, nested structures, undo operations — the LIFO property is the natural fit.
Analogy: Russian nesting dolls (matryoshka). You open the outermost first, but the last doll you opened is the first one you close. Exactly LIFO.

valid balanced matching nested

Valid Parentheses (LC 20) - Easy

public boolean isValid(String s) {
    Deque<Character> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
    for (char c : s.toCharArray()) {
        if (c == '(' || c == '{' || c == '[') {
            stack.push(c);
        } else {
            if (stack.isEmpty()) return false;
            char top = stack.pop();
            if (c == ')' && top != '(') return false;
            if (c == '}' && top != '{') return false;
            if (c == ']' && top != '[') return false;
        }
    }
    return stack.isEmpty();
}

Min Stack (LC 155) - Medium

Maintain a parallel stack that tracks the minimum at each level.

class MinStack {
    Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
    Deque<Integer> minStack = new ArrayDeque<>();

    public void push(int val) {
        stack.push(val);
        if (minStack.isEmpty() || val <= minStack.peek()) {
            minStack.push(val);
        } else {
            minStack.push(minStack.peek()); // carry forward current min
        }
    }

    public void pop() { stack.pop(); minStack.pop(); }
    public int top() { return stack.peek(); }
    public int getMin() { return minStack.peek(); }
}
Pattern 2: Next Greater / Smaller Element (Monotonic Stack)
How to Arrive at This:
1. Brute force: For each element, scan right to find next greater. O(n²).
2. Observation: If A < B < C, and you're looking for "next greater" of something before A, you'd never pick A or B if C exists. Smaller elements "blocked" by a bigger one are useless.
3. Key insight: Pop elements smaller than current — current IS their "next greater." This is the monotonic stack.
Analogy: People in a queue looking right for someone taller. When a tall person arrives, every shorter person ahead can now "see" this tall person as their answer. They get popped (resolved).

next greater element next warmer day days until next smaller

Template: Next Greater Element (Left to Right)

public int[] nextGreaterElement(int[] arr) {
    int n = arr.length;
    int[] result = new int[n];
    Arrays.fill(result, -1);
    Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>(); // stores INDICES

    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        while (!stack.isEmpty() && arr[stack.peek()] < arr[i]) {
            int idx = stack.pop();
            result[idx] = arr[i]; // current is their "next greater"
        }
        stack.push(i);
    }
    return result;
}
Right-to-left intuition for "next greater":
Add every element from right to the stack. For the current element, the stack top is the answer — unless it's less than or equal to current. Pop such tops: they can't be the answer for current or any future element (because current is both closer and bigger, permanently blocking them). The stack naturally ends up monotonically decreasing because smaller elements get popped before they can stay.

Stack type cheat sheet:
Next Greater → decreasing stack (pop smaller/equal, greater elements survive as candidate answers)
Next Smaller → increasing stack (pop larger/equal, smaller elements survive as candidate answers)

Daily Temperatures (LC 739) - Medium

"How many days until warmer?" = Next Greater but return distance, not value.

Left to Right (resolve at pop time)
public int[] dailyTemperatures(int[] temperatures) {
    int n = temperatures.length;
    int[] result = new int[n];
    Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();

    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        while (!stack.isEmpty() &&
          temperatures[stack.peek()] < temperatures[i]) {
            int idx = stack.pop();
            result[idx] = i - idx; // distance
        }
        stack.push(i);
    }
    return result;
}
Right to Left (resolve at visit time)
public int[] dailyTemperatures(int[] temperatures) {
    int n = temperatures.length;
    int[] ans = new int[n];
    Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();

    for (int i = n - 1; i >= 0; i--) {
        while (!stack.isEmpty() &&
          temperatures[i] >= temperatures[stack.peek()])
            stack.pop();
        ans[i] = stack.isEmpty() ? 0 : stack.peek() - i;
        stack.push(i);
    }
    return ans;
}

Next Greater Element II (LC 503) - Medium (Circular)

Circular array trick: Iterate through 2*n elements using i % n.
Left to Right (resolve at pop time)
public int[] nextGreaterElements(int[] nums) {
    int n = nums.length;
    int[] result = new int[n];
    Arrays.fill(result, -1);
    Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();

    for (int i = 0; i < 2 * n; i++) {
        while (!stack.isEmpty() &&
          nums[stack.peek()] < nums[i % n]) {
            result[stack.pop()] = nums[i % n];
        }
        if (i < n) stack.push(i);
    }
    return result;
}
Right to Left (resolve at visit time)
public int[] nextGreaterElements(int[] nums) {
    int n = nums.length;
    int[] result = new int[n];
    Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();

    for (int i = 2 * n - 1; i >= 0; i--) {
        while (!stack.isEmpty() &&
          stack.peek() <= nums[i % n])
            stack.pop();
        result[i % n] = stack.isEmpty() ? -1 : stack.peek();
        stack.push(nums[i % n]);
    }
    return result;
}
Pattern 3: Previous Greater / Smaller Element
How to Arrive at This:
1. "I need the nearest element to the LEFT that is greater/smaller."
2. Similar to Pattern 2, but the answer is determined at push time, not pop time.
3. When about to push element i, the current stack top IS the "previous greater/smaller" for i.
Analogy: Walking into a room of people (the stack). Before you join, you look at who's right in front — that's your "previous." But first, you kick out anyone shorter (for previous greater) because they're irrelevant for future people.

span consecutive looking back previous greater/smaller

Online Stock Span (LC 901) - Medium

class StockSpanner {
    Deque<int[]> stack; // [price, span]

    public StockSpanner() { stack = new ArrayDeque<>(); }

    public int next(int price) {
        int span = 1;
        while (!stack.isEmpty() && stack.peek()[0] <= price) {
            span += stack.pop()[1]; // absorb their spans
        }
        stack.push(new int[]{price, span});
        return span;
    }
}

Template: Previous Smaller Element

public int[] previousSmallerElement(int[] arr) {
    int n = arr.length;
    int[] result = new int[n];
    Arrays.fill(result, -1);
    Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();

    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        while (!stack.isEmpty() && arr[stack.peek()] >= arr[i]) {
            stack.pop();
        }
        result[i] = stack.isEmpty() ? -1 : stack.peek(); // top IS previous smaller
        stack.push(i);
    }
    return result;
}
Pattern 4: Stack for Expression / Calculator
How to Arrive at This:
1. Expressions have nesting (parentheses) and precedence (* before +).
2. Nesting = LIFO = stack.
3. Process high-precedence operators (* /) immediately, push low-precedence (+ -) results to stack, sum at end.
Analogy: A manager handling tasks. Urgent tasks (* and /) are handled immediately. Non-urgent tasks (+ and -) are put in a pile (stack) to deal with at the end.

evaluate calculator expression reverse polish

Evaluate Reverse Polish Notation (LC 150) - Medium

public int evalRPN(String[] tokens) {
    Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
    for (String token : tokens) {
        if ("+-*/".contains(token)) {
            int b = stack.pop(); // second operand (popped first!)
            int a = stack.pop(); // first operand
            switch (token) {
                case "+": stack.push(a + b); break;
                case "-": stack.push(a - b); break;
                case "*": stack.push(a * b); break;
                case "/": stack.push(a / b); break;
            }
        } else {
            stack.push(Integer.parseInt(token));
        }
    }
    return stack.peek();
}

Basic Calculator II (LC 227) - Medium

public int calculate(String s) {
    Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
    int num = 0;
    char prevOp = '+';

    for (int i = 0; i < s.length(); i++) {
        char c = s.charAt(i);
        if (Character.isDigit(c)) {
            num = num * 10 + (c - '0');
        }
        if ((!Character.isDigit(c) && c != ' ') || i == s.length() - 1) {
            switch (prevOp) {
                case '+': stack.push(num); break;
                case '-': stack.push(-num); break;
                case '*': stack.push(stack.pop() * num); break;
                case '/': stack.push(stack.pop() / num); break;
            }
            prevOp = c;
            num = 0;
        }
    }
    int result = 0;
    while (!stack.isEmpty()) result += stack.pop();
    return result;
}
Pattern 5: Monotonic Stack + Area / Width Calculation
HARDEST & MOST IMPORTANT pattern for SDE 2 interviews.
How to Arrive at This:
1. "Largest rectangle in histogram" — for each bar, how far left/right can it extend as the shortest?
2. Rephrased: find nearest shorter bar on left and nearest shorter bar on right.
3. This is "previous smaller" + "next smaller" = two monotonic stack passes!
4. Or a single pass: when a bar is popped, current = right boundary, new top = left boundary.
5. Width = right - left - 1. Area = height × width.
Analogy: Each histogram bar is a person who can "stretch" their arms left and right until meeting someone shorter. The rectangle = height × arm span. Monotonic stack computes this arm span efficiently.

largest rectangle maximum area trapped water subarray min/max contribution

Largest Rectangle in Histogram (LC 84) - Hard

Sentinel trick (i <= n)
public int largestRectangleArea(int[] heights) {
    int n = heights.length, maxArea = 0;
    Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();

    for (int i = 0; i <= n; i++) {
        int h = (i == n) ? 0 : heights[i];
        while (!stack.isEmpty() &&
          heights[stack.peek()] > h) {
            int height = heights[stack.pop()];
            int width = stack.isEmpty()
              ? i : (i - stack.peek() - 1);
            maxArea = Math.max(maxArea, height * width);
        }
        stack.push(i);
    }
    return maxArea;
}
Explicit cleanup loop
public int largestRectangleArea(int[] heights) {
    int n = heights.length, maxArea = 0;
    Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();

    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        while (!stack.isEmpty() &&
          heights[i] < heights[stack.peek()]) {
            int element = stack.pop();
            int nextSmallerEle = i;
            int prevSmallerEle = stack.isEmpty()
              ? -1 : stack.peek();
            int width = nextSmallerEle - prevSmallerEle - 1;
            maxArea = Math.max(maxArea,
              heights[element] * width);
        }
        stack.push(i);
    }
    while (!stack.isEmpty()) {
        int element = stack.pop();
        int nextSmallerEle = n;
        int prevSmallerEle = stack.isEmpty()
          ? -1 : stack.peek();
        int width = nextSmallerEle - prevSmallerEle - 1;
        maxArea = Math.max(maxArea,
          heights[element] * width);
    }
    return maxArea;
}
Walkthrough for heights = [2, 1, 5, 6, 2, 3]:
iHeightActionStackmaxArea
02Push 0[0]0
11Pop 0 (h=2, w=1)[1]2
25Push 2[1,2]2
36Push 3[1,2,3]2
42Pop 3 (h=6,w=1), Pop 2 (h=5,w=2)[1,4]10
53Push 5[1,4,5]10
60 (sentinel)Pop 5,4,1[]10

Answer: 10 (bar h=5, width 2: indices 2-3)

Trapping Rain Water (LC 42) - Hard

Monotonic stack approach (layer by layer, horizontal slicing):

public int trap(int[] height) {
    int water = 0;
    Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>(); // monotonic decreasing

    for (int i = 0; i < height.length; i++) {
        while (!stack.isEmpty() && height[stack.peek()] < height[i]) {
            int bottom = height[stack.pop()];
            if (stack.isEmpty()) break; // no left wall

            int leftWall = stack.peek();
            int width = i - leftWall - 1;
            int boundedHeight = Math.min(height[leftWall], height[i]) - bottom;
            water += width * boundedHeight;
        }
        stack.push(i);
    }
    return water;
}
Trapping Rain Water also has a classic two-pointer solution. Know both approaches for interviews.

Sum of Subarray Minimums (LC 907) - Medium

The "contribution" technique: Instead of finding the min of every subarray, ask "how many subarrays is arr[i] the minimum of?"
left[i] = distance to previous smaller. right[i] = distance to next smaller.
Contribution = arr[i] × left[i] × right[i] 
public int sumSubarrayMins(int[] arr) {
    int MOD = 1_000_000_007;
    int n = arr.length;

    // Previous Smaller or Equal (use >= to handle duplicates)
    int[] left = new int[n];
    Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        while (!stack.isEmpty() && arr[stack.peek()] >= arr[i]) stack.pop();
        left[i] = stack.isEmpty() ? (i + 1) : (i - stack.peek());
        stack.push(i);
    }

    // Next Strictly Smaller (use > to avoid double-counting)
    int[] right = new int[n];
    stack.clear();
    for (int i = n - 1; i >= 0; i--) {
        while (!stack.isEmpty() && arr[stack.peek()] > arr[i]) stack.pop();
        right[i] = stack.isEmpty() ? (n - i) : (stack.peek() - i);
        stack.push(i);
    }

    long result = 0;
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        result = (result + (long) arr[i] * left[i] % MOD * right[i] % MOD) % MOD;
    }
    return (int) result;
}
Duplicate handling in LC 907: Use >= on one side and > on the other to avoid double-counting. This is a very common interview follow-up!
Pattern 6: Stack for String Manipulation
How to Arrive at This: "I'm building a result character by character, and sometimes need to go back and undo." Going back to undo the last thing = LIFO = stack.
Analogy: Typing on a keyboard. Each character goes on the "stack." Backspace removes the last character. These problems are like processing a sequence of keystrokes.

remove duplicates decode simplify build string backspace

Remove All Adjacent Duplicates (LC 1047) - Easy

public String removeDuplicates(String s) {
    Deque<Character> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
    for (char c : s.toCharArray()) {
        if (!stack.isEmpty() && stack.peek() == c) {
            stack.pop();
        } else {
            stack.push(c);
        }
    }
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    while (!stack.isEmpty()) sb.append(stack.pop());
    return sb.reverse().toString();
}

Decode String (LC 394) - Medium

Two stacks (counts + strings)
public String decodeString(String s) {
    Deque<StringBuilder> stringStack = new ArrayDeque<>();
    Deque<Integer> countStack = new ArrayDeque<>();
    StringBuilder current = new StringBuilder();
    int k = 0;

    for (char c : s.toCharArray()) {
        if (Character.isDigit(c)) {
            k = k * 10 + (c - '0');
        } else if (c == '[') {
            countStack.push(k);
            stringStack.push(current);
            current = new StringBuilder();
            k = 0;
        } else if (c == ']') {
            int count = countStack.pop();
            StringBuilder prev = stringStack.pop();
            for (int i = 0; i < count; i++)
                prev.append(current);
            current = prev;
        } else {
            current.append(c);
        }
    }
    return current.toString();
}
Single string stack
Approach:
1. Push every char as a string until ]
2. On ]: Pop & prepend (insert(0)) until [ to collect content. Pop [. Pop digits to build number. Repeat content that many times. Push result back as single token.
3. After loop: Pop all remaining, prepend to result.
Key: Always prepend when popping — never append+reverse — because multi-char decoded tokens would get their internals reversed. 
public String decodeString(String s) {
    Deque<String> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
    for (char ch : s.toCharArray()) {
        if (ch != ']') {
            stack.push(ch + "");
        } else {
            StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
            while (!stack.peek().equals("["))
                sb.insert(0, stack.pop());
            stack.pop(); // remove "["
            String num = "";
            while (!stack.isEmpty() &&
              Character.isDigit(stack.peek().charAt(0)))
                num = stack.pop() + num;
            int n = Integer.parseInt(num);
            StringBuilder temp = new StringBuilder();
            for (int i = 0; i < n; i++)
                temp.append(sb);
            stack.push(temp.toString());
        }
    }
    StringBuilder result = new StringBuilder();
    while (!stack.isEmpty())
        result.insert(0, stack.pop());
    return result.toString();
}

Simplify Path (LC 71) - Medium

public String simplifyPath(String path) {
    Deque<String> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
    for (String part : path.split("/")) {
        if (part.equals("..")) {
            if (!stack.isEmpty()) stack.pop();
        } else if (!part.equals("") && !part.equals(".")) {
            stack.push(part);
        }
    }
    StringBuilder result = new StringBuilder();
    for (String dir : stack) result.insert(0, "/" + dir);
    return result.length() == 0 ? "/" : result.toString();
}

Section 4: Code Templates (Copy-Paste Ready)

Template 1: Next Greater Element

Left to Right (resolve at pop time)
// Monotonic Decreasing Stack
Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>(); // INDICES
for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
    while (!stack.isEmpty() &&
      arr[stack.peek()] < arr[i]) {
        result[stack.pop()] = arr[i];
    }
    stack.push(i);
}
Right to Left (resolve at visit time)
// Monotonic Decreasing Stack
Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
for (int i = n - 1; i >= 0; i--) {
    while (!stack.isEmpty() &&
      stack.peek() <= arr[i])
        stack.pop();
    result[i] = stack.isEmpty() ? -1 : stack.peek();
    stack.push(arr[i]); // VALUES (or indices if need distance)
}

for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
    while (!stack.isEmpty() &&
      arr[stack.peek()] > arr[i]) {
        result[stack.pop()] = arr[i];
    }
    stack.push(i);
}
Right to Left (resolve at visit time)
// Monotonic Increasing Stack

for (int i = n - 1; i >= 0; i--) {
    while (!stack.isEmpty() &&
      stack.peek() >= arr[i])
        stack.pop();

Template 3: Histogram / Area (Single Pass)

Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
for (int i = 0; i <= n; i++) {
    int h = (i == n) ? 0 : heights[i];
    while (!stack.isEmpty() && heights[stack.peek()] > h) {
        int height = heights[stack.pop()];
        int width = stack.isEmpty() ? i : (i - stack.peek() - 1);
        maxArea = Math.max(maxArea, height * width);
    }
    stack.push(i);
}

Template 4: Expression Evaluation (+, -, *, /)

Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
int num = 0; char prevOp = '+';
for (int i = 0; i < s.length(); i++) {
    char c = s.charAt(i);
    if (Character.isDigit(c)) num = num * 10 + (c - '0');
    if ((!Character.isDigit(c) && c != ' ') || i == s.length() - 1) {
        switch (prevOp) {
            case '+': stack.push(num); break;
            case '-': stack.push(-num); break;
            case '*': stack.push(stack.pop() * num); break;
            case '/': stack.push(stack.pop() / num); break;
        }
        prevOp = c; num = 0;
    }
}
int result = 0;
while (!stack.isEmpty()) result += stack.pop();

Section 5: Complexity Analysis

PatternTimeSpaceWhy O(n)?
Basic StackO(n)O(n)Single pass
Next Greater/SmallerO(n)O(n)Each element pushed once, popped once
Previous Greater/SmallerO(n)O(n)Each element pushed once, popped once
Expression EvaluationO(n)O(n)Single pass through expression
Histogram/AreaO(n)O(n)Each element pushed once, popped once
String ManipulationO(n)O(n)Single pass (O(n·k) for decode)
Interview explanation for O(n): "Each element enters the stack once and leaves at most once. Across all iterations, the inner while loop executes at most n times total. So total operations = n pushes + n pops = O(2n) = O(n)."

Section 6: Edge Cases & Common Mistakes

Edge Cases to Always Handle:
  • Empty input: Check arr.length == 0
  • Single element: Should return gracefully
  • All same elements: [3,3,3,3] — no next greater for any
  • Sorted ascending: [1,2,3,4] — stack pops immediately each time
  • Sorted descending: [4,3,2,1] — stack holds all until sentinel
  • Circular arrays: Process 2*n elements with i % n
Common Mistakes:
MistakeFix
Storing values in stackStore INDICES — you almost always need position
No isEmpty() check before peek()/pop()Always: while (!stack.isEmpty() && ...)
Wrong pop condition (< vs <=)For duplicates: >= one side, > other (LC 907)
Off-by-one in widthWidth = i - stack.peek() - 1 (exclude boundaries)
Forgetting sentinel in histogramIterate to i <= n with height 0
Using Stack<> classUse ArrayDeque — Stack is legacy
Integer overflow in areaUse (long) height * width

Section 7: Interview Tips

Recognition Speed Table

If you see...Immediately think...
"next greater/smaller"Monotonic stack (Pattern 2)
"previous greater/smaller" or "span"Monotonic stack (Pattern 3)
"largest rectangle/area"Histogram pattern (Pattern 5)
"subarray min/max contribution"Contribution technique (Pattern 5)
"matching brackets/pairs"Basic stack (Pattern 1)
"evaluate expression"Expression stack (Pattern 4)
"remove adjacent/duplicates"String stack (Pattern 6)

During the Interview

  1. State the pattern: "This is a next greater element problem, so I'll use a monotonic decreasing stack."
  2. Explain the invariant: "The stack always maintains decreasing order from bottom to top."
  3. Trace an example: Draw stack state for 2-3 iterations on the whiteboard.
  4. Prove O(n): "Each element pushed once, popped at most once → O(2n) = O(n)."
  5. Mention edge cases: empty array, all same elements, duplicates.
