Section 10: Quick Revision Card

One-line Summary of Each Pattern

PatternOne-liner
P1: Basic StackLIFO to match pairs or maintain nested state
P2: Next Greater/SmallerAnswer at POP time — current element is the answer for popped
P3: Previous Greater/SmallerAnswer at PUSH time — stack top is the answer for current
P4: Expression/CalculatorHigh-precedence ops immediately, low-precedence deferred to stack
P5: Area/WidthLeft & right boundaries via monotonic stack; area = height × width
P6: String ManipulationStack as "builder with undo" — push chars, pop on condition

Decision Tree

Problem involves...
|
+-- Matching/nesting? → Pattern 1 (Basic Stack)
|
+-- "Next greater/smaller to the right"? → Pattern 2 (Monotonic Stack)
|
+-- "Previous greater/smaller" or "span"? → Pattern 3 (Monotonic Stack)
|
+-- Evaluate expression/calculator? → Pattern 4 (Expression Stack)
|
+-- Rectangle/area/trapped water/contribution? → Pattern 5 (Histogram)
|
+-- Build/modify string with removals? → Pattern 6 (String Stack)

The THREE Things to Remember

  1. Each element pushed once, popped at most once — this is WHY it's O(n). The #1 thing to explain in interviews.
  2. Store INDICES, not values — you almost always need position for distance/width calculations.
  3. Answer at POP (for "next") vs answer at PUSH (for "previous") — this duality is the key to all monotonic stack variants.

Last updated: March 2026 | Target: SDE 2 interviews at Indian product companies

06 / 18

Linked List

Comprehensive DSA Notes for SDE 2 Interview Prep

Section 1: Topic Overview

What is a Linked List?

A linear data structure where elements (nodes) are stored in non-contiguous memory, connected via pointers/references.

Singly Linked List: Each node has data + next pointer.
Doubly Linked List: Each node has data + next + prev pointer.

Analogy: A singly linked list is like a scavenger hunt — each clue tells you where the NEXT clue is, but you can't go backwards. A doubly linked list is like a two-way street.

Interview Frequency

HIGH frequency — appears in nearly every SDE 2 interview loop.
CompanyFrequencyFavourite Problems
AmazonVery HighLRU Cache, Merge K Lists, Reverse in K Groups
MicrosoftVery HighLinked List Cycle, Copy with Random Pointer
FlipkartHighReorder List, Add Two Numbers
GoogleMedium-HighMerge K Sorted Lists, LRU Cache

Section 2: Core Concepts & Theory

Node Structures in Java

// Singly Linked List Node
class ListNode {
    int val;
    ListNode next;
    ListNode(int val) { this.val = val; this.next = null; }
    ListNode(int val, ListNode next) { this.val = val; this.next = next; }
}

// Doubly Linked List Node
class DListNode {
    int val;
    DListNode prev, next;
    DListNode(int val) { this.val = val; }
}

LinkedList vs ArrayList Trade-offs

OperationArrayListLinkedListWhy?
Access by indexO(1)O(n)Array = contiguous memory, direct offset
Insert at headO(n)O(1)Array shifts all elements
Insert at tailO(1) amortizedO(1)Array may resize
Delete at headO(n)O(1)Array shifts all elements
Cache performanceExcellentPoorArray has spatial locality

Java Implementations

// PREFERRED: ArrayDeque for stack/queue, custom ListNode for interviews
Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();

// Built-in LinkedList (use for Deque/Queue in larger problems)
LinkedList<Integer> list = new LinkedList<>();
list.addFirst(1);  list.addLast(2);  list.removeFirst();  list.removeLast();
Interview tip: In 95% of interview problems, interviewers want you to manipulate node pointers directly with a custom ListNode class. Use built-in LinkedList only when you need a Deque/Queue in a larger problem.

Sentinel / Dummy Node Technique

WHY it works: The dummy node guarantees that every real node (including the original head) has a predecessor. This makes deletion logic UNIFORM for all nodes — no special case for the head. The dummy absorbs the edge case.
Without dummy (ugly edge cases)
ListNode deleteValue(ListNode head, int val) {
    // Special handling for head!
    while (head != null && head.val == val)
        head = head.next;
    if (head == null) return null;

    ListNode curr = head;
    while (curr.next != null) {
        if (curr.next.val == val)
            curr.next = curr.next.next;
        else
            curr = curr.next;
    }
    return head;
}
With dummy (clean, uniform)
ListNode deleteValue(ListNode head, int val) {
    ListNode dummy = new ListNode(0);
    dummy.next = head;

    ListNode curr = dummy;
    while (curr.next != null) {
        if (curr.next.val == val)
            curr.next = curr.next.next;
        else
            curr = curr.next;
    }
    return dummy.next; // The real head
}

Section 3: Pattern Recognition & Intuition Building

Pattern 1: Two Pointers (Fast & Slow / Floyd's Algorithm)
How to Arrive at This:
Cycle detection: "Can I detect a cycle without O(n) extra space? Two pointers at different speeds — like two runners on a circular track, the faster MUST eventually lap the slower."
Find middle: "One pointer at 2x speed. When fast reaches end, slow is exactly at middle."
Nth from end: "Create n-step gap between two pointers. When fast reaches end, slow is at the target."
Analogy: Two people walking: one at 2 km/h and one at 1 km/h. When the fast person covers the full distance, the slow person has covered exactly half.

cycle loop middle nth from end intersection

Mathematical Proof: Why Floyd's Finds the Cycle Start

L = distance from head to cycle start, C = cycle length, K = distance from cycle start to meeting point.

At meeting: slow traveled L + K, fast traveled L + K + nC.
Since fast = 2 × slow: 2(L + K) = L + K + nC → L = nC - K.

So: put one pointer at head, one at meeting point. Both move 1 step. They meet at cycle start!

Linked List Cycle (LC 141) - Easy

public boolean hasCycle(ListNode head) {
    ListNode slow = head, fast = head;
    while (fast != null && fast.next != null) {
        slow = slow.next;
        fast = fast.next.next;
        if (slow == fast) return true;
    }
    return false;
}
Hack: Null check order matters — fast != null && fast.next != null.
Always check fast != null FIRST. If fast is null, fast.next throws NullPointerException. Java's short-circuit evaluation (&&) skips the second check when the first is false. Reversing the order (fast.next != null && fast != null) crashes on lists with even length.

Linked List Cycle II (LC 142) - Medium

How to remember: "First meeting = proof of cycle, second meeting = start of cycle"
1. Run Floyd (slow = 1 step, fast = 2 steps)
2. When they meet: move one pointer to head, keep other at meeting point
3. Move both 1 step at a time — where they meet = cycle start 
public ListNode detectCycle(ListNode head) {
    ListNode slow = head, fast = head;
    while (fast != null && fast.next != null) {
        slow = slow.next;
        fast = fast.next.next;
        if (slow == fast) {
            ListNode entry = head;
            while (entry != slow) {
                entry = entry.next;
                slow = slow.next;
            }
            return entry;
        }
    }
    return null;
}

Middle of Linked List (LC 876) - Easy

public ListNode middleNode(ListNode head) {
    ListNode slow = head, fast = head;
    while (fast != null && fast.next != null) {
        slow = slow.next;
        fast = fast.next.next;
    }
    return slow; // second middle for even-length
}

Remove Nth Node From End (LC 19) - Medium

public ListNode removeNthFromEnd(ListNode head, int n) {
    ListNode dummy = new ListNode(0);
    dummy.next = head;
    ListNode fast = dummy, slow = dummy;

    for (int i = 0; i <= n; i++) fast = fast.next; // n+1 gap

    while (fast != null) {
        slow = slow.next;
        fast = fast.next;
    }
    slow.next = slow.next.next; // remove target
    return dummy.next;
}
Pattern 2: Reversal
How to Arrive at This: "Each node's .next should point to its PREVIOUS node. But I only have a forward pointer. So I need to save 'previous' myself. Before changing curr.next, I must save curr.next first (or I lose the rest). Three variables: prev, curr, nextTemp."
Analogy: A line of people each pointing to the person in front. To reverse: go person by person, each turns around and points backward. But before turning, note who's in front (so you can move to them next).

reverse palindrome flip reverse in groups

Step-by-step visualization: 
Initial:  null   1 -> 2 -> 3 -> null
          prev  curr

Step 1:   null <- 1    2 -> 3 -> null
          prev  curr  next

Step 2:   null <- 1 <- 2    3 -> null
                 prev curr  next

Step 3:   null <- 1 <- 2 <- 3
                       prev curr  => return prev

Reverse Linked List (LC 206) - Easy

// Iterative (PREFERRED in interviews)
public ListNode reverseList(ListNode head) {
    ListNode prev = null, curr = head;
    while (curr != null) {
        ListNode nextTemp = curr.next;  // 1. Save next
        curr.next = prev;               // 2. Reverse link
        prev = curr;                    // 3. Advance prev
        curr = nextTemp;                // 4. Advance curr
    }
    return prev;
}

// Recursive (know it, prefer iterative)
public ListNode reverseListRecursive(ListNode head) {
    if (head == null || head.next == null) return head;
    ListNode newHead = reverseListRecursive(head.next);
    head.next.next = head;
    head.next = null;
    return newHead;
}

Reverse Linked List II (LC 92) - Medium

public ListNode reverseBetween(ListNode head, int left, int right) {
    ListNode dummy = new ListNode(0);
    dummy.next = head;

    ListNode prevLeft = dummy;
    for (int i = 1; i < left; i++) prevLeft = prevLeft.next;

    ListNode curr = prevLeft.next, prev = null;
    for (int i = 0; i <= right - left; i++) {
        ListNode nextTemp = curr.next;
        curr.next = prev;
        prev = curr;
        curr = nextTemp;
    }

    prevLeft.next.next = curr;  // connect tail of reversed to rest
    prevLeft.next = prev;       // connect before-left to new head
    return dummy.next;
}

Palindrome Linked List (LC 234) - Easy

public boolean isPalindrome(ListNode head) {
    // Step 1: Find middle (Pattern 1)
    ListNode slow = head, fast = head;
    while (fast != null && fast.next != null) {
        slow = slow.next;
        fast = fast.next.next;
    }
    // Step 2: Reverse second half (Pattern 2)
    ListNode secondHalf = reverseList(slow);
    // Step 3: Compare both halves
    ListNode first = head;
    while (secondHalf != null) {
        if (first.val != secondHalf.val) return false;
        first = first.next;
        secondHalf = secondHalf.next;
    }
    return true;
}
Pattern 3: Merge / Combine Lists
How to Arrive at This:
Merge two: "Exactly like the merge step in merge sort. Compare heads, take smaller, advance that pointer. Use dummy head."
Merge K: "Min-heap holding one node from each list. Pop smallest, push its .next. Heap has at most k elements → O(log k) per op → O(N log k) total."

merge sorted merge k lists combine interleave

Merge Two Sorted Lists (LC 21) - Easy

public ListNode mergeTwoLists(ListNode l1, ListNode l2) {
    ListNode dummy = new ListNode(0);
    ListNode tail = dummy;

    while (l1 != null && l2 != null) {
        if (l1.val <= l2.val) {
            tail.next = l1;
            l1 = l1.next;
        } else {
            tail.next = l2;
            l2 = l2.next;
        }
        tail = tail.next;
    }
    tail.next = (l1 != null) ? l1 : l2;
    return dummy.next;
}

Merge K Sorted Lists (LC 23) - Hard

public ListNode mergeKLists(ListNode[] lists) {
    if (lists == null || lists.length == 0) return null;

    PriorityQueue<ListNode> minHeap = new PriorityQueue<>(
        (a, b) -> a.val - b.val
    );

    for (ListNode head : lists) {
        if (head != null) minHeap.offer(head);
    }

    ListNode dummy = new ListNode(0);
    ListNode tail = dummy;

    while (!minHeap.isEmpty()) {
        ListNode smallest = minHeap.poll();
        tail.next = smallest;
        tail = tail.next;
        if (smallest.next != null) minHeap.offer(smallest.next);
    }
    return dummy.next;
}
Pattern 4: Reorder / Rearrange
How to Arrive at This: "Reorder List needs interleaving from front and back. In an array, I'd use two pointers from both ends. In a linked list, I can't go backwards. So: (1) find middle, (2) reverse second half, (3) merge alternately. This COMBINES Patterns 1 + 2 + 3!"

reorder rearrange odd-even partition rotate

Reorder List (LC 143) - Medium

This is the ultimate "combo" problem — it tests Patterns 1, 2, and 3 in a single question.
public void reorderList(ListNode head) {
    if (head == null || head.next == null) return;

    // Step 1: Find middle (Pattern 1)
    ListNode slow = head, fast = head;
    while (fast.next != null && fast.next.next != null) {
        slow = slow.next;
        fast = fast.next.next;
    }

    // Step 2: Reverse second half (Pattern 2)
    ListNode secondHalf = reverseList(slow.next);
    slow.next = null;

    // Step 3: Interleave merge (Pattern 3 variation)
    ListNode first = head;
    while (secondHalf != null) {
        ListNode temp1 = first.next, temp2 = secondHalf.next;
        first.next = secondHalf;
        secondHalf.next = temp1;
        first = temp1;
        secondHalf = temp2;
    }
}

Odd Even Linked List (LC 328) - Medium

public ListNode oddEvenList(ListNode head) {
    if (head == null) return null;
    ListNode odd = head, even = head.next, evenHead = even;

    while (even != null && even.next != null) {
        odd.next = even.next;
        odd = odd.next;
        even.next = odd.next;
        even = even.next;
    }
    odd.next = evenHead;
    return head;
}

Partition List (LC 86) - Medium

public ListNode partition(ListNode head, int x) {
    ListNode lessHead = new ListNode(0), greaterHead = new ListNode(0);
    ListNode less = lessHead, greater = greaterHead;

    while (head != null) {
        if (head.val < x) { less.next = head; less = less.next; }
        else { greater.next = head; greater = greater.next; }
        head = head.next;
    }
    greater.next = null;
    less.next = greaterHead.next;
    return lessHead.next;
}

Rotate List (LC 61) - Medium

public ListNode rotateRight(ListNode head, int k) {
    if (head == null || head.next == null || k == 0) return head;

    int length = 1;
    ListNode tail = head;
    while (tail.next != null) { tail = tail.next; length++; }

    k = k % length;
    if (k == 0) return head;

    ListNode newTail = head;
    for (int i = 0; i < length - k - 1; i++) newTail = newTail.next;

    ListNode newHead = newTail.next;
    newTail.next = null;
    tail.next = head;
    return newHead;
}
Pattern 5: Dummy Head / Sentinel Node
How to Arrive at This: "The head itself might be deleted. Without dummy, I need special handling. With a dummy node before head, EVERY real node has a predecessor and the logic is uniform. Return dummy.next at the end."
Analogy: The dummy node is a "bodyguard" standing before the first person in line. The bodyguard never gets removed, so you never worry about "what if the first person leaves?"

remove delete filter swap result may change head

Remove Linked List Elements (LC 203) - Easy

public ListNode removeElements(ListNode head, int val) {
    ListNode dummy = new ListNode(0);
    dummy.next = head;
    ListNode curr = dummy;

    while (curr.next != null) {
        if (curr.next.val == val) curr.next = curr.next.next;
        else curr = curr.next;
    }
    return dummy.next;
}

Remove Duplicates from Sorted List II (LC 82) - Medium

public ListNode deleteDuplicates(ListNode head) {
    ListNode dummy = new ListNode(0);
    dummy.next = head;
    ListNode prev = dummy;

    while (prev.next != null && prev.next.next != null) {
        if (prev.next.val == prev.next.next.val) {
            int dupVal = prev.next.val;
            while (prev.next != null && prev.next.val == dupVal)
                prev.next = prev.next.next;
        } else {
            prev = prev.next;
        }
    }
    return dummy.next;
}

Swap Nodes in Pairs (LC 24) - Medium

public ListNode swapPairs(ListNode head) {
    ListNode dummy = new ListNode(0);
    dummy.next = head;
    ListNode prev = dummy;

    while (prev.next != null && prev.next.next != null) {
        ListNode first = prev.next, second = prev.next.next;
        first.next = second.next;
        second.next = first;
        prev.next = second;
        prev = first;
    }
    return dummy.next;
}
Pattern 6: Advanced / Math on Linked Lists
How to Arrive at This:
Add Two Numbers: "Traverse both lists, add digits + carry, like manual addition."
LRU Cache: "Need O(1) get + O(1) put + eviction of LRU. HashMap for O(1) lookup + DLL for O(1) removal and order tracking."
Copy with Random: "Random might point to a node I haven't created yet. Use HashMap: original → copy."

add numbers copy/clone LRU cache flatten

Add Two Numbers (LC 2) - Medium

public ListNode addTwoNumbers(ListNode l1, ListNode l2) {
    ListNode dummy = new ListNode(0);
    ListNode curr = dummy;
    int carry = 0;

    while (l1 != null || l2 != null || carry != 0) {
        int sum = carry;
        if (l1 != null) { sum += l1.val; l1 = l1.next; }
        if (l2 != null) { sum += l2.val; l2 = l2.next; }
        carry = sum / 10;
        curr.next = new ListNode(sum % 10);
        curr = curr.next;
    }
    return dummy.next;
}

Copy List with Random Pointer (LC 138) - Medium

public Node copyRandomList(Node head) {
    if (head == null) return null;
    Map<Node, Node> map = new HashMap<>();

    // Pass 1: Create all copies
    Node curr = head;
    while (curr != null) {
        map.put(curr, new Node(curr.val));
        curr = curr.next;
    }
    // Pass 2: Set next and random pointers
    curr = head;
    while (curr != null) {
        map.get(curr).next = map.get(curr.next);
        map.get(curr).random = map.get(curr.random);
        curr = curr.next;
    }
    return map.get(head);
}

LRU Cache (LC 146) - Medium

THE most-asked linked list problem for SDE 2. Know it cold. Be able to write from scratch in under 15 minutes. 
class LRUCache {
    class DLinkedNode {
        int key, value;
        DLinkedNode prev, next;
        DLinkedNode() {}
        DLinkedNode(int key, int value) { this.key = key; this.value = value; }
    }

    private Map<Integer, DLinkedNode> cache = new HashMap<>();
    private int capacity;
    private DLinkedNode head, tail; // Sentinel nodes

    public LRUCache(int capacity) {
        this.capacity = capacity;
        head = new DLinkedNode();
        tail = new DLinkedNode();
        head.next = tail;
        tail.prev = head;
    }

    public int get(int key) {
        DLinkedNode node = cache.get(key);
        if (node == null) return -1;
        moveToHead(node);
        return node.value;
    }

    public void put(int key, int value) {
        DLinkedNode node = cache.get(key);
        if (node != null) {
            node.value = value;
            moveToHead(node);
        } else {
            DLinkedNode newNode = new DLinkedNode(key, value);
            cache.put(key, newNode);
            addToHead(newNode);
            if (cache.size() > capacity) {
                DLinkedNode tailNode = removeTail();
                cache.remove(tailNode.key);
            }
        }
    }

    private void addToHead(DLinkedNode node) {
        node.prev = head;
        node.next = head.next;
        head.next.prev = node;
        head.next = node;
    }

    private void removeNode(DLinkedNode node) {
        node.prev.next = node.next;
        node.next.prev = node.prev;
    }

    private void moveToHead(DLinkedNode node) {
        removeNode(node);
        addToHead(node);
    }

    private DLinkedNode removeTail() {
        DLinkedNode res = tail.prev;
        removeNode(res);
        return res;
    }
}

Section 4: Code Templates (Copy-Paste Ready)

Template 1: Iterative Reversal

ListNode prev = null, curr = head;
while (curr != null) {
    ListNode next = curr.next;  // Save next
    curr.next = prev;           // Reverse
    prev = curr;                // Advance prev
    curr = next;                // Advance curr
}
return prev; // new head

Template 2: Fast-Slow Pointer

// Find middle (second middle for even)
ListNode slow = head, fast = head;
while (fast != null && fast.next != null) {
    slow = slow.next;
    fast = fast.next.next;
}
// slow = middle

// Cycle detection
if (slow == fast) {  // inside the while loop
    ListNode entry = head;
    while (entry != slow) { entry = entry.next; slow = slow.next; }
    return entry; // cycle start
}

Template 3: Merge Two Sorted Lists

ListNode dummy = new ListNode(0), tail = dummy;
while (l1 != null && l2 != null) {
    if (l1.val <= l2.val) { tail.next = l1; l1 = l1.next; }
    else { tail.next = l2; l2 = l2.next; }
    tail = tail.next;
}
tail.next = (l1 != null) ? l1 : l2;
return dummy.next;

Template 4: Dummy Head Pattern

ListNode dummy = new ListNode(0);
dummy.next = head;
ListNode prev = dummy;

while (prev.next != null) {
    if (shouldRemove(prev.next)) prev.next = prev.next.next;
    else prev = prev.next;
}
return dummy.next;

Section 5: Complexity Analysis

OperationTimeSpaceNotes
Reversal (iterative)O(n)O(1)Three pointers
Reversal (recursive)O(n)O(n)Call stack
Merge two sortedO(n+m)O(1)Pointer rewiring only
Merge K sorted (heap)O(N log k)O(k)Heap holds k nodes
Floyd's cycle detectionO(n)O(1)Two pointers
Copy with random (HashMap)O(n)O(n)HashMap for mapping
LRU Cache (get/put)O(1)O(capacity)DLL + HashMap
Key insight: Most linked list algorithms achieve O(n) time and O(1) space because you're just rewiring pointers, not allocating new data structures.

Section 6: Edge Cases & Common Mistakes

Edge Cases to Always Test:
  • Empty list (head == null)
  • Single node (head.next == null)
  • Two nodes — boundary between "too small" and "normal"
  • Even vs odd length — affects where "middle" falls
  • Cycle at different positions — head, tail, self-loop
  • All same values — important for duplicate removal
Top 3 Bugs in Linked List Code:
Bug 1: Not saving .next before modifying 
// WRONG: curr = curr.next is now prev!
curr.next = prev;
curr = curr.next;

// CORRECT: save first
ListNode next = curr.next;
curr.next = prev;
curr = next;
Bug 2: Forgetting to cut the list 
// After splitting, last node of first half still points to second half!
slow.next = null;  // MUST cut the connection
Bug 3: NullPointerException 
// WRONG: crashes if fast is null
while (fast.next != null)

// CORRECT: check fast first
while (fast != null && fast.next != null)

Section 7: Interview Tips

  1. ALWAYS draw the linked list on paper/whiteboard before coding. Trace your algorithm step by step.
  2. Clarify: Singly or doubly? Can I modify the list? Return value for empty list? Unique values?
  3. Use dummy head by default — tell interviewer: "I'll use a sentinel node to simplify edge cases."
  4. For reversal, trace 3 nodes step by step to verify pointer changes.
  5. Name variables clearly: prev, curr, nextTemp, slow, fast, dummy.
  6. State your approach: "This is a fast-slow pointer problem. I'll use Floyd's to detect the cycle."
LRU Cache (LC 146) is THE most-asked linked list problem for SDE 2. Know it cold — write from scratch in under 15 minutes.

Section 10: Quick Revision Card

One-line Summary of Each Pattern

PatternOne-liner
P1: Fast-SlowTwo pointers at different speeds for cycles, middles, nth from end
P2: ReversalThree pointers (prev, curr, next) to flip link direction
P3: MergeDummy head + compare-and-attach; min-heap for k lists
P4: ReorderCombo: find middle + reverse + merge, or split into two chains
P5: Dummy HeadSentinel before head eliminates all head edge cases

Decision Tree

Problem mentions...
|
+-- "cycle" / "loop" → Pattern 1: Floyd's fast-slow
+-- "middle" → Pattern 1: fast=2x, slow=1x
+-- "nth from end" → Pattern 1: n-gap + dummy
+-- "reverse" → Pattern 2: prev-curr-next
+-- "palindrome" → Pattern 1+2: middle + reverse + compare
+-- "merge sorted" → Pattern 3: dummy + compare heads
+-- "merge k" → Pattern 3: min-heap of size k
+-- "reorder" / "rearrange" → Pattern 4: middle + reverse + merge
+-- "odd/even" → Pattern 4: two chains, connect
+-- "remove" / "delete" → Pattern 5: dummy head
+-- "add numbers" → Pattern 6: carry propagation
+-- "copy" / "clone" → Pattern 6: HashMap mapping
+-- "LRU" / "cache" → Pattern 6: DLL + HashMap

The THREE Things to Remember

  1. Always use a dummy/sentinel node — eliminates 80% of linked list bugs (head deletion, empty list, result construction).
  2. Save .next before modifying any pointer — the moment you write curr.next = X, the original curr.next is GONE. Save it first.
  3. Draw it out — linked list pointer manipulation is nearly impossible to get right abstractly. Draw 3-4 nodes, trace step by step.

Last updated: March 2026 | Target: SDE 2 interviews at Indian product companies

07 / 18

Recursion & Backtracking

Comprehensive DSA Notes for SDE 2 Interview Prep

Section 1: Topic Overview

What is Recursion?

A technique where a function calls itself to solve a smaller version of the same problem. Every recursive function has a base case (stops recursion) and a recursive case (calls itself with smaller input).

What is Backtracking?

Backtracking = Recursion + "Undo". Build candidates incrementally, abandon ("backtrack") as soon as a candidate can't lead to a valid solution.

  1. Choose — make a choice
  2. Explore — recurse with this choice
  3. Unchoose — undo the choice, try the next option
Analogy: You're in a maze. At each fork, pick a path (choose), walk down it (explore). Hit a dead end? Walk back to the fork (unchoose) and try the next path.
Very High frequency for SDE 2 at Indian product companies. Backtracking is a staple in 45-minute coding rounds. Interviewers test pattern recognition, clean recursive code, and duplicate/pruning handling.

Section 2: Core Concepts & Theory

Recursion Fundamentals

ComponentPurposeExample (Factorial)
Base CaseStop conditionif (n <= 1) return 1;
Recursive CaseSmaller subproblemreturn n * factorial(n-1);
Progress toward baseEnsure terminationn decreases by 1 each call

"Trust the Recursion" Mindset

The single most important mindset shift: Assume your recursive function already works for all smaller inputs. Your only job is: (1) define the base case, (2) figure out how to use the smaller result to solve the current problem. Do NOT trace every call mentally.

The Backtracking Framework (Master Template)

backtrack(state, choices):
    if goal_reached(state):
        record solution
        return

    for each choice in choices:
        if is_valid(choice):
            make_choice(choice)          // CHOOSE
            backtrack(updated_state)      // EXPLORE
            undo_choice(choice)          // UNCHOOSE
Analogy: Trying on outfits. Pick a shirt (choose), see how it looks (explore). Doesn't work? Take it off (unchoose) and try the next. Do this systematically until you find a complete outfit.

State Space Tree (Subsets of {1,2,3})

                    []
            /                 \
       [1]                     []
      /     \               /      \
  [1,2]    [1]          [2]        []
  /  \     /  \        /   \      /   \
[1,2,3] [1,2] [1,3] [1] [2,3] [2] [3]  []

Each leaf = valid subset. Each level = decision about one element (include/exclude).

Pruning

Cut off branches early when they can't lead to valid solutions. Without pruning: explore entire tree. With smart pruning: skip huge subtrees.

Section 3: Pattern Recognition & Intuition Building

Pattern 1: Subsets / Power Set
How to Arrive at This: "For each element, I have exactly TWO choices: include or exclude. Making this binary choice for n elements gives 2^n subsets. Classic recursion tree where each level = one element's decision."
Analogy: Packing for a trip with n items. Each item: put in suitcase or leave it. Different combinations = subsets.

all subsets power set all subsequences all combinations of any length

Subsets (LC 78) - Medium

Loop-based (iterate over choices)
class Solution {
    public List<List<Integer>> subsets(int[] nums) {
        List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();
        backtrack(nums, 0, new ArrayList<>(), result);
        return result;
    }

    private void backtrack(int[] nums, int start,
            List<Integer> current,
            List<List<Integer>> result) {
        result.add(new ArrayList<>(current));
        // ^ Every node is a valid subset

        for (int i = start; i < nums.length; i++) {
            current.add(nums[i]);              // CHOOSE
            backtrack(nums, i + 1, current, result);
            current.remove(current.size() - 1);// UNCHOOSE
        }
    }
}
Pick / Not-Pick (binary decision)
class Solution {
    List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();

    public List<List<Integer>> subsets(int[] nums) {
        dfs(nums, 0, new ArrayList<>());
        return result;
    }

    public void dfs(int[] nums, int index,
                    List<Integer> list) {
        if (index == nums.length) {
            result.add(new ArrayList<>(list));
            return;          // Leaf = valid subset
        }

        list.add(nums[index]);               // 1️⃣ PICK
        dfs(nums, index + 1, list);

        list.remove(list.size() - 1);        // Backtrack

        dfs(nums, index + 1, list);          // 2️⃣ NOT PICK
    }
}

Subsets II (LC 90) - Medium (with duplicates)

Handling duplicates: (1) Sort the array first. (2) Skip duplicates at same level: if (i > start && nums[i] == nums[i-1]) continue;
Loop-based (skip dups at same level)
class Solution {
    public List<List<Integer>> subsetsWithDup(int[] nums) {
        Arrays.sort(nums);  // MUST sort first!
        List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();
        backtrack(nums, 0, new ArrayList<>(), result);
        return result;
    }

    private void backtrack(int[] nums, int start,
            List<Integer> current,
            List<List<Integer>> result) {
        result.add(new ArrayList<>(current));
        for (int i = start; i < nums.length; i++) {
            if (i > start && nums[i] == nums[i - 1])
                continue;       // Skip dups at same level
            current.add(nums[i]);
            backtrack(nums, i + 1, current, result);
            current.remove(current.size() - 1);
        }
    }
}

Why i > start not i > 0? We only skip duplicates at the same recursion level. Using same value at different depths is fine (e.g., [2,2] is valid).

Pick / Not-Pick (skip dups on NOT PICK)
class Solution {
    List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();

    public List<List<Integer>> subsetsWithDup(int[] nums) {
        Arrays.sort(nums);  // MUST sort first!
        dfs(nums, 0, new ArrayList<>());
        return result;
    }

    public void dfs(int[] nums, int index,
                    List<Integer> list) {
        if (index == nums.length) {
            result.add(new ArrayList<>(list));
            return;
        }

        list.add(nums[index]);               // 1️⃣ PICK
        dfs(nums, index + 1, list);

        list.remove(list.size() - 1);        // Backtrack

        while (index + 1 < nums.length
               && nums[index] == nums[index + 1]) {
            index++;                         // Skip all dups
        }
        dfs(nums, index + 1, list);          // 2️⃣ NOT PICK
    }
}

Why skip dups on NOT PICK? If you skip nums[i] but pick nums[i+1] (same value), it's identical to picking nums[i] and skipping nums[i+1] — duplicate subset!

Pattern 2: Combinations (Choose k from n)
How to Arrive at This: "Like subsets but with a length constraint. Stop when current.size() == k. Prune when remaining elements can't fill remaining slots."
Analogy: You have n people and need exactly k for a project. Go through each person: include or skip. Already picked k? Stop. Not enough people left? Stop early (pruning).

combinations of size k choose k elements combination sum k numbers that sum to target

Combinations (LC 77) - Medium

Loop-based (iterate over choices)
class Solution {
    public List<List<Integer>> combine(int n, int k) {
        List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();
        backtrack(n, k, 1, new ArrayList<>(), result);
        return result;
    }

    private void backtrack(int n, int k, int start,
            List<Integer> current,
            List<List<Integer>> result) {
        if (current.size() == k) {
            result.add(new ArrayList<>(current));
            return;
        }
        // Pruning: need (k-current.size()) more,
        // only (n-i+1) available
        for (int i = start;
             i <= n - (k - current.size()) + 1; i++) {
            current.add(i);
            backtrack(n, k, i + 1, current, result);
            current.remove(current.size() - 1);
        }
    }
}
Pick / Not-Pick (binary decision)
class Solution {
    List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();

    public List<List<Integer>> combine(int n, int k) {
        getCombinations(n, k, 1, new ArrayList<>());
        return result;
    }

    void getCombinations(int n, int k, int i,
                         List<Integer> current) {
        if (current.size() == k) {
            result.add(new ArrayList<>(current));
            return;
        }
        if (i > n) return;                  // No more numbers
        // Pruning: not enough numbers left
        if (current.size() + (n - i + 1) < k) return;

        current.add(i);                     // 1️⃣ PICK
        getCombinations(n, k, i + 1, current);

        current.remove(current.size() - 1); // Backtrack

        getCombinations(n, k, i + 1, current); // 2️⃣ NOT PICK
    }
}

Combination Sum (LC 39) - Medium (reuse allowed)

Loop-based (iterate over choices)
class Solution {
    public List<List<Integer>> combinationSum(
            int[] candidates, int target) {
        List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();
        Arrays.sort(candidates);
        backtrack(candidates, target, 0,
            new ArrayList<>(), result);
        return result;
    }

    private void backtrack(int[] candidates,
            int remaining, int start,
            List<Integer> current,
            List<List<Integer>> result) {
        if (remaining == 0) {
            result.add(new ArrayList<>(current));
            return;
        }
        for (int i = start;
             i < candidates.length; i++) {
            if (candidates[i] > remaining) break;
            current.add(candidates[i]);
            backtrack(candidates,
                remaining - candidates[i],
                i, current, result); // i, NOT i+1
            current.remove(current.size() - 1);
        }
    }
}
Pick / Not-Pick (binary decision)
class Solution {
    List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();

    public List<List<Integer>> combinationSum(
            int[] candidates, int target) {
        Arrays.sort(candidates);
        dfs(candidates, target, 0, 0,
            new ArrayList<>());
        return result;
    }

    void dfs(int[] candidates, int target,
             int sum, int index,
             List<Integer> current) {
        if (sum == target) {
            result.add(new ArrayList<>(current));
            return;
        }
        if (index >= candidates.length
            || sum > target) return;
        if (candidates[index] > target - sum)
            return;                    // Pruning

        // 1️⃣ PICK (can reuse → same index)
        current.add(candidates[index]);
        dfs(candidates, target,
            sum + candidates[index],
            index, current);           // index, NOT index+1

        // Backtrack
        current.remove(current.size() - 1);

        // 2️⃣ NOT PICK (move to next)
        dfs(candidates, target, sum,
            index + 1, current);
    }
}

Combination Sum II (LC 40) - Medium (no reuse, skip dups)

Loop-based (skip dups at same level)
private void backtrack(int[] candidates,
        int remaining, int start,
        List<Integer> current,
        List<List<Integer>> result) {
    if (remaining == 0) {
        result.add(new ArrayList<>(current));
        return;
    }

    for (int i = start;
         i < candidates.length; i++) {
        if (candidates[i] > remaining) break;
        if (i > start
            && candidates[i] == candidates[i - 1])
            continue;          // Skip dups

        current.add(candidates[i]);
        backtrack(candidates,
            remaining - candidates[i],
            i + 1, current, result);   // i+1
        current.remove(current.size() - 1);
    }
}
Pick / Not-Pick (skip dups on NOT PICK)
class Solution {
    List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();

    public List<List<Integer>> combinationSum2(
            int[] candidates, int target) {
        Arrays.sort(candidates);
        dfs(candidates, target, 0, 0,
            new ArrayList<>());
        return result;
    }

    void dfs(int[] candidates, int target,
             int sum, int index,
             List<Integer> current) {
        if (sum == target) {
            result.add(new ArrayList<>(current));
            return;
        }
        if (index >= candidates.length
            || sum > target) return;
        if (candidates[index] > target) return;

        // 1️⃣ PICK
        current.add(candidates[index]);
        dfs(candidates, target,
            sum + candidates[index],
            index + 1, current);

        // Backtrack
        current.remove(current.size() - 1);

        // 2️⃣ NOT PICK — skip ALL copies
        while (index + 1 < candidates.length
            && candidates[index]
               == candidates[index + 1]) {
            index++;
        }
        dfs(candidates, target, sum,
            index + 1, current);
    }
}
Why skip on NOT PICK? Rule: "If you don't want a value, you don't want ANY copy of it at this position."
For [1,1,2], target=2: If you skip the first 1 but pick the second 1, you get [1] — identical to picking the first 1 and skipping the second. The array doesn't care which 1 you picked. So NOT PICK must skip all copies.
PICK branch is unaffected — picking the first 1 still lets the second 1 decide for itself, so [1,1] is still reachable via PICK→PICK.
⚠️ Don't use result.contains(current) to handle dups — it's O(k × result.size()) per check and causes TLE.

Combination Variants Summary

ProblemCan Reuse?Has Duplicates?Next indexExtra step
Combination Sum (39)YesNoi—
Combination Sum II (40)NoYesi + 1Sort + skip dups
Combinations (77)NoNoi + 1—
Pattern 3: Permutations
How to Arrive at This: "Permutations are about ordering. Position matters. For position 1: pick any of n. For position 2: any of remaining (n-1). This gives n! permutations. Use a boolean[] used array."
Analogy: n books and n slots on a shelf. For first slot, pick any book. For second, pick from remaining. Each different arrangement = permutation.

all permutations all arrangements all orderings rearrange

Why no Pick/Not-Pick for Permutations?

Combinations: "Should I include this element?" — binary yes/no per element. Two branches:

PICK element at index → recurse(index + 1)
NOT PICK             → recurse(index + 1)

Permutations: "Which element goes in this position?" — try every unused element for the current position. The loop already covers all choices:

for each element i:
    if not used → place it here, recurse, undo

The loop with used[] replaces the two branches. When i=0 is used and i=1 is skipped by used[i], that's effectively "not picking" 0 for this position and "picking" 1 instead.

Why Pick/Not-Pick doesn't work: In combinations, skipping element 2 means it's gone forever. In permutations, skipping element 2 for position 1 doesn't mean you skip it — you might place it at position 2 or 3. Every element must appear exactly once, just in different positions.

CombinationsPermutations
QuestionInclude or not?Which goes here?
Order matters?NoYes
ApproachPick/Not-Pick OR loop from startLoop from 0 with used[]
Each elementCan be skipped entirelyMust appear somewhere

Permutations (LC 46) - Medium

class Solution {
    public List<List<Integer>> permute(int[] nums) {
        List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();
        boolean[] used = new boolean[nums.length];
        backtrack(nums, used, new ArrayList<>(), result);
        return result;
    }

    private void backtrack(int[] nums, boolean[] used, List<Integer> current,
                           List<List<Integer>> result) {
        if (current.size() == nums.length) {
            result.add(new ArrayList<>(current));
            return;
        }
        for (int i = 0; i < nums.length; i++) {  // Loop from 0, not start!
            if (used[i]) continue;
            used[i] = true;
            current.add(nums[i]);
            backtrack(nums, used, current, result);
            current.remove(current.size() - 1);
            used[i] = false;
        }
    }
}

Permutations II (LC 47) - Medium (with duplicates)

Why !used[i-1] skips duplicates

The line: if (i > 0 && nums[i] == nums[i-1] && !used[i-1]) continue;

What each part means:

  • nums[i] == nums[i-1] — current element is a duplicate of the previous
  • !used[i-1] — the previous duplicate is not currently placed (was backtracked or skipped)

The rule: Among a group of duplicates, always pick them left to right in order. If the one before you isn't placed yet, you can't be placed either.

Trace with [1₁, 1₂, 2]:

Position 0: i=0 → try 1₁, explore, backtrack (used[0]=false)
            i=1 → 1₂ same value, used[0]=false → SKIP!
                   1₁ already had its turn at this position

Position 0: 1₁ (used[0]=true)
  Position 1: 1₂ → OK! used[0]=true means 1₁ is at
              a different position, not backtracked

Why we can firmly say it had its turn: Array is sorted + loop goes left to right (i=0,1,2...). So nums[i-1] is always encountered before nums[i] in the same loop. If nums[i-1] is not used, it was either:

  1. Tried at this position and backtracked, OR
  2. Itself skipped by the same rule (an earlier duplicate was tried)

Either way, trying nums[i] (same value) here would be redundant.

✅ 1₁ → 1₂ → 1₃    (left to right order)
❌ 1₂ → 1₁ → 1₃    (out of order — duplicate)
❌ 1₃ → 1₁ → 1₂    (out of order — duplicate)
private void backtrack(int[] nums, boolean[] used, List<Integer> current,
                       List<List<Integer>> result) {
    if (current.size() == nums.length) {
        result.add(new ArrayList<>(current));
        return;
    }
    for (int i = 0; i < nums.length; i++) {
        if (used[i]) continue;
        // Skip: same value as prev, and prev was backtracked at this same level
        if (i > 0 && nums[i] == nums[i - 1] && !used[i - 1]) continue;

        used[i] = true;
        current.add(nums[i]);
        backtrack(nums, used, current, result);
        current.remove(current.size() - 1);
        used[i] = false;
    }
}

Subsets/Combinations vs Permutations

AspectSubsets / CombinationsPermutations
Loop starti = start (only forward)i = 0 (all elements)
Avoid reusestart = i + 1used[] boolean array
Order matters?No: {1,2} = {2,1}Yes: [1,2] != [2,1]
Pattern 4: Grid/Board Search (DFS Backtracking)
How to Arrive at This: "Searching on a 2D grid or placing items on a board with constraints. Try a choice (move/place), mark visited, recurse, undo on backtrack."
Analogy: A detective following leads. Follow one lead (mark investigated), if it leads nowhere, come back and try the next. Track which leads you've followed to avoid circles.

word search solve sudoku N-queens path in grid

Word Search (LC 79) - Medium

class Solution {
    private int[][] dirs = {{0,1},{0,-1},{1,0},{-1,0}};

    public boolean exist(char[][] board, String word) {
        for (int i = 0; i < board.length; i++)
            for (int j = 0; j < board[0].length; j++)
                if (board[i][j] == word.charAt(0) && backtrack(board, word, i, j, 0))
                    return true;
        return false;
    }

    private boolean backtrack(char[][] board, String word, int r, int c, int idx) {
        if (idx == word.length()) return true;
        if (r < 0 || r >= board.length || c < 0 || c >= board[0].length
            || board[r][c] != word.charAt(idx)) return false;

        char original = board[r][c];
        board[r][c] = '#';           // CHOOSE: mark visited

        for (int[] d : dirs)
            if (backtrack(board, word, r + d[0], c + d[1], idx + 1))
                return true;         // EXPLORE

        board[r][c] = original;      // UNCHOOSE: unmark
        return false;
    }
}

N-Queens (LC 51) - Hard

Common mistakes to avoid:
  1. Don't treat it as grid DFS. This is NOT about moving up/down/left/right from a cell. It's about placing one queen per row. Go row by row — backtrack(board, row + 1). No direction array needed.
  2. Don't loop over all cells as starting points. A double for-loop trying every cell creates massive duplication. Instead, recurse row by row and only loop over columns within each row.
  3. Don't forget diagonal checks. Queens attack on rows, columns, AND diagonals. Check column + upper-left diagonal + upper-right diagonal. Only check above current row since no queens are placed below yet.
How recursion inside the for-loop works (N-Queens trace)

findQueen(board, row+1) doesn't skip remaining columns — it goes deeper (explores what happens if we place the queen here). When that recursive call finishes and backtracks, the loop continues to j+1 and tries the next column.

findQueen(board, row=0):
    j=0: valid? place Q at (0,0), call findQueen(row=1)
         findQueen returns, UNCHOOSE → board[0][0] = '.'
    j=1: valid? place Q at (0,1), call findQueen(row=1)
         findQueen returns, UNCHOOSE → board[0][1] = '.'
    j=2: valid? place Q at (0,2), call findQueen(row=1)
         ...

This is the standard CHOOSE-EXPLORE-UNCHOOSE pattern:

for (int j = 0; j < board.length; j++) {
    if (!isValidQueenPlacement(board, row, j)) continue;
    board[row][j] = 'Q';           // CHOOSE column j for this row
    findQueen(board, row + 1);     // EXPLORE: what happens with this choice?
    board[row][j] = '.';           // UNCHOOSE: try next column
}
class Solution {
    public List<List<String>> solveNQueens(int n) {
        List<List<String>> result = new ArrayList<>();
        char[][] board = new char[n][n];
        for (char[] row : board) Arrays.fill(row, '.');
        backtrack(board, 0, result);
        return result;
    }

    private void backtrack(char[][] board, int row, List<List<String>> result) {
        if (row == board.length) {
            List<String> sol = new ArrayList<>();
            for (char[] r : board) sol.add(new String(r));
            result.add(sol);
            return;
        }
        for (int col = 0; col < board.length; col++) {
            if (isValid(board, row, col)) {
                board[row][col] = 'Q';              // CHOOSE
                backtrack(board, row + 1, result);   // EXPLORE
                board[row][col] = '.';               // UNCHOOSE
            }
        }
    }

    private boolean isValid(char[][] board, int row, int col) {
        for (int i = 0; i < row; i++)
            if (board[i][col] == 'Q') return false;
        for (int i = row-1, j = col-1; i >= 0 && j >= 0; i--, j--)
            if (board[i][j] == 'Q') return false;
        for (int i = row-1, j = col+1; i >= 0 && j < board.length; i--, j++)
            if (board[i][j] == 'Q') return false;
        return true;
    }
}

Sudoku Solver (LC 37) - Hard

How to decide the approach for any backtracking problem

Ask these questions in order:

QuestionSubsets/CombosPermutationsN-QueensSudoku
What am I placing?Elements (include/exclude)All elements in orderQueens, one per rowDigits 1-9
Where?Next indexNext positionNext row, try each colNext empty cell
Constraints?Size k, target sum, etc.Each used onceCol + diagonalsRow + col + 3x3 box
When done?Collected all/enoughAll elements placedAll rows filledNo empty cells left
One or all solutions?All → voidAll → voidAll → voidOne → return boolean

Why Sudoku returns boolean: There's exactly one solution. Once found, stop everything:

if (solve(board)) return true;   // Found solution → stop immediately
board[i][j] = '.';               // Didn't work → undo, try next digit
...
return false;  // No digit 1-9 worked → dead end, tell caller to backtrack

The return false after the digit loop is the key line — it means "I tried all 9 digits for this cell, none worked, this path is a dead end."

class Solution {
    public void solveSudoku(char[][] board) { solve(board); }

    private boolean solve(char[][] board) {
        for (int i = 0; i < 9; i++) {
            for (int j = 0; j < 9; j++) {
                if (board[i][j] != '.') continue;
                for (char c = '1'; c <= '9'; c++) {
                    if (isValid(board, i, j, c)) {
                        board[i][j] = c;                    // CHOOSE
                        if (solve(board)) return true;      // EXPLORE
                        board[i][j] = '.';                  // UNCHOOSE
                    }
                }
                return false; // No valid digit → backtrack
            }
        }
        return true; // All cells filled
    }

    private boolean isValid(char[][] board, int row, int col, char c) {
        for (int i = 0; i < 9; i++) {
            if (board[row][i] == c) return false;
            if (board[i][col] == c) return false;
            if (board[3*(row/3)+i/3][3*(col/3)+i%3] == c) return false;
        }
        return true;
    }
}
How the 3x3 box check works: board[3*(row/3)+i/3][3*(col/3)+i%3]

Step 1: Find top-left corner of the box (integer division truncates)

int boxRow = 3 * (row / 3);
int boxCol = 3 * (col / 3);

Cell (4,7) → box starts at (3,6)
Cell (8,2) → box starts at (6,0)

Step 2: Loop i = 0 to 8, map to 3x3 offsets

i:    0  1  2  3  4  5  6  7  8
i/3:  0  0  0  1  1  1  2  2  2   ← row offset
i%3:  0  1  2  0  1  2  0  1  2   ← col offset

Visits: (0,0)(0,1)(0,2)(1,0)(1,1)(1,2)(2,0)(2,1)(2,2)

Combined: For cell (4,7), box starts at (3,6). Checks:

(3,6) (3,7) (3,8)
(4,6) (4,7) (4,8)
(5,6) (5,7) (5,8)   ← all 9 cells in the box
Logic in plain words:
  1. Scan the board cell by cell (left to right, top to bottom)
  2. Skip filled cells
  3. When you find an empty cell:
    • Try digits 1 through 9
    • For each digit, check: is it valid in this row, column, and 3x3 box?
    • If valid → place it, recurse (solve the rest). If recursion returns true → solved, return true. If false → wrong digit, undo, try next
    • If no digit (1-9) worked → return false (dead end, tell caller to backtrack)
  4. If the loop finishes without finding any empty cell → board is full → return true
Key insight: We don't pick which cell to fill — the double for-loop finds the next empty cell for us. We only decide which digit goes there. If no digit works, we tell the caller "your earlier choice was wrong" by returning false.
Pattern 5: Partition / Split String
How to Arrive at This: "At each position, try ALL possible cuts (substrings). If the cut is valid (palindrome, valid IP, etc.), take it and recurse on the remainder."
Analogy: Cutting a rope into pieces. At any point, cut at various positions. Each cut gives one piece + remaining rope. Keep cutting until nothing's left. If all pieces satisfy the constraint, it's a valid partitioning.

palindrome partitioning split into parts restore IP break string into

Palindrome Partitioning (LC 131) - Medium

class Solution {
    public List<List<String>> partition(String s) {
        List<List<String>> result = new ArrayList<>();
        backtrack(s, 0, new ArrayList<>(), result);
        return result;
    }

    private void backtrack(String s, int start, List<String> current,
                           List<List<String>> result) {
        if (start == s.length()) {
            result.add(new ArrayList<>(current));
            return;
        }
        for (int end = start + 1; end <= s.length(); end++) {
            String sub = s.substring(start, end);
            if (isPalindrome(sub)) {
                current.add(sub);                          // CHOOSE
                backtrack(s, end, current, result);        // EXPLORE
                current.remove(current.size() - 1);        // UNCHOOSE
            }
        }
    }

    private boolean isPalindrome(String s) {
        int l = 0, r = s.length() - 1;
        while (l < r) { if (s.charAt(l++) != s.charAt(r--)) return false; }
        return true;
    }
}

Restore IP Addresses (LC 93) - Medium

private void backtrack(String s, int start, List<String> segments, List<String> result) {
    if (segments.size() == 4 && start == s.length()) {
        result.add(String.join(".", segments));
        return;
    }
    if (segments.size() == 4 || start == s.length()) return;

    for (int len = 1; len <= 3 && start + len <= s.length(); len++) {
        String seg = s.substring(start, start + len);
        if (seg.length() > 1 && seg.charAt(0) == '0') break;  // No leading zeros
        if (Integer.parseInt(seg) > 255) break;

        segments.add(seg);
        backtrack(s, start + len, segments, result);
        segments.remove(segments.size() - 1);
    }
}

Letter Combinations of Phone (LC 17) - Medium

class Solution {
    private String[] mapping = {"","","abc","def","ghi","jkl","mno","pqrs","tuv","wxyz"};

    public List<String> letterCombinations(String digits) {
        List<String> result = new ArrayList<>();
        if (digits == null || digits.isEmpty()) return result;
        backtrack(digits, 0, new StringBuilder(), result);
        return result;
    }

    private void backtrack(String digits, int index, StringBuilder current, List<String> result) {
        if (index == digits.length()) { result.add(current.toString()); return; }

        for (char c : mapping[digits.charAt(index) - '0'].toCharArray()) {
            current.append(c);                                // CHOOSE
            backtrack(digits, index + 1, current, result);    // EXPLORE
            current.deleteCharAt(current.length() - 1);       // UNCHOOSE
        }
    }
}
Pattern 6: Generate Valid Sequences
How to Arrive at This: "Build the sequence character by character. Only make choices that maintain validity. Track constraints as parameters — this naturally prunes invalid branches."
Analogy: Building a sentence word by word with grammar rules. Only pick words that are grammatically correct given what you've written so far.

generate parentheses generate all valid construct all enumerate

Generate Parentheses (LC 22) - Medium

class Solution {
    public List<String> generateParenthesis(int n) {
        List<String> result = new ArrayList<>();
        backtrack(n, 0, 0, new StringBuilder(), result);
        return result;
    }

    private void backtrack(int n, int open, int close, StringBuilder current,
                           List<String> result) {
        if (current.length() == 2 * n) { result.add(current.toString()); return; }

        if (open < n) {          // Can add '(' if haven't used all n
            current.append('(');
            backtrack(n, open + 1, close, current, result);
            current.deleteCharAt(current.length() - 1);
        }
        if (close < open) {      // Can add ')' only if close < open
            current.append(')');
            backtrack(n, open, close + 1, current, result);
            current.deleteCharAt(current.length() - 1);
        }
    }
}
Two rules for valid parentheses: (1) Add ( if open < n. (2) Add ) if close < open.
Three things that make this problem different

1. Why no need to unpick (with String approach):

Strings are immutable in Java. current + "(" creates a new string — current itself is unchanged. Backtracking is free:

// String (immutable) — no undo needed
backtrack(current + "(", left - 1, right);  // new string, current unchanged
backtrack(current + ")", left, right - 1);  // current still the same

// StringBuilder (mutable) — MUST undo
sb.append('(');
backtrack(n, open + 1, close, sb, result);
sb.deleteCharAt(sb.length() - 1);  // must unpick

Rule: Mutable state (List, array, StringBuilder) → must unpick. Immutable state (String) → backtracking is free.

2. Why no stack needed to validate:

A stack checks if the final string is valid. But we're building the string character by character — we can enforce validity as we go using just two counters (open and close). If we never break the rules, the result is guaranteed valid. No need to check after the fact.

3. Why just two if-conditions are enough:

At each step, there are only 2 possible characters: ( or ). Each has one rule:

  • open < n → still have ( left to place → allowed to add (
  • close < open → more ( have been placed than ) → allowed to add )

These two conditions guarantee we never have more ) than ( at any point (no invalid prefix), and we use exactly n of each (correct length). No other validation needed.

Letter Case Permutation (LC 784) - Medium

private void backtrack(char[] chars, int index, List<String> result) {
    if (index == chars.length) { result.add(new String(chars)); return; }

    if (Character.isLetter(chars[index])) {
        chars[index] = Character.toLowerCase(chars[index]);
        backtrack(chars, index + 1, result);  // Choice 1: lowercase
        chars[index] = Character.toUpperCase(chars[index]);
        backtrack(chars, index + 1, result);  // Choice 2: uppercase
    } else {
        backtrack(chars, index + 1, result);  // Digit: no choice
    }
}

Section 4: Code Templates (Copy-Paste Ready)

Master Backtracking Template

void backtrack(State state, List<...> result) {
    if (isGoal(state)) {
        result.add(copy(state));   // Always COPY
        return;
    }
    for (Choice choice : getChoices(state)) {
        if (isValid(choice, state)) {
            applyChoice(choice, state);     // CHOOSE
            backtrack(state, result);        // EXPLORE
            undoChoice(choice, state);       // UNCHOOSE
        }
    }
}

Subsets Template

void subsets(int[] nums, int start, List<Integer> current, List<List<Integer>> result) {
    result.add(new ArrayList<>(current));  // Every node = valid subset
    for (int i = start; i < nums.length; i++) {
        // For dups: if (i > start && nums[i] == nums[i-1]) continue;
        current.add(nums[i]);
        subsets(nums, i + 1, current, result);
        current.remove(current.size() - 1);
    }
}

Permutations Template

void permutations(int[] nums, boolean[] used, List<Integer> current, List<List<Integer>> result) {
    if (current.size() == nums.length) { result.add(new ArrayList<>(current)); return; }
    for (int i = 0; i < nums.length; i++) {
        if (used[i]) continue;
        // For dups: if (i > 0 && nums[i] == nums[i-1] && !used[i-1]) continue;
        used[i] = true;
        current.add(nums[i]);
        permutations(nums, used, current, result);
        current.remove(current.size() - 1);
        used[i] = false;
    }
}

Grid DFS Backtracking Template

int[][] dirs = {{0,1},{0,-1},{1,0},{-1,0}};

boolean gridDFS(char[][] board, int row, int col, /* params */) {
    if (outOfBounds || visited || mismatch) return false;
    if (goalReached()) return true;

    char original = board[row][col];
    board[row][col] = '#';  // CHOOSE: mark visited

    for (int[] d : dirs)
        if (gridDFS(board, row + d[0], col + d[1] /* ... */))
            return true;    // EXPLORE

    board[row][col] = original;  // UNCHOOSE: unmark
    return false;
}

Section 5: Complexity Analysis

ProblemTimeSpace
SubsetsO(2^n × n)O(n) recursion depth
PermutationsO(n! × n)O(n)
Combinations C(n,k)O(C(n,k) × k)O(k)
N-QueensO(n!)O(n²)
Word SearchO(m·n · 4^L)O(L)
Generate ParenthesesO(4^n / √n)O(n)
Palindrome PartitionO(2^n × n)O(n)
General rule: Output size often dominates. You can't do better than generating all solutions. Efficiency gains come from pruning.

Section 6: Edge Cases & Common Mistakes

Edge Cases:
  • Empty input: Return [[]] for subsets, [] for permutations
  • Single element: Verify template handles it
  • All duplicates: [1,1,1] — sort + skip logic critical
  • Large n: n > 20 for subsets may TLE
Bug #1 (THE Java Reference Trap): 
// WRONG: all entries will be same empty list
result.add(current);

// CORRECT: make a copy
result.add(new ArrayList<>(current));
Bug #2: Forgetting to unchoose 
current.add(nums[i]);
backtrack(...);
// MISSING: current.remove(current.size() - 1);
Bug #3: Not sorting before skipping duplicates 
// Skip logic ONLY works on sorted arrays!
Arrays.sort(nums);  // MUST come first
Bug #4: Using start+1 instead of i+1 
// WRONG: always starts from same position
backtrack(nums, start + 1, current, result);

// CORRECT: advance past the element we just chose
backtrack(nums, i + 1, current, result);
Bug #5: Using result.contains() for dedup 
// WRONG: O(k × result.size()) per check — causes TLE
if (!result.contains(current))
    result.add(new ArrayList<>(current));

// CORRECT: skip duplicates structurally
// Subsets/Combos (Pick/Not-Pick): skip all dups on NOT PICK
while (index + 1 < nums.length && nums[index] == nums[index + 1]) index++;
// Subsets/Combos (loop): skip dups at same level
if (i > start && nums[i] == nums[i - 1]) continue;
Bug #6: Missing return after base case 
// WRONG: falls through, causes ArrayIndexOutOfBoundsException
if (row == board.length) {
    result.add(buildResult(board));
}
// code below still runs with row == board.length!

// CORRECT: always return after base case
if (row == board.length) {
    result.add(buildResult(board));
    return;
}
Bug #7: Forgetting pruning in combinations 
// Without pruning: explores hopeless branches
// With pruning: if remaining numbers can't fill k slots, stop early
if (current.size() + (n - i + 1) < k) return;
Key Design Decisions:
  • Pick/Not-Pick vs Loop: Both work for subsets/combinations. Pick/Not-Pick maps to "include or exclude" binary tree. Loop maps to "iterate over remaining choices."
  • Permutations can't use Pick/Not-Pick: Every element must appear — question is "which goes at this position?", not "include or not?". Use loop from 0 with used[] array.
  • !used[i-1] for permutation dups: Enforces left-to-right ordering among duplicates. If previous dup is free (backtracked), it already had its turn at this position — skip.
  • N-Queens is not grid DFS: Go row by row, loop columns per row, check column + both upper diagonals. No direction array needed.

Section 7: Interview Tips

  1. Clarify: "Can elements repeat? Duplicates in input? Does order matter? All solutions or just count?"
  2. Draw the recursion tree for n=2 or n=3 BEFORE coding. Shows your thinking and makes coding straightforward.
  3. State the pattern: "This is a classic backtracking problem. I'll use choose-explore-unchoose."
  4. Add the copy line immediately when writing result.add(...).
  5. Discuss complexity before being asked: "O(2^n) for subsets because binary choice per element."
For SDE 2: The template is nearly identical across ALL backtracking problems. The only things that change are: base case, choices, validation, and next index (i vs i+1 vs 0).

Section 8: Related Topics

TopicConnection
Dynamic ProgrammingBacktracking + memoization = top-down DP
DFS on Graphs/TreesSame recursive structure; grid backtracking IS DFS with undo
Bit ManipulationAlternative subset generation: each number 0 to 2^n-1 = one subset
Constraint SatisfactionN-Queens, Sudoku are CSPs; backtracking is the standard approach
Progression Path:
Recursion → Backtracking → Memoization → Dynamic Programming
  ↓                        ↓
DFS on Trees/Graphs      Constraint Satisfaction

Week 2 (Duplicates & Constraints): LC 90, 47, 39, 40, 17
Week 3 (Grid & Advanced): LC 79, 131, 93, 51, 37

Section 10: Quick Revision Card

The Master Template

void backtrack(input, start, current, result) {
    if (base_case) { result.add(new ArrayList<>(current)); return; }
    for (choice from start to end) {
        if (invalid) continue;    // Prune
        current.add(choice);      // CHOOSE
        backtrack(next_state);    // EXPLORE
        current.remove(last);     // UNCHOOSE
    }
}

One-line Summary

PatternOne-liner
P1: SubsetsInclude/exclude each element; collect at every node
P2: CombinationsSubsets with size constraint; prune when not enough remaining
P3: PermutationsTry every unused element at each position; used[] array
P4: Grid/BoardDFS with mark/unmark visited
P5: PartitionTry all valid cuts, recurse on remainder
P6: GenerateTrack constraints as params, only recurse on valid choices

Decision Tree

"All subsets / subsequences" → Pattern 1
"Combinations of size k" → Pattern 2
"Sum equals target" → Pattern 2 (Combination Sum)
"All orderings / arrangements" → Pattern 3
"Search in grid / place on board" → Pattern 4
"Split string into valid parts" → Pattern 5
"Generate valid sequences" → Pattern 6

Has duplicates? → Sort + skip nums[i] == nums[i-1]
Can reuse? → Pass i instead of i+1
Order matters? → Permutations (loop from 0, used[])
Order doesn't matter? → Subsets/Combinations (loop from start)

The THREE Things to Remember

  1. CHOOSE - EXPLORE - UNCHOOSE: The heart of every backtracking solution. Forget unchoose = wrong answer.
  2. ALWAYS COPY before adding to result: result.add(new ArrayList<>(current)) — the Java reference trap will silently give wrong answers.
  3. Duplicates = SORT + SKIP at same level: if (i > start && nums[i] == nums[i-1]) continue; for subsets/combos. if (i > 0 && nums[i] == nums[i-1] && !used[i-1]) continue; for permutations.

Key Differences at a Glance

SUBSETS:       loop i = start..n   next = i+1   collect at EVERY node
COMBINATIONS:  loop i = start..n   next = i+1   collect when size == k
COMB SUM:      loop i = start..n   next = i     collect when sum == target
PERMUTATIONS:  loop i = 0..n      used[]      collect when size == n
