Trees & Binary Search Trees

Comprehensive DSA Notes for SDE 2 Interview Prep

Section 1: Topic Overview

TypeDescriptionKey Property
Binary TreeEach node has at most 2 childrenNo ordering constraint
BSTBinary tree with orderingleft subtree < node < right subtree (ALL descendants)
N-ary TreeUp to N children per nodeFile systems, org charts
Analogy: A tree is like a company org chart. CEO = root, managers = nodes, direct reports = children. A BST is an org chart where everyone to the left has a smaller employee ID.
THE highest frequency topic for SDE 2 interviews. Expect 1-2 tree questions in almost every interview loop. They test recursive thinking, subproblem decomposition, and DFS/BFS understanding.

Section 2: Core Concepts & Theory

TreeNode in Java

class TreeNode {
    int val;
    TreeNode left, right;
    TreeNode(int val) { this.val = val; }
}

Binary Tree Properties

PropertyDefinition
HeightEdges from node DOWN to farthest leaf
DepthEdges from root DOWN to node
Full BTEvery node has 0 or 2 children
Complete BTAll levels filled except last (left-to-right). Heaps are complete BTs.
Balanced BTLeft/right heights differ by at most 1 → O(log n) operations
Common confusion: Height = measured going DOWN. Depth = measured going UP. Height of tree = depth of deepest leaf.

BST Property (CRITICAL)

For every node: ALL left subtree values < node < ALL right subtree values. Not just immediate children — ALL descendants.

Tree Traversals

TraversalOrderUse Case
InorderLeft, Node, RightBST → sorted order
PreorderNode, Left, RightCopy/serialize tree
PostorderLeft, Right, NodeDelete tree, evaluate expressions
Level-order (BFS)Level by levelLevel problems, min depth
Morris TraversalThreaded inorderO(1) space inorder (no stack/recursion)
Hack: Morris Traversal for O(1) space inorder.
The trick: temporarily thread the rightmost node of the left subtree back to the current node. This lets you "return" to the parent without a stack. After visiting, undo the thread.
You rarely need to code it, but mentioning it as a follow-up to "can you do inorder in O(1) space?" is an SDE 2 differentiator. The key idea: predecessor.right = curr (create thread), then later predecessor.right = null (restore tree).

DFS vs BFS

AspectDFSBFS
Data structureStack / recursionQueue
SpaceO(h) heightO(w) max width
Use forPath problems, subtreeLevel problems, shortest path

Section 3: Pattern Recognition & Intuition Building

Pattern 1: DFS Traversal (Recursive)
How to Arrive at This: Most tree problems reduce to: "Do something at current node, combining results from left and right subtrees." Base case = null. Recurse left, recurse right, combine.
Analogy: A manager asking two team leads a question. Each lead asks their sub-teams. Leaf employees give base answers. Results bubble back up. You combine what both leads reported.

traverse visit all nodes depth height path from root

Top-Down vs Bottom-Up DFS

The single most important distinction in tree problems:
Top-DownBottom-Up
DirectionParent → ChildrenChildren → Parent
HowPass parameters DOWNReturn values UP
ExamplePath Sum (pass running sum)Max Depth (children report height)

Max Depth (LC 104) - Easy (Bottom-Up)

public int maxDepth(TreeNode root) {
    if (root == null) return 0;
    return 1 + Math.max(maxDepth(root.left), maxDepth(root.right));
}

Same Tree (LC 100) - Easy

public boolean isSameTree(TreeNode p, TreeNode q) {
    if (p == null && q == null) return true;
    if (p == null || q == null) return false;
    return p.val == q.val && isSameTree(p.left, q.left) && isSameTree(p.right, q.right);
}

Invert Binary Tree (LC 226) - Easy

public TreeNode invertTree(TreeNode root) {
    if (root == null) return null;
    TreeNode temp = root.left;
    root.left = invertTree(root.right);
    root.right = invertTree(temp);
    return root;
}

Path Sum (LC 112) - Easy (Top-Down)

public boolean hasPathSum(TreeNode root, int targetSum) {
    if (root == null) return false;
    if (root.left == null && root.right == null) return root.val == targetSum;
    return hasPathSum(root.left, targetSum - root.val)
        || hasPathSum(root.right, targetSum - root.val);
}
Pattern 2: BFS / Level-Order Traversal
How to Arrive at This: "Level by level" = BFS. The critical trick: capture size = queue.size() BEFORE processing to know how many nodes belong to the current level.
Analogy: Taking a class photo row by row. You count people in each row BEFORE they step forward. That count is queue.size().

level by level level order right side view zigzag minimum depth

Level Order Traversal (LC 102) - Medium

public List<List<Integer>> levelOrder(TreeNode root) {
    List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();
    if (root == null) return result;

    Queue<TreeNode> queue = new LinkedList<>();
    queue.offer(root);

    while (!queue.isEmpty()) {
        int size = queue.size();  // KEY: capture BEFORE processing
        List<Integer> level = new ArrayList<>();

        for (int i = 0; i < size; i++) {
            TreeNode node = queue.poll();
            level.add(node.val);
            if (node.left != null) queue.offer(node.left);
            if (node.right != null) queue.offer(node.right);
        }
        result.add(level);
    }
    return result;
}

BFS Variations

ProblemTwist
Right Side View (LC 199)Take LAST node of each level
Zigzag (LC 103)Reverse alternate levels
Min Depth (LC 111)Stop at FIRST leaf (BFS finds it first)
Pattern 3: BST Operations
How to Arrive at This: Two key insights:
1. Inorder traversal of BST = sorted order. Secret weapon for many BST problems.
2. Use BST property to go left or right — just like binary search on an array.
Analogy: A BST is like a phone book. To find a name, open the middle — if your name comes before, look left; if after, look right. Reading front to back (inorder) gives sorted order.

BST sorted order validate search kth smallest delete

Validate BST (LC 98) - Medium

Pass a valid range [min, max] down. Narrow the range at each node.

public boolean isValidBST(TreeNode root) {
    return validate(root, Long.MIN_VALUE, Long.MAX_VALUE);
}

private boolean validate(TreeNode node, long min, long max) {
    if (node == null) return true;
    if (node.val <= min || node.val >= max) return false;
    return validate(node.left, min, node.val) &&   // left must be < node
           validate(node.right, node.val, max);     // right must be > node
}
Why long? Node values can be Integer.MIN_VALUE, so sentinel bounds must be outside int range.

Kth Smallest in BST (LC 230) - Medium

private int count = 0, result = 0;

public int kthSmallest(TreeNode root, int k) {
    inorder(root, k);
    return result;
}

private void inorder(TreeNode node, int k) {
    if (node == null) return;
    inorder(node.left, k);
    if (++count == k) { result = node.val; return; }
    inorder(node.right, k);
}

Delete Node in BST (LC 450) - Medium

Copy value approach
public TreeNode deleteNode(TreeNode root, int key) {
    if (root == null) return null;
    if (key < root.val)
        root.left = deleteNode(root.left, key);
    else if (key > root.val)
        root.right = deleteNode(root.right, key);
    else {
        if (root.left == null) return root.right;
        if (root.right == null) return root.left;
        // Copy successor's value, delete successor
        TreeNode succ = root.right;
        while (succ.left != null) succ = succ.left;
        root.val = succ.val;
        root.right = deleteNode(root.right, succ.val);
    }
    return root;
}
Restructure approach
public TreeNode deleteNode(TreeNode root, int key) {
    if (root == null) return null;
    if (key < root.val)
        root.left = deleteNode(root.left, key);
    else if (key > root.val)
        root.right = deleteNode(root.right, key);
    else {
        if (root.left == null) return root.right;
        if (root.right == null) return root.left;
        // Attach left subtree to bottom-left of right
        TreeNode leftNode = root.left;
        TreeNode rightNode = root.right;
        while (rightNode.left != null)
            rightNode = rightNode.left;
        rightNode.left = leftNode;
        root = root.right;
    }
    return root;
}
Why no parent tracking? root.left = deleteNode(root.left, key) is the parent-child link management. Parent says "my child is whatever you return."
When key not found: returns same node unchanged — assignment is a no-op.
When key found: Leaf → return null. One child → return the other. Two children → copy value approach overwrites value and deletes successor; restructure approach hangs entire left subtree to bottom-left of right subtree and returns right child.
Same pattern as invertTree: return value carries the rewiring info back up.
Pattern 4: Lowest Common Ancestor (LCA)
How to Arrive at This:
BST: Both < current? Go left. Both > current? Go right. They split? Current IS the LCA.
General BT: Recurse both sides. Non-null from both = current is LCA.
Analogy: At a fork in the road: if both friends went left, follow left. If they split (one left, one right), the fork itself is the meeting point — that's the LCA.

LCA of BST (LC 235) - Medium

public TreeNode lowestCommonAncestor(TreeNode root, TreeNode p, TreeNode q) {
    TreeNode curr = root;
    while (curr != null) {
        if (p.val < curr.val && q.val < curr.val) curr = curr.left;
        else if (p.val > curr.val && q.val > curr.val) curr = curr.right;
        else return curr;  // Split point = LCA
    }
    return null;
}

LCA of Binary Tree (LC 236) - Medium

public TreeNode lowestCommonAncestor(TreeNode root, TreeNode p, TreeNode q) {
    if (root == null || root == p || root == q) return root;

    TreeNode left = lowestCommonAncestor(root.left, p, q);
    TreeNode right = lowestCommonAncestor(root.right, p, q);

    if (left != null && right != null) return root; // split = LCA
    return left != null ? left : right;
}
Pattern 5: Tree Construction / Serialization
How to Arrive at This: Preorder's first element = root. Find that root in inorder — everything left is left subtree, everything right is right subtree. Use HashMap for O(1) lookup.
Analogy: Preorder is a general announcing formations: "I'm the leader. Here's my left battalion. Here's my right battalion." Inorder tells you who's in which battalion.

Construct from Preorder+Inorder (LC 105) - Medium

private Map<Integer, Integer> inorderMap;
private int preIndex = 0;

public TreeNode buildTree(int[] preorder, int[] inorder) {
    inorderMap = new HashMap<>();
    for (int i = 0; i < inorder.length; i++) inorderMap.put(inorder[i], i);
    return build(preorder, 0, inorder.length - 1);
}

private TreeNode build(int[] preorder, int inLeft, int inRight) {
    if (inLeft > inRight) return null;
    int rootVal = preorder[preIndex++];
    TreeNode root = new TreeNode(rootVal);
    int inMid = inorderMap.get(rootVal);
    root.left = build(preorder, inLeft, inMid - 1);
    root.right = build(preorder, inMid + 1, inRight);
    return root;
}

Serialize/Deserialize (LC 297) - Hard

// Serialize: preorder with null markers
public String serialize(TreeNode root) {
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    serHelper(root, sb);
    return sb.toString();
}
private void serHelper(TreeNode node, StringBuilder sb) {
    if (node == null) { sb.append("null,"); return; }
    sb.append(node.val).append(",");
    serHelper(node.left, sb);
    serHelper(node.right, sb);
}

// Deserialize: consume tokens from queue
public TreeNode deserialize(String data) {
    Queue<String> q = new LinkedList<>(Arrays.asList(data.split(",")));
    return desHelper(q);
}
private TreeNode desHelper(Queue<String> q) {
    String val = q.poll();
    if (val.equals("null")) return null;
    TreeNode node = new TreeNode(Integer.parseInt(val));
    node.left = desHelper(q);
    node.right = desHelper(q);
    return node;
}
Hack: Always serialize null nodes for unique subtree identification.
For duplicate subtree detection (LC 652), your serialization MUST include # (or null) for null children. Without it, different tree shapes produce the same string. E.g., a left-skewed 1-2 and a right-skewed 1-2 both serialize to "1,2". With nulls: "1,2,#,#,#" vs "1,#,2,#,#" — correctly distinct.
Pattern 6 & 7: Path Problems + Global Variable Pattern
How to Arrive at This:
Root-to-leaf paths: Top-down DFS, pass accumulated state down.
Any-to-any paths: At each node, compute best path THROUGH this node (both children) → update global max. But RETURN only single-branch gain to parent (can't fork).
Analogy: At a hiking junction: "best VIEW from here" considers BOTH trails (global answer). "Best trail FROM here to the mountaintop (parent)" can only pick ONE trail — you can't walk both at once.
The key insight: What you RETURN to parent (single branch) is DIFFERENT from what might be the ANSWER (both branches). Use a global variable for the answer.

diameter max path sum path sum all paths longest path

Diameter of Binary Tree (LC 543) - Easy

The diameter of a binary tree is the length of the longest path between any two nodes.
The path may or may not pass through the root Length = number of edges (not nodes)

Key Insight: At any node: The longest path through that node = height(left subtree) + height(right subtree)
private int maxDiameter = 0;

public int diameterOfBinaryTree(TreeNode root) {
    height(root);
    return maxDiameter;
}

private int height(TreeNode node) {
    if (node == null) return 0;
    int left = height(node.left);
    int right = height(node.right);

    maxDiameter = Math.max(maxDiameter, left + right);  // GLOBAL: through this node
    return 1 + Math.max(left, right);                    // LOCAL: height to parent
}

Max Path Sum (LC 124) - Hard

private int maxSum = Integer.MIN_VALUE;

public int maxPathSum(TreeNode root) {
    maxGain(root);
    return maxSum;
}

private int maxGain(TreeNode node) {
    if (node == null) return 0;
    int leftGain = Math.max(maxGain(node.left), 0);   // ignore negative
    int rightGain = Math.max(maxGain(node.right), 0);

    maxSum = Math.max(maxSum, node.val + leftGain + rightGain); // GLOBAL
    return node.val + Math.max(leftGain, rightGain);             // LOCAL
}

Path Sum II (LC 113) - Medium (Top-Down)

public List<List<Integer>> pathSum(TreeNode root, int targetSum) {
    List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();
    dfs(root, targetSum, new ArrayList<>(), result);
    return result;
}

private void dfs(TreeNode node, int remaining, List<Integer> path,
                 List<List<Integer>> result) {
    if (node == null) return;
    path.add(node.val);
    if (node.left == null && node.right == null && remaining == node.val) {
        result.add(new ArrayList<>(path));  // COPY!
    }
    dfs(node.left, remaining - node.val, path, result);
    dfs(node.right, remaining - node.val, path, result);
    path.remove(path.size() - 1);  // backtrack
}

Section 4: Code Templates

Recursive DFS (All Three Orders)

void preorder(TreeNode n)  { if(n==null) return; process(n); preorder(n.left); preorder(n.right); }
void inorder(TreeNode n)   { if(n==null) return; inorder(n.left); process(n); inorder(n.right); }
void postorder(TreeNode n) { if(n==null) return; postorder(n.left); postorder(n.right); process(n); }

Iterative Inorder (Stack)

Deque<TreeNode> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
TreeNode curr = root;
while (curr != null || !stack.isEmpty()) {
    while (curr != null) { stack.push(curr); curr = curr.left; }  // push all lefts
    curr = stack.pop();
    process(curr);                                                  // visit
    curr = curr.right;                                              // go right
}
Mnemonic: "Push all lefts, pop and visit, go right." Repeat.

BFS Level-Order

Queue<TreeNode> queue = new LinkedList<>();
queue.offer(root);
while (!queue.isEmpty()) {
    int size = queue.size();        // capture level size
    for (int i = 0; i < size; i++) {
        TreeNode node = queue.poll();
        process(node);
        if (node.left != null) queue.offer(node.left);
        if (node.right != null) queue.offer(node.right);
    }
}

Diameter / Max Path (Global Variable)

private int globalAnswer;
private int dfs(TreeNode node) {
    if (node == null) return 0;
    int left = dfs(node.left), right = dfs(node.right);
    globalAnswer = Math.max(globalAnswer, COMBINE(left, right, node)); // both sides
    return SINGLE_BRANCH(left, right, node);                           // one side only
}

Section 5: Complexity Analysis

OperationTimeSpace
DFS / BFS traversalO(n)O(h) / O(w)
BST search/insert/delete (balanced)O(log n)O(log n)
BST operations (skewed)O(n)O(n)
Build from traversalsO(n)O(n)
Key: Balanced tree: h = O(log n). Skewed tree: h = O(n). Perfect tree: nodes = 2^(h+1) - 1.

Section 6: Edge Cases & Common Mistakes

Edge Cases: Null root, single node, skewed tree (linked list), BST with duplicates, negative values in path sums, integer overflow (use long).
Bug: BST validation checking only parent-child 
// WRONG: only checks immediate children
if (node.left != null && node.left.val >= node.val) return false;

// CORRECT: pass min/max range for ALL ancestors
if (node.val <= min || node.val >= max) return false;
Bug: Returning combined path instead of single branch 
// WRONG: can't fork when returning to parent
return leftGain + rightGain + node.val;

// CORRECT: pick ONE side
return node.val + Math.max(leftGain, rightGain);
Bug: BFS not capturing level size 
// WRONG: queue.size() changes during iteration!
for (int i = 0; i < queue.size(); i++) { ... }

// CORRECT: capture first
int size = queue.size();
for (int i = 0; i < size; i++) { ... }
Bug: Diameter of a binary tree
 Counting nodes instead of edges
Assuming diameter always passes through root

Section 7: Interview Tips

  1. Trees = #1 topic for SDE 2. Over-prepare here.
  2. First question: "Is this top-down or bottom-up DFS?"
  3. For BST: Always ask "Can I leverage sorted property?"
  4. Draw the tree for the example. Trace your algorithm on it.
  5. Start recursive, go iterative only if asked.
Communication template: "I see this is a [tree type] problem. I'll use [pattern]. The key insight is [insight]. I'll use [top-down/bottom-up] DFS because [reason]. Time: O(n), Space: O(h)."

Section 8: Related Topics

TopicConnection
GraphsTrees are acyclic connected graphs. Graph DFS/BFS generalizes tree DFS/BFS.
HeapsHeap = complete binary tree with heap property. NOT a BST.
TrieTree-like structure for string prefix matching.
RecursionTree problems are the best way to practice recursion.

Section 10: Quick Revision Card

One-line Summary

PatternOne-liner
P1: DFSBase=null, recurse left+right, combine. Top-down passes info down, bottom-up returns up.
P2: BFSQueue + size = queue.size() before processing each level.
P3: BSTInorder = sorted. Use BST property to go left/right like binary search.
P4: LCABST: follow split. BT: non-null from both sides = current is LCA.
P5: ConstructionPreorder = root. Inorder splits left/right. HashMap for O(1) lookup.
P6: PathsRoot-to-leaf = top-down. Any-to-any = both-children global + single-branch return.
P7: Global VarGlobal tracks best across all nodes. Return = local info to parent.

Decision Tree

Is it a BST?
  YES → Inorder (sorted)? → Kth smallest, validate
        → Go left/right? → Search, LCA of BST
  NO → Level-related? → BFS (level order, right view, min depth)
      → Path problem?
          Through any node? → Global variable (diameter, max path sum)
          Root-to-leaf? → Top-down DFS with state
      → Construction? → Pattern 5 (preorder+inorder, serialize)
      → Standard DFS (top-down or bottom-up)

The THREE Things to Remember

  1. Almost every tree problem is DFS. Default: base case=null, recurse left+right, combine. Decide top-down vs bottom-up.
  2. BST = sorted order. Inorder gives sorted output. Eliminate half the tree at each step.
  3. Global vs Return in path problems. Answer may use BOTH children (global variable). Return to parent can only use ONE child. This solves Diameter, Max Path Sum, Longest Univalue Path.

Last updated: March 2026 | Target: SDE 2 interviews at Indian product companies

09 / 18

Heaps & Priority Queues

Comprehensive DSA Notes for SDE 2 Interview Prep

AspectDetail
Frequency★★★★☆ — 8-12% of DSA rounds; almost guaranteed in Amazon/Google
CompaniesGoogle, Amazon, Microsoft, Flipkart, Uber, PhonePe
Difficulty spread30% Easy, 50% Medium, 20% Hard
PrerequisitesArrays, Basic Sorting, Trees (conceptual)
Often combined withGreedy, Graphs, Sliding Window, HashMaps
Key insight: A heap is the engine behind "give me the best/worst option right now" problems. At SDE 2 level, you're expected to not just use PriorityQueue but to know WHY it's the right choice and articulate the alternative (sorting, BST, etc.).

Section 1: Topic Overview

Heaps sit at the intersection of multiple topics (greedy, graphs, streaming data) and test whether you truly understand trade-offs — not just API usage.

What they testSDE 2 Expectation
Pattern recognitionIdentify which of 6 patterns applies within 30 seconds
Comparator fluencyWrite multi-field comparators instantly without fumbling
Complexity depthKnow O(n) heapify, O(n log k) for Top K, when heap beats sort
Trade-off articulation"Why heap and not X?" — know sort, QuickSelect, deque alternatives
SDE 1 vs SDE 2: At SDE 1 level, "use a heap" is the answer. At SDE 2 level, "why a heap and not X?" is the question.

Section 2: Core Concepts & Fundamentals

2.1 Three Questions to Recognize Heap Problems

#QuestionIf YES...
1Do I need the smallest/largest element repeatedly?Heap is a strong candidate
2Is the data streaming (coming one at a time)?Heap, because you can't sort a stream
3Do I need only K elements, not the full sorted order?Heap of size K beats full sort
Real-world analogy: Think of a hospital ER. Patients arrive at random times (stream), and the doctor always sees the most critical patient next. You don't sort ALL patients — you just need the most urgent one. That's a heap.

2.2 The "Heap Smell Test" — Keyword Recognition

Keyword/PhraseLikely Pattern
"K largest" / "K smallest" / "K closest"Top K
"Kth largest" / "Kth smallest"Kth Element
"merge K sorted lists/arrays"Merge K Sorted
"median of stream"Two Heaps
"schedule tasks" / "most frequent first"Greedy + Heap
"shortest path" / "minimum cost path"Dijkstra
"top K frequent"Top K + HashMap

2.3 When It's NOT a Heap (Common Traps)

ScenarioUse InsteadWhy
Sliding window min/maxMonotonic DequeO(1) amortized vs O(log n)
Need ALL data sortedSort the arrayO(n log n) either way, sort is simpler
Need both min AND maxTreeMap / balanced BSTHeap only gives one extreme
Fixed data, single Kth queryQuickSelectO(n) average vs O(n log k)

2.4 Decision Tree

Need extreme element (min/max)?
├── YES
│   ├── Repeatedly? (stream / dynamic data)
│   │   ├── YES → HEAP
│   │   └── NO (one-time) → Sort or QuickSelect
│   ├── Only K elements matter?
│   │   ├── YES → HEAP of size K
│   │   └── NO (need all sorted) → Sort
│   └── Need BOTH min and max?
│       ├── YES → Two Heaps or TreeMap
│       └── NO → Single Heap
└── NO → Probably not a heap problem

2.5 Min-Heap vs Max-Heap

         Min-Heap                    Max-Heap
           1                            9
          / \                          / \
         3   5                        7   8
        / \                          / \
       7   9                        3   5

    Root = SMALLEST              Root = LARGEST
    Parent <= Children           Parent >= Children

Array representation (0-indexed):

  • Parent of i = (i - 1) / 2
  • Left child of i = 2 * i + 1
  • Right child of i = 2 * i + 2
Key insight: A heap is a complete binary tree stored as an array. No pointers, no nodes — just an array with a clever indexing scheme. This is why it's cache-friendly and fast.

2.6 Java PriorityQueue

// MIN-HEAP (default) — smallest element at top
PriorityQueue<Integer> minHeap = new PriorityQueue<>();

// MAX-HEAP — largest element at top
PriorityQueue<Integer> maxHeap = new PriorityQueue<>(Collections.reverseOrder());
// OR
PriorityQueue<Integer> maxHeap = new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> b - a);

// CUSTOM COMPARATOR — sort by second element of array
PriorityQueue<int[]> pq = new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> a[1] - b[1]);

// MULTI-FIELD COMPARATOR — sort by freq, then by value
PriorityQueue<int[]> pq = new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) ->
    a[0] != b[0] ? a[0] - b[0] : a[1] - b[1]);
MethodWhat it doesTime
offer(e)Add elementO(log n)
poll()Remove & return topO(log n)
peek()Return top without removingO(1)
size()Number of elementsO(1)
isEmpty()Check if emptyO(1)
remove(e)Remove specific elementO(n)
Warning: PriorityQueue iteration order is NOT sorted. Only poll() gives elements in heap order. Iterating with a for-each loop gives arbitrary order.

Section 3: Pattern Recognition Framework

Six patterns cover ~95% of heap interview problems.

Pattern 1: Kth Largest / Smallest Element

When to use: Find the single Kth largest or Kth smallest element, especially in a stream.

How to arrive at this (thinking process):
  1. You see "Kth largest" or "Kth smallest."
  2. For static data: QuickSelect O(n) average is optimal. But for streaming data, you can't re-run QuickSelect every time.
  3. Maintain a min-heap of size K. The root is always the Kth largest.
  4. Why? The heap holds the K largest elements seen so far. The smallest among those K (the root) is by definition the Kth largest.
Analogy: A leaderboard that only shows the top K players. The player at position K (bottom of leaderboard) is the Kth largest. New scores come in — if they beat position K, they push someone off.
Why min-heap for Kth LARGEST? The heap holds only K elements — the K largest seen so far. The smallest among them IS the Kth largest: 
nums = [3, 1, 5, 12, 2, 11], k = 3

Sorted:            [1, 2, 3, 5, 11, 12]
                             ↑ 3rd largest = 5

Min-heap (size 3): [5, 11, 12]
                    ↑ root = smallest in heap = 5 = same!
Heap holds [5, 11, 12]: 12 = 1st largest, 11 = 2nd, 5 = 3rd ← smallest of the top K = root of min-heap. 
// LC 703: Kth Largest Element in a Stream
class KthLargest {
    private PriorityQueue<Integer> minHeap;
    private int k;

    public KthLargest(int k, int[] nums) {
        this.k = k;
        minHeap = new PriorityQueue<>();
        for (int n : nums) add(n);
    }

    public int add(int val) {
        minHeap.offer(val);
        if (minHeap.size() > k) minHeap.poll();
        return minHeap.peek(); // root = Kth largest
    }
}
Pattern 1 vs 2: Pattern 1 returns a single value and often involves a stream; Pattern 2 returns a collection of K elements.
Edge cases: Stream has fewer than K elements initially, duplicate values in stream, val equals current Kth largest.
Pattern 2: Top K Elements

When to use: Find K largest, K smallest, K most frequent, K closest.

How to arrive at this (thinking process):
  1. You see "K largest/smallest/closest/frequent" in the problem.
  2. Ask: "Do I need ALL elements sorted?" No, just K of them.
  3. Sorting everything is O(n log n). Can I do better?
  4. If I maintain a min-heap of size K for K largest, I process each element in O(log k). Total: O(n log k). When k << n, this is much better.
  5. Why min-heap for K largest? Because the root is the smallest among the K largest — it acts as a bouncer.
The Bouncer Analogy: Imagine a VIP section with exactly K seats. The bouncer is the weakest person inside (min-heap root). When someone new arrives: if they're stronger than the bouncer → bouncer gets kicked out, new person enters. If weaker → "Sorry, VIP is full." At the end, everyone inside is top K.
The counterintuitive trick:
K largest → use Min-Heap of size K
K smallest → use Max-Heap of size K
Why? The heap root is the "gatekeeper" — it's the weakest member of the elite group. 
// Top K Largest Elements
public int[] topKLargest(int[] nums, int k) {
    PriorityQueue<Integer> minHeap = new PriorityQueue<>();
    for (int num : nums) {
        minHeap.offer(num);
        if (minHeap.size() > k) minHeap.poll(); // bouncer kicks smallest
    }
    return minHeap.stream().mapToInt(i -> i).toArray();
}

Top K Frequent — HashMap + Heap combo:

Why do we need both HashMap and Heap?

Each solves a different part:

  • HashMap → "how many times does each element appear?" [1,1,1,2,2,3] → {1:3, 2:2, 3:1}
  • Heap → "which k elements have the highest frequency?" top 2 → [1, 2]

Brute force: HashMap to count, then sort all entries by frequency, return top k → O(n log n)

Optimized: HashMap to count, then min-heap of size k to keep only top k → O(n log k)

Brute force (sort)Heap (size k)
Count freqO(n)O(n)
Find top kO(n log n) — sort allO(n log k) — heap of size k
TotalO(n log n)O(n log k)

Why min-heap, not max-heap? Min-heap of size k keeps the smallest frequency on top. When a new element has higher frequency, it kicks out the smallest. After processing all entries, the heap holds exactly the top k.

Solution in words:

  1. Iterate over array, put each number in a HashMap to count frequencies.
  2. Iterate over the map, add each {number, frequency} pair to a min-heap.
  3. Keep only k elements in the heap — whenever size exceeds k, poll the minimum (lowest frequency gets kicked out).
  4. Return the remaining k elements as an array — these are the top k frequent.
// LC 347: Top K Frequent Elements
public int[] topKFrequent(int[] nums, int k) {
    Map<Integer, Integer> freq = new HashMap<>();
    for (int n : nums) freq.merge(n, 1, Integer::sum);

    // Min-heap by frequency — bouncer is least frequent in VIP
    PriorityQueue<Map.Entry<Integer, Integer>> pq =
        new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> a.getValue() - b.getValue());

    for (Map.Entry<Integer, Integer> entry : freq.entrySet()) {
        pq.offer(entry);
        if (pq.size() > k) pq.poll();
    }
    return pq.stream().mapToInt(e -> e.getKey()).toArray();
}
Common PriorityQueue comparator patterns

The pattern: (a, b) -> a.X - b.X for min-heap, (a, b) -> b.X - a.X for max-heap.

// 1. Map entries — sort by frequency (Top K Frequent)
PriorityQueue<Map.Entry<Integer, Integer>> pq =
    new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> a.getValue() - b.getValue());

// 2. int arrays — sort by specific index
// e.g., intervals, edges [cost, node], points [x, y]
new PriorityQueue<int[]>((a, b) -> a[0] - b[0]);  // by first element
new PriorityQueue<int[]>((a, b) -> a[1] - b[1]);  // by second element

// 3. Integers — min vs max
PriorityQueue<Integer> minHeap = new PriorityQueue<>();
PriorityQueue<Integer> maxHeap = new PriorityQueue<>(Collections.reverseOrder());

// 4. Multi-field — e.g., Dijkstra [distance, node]
new PriorityQueue<int[]>((a, b) -> a[0] != b[0]
    ? a[0] - b[0] : a[1] - b[1]);

// 5. ListNode — sort by value (Merge K Lists)
new PriorityQueue<ListNode>((a, b) -> a.val - b.val);
ProblemHeap containsSort by
Top K Frequent (LC 347)Map.Entry.getValue() (frequency)
K Closest Points (LC 973)int[]x²+y² (distance)
Merge K Lists (LC 23)ListNode.val
Meeting Rooms II (LC 253)intend time
Dijkstraint[][0] (distance)
Edge cases: k == nums.length (return all), k == 0 (empty), all same frequency (any K valid), k > unique elements.

Classic problems: LC 347, LC 973, LC 215, LC 451

Pattern 3: Merge K Sorted Structures

When to use: K sorted lists/arrays/streams need to be merged into one sorted output.

How to arrive at this (thinking process):
  1. You have K sorted structures. Need one merged sorted output.
  2. Brute force: dump everything, sort. O(N log N) where N = total elements.
  3. Better: at any point, the global minimum must be among the K "front" elements (heads of each list). Each list is sorted, so its smallest unprocessed element is its head.
  4. Min-heap of size K tracks these heads. Poll smallest, advance that list, push new head.
  5. Heap size always ≤ K, so each operation is O(log K). Total: O(N log K).
Analogy: K assembly lines producing items in order. A supervisor checks the front item of each line, picks the smallest, ships it, and the line advances. The supervisor's "comparison board" is the heap.
// LC 23: Merge K Sorted Lists
public ListNode mergeKLists(ListNode[] lists) {
    PriorityQueue<ListNode> pq = new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> a.val - b.val);

    // Seed with heads
    for (ListNode l : lists) {
        if (l != null) pq.offer(l);
    }

    ListNode dummy = new ListNode(0), curr = dummy;
    while (!pq.isEmpty()) {
        ListNode smallest = pq.poll();
        curr.next = smallest;
        curr = curr.next;
        if (smallest.next != null) pq.offer(smallest.next);
    }
    return dummy.next;
}
Edge cases: Some lists are null/empty, K = 1 (return that list), K = 0, lists of very different lengths, all lists have same values.

Classic problems: LC 23, LC 632

Pattern 4: Two Heaps (Median Finding)

When to use: Track the median of a dynamic/streaming dataset, or partition data into two halves.

How to arrive at this (thinking process):
  1. You need the median = the "middle" of sorted data.
  2. Sorting every time is O(n log n). Can we maintain sortedness incrementally?
  3. Key insight: you don't need FULL sorted order. Just the boundary between lower and upper halves.
  4. Lower half → max-heap (gives you the largest of the small half).
  5. Upper half → min-heap (gives you the smallest of the large half).
  6. Median = top of max-heap (odd count) or average of both tops (even count).
Analogy: Split a class into "shorter half" and "taller half." The tallest in the short group (max-heap root) and the shortest in the tall group (min-heap root) are the two people closest to the median height.
Lower Half      |     Upper Half
[1, 2, 3]       |     [4, 5, 6]
 maxHeap               minHeap
 root = 3              root = 4
 Median = (3 + 4) / 2 = 3.5

Invariants to maintain:

  1. Every element in maxHeap ≤ every element in minHeap
  2. maxHeap.size() ≥ minHeap.size() (maxHeap can have at most 1 extra)
  3. Size difference is at most 1
// LC 295: Find Median from Data Stream
class MedianFinder {
    PriorityQueue<Integer> maxHeap = new PriorityQueue<>(Collections.reverseOrder()); // lower
    PriorityQueue<Integer> minHeap = new PriorityQueue<>(); // upper

    public void addNum(int num) {
        maxHeap.offer(num);                          // Step 1: add to lower
        minHeap.offer(maxHeap.poll());               // Step 2: balance values
        if (minHeap.size() > maxHeap.size()) {       // Step 3: balance sizes
            maxHeap.offer(minHeap.poll());
        }
    }

    public double findMedian() {
        if (maxHeap.size() > minHeap.size()) {
            return maxHeap.peek();
        }
        return (maxHeap.peek() + minHeap.peek()) / 2.0;
    }
}
The 3-step add process is elegant and foolproof:
  1. Add to maxHeap (tentatively put in lower half)
  2. Move maxHeap's root to minHeap (ensures max of lower ≤ min of upper)
  3. If minHeap got too big, move its root back to maxHeap (balance sizes)
This guarantees both invariants are maintained after every insert.
Edge cases: First element (single, median = itself), two elements (one in each), all identical, elements in sorted order, integer overflow when averaging → use a / 2.0 + b / 2.0.

Classic problems: LC 295, LC 480, LC 502

Pattern 5: Greedy + Heap

When to use: Repeatedly pick the best available option to build an optimal solution. The heap is the tool; greedy is the algorithm.

How to arrive at this (thinking process):
  1. The problem asks you to make a sequence of choices, each time picking the "best" option.
  2. After each choice, the available options may change.
  3. You need a structure that always gives you the current best in O(log n).
  4. That's a heap. The greedy strategy defines "best" (most frequent, cheapest, most urgent), and the heap serves it up.
Analogy: A chef preparing a buffet with a limited oven. Each time the oven is free, they cook the dish that the most people are waiting for (greedy choice). The heap tracks which dishes have the most pending orders.
// LC 621: Task Scheduler
public int leastInterval(char[] tasks, int n) {
    int[] freq = new int[26];
    for (char t : tasks) freq[t - 'A']++;

    PriorityQueue<Integer> maxHeap = new PriorityQueue<>(Collections.reverseOrder());
    for (int f : freq) if (f > 0) maxHeap.offer(f);

    Queue<int[]> cooldown = new LinkedList<>(); // [remaining_count, available_at_time]
    int time = 0;

    while (!maxHeap.isEmpty() || !cooldown.isEmpty()) {
        time++;
        if (!maxHeap.isEmpty()) {
            int count = maxHeap.poll() - 1;
            if (count > 0) cooldown.offer(new int[]{count, time + n});
        }
        if (!cooldown.isEmpty() && cooldown.peek()[1] == time) {
            maxHeap.offer(cooldown.poll()[0]);
        }
    }
    return time;
}

Reorganize String (LC 767) — Same pattern:

public String reorganizeString(String s) {
    int[] freq = new int[26];
    for (char c : s.toCharArray()) freq[c - 'a']++;

    PriorityQueue<int[]> maxHeap = new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> b[1] - a[1]);
    for (int i = 0; i < 26; i++)
        if (freq[i] > 0) maxHeap.offer(new int[]{i, freq[i]});

    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    while (maxHeap.size() >= 2) {
        int[] first = maxHeap.poll();
        int[] second = maxHeap.poll();
        sb.append((char)(first[0] + 'a'));
        sb.append((char)(second[0] + 'a'));
        if (--first[1] > 0) maxHeap.offer(first);
        if (--second[1] > 0) maxHeap.offer(second);
    }
    if (!maxHeap.isEmpty()) {
        if (maxHeap.peek()[1] > 1) return ""; // impossible
        sb.append((char)(maxHeap.poll()[0] + 'a'));
    }
    return sb.toString();
}
Edge cases: Cooldown n = 0 (no constraint), single task type, all tasks same frequency, impossible case in reorganize (one char freq > (len+1)/2).

Classic problems: LC 621, LC 767, LC 502

Pattern 6: Dijkstra (Graph + Heap)

When to use: Shortest path in a weighted graph with non-negative edge weights.

How to arrive at this (thinking process):
  1. You see "shortest path" or "minimum cost" in a weighted graph.
  2. BFS works for unweighted graphs. But with weights, BFS doesn't guarantee shortest path.
  3. Replace BFS's regular queue with a priority queue — always expand the node with the smallest tentative distance.
  4. This is Dijkstra's algorithm. The heap ensures we process nodes in order of increasing distance.
Analogy: Navigating a city with roads of different lengths. Instead of exploring all roads equally (BFS), you always drive down the route with the shortest total distance from start. The priority queue is your GPS.
// Dijkstra's Algorithm
int[] dijkstra(List<List<int[]>> graph, int src, int n) {
    int[] dist = new int[n];
    Arrays.fill(dist, Integer.MAX_VALUE);
    dist[src] = 0;

    // [distance, node]
    PriorityQueue<int[]> pq = new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> a[0] - b[0]);
    pq.offer(new int[]{0, src});

    while (!pq.isEmpty()) {
        int[] cur = pq.poll();
        int d = cur[0], u = cur[1];

        if (d > dist[u]) continue; // STALE ENTRY — critical optimization

        for (int[] nei : graph.get(u)) {
            int v = nei[0], w = nei[1];
            if (dist[u] + w < dist[v]) {
                dist[v] = dist[u] + w;
                pq.offer(new int[]{dist[v], v});
            }
        }
    }
    return dist;
}
Critical: The if (d > dist[u]) continue line skips stale (outdated) entries. Since Java's PriorityQueue doesn't support decrease-key, we add duplicate entries and skip stale ones. Without this, the algorithm is correct but may TLE.
Note: Full Dijkstra coverage including variations (multi-source, path reconstruction, bidirectional) is in Topic 11: Graphs Advanced. This section covers the heap-focused essentials.
Edge cases: Disconnected graph (dist remains MAX_VALUE), no outgoing edges from source, self-loops, multiple edges between same pair, negative weights: Dijkstra DOES NOT WORK — use Bellman-Ford.

Classic problems: LC 743, LC 787, LC 1631

Section 4: Code Templates

Clean, copy-paste-ready templates for each pattern.

Template 1: Top K Pattern

public int[] topK(int[] nums, int k) {
    PriorityQueue<Integer> minHeap = new PriorityQueue<>();
    for (int num : nums) {
        minHeap.offer(num);
        if (minHeap.size() > k) minHeap.poll();
    }
    return minHeap.stream().mapToInt(i -> i).toArray();
}

Template 2: Merge K Sorted Pattern

public ListNode mergeK(ListNode[] lists) {
    PriorityQueue<ListNode> pq = new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> a.val - b.val);
    for (ListNode l : lists) if (l != null) pq.offer(l);
    ListNode dummy = new ListNode(0), curr = dummy;
    while (!pq.isEmpty()) {
        curr.next = pq.poll();
        curr = curr.next;
        if (curr.next != null) pq.offer(curr.next);
    }
    return dummy.next;
}

Template 3: Two Heaps Pattern

PriorityQueue<Integer> maxHeap = new PriorityQueue<>(Collections.reverseOrder());
PriorityQueue<Integer> minHeap = new PriorityQueue<>();

void addNum(int num) {
    maxHeap.offer(num);
    minHeap.offer(maxHeap.poll());
    if (minHeap.size() > maxHeap.size()) maxHeap.offer(minHeap.poll());
}

double findMedian() {
    return maxHeap.size() > minHeap.size() ? maxHeap.peek()
           : (maxHeap.peek() + minHeap.peek()) / 2.0;
}

Template 4: Frequency + Heap Combo

public int[] topKByFrequency(int[] nums, int k) {
    Map<Integer, Integer> freq = new HashMap<>();
    for (int n : nums) freq.merge(n, 1, Integer::sum);

    PriorityQueue<Map.Entry<Integer, Integer>> pq =
        new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> a.getValue() - b.getValue());

    for (Map.Entry<Integer, Integer> e : freq.entrySet()) {
        pq.offer(e);
        if (pq.size() > k) pq.poll();
    }
    return pq.stream().mapToInt(Map.Entry::getKey).toArray();
}

Template 5: Dijkstra Pattern

int[] dijkstra(List<List<int[]>> graph, int src, int n) {
    int[] dist = new int[n];
    Arrays.fill(dist, Integer.MAX_VALUE);
    dist[src] = 0;
    PriorityQueue<int[]> pq = new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> a[0] - b[0]);
    pq.offer(new int[]{0, src});
    while (!pq.isEmpty()) {
        int[] cur = pq.poll();
        if (cur[0] > dist[cur[1]]) continue; // skip stale entries
        for (int[] nei : graph.get(cur[1])) {
            if (dist[cur[1]] + nei[1] < dist[nei[0]]) {
                dist[nei[0]] = dist[cur[1]] + nei[1];
                pq.offer(new int[]{dist[nei[0]], nei[0]});
            }
        }
    }
    return dist;
}

Section 5: Complexity Analysis

Core Heap Operations

OperationTimeNotes
Insert (offer)O(log n)Bubble up
Delete top (poll)O(log n)Bubble down
Peek top (peek)O(1)Just read root
Build heap (heapify)O(n)NOT O(n log n) — explain in interview!
SearchO(n)Heap is NOT for searching
Remove arbitraryO(n)Find first O(n), then O(log n) to fix
Why is build heap O(n)? Leaf nodes (half the tree) need 0 swaps, nodes one level up need at most 1 swap, etc. The math works out to O(n) via a geometric series. Mentioning this in an interview signals depth.

Pattern-Specific Complexities

PatternTimeSpaceNotes
Top KO(n log k)O(k)k << n makes this much better than sort
Kth Element (stream)O(n log k)O(k)Per add: O(log k)
Merge K SortedO(N log k)O(k)N = total elements, k = number of lists
Two HeapsO(n log n)O(n)Each insert O(log n), store all n
Greedy + HeapO(n log n)O(n)Depends on specific problem
DijkstraO(E log V)O(V + E)With adjacency list + binary heap

When Heap Beats Sort (and Vice Versa)

ScenarioHeapSortWinner
Top K of n elements (k << n)O(n log k)O(n log n)Heap
Full sorted order neededO(n log n)O(n log n)Sort (simpler)
Streaming data, need KthO(log k) per addO(n log n) redoHeap
Single Kth element, staticO(n log k)O(n) QuickSelectNeither (QS wins)
Repeated access to extremesO(log n) per opO(n) per re-sortHeap

Section 6: Edge Cases & Gotchas

Edge Cases to Always Check

Edge CaseHow to Handle
Empty input / no elementsReturn empty result; check before heap operations
K > array length or K == 0Handle upfront; K=0 means empty result
All elements identicalHeap still works; comparator returns 0
Single elementTrivial case; often the answer itself
Negative numbersComparator still works correctly
Integer overflow in comparatorUse Integer.compare() not a - b
Stream with fewer than K elementsHeap size < K; peek may not be meaningful
Null entries in list of listsFilter nulls before adding to heap

The 8 Common Mistakes (SDE 2 Must-Know)

1. Using Max-Heap for K largest: Use Min-Heap of size K instead. The root is the bouncer/gatekeeper. Max-Heap of size K gives K smallest.
2. Forgetting stale entry check in Dijkstra: Without if (d > dist[u]) continue, you process outdated entries and waste time.
3. Integer overflow in comparator: (a, b) -> a - b overflows for large ints. Always use Integer.compare(a, b). 
// WRONG — overflows for Integer.MAX_VALUE - (-1)
PriorityQueue<Integer> pq = new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> a - b);

// CORRECT
PriorityQueue<Integer> pq = new PriorityQueue<>(Integer::compare);
4. Assuming PriorityQueue iterates in order: Only poll() gives sorted order. For-each loop gives arbitrary order.
5. Two Heaps invariant breaking: Always rebalance sizes after every insert. Skip rebalance = wrong median.
6. Using heap for sliding window min/max: Use Monotonic Deque instead. O(1) amortized vs O(log n).
7. Forgetting null checks in Merge K: Always check if (l != null) before adding to heap.
8. Dijkstra with negative weights: Dijkstra does NOT work. Immediately pivot to Bellman-Ford.

Section 7: Interview Tips

1. Always state why heap over sorting: "I need repeated access to the extreme, not a full sort. Heap gives O(1) peek with O(log n) maintenance vs O(n log n) re-sort."
2. Mention O(n) heapify: When the interviewer asks complexity, mention that building a heap from an array is O(n), not O(n log n). This shows depth.
3. Min vs Max clarification: Before coding, say "I'll use a min-heap of size K for K largest because the root acts as a gatekeeper — anything below it doesn't make the cut."
4. Know the alternatives: For Top K Frequent, mention QuickSelect O(n) average. For sliding window, mention monotonic deque. Shows breadth.
5. Custom comparator fluency: Write multi-field comparators instantly: (a, b) -> a[1] != b[1] ? a[1] - b[1] : a[0] - b[0]. Fumbling wastes interview time.
6. Dijkstra negative weight trap: If the graph has negative weights, say "Dijkstra won't work here, we need Bellman-Ford." Classic SDE 2 differentiator.
7. Two Heaps invariant: Explain the size invariant before coding: "maxHeap.size() ≥ minHeap.size(), difference at most 1." Explaining invariants before code shows maturity.
8. Stream vs static: Ask the interviewer: "Is the data given upfront or streaming? That changes whether I sort or use a heap."
9. Time-space trade-off: "I'm trading O(k) space for O(n log k) time instead of O(1) space with O(n log n) sort. Since k << n, this is a net win."
10. Dry run with small examples: After writing heap code, trace 3-4 elements showing heap state at each step. Interviewers love this.

Section 10: Quick Revision Card

1-Minute Before Interview

HEAP = Complete Binary Tree + Heap Property
Java: PriorityQueue (MIN by default)
Max-Heap: new PriorityQueue<>(Collections.reverseOrder())
Custom: new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> a[1] - b[1])

6 PATTERNS:
1. Top K         → Min-Heap size K (bouncer analogy)
2. Kth in Stream → Min-Heap size K, peek() = answer
3. Merge K Sorted → Min-Heap of K heads, always poll smallest
4. Two Heaps     → maxHeap(lower) + minHeap(upper), rebalance!
5. Greedy + Heap → Heap powers greedy, always pick best available
6. Dijkstra      → BFS with PQ instead of Queue

REMEMBER:
- Min-Heap for K LARGEST (counterintuitive!)
- Build heap = O(n), NOT O(n log n)
- Integer.compare() not a-b for large numbers
- poll() gives order, iterator does NOT
- Sliding window min/max = Monotonic Deque, NOT heap

3 QUESTIONS TO ASK YOURSELF:
1. Do I need ALL data sorted, or just the extreme?
2. Is data streaming/dynamic or fixed?
3. Am I repeatedly picking the best option? (→ greedy + heap)

COMPARATOR CHEAT SHEET:
Min-Heap: (a, b) -> a - b  OR  Integer::compare
Max-Heap: (a, b) -> b - a  OR  Collections.reverseOrder()
By field: (a, b) -> a[1] - b[1]
Multi:    (a, b) -> a[0] != b[0] ? a[0] - b[0] : a[1] - b[1]

Graphs — BFS & DFS

Comprehensive DSA Notes for SDE 2 Interview Prep

Key insight: About 30-40% of graph problems in interviews are actually grid/matrix problems in disguise. The word "graph" may never appear in the problem statement.

Section 1: Topic Overview

Graphs are THE most versatile topic in SDE 2 interviews. The real skill is recognizing WHEN something is a graph problem.

Company TierFrequencyFavorite Subtopics
FAANG / Big TechVery HighGrid BFS, Topological Sort, Implicit Graphs
Indian Product (Tier 1)Very HighNumber of Islands, Course Schedule, Word Ladder
