Space Optimization Summary

TechniqueApplicable WhenHow
Two variablesdp[i] depends on dp[i-1], dp[i-2] onlyReplace array with prev1, prev2
1D array (backward)2D 0/1 knapsack (each item used once)Single row, iterate w from W to 0
1D array (forward)2D unbounded knapsack (items reusable)Single row, iterate w from 0 to W
Rolling two rowsdp[i] depends on dp[i-1] row onlyAlternate: dp[i%2]

Section 6: Edge Cases & Gotchas

⚠ Empty array / n = 0: Always handle empty input before accessing array elements. Many DP solutions throw ArrayIndexOutOfBoundsException if you forget.
⚠ Single element: For House Robber with 1 element, return that element. For knapsack with 1 item, check if it fits. Always test with n = 1.
⚠ All elements same: Test with arrays like [5, 5, 5, 5]. Exposes bugs in comparison logic or duplicate handling.
Integer overflow in counting problems: Results grow exponentially. Use % (1_000_000_007) when required. In Java, intermediate int addition can overflow — use long if needed. 
int MOD = 1_000_000_007;
dp[i] = (dp[i] + dp[i - coin]) % MOD;
⚠ Knapsack with capacity 0: dp[0] should be 0 (for max value) or true (for subset sum with target 0). Ensure loop bounds handle W = 0.
⚠ Negative numbers in subset sum: Standard subset sum assumes non-negative numbers. With negatives, use an offset (dp[s + OFFSET]) or HashMap for memoization.
Off-by-one in bottom-up DP: The most common bug. If items are 0-indexed but DP table is 1-indexed (or vice versa), you read wrong values. Decide convention and be consistent. 
// Common 1-indexed convention:
// dp[i] uses items[i-1] (item index shifted by 1)
for (int i = 1; i <= n; i++) {
    // use weight[i-1], value[i-1]
}
0/1 Knapsack backward iteration: If you iterate forward in the 1D 0/1 knapsack, you use an item multiple times (turning it into unbounded). This is one of the most common interview mistakes.
⚠ Forgetting base cases: Missing or wrong base cases is the #1 reason DP solutions fail. Always explicitly write out base cases before coding the transition.

Section 7: Interview Tips

Tip: The 3-Step Approach
Always follow this order in an interview:
  1. Recursion — Write brute force recursive solution first
  2. Memoization — Add a cache to avoid recomputation
  3. Tabulation — Convert to iterative bottom-up if time permits
This shows structured thinking and lets the interviewer see your thought process.
Tip: “What’s the last decision?” trick
To find the recurrence, ask: “What was the last thing I did?” For House Robber: “Did I rob house n or skip it?” This naturally gives you the two branches of the recurrence.
Tip: State definition is 80% of the problem
Spend time defining what dp[i] means in plain English before writing any code. If you define the state correctly, the transition almost writes itself.
Tip: Recognizing DP problems
If the problem asks for: “minimum cost”, “maximum profit”, “number of ways”, “is it possible”, “longest/shortest” — think DP. Then verify: does it have overlapping subproblems?
Tip: Space optimization shows mastery
Even if you code the 2D version, mention: “This can be optimized to O(W) space by using a 1D array.” Interviewers love this.
Tip: Printing the actual solution (backtracking)
If asked “which items were selected?”, backtrack through the DP table: 
// Backtrack through 2D knapsack table
List<Integer> items = new ArrayList<>();
int w = W;
for (int i = n; i > 0; i--) {
    if (dp[i][w] != dp[i - 1][w]) {
        items.add(i - 1); // item i-1 was included
        w -= weight[i - 1];
    }
}
Tip: Time management
Do not jump to DP immediately. First confirm: (1) overlapping subproblems exist, (2) greedy does not work. Spending 2 minutes on this analysis saves you from going down the wrong path.
Tip: Draw the recursion tree
When explaining your approach, draw a small recursion tree (n=4 or 5) to show overlapping subproblems. This convinces the interviewer that DP is correct and clarifies the recurrence.

Week 3: Problems 10, 11, 8, 9 (0/1 Knapsack + Subset Sum family)
Week 4: Problems 13, 14, 15 (Miscellaneous 1D DP)

Section 10: Quick Revision Card

DP Part 1 — Quick Revision Card

Decision Flowchart: “What pattern is this?”

Is the problem asking for min/max/count/possible?
  |
  YES → Does it have overlapping subproblems?
    |
    YES → IT'S DP! Now which pattern?
    |
    | Q: Does dp[i] depend on only i-1 and i-2?
    |    YES → PATTERN 1: Fibonacci/Linear
    |
    | Q: "Take or skip" with adjacency constraint?
    |    YES → PATTERN 2: House Robber
    |
    | Q: Fixed capacity + items used ONCE?
    |    YES → PATTERN 3: 0/1 Knapsack
    |
    | Q: Fixed capacity + items REUSABLE?
    |    YES → PATTERN 4: Unbounded Knapsack
    |
    | Q: "Can we achieve exact sum?" or "Partition?"
    |    YES → PATTERN 5: Subset Sum
    |
    | Q: "How many ways?" with additive counting?
    |    YES → PATTERN 6: Counting Ways

Key Formulas

PatternFormula
Fibonaccidp[i] = dp[i-1] + dp[i-2]
House Robberdp[i] = max(dp[i-1], dp[i-2] + nums[i])
0/1 Knapsackdp[w] = max(dp[w], dp[w-wt[i]] + val[i]) (backward w)
Unbounded KSdp[w] = max(dp[w], dp[w-wt[i]] + val[i]) (forward w)
Subset Sumdp[s] = dp[s] || dp[s - num] (backward s)
Count Waysdp[s] = dp[s] + dp[s - num] (direction varies)

Space Optimization Tricks

  • Fibonacci pattern → two variables (prev1, prev2) → O(1)
  • 0/1 Knapsack → 1D array, iterate BACKWARDS → O(W)
  • Unbounded Knapsack → 1D array, iterate FORWARDS → O(W)
  • Subset Sum → 1D boolean array, iterate BACKWARDS → O(S)
  • 2D Grid → single row array (rolling) → O(cols)
Hack: When dp[i] depends only on dp[i-1] and dp[i-2], use two variables, not an array.
Fibonacci, House Robber, Climbing Stairs — all follow this pattern:
int prev2 = base0, prev1 = base1;
for (...) { int curr = f(prev1, prev2); prev2 = prev1; prev1 = curr; }
O(1) space instead of O(n). Interviewers notice when you optimize this without being asked.
Hack: Debug DP by printing the table, not tracing recursion.
If your DP gives the wrong answer, don't trace the recursion tree mentally. Fill a small table (3-4 rows/cols) by hand on paper. Compare with your code's output. Finds the bug in seconds. In interviews, sketch the table before coding — it also impresses interviewers.

DP Signal Words

“minimum cost” • “maximum profit” • “number of ways” • “is it possible” • “longest/shortest” • “count distinct” • “how many” • “can you partition” • “optimal strategy” • “at most K” • “exact sum/target” • “choose subset”

Common Mistakes Checklist

  • Did I handle n=0 and n=1 base cases?
  • Is my loop direction correct? (backward for 0/1, forward for unbounded)
  • Am I using 0-indexed or 1-indexed consistently?
  • Did I initialize dp[0] correctly?
  • For counting: am I using modulo 10&sup9;+7 if required?
  • For min problems: did I initialize dp[] to Integer.MAX_VALUE / infinity?
  • For Coin Change: did I handle the -1 (impossible) case?
  • Combinations vs Permutations: are my loop orders correct?
  • Can I optimize space? (mention even if you don't implement)

These notes cover Topic 12: DP Part 1. Continue to Topic 13 for 2D DP, String DP (LCS, Edit Distance), and Interval DP patterns.

13 / 18

Dynamic Programming Part 2 — 2D DP, String DP, Interval DP

Comprehensive DSA Notes for SDE 2 Interview Prep

AspectDetail
Frequency★★★★★ — 20-30% of interviews feature 2D/String DP
CompaniesGoogle, Amazon, Microsoft, Flipkart, Goldman Sachs, DE Shaw, Uber, Atlassian
PrerequisitesDP Part 1 (Topic 12) — 1D DP, Knapsack patterns
Often combined withTwo Pointers, Greedy, Tries
Prerequisite: If you haven’t mastered 1D DP and Knapsack patterns from Topic 12, go back first — everything here builds on that foundation.

Section 1: Topic Overview

What’s Different from Part 1?

AspectPart 1 (1D DP)Part 2 (2D DP)
Statedp[i] — single indexdp[i][j] — two indices
Table1D array2D matrix
Typical inputSingle array/sequenceGrid, two strings, range
Fill orderLeft to rightRow-by-row, diagonal, or gap-based
Space optimizationO(1) or O(few variables)O(n) rolling array from O(n²)

The three major families:

  1. Grid DP — paths and costs on a 2D grid
  2. String DP — comparing/transforming two strings (LCS, Edit Distance, Matching)
  3. Interval DP — optimal splitting/merging of a range [i..j]

Section 2: Core Concepts & Data Structures

2D DP Table Fill Order

  • Grid DP: Row by row, left to right (top-left to bottom-right)
  • String DP: Row by row (comparing prefixes of two strings)
  • Interval DP: By increasing gap size (diagonal filling)

String DP Fundamentals

Subsequence vs Substring — Clarify IMMEDIATELY in interviews.
  • Subsequence: characters in order but NOT necessarily contiguous. “ace” is a subsequence of “abcde”.
  • Substring: characters in order AND contiguous. “bcd” is a substring of “abcde”.

When comparing two strings s1[0..m-1] and s2[0..n-1]:

  • State is usually dp[i][j] = answer considering first i chars of s1 and first j chars of s2
  • Use 1-indexed DP table of size (m+1) × (n+1) to handle empty string base cases cleanly

Interval DP Structure

For a range [i..j]:

  • Gap method: iterate gap from 0 to n-1, then for each gap iterate starting index
  • This ensures when computing dp[i][j], all shorter intervals are already computed

State Compression: 2D to 1D

Many 2D DP problems only look at the previous row. Reduce space from O(m×n) to O(n) using a rolling 1D array. Key: when dp[i][j] depends only on dp[i-1][...] and dp[i][j-1], a single 1D array suffices filling left to right.

Memoization with Multiple Parameters

int[][] memo; // initialized to -1

int solve(int i, int j) {
    if (memo[i][j] != -1) return memo[i][j];
    // compute and store
    memo[i][j] = result;
    return result;
}

Section 3: Pattern Recognition Framework

The most important section. The “How to Arrive” boxes explain the mental process to recognize each pattern.

Pattern 1: Grid DP (Unique Paths / Min Path Sum)
How to Arrive at This Pattern:
Ask yourself: “Am I traversing a 2D grid with movement constraints (right/down only)?”

Signal words: grid, matrix, paths, reach bottom-right, obstacles, minimum cost path
Thinking:
  1. I have a grid. I can only move right or down.
  2. To reach cell (i,j), I must come from (i-1,j) or (i,j-1).
  3. This gives a natural recurrence. That’s Grid DP.

State: dp[i][j] = answer for reaching cell (i, j) from (0, 0)

Transition:

  • Counting paths: dp[i][j] = dp[i-1][j] + dp[i][j-1]
  • Min cost: dp[i][j] = grid[i][j] + min(dp[i-1][j], dp[i][j-1])

Unique Paths (LC 62)

public int uniquePaths(int m, int n) {
    int[][] dp = new int[m][n];
    for (int i = 0; i < m; i++) dp[i][0] = 1;
    for (int j = 0; j < n; j++) dp[0][j] = 1;

    for (int i = 1; i < m; i++) {
        for (int j = 1; j < n; j++) {
            dp[i][j] = dp[i - 1][j] + dp[i][j - 1];
        }
    }
    return dp[m - 1][n - 1];
}

Unique Paths II — With Obstacles (LC 63)

public int uniquePathsWithObstacles(int[][] grid) {
    int m = grid.length, n = grid[0].length;
    if (grid[0][0] == 1) return 0;

    int[] dp = new int[n]; // space-optimized to 1D
    dp[0] = 1;

    for (int i = 0; i < m; i++) {
        for (int j = 0; j < n; j++) {
            if (grid[i][j] == 1) {
                dp[j] = 0;
            } else if (j > 0) {
                dp[j] += dp[j - 1];
            }
        }
    }
    return dp[n - 1];
}

Minimum Path Sum (LC 64)

public int minPathSum(int[][] grid) {
    int m = grid.length, n = grid[0].length;
    int[] dp = new int[n];

    dp[0] = grid[0][0];
    for (int j = 1; j < n; j++) dp[j] = dp[j - 1] + grid[0][j];

    for (int i = 1; i < m; i++) {
        dp[0] += grid[i][0];
        for (int j = 1; j < n; j++) {
            dp[j] = grid[i][j] + Math.min(dp[j], dp[j - 1]);
        }
    }
    return dp[n - 1];
}

Maximal Square (LC 221)

public int maximalSquare(char[][] matrix) {
    int m = matrix.length, n = matrix[0].length;
    int[][] dp = new int[m + 1][n + 1];
    int maxSide = 0;

    for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++) {
        for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++) {
            if (matrix[i - 1][j - 1] == '1') {
                dp[i][j] = 1 + Math.min(dp[i - 1][j - 1],
                                Math.min(dp[i - 1][j], dp[i][j - 1]));
                maxSide = Math.max(maxSide, dp[i][j]);
            }
        }
    }
    return maxSide * maxSide;
}

Complexity: Time O(m × n), Space O(m × n) or O(n) with optimization

Pattern 2: Longest Common Subsequence (LCS)
How to Arrive at This Pattern:
Ask yourself: “Do I have TWO sequences and need to find the longest common part (not necessarily contiguous)?”

Signal words: two strings/arrays, common, subsequence, longest, supersequence
Thinking:
  1. Two strings. Compare character by character.
  2. At each position pair (i, j): characters match or they don’t.
  3. Match: take it, move both pointers. No match: skip one from either string.
  4. Classic LCS structure.

State: dp[i][j] = LCS length of s1[0..i-1] and s2[0..j-1]

Transition:

  • If s1[i-1] == s2[j-1]: dp[i][j] = dp[i-1][j-1] + 1
  • Else: dp[i][j] = max(dp[i-1][j], dp[i][j-1])

LCS — Standard (LC 1143)

public int longestCommonSubsequence(String text1, String text2) {
    int m = text1.length(), n = text2.length();
    int[][] dp = new int[m + 1][n + 1];

    for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++) {
        for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++) {
            if (text1.charAt(i - 1) == text2.charAt(j - 1)) {
                dp[i][j] = dp[i - 1][j - 1] + 1;
            } else {
                dp[i][j] = Math.max(dp[i - 1][j], dp[i][j - 1]);
            }
        }
    }
    return dp[m][n];
}

LCS — Space Optimized O(n)

public int lcsOptimized(String s1, String s2) {
    int m = s1.length(), n = s2.length();
    int[] dp = new int[n + 1];

    for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++) {
        int prev = 0; // stores dp[i-1][j-1]
        for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++) {
            int temp = dp[j]; // save dp[i-1][j] before overwriting
            if (s1.charAt(i - 1) == s2.charAt(j - 1)) {
                dp[j] = prev + 1;
            } else {
                dp[j] = Math.max(dp[j], dp[j - 1]);
            }
            prev = temp;
        }
    }
    return dp[n];
}

Reconstruct the Actual LCS String

public String reconstructLCS(String s1, String s2) {
    int m = s1.length(), n = s2.length();
    int[][] dp = new int[m + 1][n + 1];

    for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++)
        for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++)
            if (s1.charAt(i - 1) == s2.charAt(j - 1))
                dp[i][j] = dp[i - 1][j - 1] + 1;
            else
                dp[i][j] = Math.max(dp[i - 1][j], dp[i][j - 1]);

    // Backtrack to build LCS
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    int i = m, j = n;
    while (i > 0 && j > 0) {
        if (s1.charAt(i - 1) == s2.charAt(j - 1)) {
            sb.append(s1.charAt(i - 1));
            i--; j--;
        } else if (dp[i - 1][j] > dp[i][j - 1]) {
            i--;
        } else {
            j--;
        }
    }
    return sb.reverse().toString();
}

Shortest Common Supersequence (LC 1092) — Uses LCS

public String shortestCommonSupersequence(String s1, String s2) {
    int m = s1.length(), n = s2.length();
    int[][] dp = new int[m + 1][n + 1];

    for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++)
        for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++)
            if (s1.charAt(i - 1) == s2.charAt(j - 1))
                dp[i][j] = dp[i - 1][j - 1] + 1;
            else
                dp[i][j] = Math.max(dp[i - 1][j], dp[i][j - 1]);

    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    int i = m, j = n;
    while (i > 0 && j > 0) {
        if (s1.charAt(i - 1) == s2.charAt(j - 1)) {
            sb.append(s1.charAt(i - 1));
            i--; j--;
        } else if (dp[i - 1][j] > dp[i][j - 1]) {
            sb.append(s1.charAt(i - 1));
            i--;
        } else {
            sb.append(s2.charAt(j - 1));
            j--;
        }
    }
    while (i > 0) sb.append(s1.charAt(--i));
    while (j > 0) sb.append(s2.charAt(--j));
    return sb.reverse().toString();
}
Longest Common Substring (contiguous) variation: on mismatch, set dp[i][j] = 0 instead of taking max. Track global max.

Complexity: Time O(m × n), Space O(m × n) or O(n) with optimization

Pattern 3: Edit Distance / String Transformation
How to Arrive at This Pattern:
Ask yourself: “Do I need to transform one string into another using insert, delete, replace?”

Signal words: edit distance, transform, convert, minimum operations, Levenshtein
Thinking:
  1. Convert string A to string B with minimum cost.
  2. At each character pair: match (free) or pay for insert/delete/replace.
  3. Three choices when characters differ: insert, delete, replace.
  4. That’s Edit Distance.

State: dp[i][j] = min operations to convert s1[0..i-1] to s2[0..j-1]

Transition:

  • If s1[i-1] == s2[j-1]: dp[i][j] = dp[i-1][j-1]
  • Else: dp[i][j] = 1 + min(dp[i-1][j-1], dp[i-1][j], dp[i][j-1]) (replace, delete, insert)

Base cases: dp[i][0] = i (delete all), dp[0][j] = j (insert all)

Edit Distance (LC 72)

public int minDistance(String word1, String word2) {
    int m = word1.length(), n = word2.length();
    int[][] dp = new int[m + 1][n + 1];

    for (int i = 0; i <= m; i++) dp[i][0] = i;
    for (int j = 0; j <= n; j++) dp[0][j] = j;

    for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++) {
        for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++) {
            if (word1.charAt(i - 1) == word2.charAt(j - 1)) {
                dp[i][j] = dp[i - 1][j - 1];
            } else {
                dp[i][j] = 1 + Math.min(dp[i - 1][j - 1],   // replace
                                Math.min(dp[i - 1][j],        // delete
                                         dp[i][j - 1]));      // insert
            }
        }
    }
    return dp[m][n];
}

Space-Optimized Edit Distance — O(n)

public int minDistanceOptimized(String word1, String word2) {
    int m = word1.length(), n = word2.length();
    int[] dp = new int[n + 1];

    for (int j = 0; j <= n; j++) dp[j] = j;

    for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++) {
        int prev = dp[0];
        dp[0] = i;
        for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++) {
            int temp = dp[j];
            if (word1.charAt(i - 1) == word2.charAt(j - 1)) {
                dp[j] = prev;
            } else {
                dp[j] = 1 + Math.min(prev, Math.min(dp[j], dp[j - 1]));
            }
            prev = temp;
        }
    }
    return dp[n];
}

Complexity: Time O(m × n), Space O(n) with optimization

Pattern 4: Longest Palindromic Subsequence / Substring
How to Arrive at This Pattern:
Ask yourself: “Does the problem involve palindromes on a string?”

Signal words: palindrome, longest, subsequence, substring, reverse
Thinking:
  1. Problem mentions palindromes.
  2. For subsequence: interval DP on the string — compare s[i] and s[j] for range [i..j].
  3. For substring: expand around center or DP.
  4. Key reduction: LPS(s) = LCS(s, reverse(s)).

Longest Palindromic Subsequence (LC 516) — Gap Method

State: dp[i][j] = LPS length of s[i..j]

Transition: If s[i] == s[j]: dp[i][j] = dp[i+1][j-1] + 2. Else: max(dp[i+1][j], dp[i][j-1])

public int longestPalinSubseq(String s) {
    int n = s.length();
    int[][] dp = new int[n][n];

    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) dp[i][i] = 1;

    for (int gap = 1; gap < n; gap++) {
        for (int i = 0; i + gap < n; i++) {
            int j = i + gap;
            if (s.charAt(i) == s.charAt(j)) {
                dp[i][j] = dp[i + 1][j - 1] + 2;
            } else {
                dp[i][j] = Math.max(dp[i + 1][j], dp[i][j - 1]);
            }
        }
    }
    return dp[0][n - 1];
}
Alternative: LPS via LCS reduction — LPS(s) = LCS(s, reverse(s)). Clean trick to mention in interviews.

Longest Palindromic Substring (LC 5) — Expand Around Center

public String longestPalindrome(String s) {
    int n = s.length();
    int start = 0, maxLen = 1;

    for (int center = 0; center < n; center++) {
        // Odd-length palindromes
        int lo = center, hi = center;
        while (lo >= 0 && hi < n && s.charAt(lo) == s.charAt(hi)) {
            if (hi - lo + 1 > maxLen) {
                start = lo;
                maxLen = hi - lo + 1;
            }
            lo--; hi++;
        }
        // Even-length palindromes
        lo = center; hi = center + 1;
        while (lo >= 0 && hi < n && s.charAt(lo) == s.charAt(hi)) {
            if (hi - lo + 1 > maxLen) {
                start = lo;
                maxLen = hi - lo + 1;
            }
            lo--; hi++;
        }
    }
    return s.substring(start, start + maxLen);
}

Complexity: Subsequence: Time O(n²), Space O(n²). Substring (expand): Time O(n²), Space O(1).

Pattern 5: Interval DP (Matrix Chain, Burst Balloons)
How to Arrive at This Pattern:
Ask yourself: “Am I choosing where to split/merge a range to minimize/maximize a cost?”

Signal words: split, merge, partition, range [i..j], balloons, matrix chain, cost of combining
Thinking:
  1. Linear sequence, find optimal way to break/combine it.
  2. For range [i..j], try every split point k.
  3. Answer for [i..j] depends on [i..k] and [k+1..j].
  4. Fill by increasing interval length (gap method).
  5. That’s Interval DP.

State: dp[i][j] = optimal answer for range [i..j]

Transition: dp[i][j] = optimal over all k of dp[i][k] + dp[k+1][j] + cost(i, k, j)

Fill order: Gap method — gap 0, gap 1, gap 2, ..., gap n-1

Matrix Chain Multiplication

public int matrixChainMultiplication(int[] dims) {
    int n = dims.length - 1;
    int[][] dp = new int[n][n];

    for (int gap = 1; gap < n; gap++) {
        for (int i = 0; i + gap < n; i++) {
            int j = i + gap;
            dp[i][j] = Integer.MAX_VALUE;
            for (int k = i; k < j; k++) {
                int cost = dp[i][k] + dp[k + 1][j]
                         + dims[i] * dims[k + 1] * dims[j + 1];
                dp[i][j] = Math.min(dp[i][j], cost);
            }
        }
    }
    return dp[0][n - 1];
}

Burst Balloons (LC 312)

Key insight: Think of the LAST balloon to burst in range [i..j], not the first. This makes subproblems independent. 
public int maxCoins(int[] nums) {
    int n = nums.length;
    int[] arr = new int[n + 2];
    arr[0] = arr[n + 1] = 1;
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) arr[i + 1] = nums[i];

    int N = n + 2;
    int[][] dp = new int[N][N];

    for (int gap = 2; gap < N; gap++) {
        for (int i = 0; i + gap < N; i++) {
            int j = i + gap;
            for (int k = i + 1; k < j; k++) {
                int coins = dp[i][k] + dp[k][j]
                          + arr[i] * arr[k] * arr[j];
                dp[i][j] = Math.max(dp[i][j], coins);
            }
        }
    }
    return dp[0][N - 1];
}

Minimum Cost Tree From Leaf Values (LC 1130)

public int mctFromLeafValues(int[] arr) {
    int n = arr.length;
    int[][] dp = new int[n][n];
    int[][] maxVal = new int[n][n];

    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        maxVal[i][i] = arr[i];
        for (int j = i + 1; j < n; j++)
            maxVal[i][j] = Math.max(maxVal[i][j - 1], arr[j]);
    }

    for (int gap = 1; gap < n; gap++) {
        for (int i = 0; i + gap < n; i++) {
            int j = i + gap;
            dp[i][j] = Integer.MAX_VALUE;
            for (int k = i; k < j; k++) {
                int cost = dp[i][k] + dp[k + 1][j]
                         + maxVal[i][k] * maxVal[k + 1][j];
                dp[i][j] = Math.min(dp[i][j], cost);
            }
        }
    }
    return dp[0][n - 1];
}

Complexity: Time O(n³), Space O(n²)

Pattern 6: DP on Strings (Distinct Subsequences, Interleaving, Regex)
How to Arrive at This Pattern:
Ask yourself: “Am I matching/counting strings with special constraints like subsequences, interleaving, or wildcard/regex rules?”

Signal words: subsequences count, interleave, regex, wildcard, pattern matching, *, ?, .
Thinking:
  1. One or two strings with a matching/counting constraint.
  2. Doesn’t fit simple LCS or Edit Distance.
  3. Involves counting subsequences, checking interleaving, or pattern matching with wildcards.
  4. Define dp[i][j] based on string indices, figure out transitions from constraint rules.

Distinct Subsequences (LC 115)

How many times does t appear as a subsequence in s?

public int numDistinct(String s, String t) {
    int m = s.length(), n = t.length();
    long[][] dp = new long[m + 1][n + 1]; // use long to avoid overflow

    for (int i = 0; i <= m; i++) dp[i][0] = 1;

    for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++) {
        for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++) {
            dp[i][j] = dp[i - 1][j]; // skip s[i-1]
            if (s.charAt(i - 1) == t.charAt(j - 1)) {
                dp[i][j] += dp[i - 1][j - 1]; // use s[i-1] to match t[j-1]
            }
        }
    }
    return (int) dp[m][n];
}

Interleaving String (LC 97)

Can s3 be formed by interleaving s1 and s2?

public boolean isInterleave(String s1, String s2, String s3) {
    int m = s1.length(), n = s2.length();
    if (m + n != s3.length()) return false;

    boolean[][] dp = new boolean[m + 1][n + 1];
    dp[0][0] = true;

    for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++)
        dp[i][0] = dp[i - 1][0] && s1.charAt(i - 1) == s3.charAt(i - 1);
    for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++)
        dp[0][j] = dp[0][j - 1] && s2.charAt(j - 1) == s3.charAt(j - 1);

    for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++) {
        for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++) {
            dp[i][j] = (dp[i - 1][j] && s1.charAt(i - 1) == s3.charAt(i + j - 1))
                     || (dp[i][j - 1] && s2.charAt(j - 1) == s3.charAt(i + j - 1));
        }
    }
    return dp[m][n];
}

Wildcard Matching (LC 44)

? matches any single char, * matches any sequence (including empty).

public boolean isMatch(String s, String p) {
    int m = s.length(), n = p.length();
    boolean[][] dp = new boolean[m + 1][n + 1];
    dp[0][0] = true;

    for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++) {
        if (p.charAt(j - 1) == '*') dp[0][j] = dp[0][j - 1];
    }

    for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++) {
        for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++) {
            char sc = s.charAt(i - 1), pc = p.charAt(j - 1);
            if (pc == '?' || pc == sc) {
                dp[i][j] = dp[i - 1][j - 1];
            } else if (pc == '*') {
                dp[i][j] = dp[i][j - 1]     // * matches empty
                         || dp[i - 1][j];    // * matches one more char
            }
        }
    }
    return dp[m][n];
}

Regular Expression Matching (LC 10)

. matches any single char, * means zero or more of the preceding element.

public boolean isMatchRegex(String s, String p) {
    int m = s.length(), n = p.length();
    boolean[][] dp = new boolean[m + 1][n + 1];
    dp[0][0] = true;

    for (int j = 2; j <= n; j++) {
        if (p.charAt(j - 1) == '*') dp[0][j] = dp[0][j - 2];
    }

    for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++) {
        for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++) {
            char sc = s.charAt(i - 1), pc = p.charAt(j - 1);
            if (pc == '.' || pc == sc) {
                dp[i][j] = dp[i - 1][j - 1];
            } else if (pc == '*') {
                char prevP = p.charAt(j - 2);
                dp[i][j] = dp[i][j - 2]; // zero occurrences
                if (prevP == '.' || prevP == sc) {
                    dp[i][j] = dp[i][j] || dp[i - 1][j]; // one or more
                }
            }
        }
    }
    return dp[m][n];
}
Wildcard vs Regex *:
  • Wildcard *: matches any sequence of characters (including empty)
  • Regex *: means zero or more of the preceding element
These are fundamentally different! Don’t mix them up.

Complexity: All O(m × n) time and space. Space-optimizable to O(n) for most.

Section 4: Code Templates

Template 1: Grid DP (Space-Optimized)

int gridDP(int[][] grid) {
    int m = grid.length, n = grid[0].length;
    int[] dp = new int[n];
    dp[0] = grid[0][0];
    for (int j = 1; j < n; j++) dp[j] = dp[j-1] + grid[0][j];

    for (int i = 1; i < m; i++) {
        dp[0] += grid[i][0];
        for (int j = 1; j < n; j++) {
            dp[j] = grid[i][j] + Math.min(dp[j], dp[j-1]);
        }
    }
    return dp[n-1];
}

Template 2: LCS (Space-Optimized)

int lcs(String a, String b) {
    int m = a.length(), n = b.length();
    int[] dp = new int[n+1];
    for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++) {
        int prev = 0;
        for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++) {
            int temp = dp[j];
            dp[j] = (a.charAt(i-1) == b.charAt(j-1))
                   ? prev + 1 : Math.max(dp[j], dp[j-1]);
            prev = temp;
        }
    }
    return dp[n];
}

Template 3: Edit Distance

int editDist(String a, String b) {
    int m = a.length(), n = b.length();
    int[] dp = new int[n+1];
    for (int j = 0; j <= n; j++) dp[j] = j;
    for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++) {
        int prev = dp[0]; dp[0] = i;
        for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++) {
            int temp = dp[j];
            dp[j] = (a.charAt(i-1) == b.charAt(j-1)) ? prev
                   : 1 + Math.min(prev, Math.min(dp[j], dp[j-1]));
            prev = temp;
        }
    }
    return dp[n];
}

Template 4: Longest Palindromic Subsequence

int lps(String s) {
    int n = s.length();
    int[][] dp = new int[n][n];
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) dp[i][i] = 1;
    for (int gap = 1; gap < n; gap++)
        for (int i = 0; i + gap < n; i++) {
            int j = i + gap;
            dp[i][j] = (s.charAt(i) == s.charAt(j)) ? dp[i+1][j-1] + 2
                       : Math.max(dp[i+1][j], dp[i][j-1]);
        }
    return dp[0][n-1];
}

Template 5: Interval DP (Gap Method)

int intervalDP(int[] arr) {
    int n = arr.length;
    int[][] dp = new int[n][n];

    for (int gap = 1; gap < n; gap++) {
        for (int i = 0; i + gap < n; i++) {
            int j = i + gap;
            dp[i][j] = Integer.MAX_VALUE;
            for (int k = i; k < j; k++) {
                dp[i][j] = Math.min(dp[i][j],
                    dp[i][k] + dp[k+1][j] + cost(arr, i, k, j));
            }
        }
    }
    return dp[0][n-1];
}

Template 6: String Matching DP (Wildcard)

boolean matchDP(String s, String p) {
    int m = s.length(), n = p.length();
    boolean[][] dp = new boolean[m+1][n+1];
    dp[0][0] = true;
    for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++)
        if (p.charAt(j-1) == '*') dp[0][j] = dp[0][j-1];
    for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++)
        for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++) {
            char pc = p.charAt(j-1);
            if (pc == '?' || pc == s.charAt(i-1)) dp[i][j] = dp[i-1][j-1];
            else if (pc == '*') dp[i][j] = dp[i][j-1] || dp[i-1][j];
        }
    return dp[m][n];
}

Section 5: Complexity Analysis

PatternTimeSpace (Full)Space (Optimized)
Grid DPO(m × n)O(m × n)O(n)
LCSO(m × n)O(m × n)O(n) *
Edit DistanceO(m × n)O(m × n)O(n)
Longest Palindromic SubseqO(n²)O(n²)O(n)
Longest Palindromic SubstrO(n²)O(1)O(1)
Interval DPO(n³)O(n²)O(n²) †
Distinct SubsequencesO(m × n)O(m × n)O(n)
Interleaving StringO(m × n)O(m × n)O(n)
Wildcard/Regex MatchingO(m × n)O(m × n)O(n)

* Reconstruction needs full table. † Interval DP cannot be reduced — needs all subintervals.

Section 6: Edge Cases & Gotchas

⚠ Empty Strings: Always handle empty strings. DP table should have dimensions (m+1) × (n+1). Check dp[0][j] and dp[i][0] base cases carefully.
⚠ Single Character Strings: Single char is its own palindrome (length 1). LCS of single char with itself is 1. Edit distance from single char to empty is 1.
⚠ All Same Characters: “aaaa” — LPS is the entire string. LCS of “aaa” and “aaaa” is 3. Tests base cases.
⚠ Grid with Single Row or Column: Only one path exists. Make sure first-row and first-column initialization handles this.
⚠ Grid with All Obstacles: If grid[0][0] is obstacle, return 0 immediately.
Off-by-One in 2D DP (1-indexed vs 0-indexed): The most common bug. When using 1-indexed DP: dp[i][j] corresponds to s.charAt(i-1). Double-check every charAt call.
Forgetting the Diagonal in Space-Optimized LCS/Edit Distance: The match case uses dp[i-1][j-1] (diagonal). You MUST save this in a prev variable before it gets overwritten.
Interval DP Base Cases: Gap 0 (single element): usually dp[i][i] = 0 or arr[i]. Gap 1 (two elements): sometimes needs explicit handling. Getting these wrong breaks everything.
⚠ Integer Overflow in Counting Paths: Unique Paths for large grids can overflow int. Use long or check constraints.

Section 7: Interview Tips

Tip: Draw the 2D Table
For ANY string DP problem, draw the table for a small example (3-4 chars each). Fill manually. This catches bugs and impresses interviewers.
Tip: Clarify Subsequence vs Substring Immediately
These are DIFFERENT problems with different complexities. Ask the interviewer explicitly which one they mean.
Tip: Edit Distance Variations
Some problems have different costs for insert/delete/replace. Template stays the same — just change +1 to the respective cost.
Tip: LCS to LPS Reduction
Longest Palindromic Subsequence of s = LCS(s, reverse(s)). Clean trick to mention.
Tip: Gap Method vs Row-by-Row
  • String comparisons (LCS, Edit Distance): row-by-row
  • Range problems (Burst Balloons, Matrix Chain): gap method
Know BOTH filling strategies.
Tip: Space Optimization for 2D DP
Always mention as follow-up even if you code the full 2D version first. Shows maturity.
Tip: “Print the Actual Solution” Follow-Up
Interviewers love asking “now print the actual LCS / edit sequence / path”. You need the full 2D table for backtracking — space optimization sacrifices this ability. Mention this tradeoff.
Tip: Top-Down vs Bottom-Up
If complex boundary conditions, start with memoized recursion (top-down). Convert to bottom-up only if needed for space optimization or to avoid stack overflow.

Section 10: Quick Revision Card

2D DP / String DP / Interval DP — Quick Revision Card

Pattern Decision Flowchart

Is the input a 2D GRID?
  YES → Pattern 1: GRID DP — dp[i][j] = f(dp[i-1][j], dp[i][j-1])

Are there TWO STRINGS to compare?
  YES → Need longest COMMON part?
    YES → Pattern 2: LCS — match: diag+1, else: max(skip either)
  YES → Need to TRANSFORM one to another?
    YES → Pattern 3: EDIT DISTANCE — 1 + min(replace, delete, insert)
  YES → COUNT subsequences or MATCH/INTERLEAVE?
    YES → Pattern 6: STRING DP (Distinct Subseq, Interleaving, Regex)

Is there ONE STRING with palindrome requirement?
  YES → Pattern 4: PALINDROME DP — Subseq: gap method or LCS(s, rev(s))

Need to SPLIT/MERGE a RANGE optimally?
  YES → Pattern 5: INTERVAL DP — try all split points k, fill by gap

LCS vs Edit Distance vs LPS Comparison

LCSEdit DistanceLPS
Input2 strings2 strings1 string
GoalLongest common subseqMin ops to transformLongest palindromic subseq
Match casediag + 1diag (free)inner + 2
Mismatchmax(up, left)1 + min(3 neighbors)max(shrink L/R)
Base dp[0][j]0jN/A (gap method)
Base dp[i][0]0idp[i][i] = 1

Common 2D DP Mistakes

  1. Off-by-one: dp[i][j] uses s.charAt(i-1) not s.charAt(i)
  2. Forgetting to save diagonal (prev) in space-optimized version
  3. Wrong fill order: interval DP needs gap method, not row-by-row
  4. Burst Balloons: think LAST to burst, not first
  5. Regex *: zero or more of PRECEDING char (not any char)
  6. Wildcard *: matches any SEQUENCE (different from regex *)
  7. Integer overflow when counting paths — use long
  8. Reconstruction needs full 2D table — can’t space-optimize

“Which Pattern?” Quick Guide

Problem CluePattern
“Grid / matrix / paths”Grid DP
“Two strings, longest common”LCS
“Transform / edit / convert”Edit Distance
“Palindrome in a string”Palindrome DP
“Split / merge / partition range”Interval DP
“Count subsequences”Distinct Subsequences
“Interleave two strings”Interleaving String DP
“Match with * or ? or .”Regex / Wildcard DP
“Largest square/rectangle of 1s”Grid DP (min of 3 neighbors + 1)
“Shortest common supersequence”LCS then reconstruct

End of Topic 13: DP Part 2. Master these 6 patterns and 15 problems, and you’ll handle any 2D DP / String DP / Interval DP question in interviews.

14 / 18

Greedy Algorithms — SDE 2 Interview Notes

Comprehensive DSA Notes for SDE 2 Interview Prep

AspectDetail
Frequency★★★★☆ — 15-20% of interviews, often combined with sorting/heaps
CompaniesAmazon, Google, Microsoft, Flipkart, Uber, Goldman Sachs, Walmart
PrerequisitesSorting, Heaps (Topic 9), Two Pointers (Topic 2)
Often combined withSorting, Heaps, Stacks, Intervals
The key question: “Does the locally optimal choice at every step lead to the globally optimal solution?” If yes → greedy. If not → DP.

Section 1: Topic Overview

Two properties must hold for greedy to work:

  1. Greedy Choice Property — A globally optimal solution can be arrived at by making locally optimal choices
  2. Optimal Substructure — An optimal solution contains optimal solutions to sub-problems

Greedy vs DP — When to Use Which

CriteriaGreedyDynamic Programming
Choice dependencyEach choice is independentChoices depend on sub-problem results
Revisiting decisionsNever revisitsConsiders all sub-problems
Proof requiredExchange argumentOptimal substructure + overlapping
ComplexityUsually O(n log n) or O(n)Usually O(n²) or O(n×W)
ExampleFractional Knapsack0/1 Knapsack
ExampleActivity SelectionLongest Increasing Subsequence

Section 2: Core Concepts & Data Structures

Common Data Structures Paired with Greedy

  • Sorting — Most common preprocessing step (80%+ of greedy problems)
  • Heaps / Priority Queues — Repeatedly pick min/max element
  • Stacks — Build sequences greedily (remove K digits, monotonic stack)
  • Two Pointers — Match/assign from sorted arrays

When Greedy Fails (Need DP Instead)

ProblemWhy Greedy FailsCorrect Approach
0/1 KnapsackHighest value/weight ratio may skip better combosDP on weight capacity
Longest Increasing SubsequenceGreedily extending doesn’t guarantee longestDP or patience sorting
Coin Change (arbitrary denoms)Largest coin first may not reach exact targetDP on amount

Proof Techniques

  • Exchange Argument: Assume optimal differs from greedy. Show you can swap elements to match greedy without worsening the result.
  • Greedy Stays Ahead: Show by induction that after each step, greedy is at least as good as any other algorithm.
Sorting as preprocessing: In 80%+ of greedy problems, the first step is sorting. The key insight is figuring out what to sort by — start time, end time, size, ratio, etc.

Section 3: Pattern Recognition Framework

Pattern 1: Activity Selection / Interval Scheduling
How to Arrive at This Pattern:
  • Problem asks to select maximum non-overlapping intervals
  • Or minimize intervals to remove so the rest don’t overlap
  • Or find minimum resources (rooms, platforms) for overlapping intervals
Signal words: intervals, non-overlapping, meeting rooms, minimum platforms, arrows
Strategy: Sort by end time. Greedily pick the interval that finishes earliest.

Why it works: Choosing the earliest-finishing activity leaves maximum room for remaining activities.

// Activity Selection: Maximum non-overlapping intervals
public int maxNonOverlapping(int[][] intervals) {
    Arrays.sort(intervals, (a, b) -> a[1] - b[1]); // sort by end time
    int count = 1, lastEnd = intervals[0][1];
    for (int i = 1; i < intervals.length; i++) {
        if (intervals[i][0] >= lastEnd) {
            count++;
            lastEnd = intervals[i][1];
        }
    }
    return count;
}
Pattern 2: Jump Game / Reach Problems
How to Arrive at This Pattern:
  • Problem asks “Can you reach the end?” or “Minimum jumps?”
  • Array where each element = max forward distance
  • Or circular arrangement to find valid starting point (Gas Station)
Strategy: Track farthest reachable position. If current index exceeds farthest, you’re stuck.
How Jump Game works — what is farthest?

farthest = the maximum index you can reach from any position visited so far.

i + nums[i] = "I'm at index i, can jump nums[i] steps, so I can land on index i + nums[i]."

You don't add jumps together — each index gives a fresh jump range from that position. farthest tracks the best reach across all visited indices.

nums = [2, 3, 1, 1, 4]    ← reachable

i=0: farthest = max(0, 0+2) = 2  → from 0, can reach index 2
i=1: farthest = max(2, 1+3) = 4  → from 1, can reach index 4 (the end!)
i=2: farthest = max(4, 2+1) = 4
i=3: farthest = max(4, 3+1) = 4
i=4: reached the end → return true ✅
nums = [3, 2, 1, 0, 4]    ← unreachable

i=0: farthest = max(0, 0+3) = 3
i=1: farthest = max(3, 1+2) = 3
i=2: farthest = max(3, 2+1) = 3
i=3: farthest = max(3, 3+0) = 3  → nums[3]=0, stuck!
i=4: i(4) > farthest(3) → return false ❌
     "No visited index could ever reach here"

The guarantee: By walking i=0,1,2,3... and tracking max reach, you check: "across ALL positions I can visit, can ANY of them reach the end?" If farthest never reaches the last index, no chain of jumps can get there.

// Jump Game: Can you reach the end?
public boolean canJump(int[] nums) {
    int farthest = 0;
    for (int i = 0; i < nums.length; i++) {
        if (i > farthest) return false;
        farthest = Math.max(farthest, i + nums[i]);
    }
    return true;
}

// Jump Game II: Minimum jumps to reach end
// Hack: track maxReach, not actual jumps. If i > maxReach, unreachable.
public int jump(int[] nums) {
    int jumps = 0, curEnd = 0, farthest = 0;
    for (int i = 0; i < nums.length - 1; i++) {
        farthest = Math.max(farthest, i + nums[i]);
        if (i == curEnd) {
            jumps++;
            curEnd = farthest;
        }
    }
    return jumps;
}
Hack: Track maxReach, not actual jumps.
Don't simulate jumping step by step. Just maintain the farthest reachable index. Sweep left to right: if i > maxReach, you can't get here, return false. Otherwise update maxReach = max(maxReach, i + nums[i]). One pass, O(n), zero confusion.
Pattern 3: Assign / Match Greedily (Two Sorted Arrays)
How to Arrive at This Pattern:
  • Two sets: resources and requirements (cookies & children, boats & people)
  • Problem asks to maximize matches or minimize resources used
Strategy: Sort both arrays. Use two pointers to match smallest resource to smallest satisfiable demand. 
// Assign Cookies: Maximize content children
public int findContentChildren(int[] children, int[] cookies) {
    Arrays.sort(children);
    Arrays.sort(cookies);
    int child = 0, cookie = 0;
    while (child < children.length && cookie < cookies.length) {
        if (cookies[cookie] >= children[child]) child++;
        cookie++;
    }
    return child;
}
Boats to Save People — Why pair lightest + heaviest, not two lightest?

Pairing two light people wastes boat capacity. The heavy person ends up riding alone anyway.

people = [1, 2, 3, 8], limit = 9

Two lightest first:              Lightest + heaviest:
  Boat 1: 1+2 = 3 (wasted 6!)     Boat 1: 1+8 = 9 (perfect!)
  Boat 2: 3 alone                  Boat 2: 2+3 = 5
  Boat 3: 8 alone                  Total: 2 boats ✅
  Total: 3 boats ❌

The insight: The heaviest person either shares a boat with someone, or rides alone. Pair them with the lightest — best chance to fit. The lightest person is the most flexible (can pair with anyone), so use that flexibility on the hardest case first.

// Boats to Save People: Min boats (max 2 people, weight limit)
public int numRescueBoats(int[] people, int limit) {
    Arrays.sort(people);
    int lo = 0, hi = people.length - 1, boats = 0;
    while (lo <= hi) {
        if (people[lo] + people[hi] <= limit) lo++;
        hi--;
        boats++;
    }
    return boats;
}
Pattern 4: Huffman / Priority Queue Greedy
How to Arrive at This Pattern:
  • Problem requires repeatedly picking smallest (or largest) and combining/processing
  • Or rearrange elements so no two same elements are adjacent
Signal words: “minimum cost to combine”, “reorganize to avoid adjacency”, “schedule with cooldown”
Strategy: Use min-heap or max-heap. Always process the best available option first.
Minimum Cost to Connect Sticks — Why always pick the two smallest?

The cost of connecting two sticks = sum of their lengths. The combined stick gets used in future connections, so its cost compounds. Bigger sticks created early get added to the total more times.

sticks = [1, 3, 5]

Greedy (smallest first):         Non-greedy (largest first):
  Connect 1+3 = 4, cost = 4       Connect 3+5 = 8, cost = 8
  Connect 4+5 = 9, cost = 9       Connect 1+8 = 9, cost = 9
  Total: 13 ✅                     Total: 17 ❌

Why min-heap? Need to repeatedly pick the two smallest. Min-heap gives smallest in O(log n). After combining, push result back — it might not be the smallest anymore.

sticks = [2, 4, 3] → Heap: [2, 3, 4]

Step 1: Poll 2 and 3 → cost = 5, total = 5
        Push 5 → Heap: [4, 5]

Step 2: Poll 4 and 5 → cost = 9, total = 5+9 = 14
        Push 9 → Heap: [9]

Size = 1 → done. Answer: 14
// Minimum Cost to Connect Sticks (Huffman-style)
public int connectSticks(int[] sticks) {
    PriorityQueue<Integer> pq = new PriorityQueue<>();
    for (int s : sticks) pq.offer(s);
    int totalCost = 0;
    while (pq.size() > 1) {
        int cost = pq.poll() + pq.poll();
        totalCost += cost;
        pq.offer(cost);
    }
    return totalCost;
}

// Reorganize String: No two adjacent chars same
Reorganize String — Why max-heap?

Always place the most frequent character next. If high-frequency chars aren't used early, they pile up and become adjacent.

Algorithm:

  1. Count frequency of each character
  2. Max-heap of {char, freq} sorted by frequency
  3. At each step:
    • Most frequent != last placed → place it
    • Most frequent == last placed → place second most frequent instead
    • No second option → impossible, return ""
Trace: "aab" → freq: a=2, b=1 → Heap: [{a,2}, {b,1}]

Step 1: Poll {a,2}, last=none → place 'a' → "a"
Step 2: Poll {a,1}, last='a' → SAME! Poll {b,1} → place 'b' → "ab"
Step 3: Poll {a,1}, last='b' → OK → place 'a' → "aba" ✅

When is it impossible? Any char's frequency > (length+1)/2. For "aaab", a=3 but max allowed = (4+1)/2 = 2 → can't avoid adjacency.

public String reorganizeString(String s) {
    int[] freq = new int[26];
    for (char c : s.toCharArray()) freq[c - 'a']++;
    PriorityQueue<int[]> pq = new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> b[1] - a[1]);
    for (int i = 0; i < 26; i++)
        if (freq[i] > 0) pq.offer(new int[]{i, freq[i]});

    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    while (!pq.isEmpty()) {
        int[] first = pq.poll();
        if (sb.length() == 0 || sb.charAt(sb.length()-1) != (char)(first[0]+'a')) {
            sb.append((char)(first[0] + 'a'));
            if (--first[1] > 0) pq.offer(first);
        } else {
            if (pq.isEmpty()) return "";
            int[] second = pq.poll();
            sb.append((char)(second[0] + 'a'));
            if (--second[1] > 0) pq.offer(second);
            pq.offer(first);
        }
    }
    return sb.toString();
}
Pattern 5: Greedy on Strings/Arrays (Stack-Based)
How to Arrive at This Pattern:
  • Problem asks to build lexicographically smallest/largest result
  • Or remove K elements to optimize some property
Signal words: “remove K digits”, “smallest subsequence”, “remove duplicate letters”
Strategy: Use a stack. For each new element, pop while stack top is worse and removals remain.
Remove K Digits — How the stack greedy works

Idea: A number is smaller when larger digits appear later. So whenever a new digit is smaller than the stack top, pop the larger digit (use one removal). This greedily pushes large digits out early.

num = "1432219", k = 3

Stack: []
'1' → push → [1]
'4' → push → [1,4]
'3' → 3 < 4 → pop 4 (k=2) → [1] → push 3 → [1,3]
'2' → 2 < 3 → pop 3 (k=1) → [1] → push 2 → [1,2]
'2' → push → [1,2,2]
'1' → 1 < 2 → pop 2 (k=0) → [1,2] → push 1 → [1,2,1]
'9' → push → [1,2,1,9]

k=0, done. Result: "1219" ✅

Edge cases: If k remains after scanning (e.g., "1234", k=2), pop from the end. Strip leading zeros. Return "0" if empty.

// Remove K Digits: Smallest number after removing k digits
public String removeKdigits(String num, int k) {
    Deque<Character> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
    for (char c : num.toCharArray()) {
        while (!stack.isEmpty() && k > 0 && stack.peek() > c) {
            stack.pop();
            k--;
        }
        stack.push(c);
    }
    while (k-- > 0) stack.pop();

    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    while (!stack.isEmpty()) sb.append(stack.pollLast());
    while (sb.length() > 0 && sb.charAt(0) == '0') sb.deleteCharAt(0);
    return sb.length() == 0 ? "0" : sb.toString();
}
Remove Duplicate Letters — Stack + last index + inStack

Goal: Smallest subsequence containing every unique character exactly once.

Three tools:

  • lastIndex[c] — last occurrence of char c. Tells us: "can I safely remove this char now and find it later?"
  • inStack[c] — is char c already in the result? Skip if yes.
  • Stack — build result. Pop top if it's larger than current AND appears later in the string.
s = "bcabc"
lastIndex: a=2, b=3, c=4

'b' → stack: [b]
'c' → c > b → push → [b,c]
'a' → a < c, c appears later (4>2) → pop c → [b]
       a < b, b appears later (3>2) → pop b → []
       push a → [a]
'b' → push → [a,b]
'c' → push → [a,b,c]

Result: "abc" ✅
// Remove Duplicate Letters: Smallest subsequence with all unique chars
public String removeDuplicateLetters(String s) {
    int[] lastIndex = new int[26];
    for (int i = 0; i < s.length(); i++) lastIndex[s.charAt(i) - 'a'] = i;

    boolean[] inStack = new boolean[26];
    Deque<Character> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();

    for (int i = 0; i < s.length(); i++) {
        char c = s.charAt(i);
        if (inStack[c - 'a']) continue;
        while (!stack.isEmpty() && stack.peek() > c
               && lastIndex[stack.peek() - 'a'] > i) {
            inStack[stack.pop() - 'a'] = false;
        }
        stack.push(c);
        inStack[c - 'a'] = true;
    }
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    while (!stack.isEmpty()) sb.append(stack.pollLast());
    return sb.toString();
}
Pattern 6: Greedy + Sorting (Intervals, Ranges, Partitions)
How to Arrive at This Pattern:
  • Problem gives intervals/ranges: merge, insert, split, or partition
  • Or find minimum groups/arrows/points covering all intervals
Signal words: intervals, merge, partition, ranges, last occurrence
Strategy: Sort by start (or end), then scan linearly and merge/extend/split.
Partition Labels — How last occurrence defines partitions

Idea: For each character, find its last occurrence. A partition can end at index i only if ALL characters seen so far have their last occurrence ≤ i. Track the farthest last occurrence as you scan.

s = "ababcbacadefegde"

Scan:
i=0 'a': lastIndex[a]=8 → end=8
i=1 'b': lastIndex[b]=5 → end=8
...
i=8 'a': i == end(8) → partition! length = 8-0+1 = 9

i=9  'd': lastIndex[d]=14 → end=14
i=10 'e': lastIndex[e]=15 → end=15
...
i=15 'e': i == end(15) → partition! length = 15-9+1 = 7

Result: [9, 7, 8]

i == end means: every character in this partition has been fully contained. Safe to cut here.

// Partition Labels: Max partitions where each letter in at most one
public List<Integer> partitionLabels(String s) {
    int[] lastIndex = new int[26];
    for (int i = 0; i < s.length(); i++)
        lastIndex[s.charAt(i) - 'a'] = i;

    List<Integer> result = new ArrayList<>();
    int start = 0, end = 0;
    for (int i = 0; i < s.length(); i++) {
        end = Math.max(end, lastIndex[s.charAt(i) - 'a']);
        if (i == end) {
            result.add(end - start + 1);
            start = i + 1;
        }
    }
    return result;
}
Minimum Arrows to Burst Balloons — Sort by end, count non-overlapping groups

Idea: Sort balloons by end position. Shoot an arrow at the first balloon's end. Any balloon that starts after this arrow needs a new arrow.

points = [[10,16],[2,8],[1,6],[7,12]]
Sorted by end: [[1,6],[2,8],[7,12],[10,16]]

Arrow 1 at 6: bursts [1,6] and [2,8] (2 ≤ 6)
              [7,12] starts at 7 > 6 → new arrow needed
Arrow 2 at 12: bursts [7,12] and [10,16] (10 ≤ 12)

Answer: 2 arrows

Why sort by end? Shooting at the earliest end point maximizes overlap with subsequent balloons. Greedy: use each arrow as efficiently as possible.

// Minimum Arrows to Burst Balloons
public int findMinArrowShots(int[][] points) {
    Arrays.sort(points, (a, b) -> Integer.compare(a[1], b[1]));
    int arrows = 1, arrowPos = points[0][1];
    for (int i = 1; i < points.length; i++) {
        if (points[i][0] > arrowPos) {
            arrows++;
            arrowPos = points[i][1];
        }
    }
    return arrows;
}

Section 4: Code Templates

Template 1: Activity Selection (Sort by End Time)

public int activitySelection(int[][] intervals) {
    Arrays.sort(intervals, (a, b) -> Integer.compare(a[1], b[1]));
    int count = 1, lastEnd = intervals[0][1];
    for (int i = 1; i < intervals.length; i++) {
        if (intervals[i][0] >= lastEnd) {
            count++;
            lastEnd = intervals[i][1];
        }
    }
    return count;
}

Template 2: Farthest Reach (Jump Game)

public boolean canReach(int[] nums) {
    int farthest = 0;
    for (int i = 0; i < nums.length; i++) {
        if (i > farthest) return false;
        farthest = Math.max(farthest, i + nums[i]);
        if (farthest >= nums.length - 1) return true;
    }
    return true;
}

Template 3: Two-Pointer Greedy Assignment

public int greedyAssign(int[] resources, int[] demands) {
    Arrays.sort(resources);
    Arrays.sort(demands);
    int i = 0, j = 0, matched = 0;
    while (i < demands.length && j < resources.length) {
        if (resources[j] >= demands[i]) { matched++; i++; }
        j++;
    }
    return matched;
}

Template 4: Heap-Based Greedy

public int heapGreedy(int[] values) {
    PriorityQueue<Integer> pq = new PriorityQueue<>();
    for (int v : values) pq.offer(v);
    int totalCost = 0;
    while (pq.size() > 1) {
        int combined = pq.poll() + pq.poll();
        totalCost += combined;
        pq.offer(combined);
    }
    return totalCost;
}

Template 5: Stack-Based Greedy

public String stackGreedy(String s, int k) {
    Deque<Character> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
    for (char c : s.toCharArray()) {
        while (!stack.isEmpty() && k > 0 && stack.peek() > c) {
            stack.pop(); k--;
        }
        stack.push(c);
    }
    while (k-- > 0) stack.pop();
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    while (!stack.isEmpty()) sb.append(stack.pollLast());
    while (sb.length() > 1 && sb.charAt(0) == '0') sb.deleteCharAt(0);
    return sb.length() == 0 ? "0" : sb.toString();
}

Template 6: Interval Merge

public int[][] mergeIntervals(int[][] intervals) {
    Arrays.sort(intervals, (a, b) -> Integer.compare(a[0], b[0]));
    List<int[]> merged = new ArrayList<>();
    merged.add(intervals[0]);
    for (int i = 1; i < intervals.length; i++) {
        int[] last = merged.get(merged.size() - 1);
        if (intervals[i][0] <= last[1]) {
            last[1] = Math.max(last[1], intervals[i][1]);
        } else {
            merged.add(intervals[i]);
        }
    }
    return merged.toArray(new int[0][]);
}
Hack: <= not < for overlapping endpoints.
[1,3] and [3,5] overlap — they share endpoint 3 and should merge to [1,5]. The condition must be intervals[i][0] <= last[1] (not <). This off-by-one fails silently because most test cases have clear overlaps. Edge cases with touching endpoints catch it.

Section 6: Edge Cases & Gotchas

⚠ Empty / Single Element: Empty intervals → return 0. Single element in Jump Game → already at end.
⚠ All Intervals Overlap: Meeting Rooms II: answer = number of intervals. Non-overlapping: must remove all but one.
⚠ Cannot Reach End: Jump Game returns false when i > farthest.
Integer overflow in comparators: Use Integer.compare(a, b) instead of a - b to avoid overflow.

Gas Station (LC 134)

public int canCompleteCircuit(int[] gas, int[] cost) {
    int totalSurplus = 0, currentSurplus = 0, start = 0;
    for (int i = 0; i < gas.length; i++) {
        totalSurplus += gas[i] - cost[i];
        currentSurplus += gas[i] - cost[i];
        if (currentSurplus < 0) {
            start = i + 1;
            currentSurplus = 0;
        }
    }
    return totalSurplus >= 0 ? start : -1;
}
When Greedy Seems Right But Isn’t:
ProblemGreedy TemptationWhy It Fails
0/1 KnapsackPick highest value/weight ratioCan’t take fractions; may skip better combo
Coin Change (general)Largest denomination firstcoins [1,3,4], amount=6: greedy=3 coins, optimal=2
LISGreedily extend currentSmaller element now may allow longer sequence

Section 7: Interview Tips

Tip: Always Explain WHY Greedy Works
Interviewers will ask: “How do you know greedy is optimal?” Prepare a 2-3 sentence exchange argument: “Assume an optimal solution differs from greedy. At the first difference, I can swap to match greedy without worsening the result.”
Tip: “Sort First” Is the Most Common Strategy
80%+ of greedy problems start with sorting. Ask: “What should I sort by?” — start time, end time, size, ratio, deadline.
Tip: Greedy vs DP Decision Process
  1. Can I make a local choice always part of some optimal solution? → Greedy
  2. Does the local choice reduce to a single smaller sub-problem? → Greedy
  3. Do I need to consider multiple sub-problems and pick best? → DP
Tip: Try to Construct a Counterexample
Before committing to greedy, spend 1-2 minutes trying to find a case where it fails. If you can’t, proceed with greedy.
Tip: Time Management
Greedy solutions are usually short (15-25 lines) but need justification. Spend 5 min on approach + proof, 10 min coding, 5 min testing.

Section 8: Related Topics

TopicConnection
DP (Topics 12, 13)When greedy fails, DP is the fallback. Many greedy problems have DP alternatives.
SortingMost greedy algorithms require sorting as preprocessing.
Heaps / PQ (Topic 9)Heap-based greedy: always process min/max first.
Intervals (Topic 15)Interval scheduling is classic greedy domain.
Two Pointers (Topic 2)Assign Cookies, Boats use two-pointer after sorting.
StacksStack-based greedy for optimal sequences.

Key Solution Sketches

Task Scheduler (LC 621)

public int leastInterval(char[] tasks, int n) {
    int[] freq = new int[26];
    for (char t : tasks) freq[t - 'A']++;
    int maxFreq = 0, maxCount = 0;
    for (int f : freq) {
        if (f > maxFreq) { maxFreq = f; maxCount = 1; }
        else if (f == maxFreq) maxCount++;
    }
    int result = (maxFreq - 1) * (n + 1) + maxCount;
    return Math.max(result, tasks.length);
}

Largest Number (LC 179)

public String largestNumber(int[] nums) {
    String[] strs = new String[nums.length];
    for (int i = 0; i < nums.length; i++) strs[i] = String.valueOf(nums[i]);
    Arrays.sort(strs, (a, b) -> (b + a).compareTo(a + b));
    if (strs[0].equals("0")) return "0";
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    for (String s : strs) sb.append(s);
    return sb.toString();
}

Candy (LC 135)

public int candy(int[] ratings) {
    int n = ratings.length;
    int[] candies = new int[n];
    Arrays.fill(candies, 1);
    for (int i = 1; i < n; i++)
        if (ratings[i] > ratings[i - 1]) candies[i] = candies[i - 1] + 1;
    for (int i = n - 2; i >= 0; i--)
        if (ratings[i] > ratings[i + 1])
            candies[i] = Math.max(candies[i], candies[i + 1] + 1);
    int total = 0;
    for (int c : candies) total += c;
    return total;
}

Meeting Rooms II (LC 253)

public int minMeetingRooms(int[][] intervals) {
    int[] starts = new int[intervals.length];
    int[] ends = new int[intervals.length];
    for (int i = 0; i < intervals.length; i++) {
        starts[i] = intervals[i][0];
        ends[i] = intervals[i][1];
    }
    Arrays.sort(starts);
    Arrays.sort(ends);
    int rooms = 0, endPtr = 0;
    for (int i = 0; i < starts.length; i++) {
        if (starts[i] < ends[endPtr]) rooms++;
        else endPtr++;
    }
    return rooms;
}

Section 10: Quick Revision Card

Greedy Algorithms — Quick Revision Card

Pattern Flowchart

Given a greedy problem:
  |
  +→ Intervals/Ranges?
  |   +→ Max non-overlapping? → Pattern 1 (Sort by END time)
  |   +→ Merge/insert/partition? → Pattern 6 (Sort by START time)
  |   +→ Min rooms/arrows? → Pattern 1 or 6 (sweep line)
  |
  +→ Array with jumps/reach?
  |   +→ Pattern 2 (Track farthest reachable)
  |
  +→ Two sets to match?
  |   +→ Pattern 3 (Sort both, two pointers)
  |
  +→ Repeatedly pick min/max?
  |   +→ Pattern 4 (Heap / Priority Queue)
  |
  +→ Build optimal string/number?
      +→ Pattern 5 (Stack-based, lexicographic)

“Sort by What?” Decision Guide

Sort ByWhenExamples
End timeMaximize non-overlappingActivity Selection, Arrows
Start timeMerge/process intervalsMerge Intervals
Size/WeightMatch resources to demandsAssign Cookies, Boats
Custom comparatorLexicographic / concat orderLargest Number (b+a vs a+b)
FrequencySchedule by frequencyTask Scheduler, Reorganize String

Greedy vs DP Decision

1. Can I make ONE choice and reduce to ONE sub-problem? YES → Greedy
2. Does choosing the best now ever hurt me later? NO → Greedy
3. Can I find a counterexample in 1 minute? YES → Not Greedy, use DP

Common Mistakes

  1. Forgetting to sort — most greedy problems need sorting first
  2. Wrong sort key — start vs end changes correctness
  3. Integer overflow in comparators — use Integer.compare()
  4. Not handling leading zeros — Remove K Digits
  5. Assuming greedy without proof — always sanity-check
  6. Missing “impossible” case — Gas Station returns -1
  7. Off-by-one in Jump Game II — loop to nums.length - 1

Total Patterns: 6 | Must-Solve: 15 | Key Insight: Sort first, choose greedily, prove by exchange argument.

15 / 18

Intervals & Merge — Complete DSA Notes

Comprehensive DSA Notes for SDE 2 Interview Prep

AspectDetail
Frequency★★★★☆ — 10-15% of interviews
CompaniesAmazon, Google, Microsoft, Flipkart, Uber, Swiggy, PhonePe
PrerequisitesSorting, Greedy (Topic 14), Heaps (Topic 9)

Section 1: Topic Overview

What qualifies: Any problem with [start, end] ranges — scheduling, resource allocation, calendar conflicts, range merging.

Hidden Interval Problems

ProblemWhy It’s Interval
Partition Labels (LC 763)Each char’s first/last occurrence = interval
Car Pooling (LC 1094)Pickup/drop = start/end events
Summary Ranges (LC 228)Consecutive numbers form intervals

Section 2: Core Concepts

Sorting Strategies

Sort ByWhen
Start timeMerging, inserting
End timeGreedy scheduling (max non-overlapping)
Start asc, end descRemove covered intervals
WARNING: a[0] - b[0] can overflow. Use Integer.compare(a[0], b[0]).

Overlap Detection

Intervals A=[a1,a2] and B=[b1,b2] overlap iff:
    a1 < b2 AND b1 < a2   (exclusive ends)
    a1 <= b2 AND b1 <= a2  (inclusive/touching)

Data Structure Choices

StructureUse Case
Sorted ArrayMost interval problems (sort once, scan)
PriorityQueueMeeting Rooms II — track earliest ending
TreeMapMy Calendar — dynamic interval insertion
Two PointersInterval intersection of two sorted lists

Section 3: Pattern Recognition Framework

Pattern 1: Merge Overlapping Intervals
How to Arrive: List of intervals, need to combine overlapping. Keywords: “merge”, “combine”, “union”. Strategy: Sort by start, greedily extend end.
public int[][] merge(int[][] intervals) {
    Arrays.sort(intervals, (a, b) -> Integer.compare(a[0], b[0]));
    List<int[]> merged = new ArrayList<>();
    int[] current = intervals[0];
    merged.add(current);
    for (int i = 1; i < intervals.length; i++) {
        if (intervals[i][0] <= current[1]) {
            current[1] = Math.max(current[1], intervals[i][1]);
        } else {
            current = intervals[i];
            merged.add(current);
        }
    }
    return merged.toArray(new int[merged.size()][]);
}
Pattern 2: Insert Interval
How to Arrive: Sorted, non-overlapping list + new interval to insert. Strategy: Three phases — add before, merge overlapping, add after.
public int[][] insert(int[][] intervals, int[] newInterval) {
    List<int[]> result = new ArrayList<>();
    int i = 0, n = intervals.length;
    while (i < n && intervals[i][1] < newInterval[0])
        result.add(intervals[i++]);
    while (i < n && intervals[i][0] <= newInterval[1]) {
        newInterval[0] = Math.min(newInterval[0], intervals[i][0]);
        newInterval[1] = Math.max(newInterval[1], intervals[i][1]);
        i++;
    }
    result.add(newInterval);
    while (i < n) result.add(intervals[i++]);
    return result.toArray(new int[result.size()][]);
}
Pattern 3: Interval Scheduling (Max Non-Overlapping)
How to Arrive: Maximize non-overlapping or minimize removals. Keywords: “minimum removals”, “minimum arrows”. Strategy: Sort by end time, greedily pick earliest-ending.
// LC 435: Min removals
public int eraseOverlapIntervals(int[][] intervals) {
    Arrays.sort(intervals, (a, b) -> Integer.compare(a[1], b[1]));
    int kept = 1, prevEnd = intervals[0][1];
    for (int i = 1; i < intervals.length; i++) {
        if (intervals[i][0] >= prevEnd) {
            kept++;
            prevEnd = intervals[i][1];
        }
    }
    return intervals.length - kept;
}

// LC 452: Min arrows
public int findMinArrowShots(int[][] points) {
    Arrays.sort(points, (a, b) -> Integer.compare(a[1], b[1]));
    int arrows = 1, arrowPos = points[0][1];
    for (int i = 1; i < points.length; i++) {
        if (points[i][0] > arrowPos) {
            arrows++;
            arrowPos = points[i][1];
        }
    }
    return arrows;
}
Pattern 4: Meeting Rooms / Sweep Line
How to Arrive: Find max concurrent overlapping or min resources. Keywords: “meeting rooms”, “min platforms”, “max overlap”. Strategy A: Sweep line with +1/-1 events. Strategy B: Min-heap of end times.
// LC 252: Can attend all meetings?
public boolean canAttendMeetings(int[][] intervals) {
    Arrays.sort(intervals, (a, b) -> Integer.compare(a[0], b[0]));
    for (int i = 1; i < intervals.length; i++)
        if (intervals[i][0] < intervals[i - 1][1]) return false;
    return true;
}

// LC 253: Min meeting rooms — Sweep Line
public int minMeetingRooms(int[][] intervals) {
    int n = intervals.length;
    int[] starts = new int[n], ends = new int[n];
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        starts[i] = intervals[i][0];
        ends[i] = intervals[i][1];
    }
    Arrays.sort(starts);
    Arrays.sort(ends);
    int rooms = 0, maxRooms = 0, s = 0, e = 0;
    while (s < n) {
        if (starts[s] < ends[e]) { rooms++; maxRooms = Math.max(maxRooms, rooms); s++; }
        else { rooms--; e++; }
    }
    return maxRooms;
}

// LC 253: Min meeting rooms — Heap
public int minMeetingRoomsHeap(int[][] intervals) {
    Arrays.sort(intervals, (a, b) -> Integer.compare(a[0], b[0]));
    PriorityQueue<Integer> heap = new PriorityQueue<>();
    for (int[] iv : intervals) {
        if (!heap.isEmpty() && heap.peek() <= iv[0]) heap.poll();
        heap.offer(iv[1]);
    }
    return heap.size();
}
Pattern 5: Interval Intersection
How to Arrive: Two sorted lists, find overlapping segments. Keywords: “intersection”, “common time”. Strategy: Two pointers — advance the one with smaller end.
public int[][] intervalIntersection(int[][] A, int[][] B) {
    List<int[]> result = new ArrayList<>();
    int i = 0, j = 0;
    while (i < A.length && j < B.length) {
        int lo = Math.max(A[i][0], B[j][0]);
        int hi = Math.min(A[i][1], B[j][1]);
        if (lo <= hi) result.add(new int[]{lo, hi});
        if (A[i][1] < B[j][1]) i++; else j++;
    }
    return result.toArray(new int[result.size()][]);
}
Pattern 6: Range Coverage / Gaps
How to Arrive: Find gaps between intervals, summarize ranges, or check coverage. Keywords: “summary”, “missing ranges”, “free time”.
// LC 228: Summary Ranges
public List<String> summaryRanges(int[] nums) {
    List<String> result = new ArrayList<>();
    int i = 0;
    while (i < nums.length) {
        int start = nums[i];
        while (i + 1 < nums.length && nums[i + 1] == nums[i] + 1) i++;
        result.add(start == nums[i] ? "" + start : start + "->" + nums[i]);
        i++;
    }
    return result;
}

Section 4: Code Templates

Template: Sweep Line for Max Concurrency

public static int maxConcurrency(int[][] intervals) {
    List<int[]> events = new ArrayList<>();
    for (int[] iv : intervals) {
        events.add(new int[]{iv[0], 1});
        events.add(new int[]{iv[1], -1});
    }
    events.sort((a, b) -> a[0] != b[0] ? a[0] - b[0] : a[1] - b[1]);
    int current = 0, max = 0;
    for (int[] event : events) {
        current += event[1];
        max = Math.max(max, current);
    }
    return max;
}

Template: TreeMap Booking

class MyCalendar {
    TreeMap<Integer, Integer> calendar = new TreeMap<>();
    public boolean book(int start, int end) {
        Integer prev = calendar.floorKey(start);
        Integer next = calendar.ceilingKey(start);
        if (prev != null && calendar.get(prev) > start) return false;
        if (next != null && next < end) return false;
        calendar.put(start, end);
        return true;
    }
}

Template: Difference Array (Car Pooling)

public static boolean carPooling(int[][] trips, int capacity) {
    int[] diff = new int[1001];
    for (int[] trip : trips) {
        diff[trip[1]] += trip[0];
        diff[trip[2]] -= trip[0];
    }
    int current = 0;
    for (int d : diff) {
        current += d;
        if (current > capacity) return false;
    }
    return true;
}

Section 5: Complexity Analysis

Merge IntervalsO(n log n)O(n)
Insert IntervalO(n)O(n)
Interval SchedulingO(n log n)O(1)
Sweep LineO(n log n)O(n)
Heap (Meeting Rooms II)O(n log n)O(n)
Two-Pointer IntersectionO(m + n)O(1)*
TreeMap BookingO(log n) per opO(n)
Difference ArrayO(n + R)O(R)

Section 6: Edge Cases & Gotchas

⚠ Touching intervals [1,2] and [2,3]: Depends on problem! Merge Intervals (LC 56): overlap (<=). Meeting Rooms (LC 252): no overlap (<). Always check.
Integer overflow: Use Integer.compare(a[0], b[0]) instead of a[0] - b[0].
⚠ Unsorted input: Always sort first unless explicitly stated as sorted. #1 bug in interval problems.
⚠ Empty array / single interval: Return as-is, don’t crash.

Section 7: Interview Tips

Tip: “Sort First” Mantra — Almost every interval problem begins with sorting. The question is: sort by start or end?
Tip: Follow-ups — “What if intervals come in a stream?” → TreeMap. “Already sorted?” → Skip sort, O(n).
