Trie (Prefix Tree) — DSA Interview Notes

Comprehensive DSA Notes for SDE 2 Interview Prep

AspectDetail
Frequency★★★☆☆ — 5-8% of interviews
CompaniesGoogle, Amazon, Microsoft, Uber, Flipkart
PrerequisitesStrings, Recursion, DFS, HashMap basics
Rule of thumb: If the problem involves prefixes, autocomplete, wildcard matching, or lexicographic order — think Trie first.

Section 1: Topic Overview

A Trie (pronounced “try”) is a tree where each edge represents a character. Paths from root to marked nodes represent stored words.

Trie vs HashMap

ScenarioTrieHashMap
Prefix search (“starts with”)O(L) — natural fitO(N×L) — must scan all
AutocompleteExcellentPoor
Exact lookupO(L)O(L) avg — slightly faster
Wildcard search (.)Natural recursive fitVery difficult
Lexicographic orderingBuilt-in via DFSRequires sorting
Memory (many shared prefixes)Efficient — shares nodesStores full keys

Section 2: Core Concepts

TrieNode Structure

// Array-based (faster, fixed alphabet)
class TrieNode {
    TrieNode[] children = new TrieNode[26];
    boolean isEnd = false;
}

// HashMap-based (flexible, any character set)
class TrieNode {
    Map<Character, TrieNode> children = new HashMap<>();
    boolean isEnd = false;
}

Core Operations — All O(L)

public void insert(String word) {
    TrieNode node = root;
    for (char c : word.toCharArray()) {
        int idx = c - 'a';
        if (node.children[idx] == null) node.children[idx] = new TrieNode();
        node = node.children[idx];
    }
    node.isEnd = true;
}

public boolean search(String word) {
    TrieNode node = traverse(word);
    return node != null && node.isEnd; // must be complete word
}

public boolean startsWith(String prefix) {
    return traverse(prefix) != null; // just needs to exist
}

Trie vs HashMap vs HashSet

OperationTrieHashMap
Prefix searchO(L)O(N×L)
Autocomplete (k results)O(L + k)O(N×L)
Sorted iterationO(N×L) naturalO(N×L×logN)

Section 3: Pattern Recognition Framework

Pattern 1: Basic Trie Operations
How to Arrive: Problem says “implement”, “prefix”, “starts with”, or requires storing a dictionary with efficient lookup.

Implement Trie (LC 208)

class Trie {
    private TrieNode root = new TrieNode();

    class TrieNode {
        TrieNode[] children = new TrieNode[26];
        boolean isEnd = false;
    }

    public void insert(String word) {
        TrieNode node = root;
        for (char c : word.toCharArray()) {
            if (node.children[c - 'a'] == null)
                node.children[c - 'a'] = new TrieNode();
            node = node.children[c - 'a'];
        }
        node.isEnd = true;
    }

    public boolean search(String word) {
        TrieNode node = traverse(word);
        return node != null && node.isEnd;
    }

    public boolean startsWith(String prefix) {
        return traverse(prefix) != null;
    }

    private TrieNode traverse(String s) {
        TrieNode node = root;
        for (char c : s.toCharArray()) {
            if (node.children[c - 'a'] == null) return null;
            node = node.children[c - 'a'];
        }
        return node;
    }
}
Pattern 2: Word Search with Trie (Trie + DFS on Grid)
How to Arrive: 2D board + list of words to find. Brute-force DFS per word is TLE. Build Trie from word list, do ONE DFS pass. Signal: “find ALL words from dictionary in grid.”

Word Search II (LC 212)

class Solution {
    class TrieNode {
        TrieNode[] children = new TrieNode[26];
        String word = null;
    }
    int[][] dirs = {{0,1},{0,-1},{1,0},{-1,0}};

    public List<String> findWords(char[][] board, String[] words) {
        TrieNode root = new TrieNode();
        for (String w : words) {
            TrieNode node = root;
            for (char c : w.toCharArray()) {
                if (node.children[c-'a'] == null) node.children[c-'a'] = new TrieNode();
                node = node.children[c-'a'];
            }
            node.word = w;
        }
        List<String> result = new ArrayList<>();
        for (int i = 0; i < board.length; i++)
            for (int j = 0; j < board[0].length; j++)
                if (root.children[board[i][j]-'a'] != null)
                    dfs(board, i, j, root, result);
        return result;
    }

    void dfs(char[][] board, int i, int j, TrieNode node, List<String> result) {
        char c = board[i][j];
        TrieNode next = node.children[c - 'a'];
        if (next == null) return;
        if (next.word != null) { result.add(next.word); next.word = null; }
        board[i][j] = '#';
        for (int[] d : dirs) {
            int ni = i+d[0], nj = j+d[1];
            if (ni >= 0 && ni < board.length && nj >= 0 && nj < board[0].length && board[ni][nj] != '#')
                dfs(board, ni, nj, next, result);
        }
        board[i][j] = c;
        // Prune empty branches
        boolean hasChild = false;
        for (TrieNode ch : next.children) if (ch != null) { hasChild = true; break; }
        if (!hasChild) node.children[c - 'a'] = null;
    }
}
Pattern 3: Prefix-Based Problems
How to Arrive: Finding common prefixes, suggesting words by prefix, or summing values by prefix. Keywords: “prefix”, “suggest”, “sum of keys starting with”.

Search Suggestions System (LC 1268)

public List<List<String>> suggestedProducts(String[] products, String searchWord) {
    Arrays.sort(products);
    TrieNode root = new TrieNode();
    for (String p : products) {
        TrieNode node = root;
        for (char c : p.toCharArray()) {
            if (node.children[c-'a'] == null) node.children[c-'a'] = new TrieNode();
            node = node.children[c-'a'];
            if (node.suggestions.size() < 3) node.suggestions.add(p);
        }
    }
    List<List<String>> result = new ArrayList<>();
    TrieNode node = root;
    boolean broken = false;
    for (char c : searchWord.toCharArray()) {
        if (!broken && node.children[c-'a'] != null) {
            node = node.children[c-'a'];
            result.add(node.suggestions);
        } else { broken = true; result.add(new ArrayList<>()); }
    }
    return result;
}
// TrieNode has: TrieNode[] children; List<String> suggestions = new ArrayList<>();
Pattern 4: XOR Trie (Bit Manipulation + Trie)
How to Arrive: “Maximum XOR” between numbers. XOR maximized by choosing opposite bit at each position. Build binary trie, greedily walk opposite path.

Maximum XOR of Two Numbers (LC 421)

public int findMaximumXOR(int[] nums) {
    TrieNode root = new TrieNode();
    // Build binary Trie (32 bits, MSB first)
    for (int num : nums) {
        TrieNode node = root;
        for (int i = 31; i >= 0; i--) {
            int bit = (num >> i) & 1;
            if (node.children[bit] == null) node.children[bit] = new TrieNode();
            node = node.children[bit];
        }
    }
    // Query: greedily pick opposite bit
    int maxXor = 0;
    for (int num : nums) {
        TrieNode node = root;
        int curXor = 0;
        for (int i = 31; i >= 0; i--) {
            int bit = (num >> i) & 1;
            int opp = 1 - bit;
            if (node.children[opp] != null) {
                curXor |= (1 << i);
                node = node.children[opp];
            } else {
                node = node.children[bit];
            }
        }
        maxXor = Math.max(maxXor, curXor);
    }
    return maxXor;
}
// TrieNode: TrieNode[] children = new TrieNode[2]; (0 and 1)
Pattern 5: Design Problems with Trie
How to Arrive: Design problem with word dictionaries + special search (wildcards, autocomplete, replacement). Wildcard . = try all children recursively.

Add and Search Word — Wildcard (LC 211)

class WordDictionary {
    TrieNode root = new TrieNode();

    public void addWord(String word) {
        TrieNode node = root;
        for (char c : word.toCharArray()) {
            if (node.children[c-'a'] == null) node.children[c-'a'] = new TrieNode();
            node = node.children[c-'a'];
        }
        node.isEnd = true;
    }

    public boolean search(String word) { return dfs(word, 0, root); }

    private boolean dfs(String word, int idx, TrieNode node) {
        if (idx == word.length()) return node.isEnd;
        char c = word.charAt(idx);
        if (c == '.') {
            for (TrieNode child : node.children)
                if (child != null && dfs(word, idx + 1, child)) return true;
            return false;
        } else {
            if (node.children[c-'a'] == null) return false;
            return dfs(word, idx + 1, node.children[c-'a']);
        }
    }
}

Replace Words (LC 648)

public String replaceWords(List<String> dictionary, String sentence) {
    TrieNode root = new TrieNode();
    for (String w : dictionary) {
        TrieNode node = root;
        for (char c : w.toCharArray()) {
            if (node.children[c-'a'] == null) node.children[c-'a'] = new TrieNode();
            node = node.children[c-'a'];
        }
        node.word = w;
    }
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    for (String word : sentence.split(" ")) {
        if (sb.length() > 0) sb.append(" ");
        TrieNode node = root;
        String replacement = word;
        for (char c : word.toCharArray()) {
            if (node.children[c-'a'] == null) break;
            node = node.children[c-'a'];
            if (node.word != null) { replacement = node.word; break; }
        }
        sb.append(replacement);
    }
    return sb.toString();
}

Section 5: Complexity Analysis

OperationTimeSpace
InsertO(L)O(L) new nodes
Search / StartsWithO(L)O(1)
Wildcard search (.)O(26W × L)O(L) stack
Word Search IIO(M×N×4L)O(sum of word lengths)
XOR max queryO(32) = O(1)O(32×N)
Build Trie from N wordsO(N × Lavg)O(26 × N × Lavg)

Section 6: Edge Cases & Gotchas

⚠ search("app") after inserting "apple": Returns false! isEnd not set at “app”. startsWith("app") returns true.
Forgetting isEnd check: startsWith ≠ search. Search must verify isEnd.
⚠ XOR Trie bit direction: Always MSB (bit 31) to LSB (bit 0). Wrong direction = wrong results.
⚠ Word Search II duplicates: Nullify node.word after first find to prevent duplicates.
⚠ Memory: 26 pointers per node adds up fast. Use HashMap-based for sparse tries.

Section 7: Interview Tips

When Trie beats HashMap: “I’m choosing a Trie because the problem requires prefix operations. HashMap needs O(N) to find all keys with a prefix, Trie does it in O(L).”
How to explain Trie: “An N-ary tree where each edge = character. Words sharing a prefix share the same path. Each node has up to 26 children and a boolean marking if a word ends there.”
Space optimization mentions: Compressed Trie (Radix Tree) merges single-child chains. Array pooling for competitive programming. Show awareness even if you don’t implement.

Bit ManipulationXOR Trie uses binary representation
Dynamic ProgrammingWord Break can use Trie for prefix lookups

Section 10: Quick Revision Card

Trie — 60-Second Revision

Structure: Tree where each edge = character. children[26] + isEnd boolean.

Bit Manipulation — Quick Wins with Binary Tricks

Comprehensive DSA Notes for SDE 2 Interview Prep

AspectDetail
FrequencyMedium — ~10-15% of interviews, but almost always quick sub-problems or standalone easy/medium
CompaniesAmazon, Google, Microsoft, Goldman Sachs, Flipkart, Samsung
PrerequisitesBinary number system, bitwise operators, two’s complement
Why it mattersBit problems are “quick wins” — solve them in 5 minutes and save time for harder problems
Key insight: Bit manipulation problems test whether you know a small set of tricks. Once memorized, they’re the fastest problems in any interview.

Section 1: Topic Overview

Bit manipulation operates on data at the level of individual bits using bitwise operators. Solutions are often O(1) space and extremely fast because bitwise operations map directly to single CPU instructions.

Where It AppearsExamples
Standalone bit questionsSingle Number, Reverse Bits, Power of Two
Optimizations inside DPBitmask DP (TSP, assignment problems)
Subset generationEnumerate all subsets for small n
Math tricksAdd without +, divide without /, missing number

Section 2: Core Concepts

Bitwise Operators Reference

OperatorNameDescriptionExample (a=5 0101, b=3 0011)
&AND1 only if both bits are 1a & b = 1 (0001)
|OR1 if at least one bit is 1a | b = 7 (0111)
^XOR1 if bits are differenta ^ b = 6 (0110)
~NOTFlips all bits~a = -6 (two’s complement)
<<Left ShiftShifts bits left, fills with 0a << 1 = 10 (1010)
>>Arithmetic Right ShiftShifts right, preserves sign bit-4 >> 1 = -2
>>>Logical Right ShiftShifts right, fills with 0 (Java)-1 >>> 1 = 2147483647

Two’s Complement

Java integers are stored in two’s complement (32 bits for int). Negative numbers: invert all bits of positive value, then add 1. Range: -2^31 to 2^31 - 1.

 5 in binary:  00000000 00000000 00000000 00000101
-5 in binary:  11111111 11111111 11111111 11111011  (invert + 1)
Key insight: ~n = -(n+1) and -n = ~n + 1.

Signed vs. Unsigned Right Shift

int x = -8;           // 11111111...11111000
x >> 1;               // 11111111...11111100  = -4  (sign bit preserved)
x >>> 1;              // 01111111...11111100  = 2147483644  (zero-filled)

Use >> for signed division by 2. Use >>> when treating int as unsigned 32-bit value (e.g., Reverse Bits).

Common Bit Tricks — Master Table

Memorize this table. It’s the single most important reference for bit manipulation.
OperationFormulaExample
Check if k-th bit is set(n >> k) & 1n=5(101), k=2 → 1
Set k-th bitn | (1 << k)n=5(101), k=1 → 7(111)
Clear k-th bitn & ~(1 << k)n=7(111), k=1 → 5(101)
Toggle k-th bitn ^ (1 << k)n=5(101), k=0 → 4(100)
Isolate lowest set bitn & (-n)n=12(1100) → 4(0100)
Clear lowest set bitn & (n - 1)n=12(1100) → 8(1000)
Check if power of 2n > 0 && (n & (n-1)) == 0n=8 → true
Get lowest k bitsn & ((1 << k) - 1)n=13(1101), k=3 → 5(101)
Swap two numbersa^=b; b^=a; a^=b;—
Hack: n & (n - 1) — drops the lowest set bit.
Count set bits in O(k) where k = number of set bits (not O(32)): while (n != 0) { n &= (n - 1); count++; }
Also the cleanest power-of-2 check: n > 0 && (n & (n - 1)) == 0. One line, no loops.
Hack: n & (-n) — isolates the lowest set bit.
Critical for LC 260 (two single numbers). XOR all numbers to get xor = a ^ b. Then diff = xor & (-xor) gives a bit where a and b differ. Partition all numbers by that bit into two groups — each group contains exactly one unique number. XOR each group separately.

XOR Properties (Critical)

a ^ a = 0          (self-cancellation)
a ^ 0 = a          (identity)
a ^ b = b ^ a      (commutative)
(a ^ b) ^ c = a ^ (b ^ c)  (associative)

These properties are the foundation of nearly every XOR-based interview problem.

Section 3: Pattern Recognition Framework

Pattern 1: XOR Tricks — Finding Unique / Missing / Duplicate Numbers

Problems: Single Number (LC 136), Single Number II (LC 137), Single Number III (LC 260), Missing Number (LC 268)

How to Arrive at This

Ask yourself: “Are elements appearing in pairs that should cancel out, leaving one or two unique elements?”

Trigger clues:
  • “Every element appears twice except one” → classic XOR all elements
  • “Find the missing number in 0..n” → XOR indices with values; the unpaired one survives
  • “Two elements appear once, rest appear twice” → XOR all to get a^b, then split by a distinguishing bit
Thinking process for LC 260 (two unique numbers):
  1. XOR everything → you get a ^ b
  2. Since a != b, at least one bit in a^b is 1. Find it: diff & (-diff)
  3. That bit is set in one of {a, b} and not the other. Partition all numbers by that bit.
  4. XOR within each group → each group yields one unique number.

Core Idea: XOR cancels pairs. Whatever doesn’t cancel is your answer.

// LC 136: Single Number — every element appears twice except one
public int singleNumber(int[] nums) {
    int result = 0;
    for (int n : nums) result ^= n;
    return result;
}

LC 137 (Single Number II) — every element appears three times except one. XOR alone won’t cancel triples. Use bit counting: for each of the 32 bit positions, sum how many numbers have that bit set, take modulo 3.

// LC 137: Single Number II — every element appears 3 times except one
public int singleNumber(int[] nums) {
    int result = 0;
    for (int i = 0; i < 32; i++) {
        int sum = 0;
        for (int n : nums) {
            sum += (n >> i) & 1;
        }
        if (sum % 3 != 0) {
            result |= (1 << i);
        }
    }
    return result;
}
Pattern 2: Bit Masking for Subsets

Problems: Subsets (LC 78), Subsets II (LC 90)

How to Arrive at This

Ask yourself: “Do I need to enumerate all subsets of a small set (n ≤ 20)?”

Thinking process:
  1. A set of n elements has 2n subsets.
  2. Each subset can be represented as an n-bit number: bit j is 1 if element j is included.
  3. Iterate from 0 to 2^n - 1. For each number, check which bits are set.
Why bitmask over backtracking? For small n, bitmask iteration is simpler, harder to get wrong, and avoids recursion overhead. Also integrates naturally with bitmask DP. 
// LC 78: Subsets using bitmask
public List<List<Integer>> subsets(int[] nums) {
    int n = nums.length;
    List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();
    for (int mask = 0; mask < (1 << n); mask++) {
        List<Integer> subset = new ArrayList<>();
        for (int j = 0; j < n; j++) {
            if ((mask & (1 << j)) != 0) {
                subset.add(nums[j]);
            }
        }
        result.add(subset);
    }
    return result;
}
Bitmask DP connection: When the state space is “which elements have been used/visited” and n is small, represent the state as a bitmask integer. Foundation of Travelling Salesman DP, assignment problems, etc.
Pattern 3: Brian Kernighan’s Algorithm / Counting Bits

Problems: Number of 1 Bits (LC 191), Counting Bits (LC 338), Hamming Distance (LC 461)

How to Arrive at This

Ask yourself: “Do I need to count how many bits are set (popcount)?”

The key trick: n & (n - 1) clears the lowest set bit of n.

Why does this work?
  • n - 1 flips the lowest set bit and all bits below it.
  • AND-ing with n removes exactly that lowest set bit.
  • Example: n = 12 (1100), n-1 = 11 (1011), n & (n-1) = 8 (1000)
Counting: Repeat n = n & (n-1) until n == 0. Iterations = set bits.

For Hamming Distance: XOR the two numbers first (different bits become 1), then count set bits. 
// LC 191: Number of 1 Bits (Hamming Weight)
public int hammingWeight(int n) {
    int count = 0;
    while (n != 0) {
        n &= (n - 1);  // clear lowest set bit
        count++;
    }
    return count;
}
// LC 338: Counting Bits — DP: dp[i] = dp[i & (i-1)] + 1
public int[] countBits(int n) {
    int[] dp = new int[n + 1];
    for (int i = 1; i <= n; i++) {
        dp[i] = dp[i & (i - 1)] + 1;
    }
    return dp;
}
// LC 461: Hamming Distance — XOR then count set bits
public int hammingDistance(int x, int y) {
    int xor = x ^ y;
    int count = 0;
    while (xor != 0) {
        xor &= (xor - 1);
        count++;
    }
    return count;
}
Pattern 4: Power of Two / Powers

Problems: Power of Two (LC 231), Power of Four (LC 342)

How to Arrive at This

Ask yourself: “Does the problem involve checking if a number has a special binary form?”

Key observation: A power of 2 has exactly one set bit: 1, 10, 100, 1000, ...

Thinking process:
  1. If only one bit is set, then n & (n-1) must be 0.
  2. Also need n > 0 (0 is not a power of 2).
Power of 4 extension: It’s a power of 2 AND the set bit is at an even position (0, 2, 4, ...). Use mask 0x55555555 (alternating 1s at even positions). 
// LC 231: Power of Two
public boolean isPowerOfTwo(int n) {
    return n > 0 && (n & (n - 1)) == 0;
}

// LC 342: Power of Four
public boolean isPowerOfFour(int n) {
    return n > 0 && (n & (n - 1)) == 0 && (n & 0x55555555) != 0;
}
Pattern 5: Bit Manipulation in Math/Array Problems

Problems: Reverse Bits (LC 190), Missing Number (LC 268), Sum of Two Integers (LC 371), Divide Two Integers (LC 29)

How to Arrive at This

Ask yourself: “Does the problem say ‘without using +/-’ or ‘manipulate a 32-bit integer’?”

Thinking process for Sum Without + (LC 371):
  1. Binary addition: a + b = (sum without carry) + (carry shifted left).
  2. Sum without carry = a ^ b (XOR gives digit-by-digit sum ignoring carries).
  3. Carry = (a & b) << 1 (AND finds where both bits are 1, shift to carry).
  4. Repeat until carry is 0.
Thinking process for Reverse Bits (LC 190):
  1. Process bit by bit from right to left.
  2. Extract rightmost bit, place it at correct position in result.
  3. Shift input right, shift result left, repeat 32 times.
// LC 371: Sum of Two Integers (no + or - operator)
public int getSum(int a, int b) {
    while (b != 0) {
        int carry = (a & b) << 1;
        a = a ^ b;       // sum without carry
        b = carry;        // carry to add next
    }
    return a;
}
// LC 190: Reverse Bits
public int reverseBits(int n) {
    int result = 0;
    for (int i = 0; i < 32; i++) {
        result <<= 1;          // make room for next bit
        result |= (n & 1);     // extract last bit of n
        n >>>= 1;              // unsigned right shift (important!)
    }
    return result;
}
// LC 268: Missing Number (XOR approach)
public int missingNumber(int[] nums) {
    int xor = nums.length;
    for (int i = 0; i < nums.length; i++) {
        xor ^= i ^ nums[i];
    }
    return xor;
}
Pattern 6: Advanced Bit Tricks

Problems: Maximum XOR of Two Numbers (LC 421), Bitwise AND of Range (LC 201), UTF-8 Validation (LC 393)

How to Arrive at This

Recognize these by:
  • “Maximum XOR” → Binary Trie approach (greedy bit-by-bit from MSB, see Topic 16)
  • “AND of a range” → AND over consecutive numbers zeros out differing bits. Answer is the common prefix.
  • “Validate encoding” → Bit-by-bit inspection using masks.
Thinking for Bitwise AND of Range (LC 201):
  1. AND-ing consecutive numbers: any bit where they differ becomes 0.
  2. Keep shifting both right until they are equal (finding common prefix).
  3. Shift back left by the number of shifts.
// LC 201: Bitwise AND of Numbers Range
public int rangeBitwiseAnd(int left, int right) {
    int shift = 0;
    while (left != right) {
        left >>= 1;
        right >>= 1;
        shift++;
    }
    return left << shift;
}
// LC 421: Maximum XOR — Binary Trie approach (O(32*n))
public int findMaximumXOR(int[] nums) {
    int[][] trie = new int[32 * nums.length + 1][2];
    int idx = 0;

    for (int num : nums) {
        int node = 0;
        for (int i = 31; i >= 0; i--) {
            int bit = (num >> i) & 1;
            if (trie[node][bit] == 0) trie[node][bit] = ++idx;
            node = trie[node][bit];
        }
    }

    int maxXor = 0;
    for (int num : nums) {
        int node = 0, curXor = 0;
        for (int i = 31; i >= 0; i--) {
            int bit = (num >> i) & 1;
            int oppBit = 1 - bit;
            if (trie[node][oppBit] != 0) {
                curXor |= (1 << i);
                node = trie[node][oppBit];
            } else {
                node = trie[node][bit];
            }
        }
        maxXor = Math.max(maxXor, curXor);
    }
    return maxXor;
}

Section 4: Code Templates

Template 1: XOR Find Unique Element

public int findUnique(int[] nums) {
    int result = 0;
    for (int n : nums) result ^= n;
    return result;
}

Template 2: Bitmask Subset Enumeration

// Time: O(2^n * n), Only for n <= 20
public List<List<Integer>> enumerateSubsets(int[] nums) {
    int n = nums.length;
    List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();
    for (int mask = 0; mask < (1 << n); mask++) {
        List<Integer> subset = new ArrayList<>();
        for (int j = 0; j < n; j++) {
            if ((mask >> j & 1) == 1) subset.add(nums[j]);
        }
        result.add(subset);
    }
    return result;
}

Template 3: Count Set Bits (Brian Kernighan)

// Runs in O(number of set bits)
public int countSetBits(int n) {
    int count = 0;
    while (n != 0) {
        n &= (n - 1);
        count++;
    }
    return count;
}

Template 4: Bit-by-Bit Construction

// Process each of 32 bits individually
public int bitByBitProcess(int n) {
    int result = 0;
    for (int i = 0; i < 32; i++) {
        int bit = (n >> i) & 1;
        result |= (bit << (31 - i));  // place at reversed position
    }
    return result;
}

Template 5: Add Without Arithmetic Operators

public int bitwiseAdd(int a, int b) {
    while (b != 0) {
        int carry = (a & b) << 1;
        a = a ^ b;
        b = carry;
    }
    return a;
}

Section 6: Edge Cases & Gotchas

Negative Numbers & Two’s Complement: -1 is all 1s in binary. while (n > 0) will never execute for negative numbers — always use while (n != 0).
Integer.MIN_VALUE Trap:
Math.abs(Integer.MIN_VALUE) overflows and returns -2147483648 (same value).
-Integer.MIN_VALUE also overflows. Always handle MIN_VALUE as a special case (e.g., LC 29).
Arithmetic vs. Logical Right Shift:
  • >> preserves sign bit (arithmetic) — use for signed division by 2
  • >>> fills with 0 (logical) — use for raw bit patterns (Reverse Bits, hashing)
Shift Overflow:
1 << 31 = Integer.MIN_VALUE (not 231!).
1 << 32 = 1 in Java (shift amount is mod 32).
Always use 1L << k when k ≥ 31 or when result needs to be long.
Java’s Lack of Unsigned Int: Use >>> for unsigned right shift. Use Integer.toUnsignedLong(n) for comparisons. Use Integer.compareUnsigned(a, b) for unsigned comparisons.
XOR Quick Reference: n ^ 0 = n (identity), n ^ (-1) = ~n (flip all), n ^ n = 0 (cancel).

Section 7: Interview Tips

When to Think “Bits”

Trigger in Problem StatementLikely Bit Technique
“Every element appears twice except one”XOR all elements
“Find missing number”XOR indices with values
“Power of 2” or “power of 4”n & (n-1) == 0
“Without using +, -, *, /”Bitwise add/subtract/multiply
“Generate all subsets” with small nBitmask enumeration
“Count number of 1 bits”Brian Kernighan’s algorithm
“Maximum XOR”Binary Trie
“Appears three times except one”Bit counting per position mod 3

How to Explain Bit Solutions Clearly

  1. Start with the property: “XOR cancels pairs, so if we XOR everything, only the unique element remains.”
  2. Draw the binary: Write out a small example in binary on the whiteboard.
  3. Trace through: Show 2-3 iterations on the example.
  4. State the invariant: “After each iteration of Brian Kernighan’s, exactly one set bit is removed.”
  5. Don’t assume the interviewer knows bit tricks: Briefly explain n & (n-1) before using it.

Common Follow-Ups

  • “Can you do it in O(1) space?” → Often the hint to use bit manipulation.
  • “What if numbers appear three times?” → Bit counting mod 3 (LC 137).
  • “What about negative numbers?” → Clarify two’s complement, handle MIN_VALUE.

Full Solutions

Solution 1: Single Number III (LC 260)

class Solution {
    public int[] singleNumber(int[] nums) {
        // Step 1: XOR all to get a ^ b
        int xor = 0;
        for (int n : nums) xor ^= n;

        // Step 2: Isolate lowest set bit
        int lowestBit = xor & (-xor);

        // Step 3: Partition and XOR
        int a = 0, b = 0;
        for (int n : nums) {
            if ((n & lowestBit) != 0) {
                a ^= n;
            } else {
                b ^= n;
            }
        }
        return new int[]{a, b};
    }
}
// Time: O(n), Space: O(1)

Solution 2: Counting Bits (LC 338)

class Solution {
    public int[] countBits(int n) {
        int[] dp = new int[n + 1];
        for (int i = 1; i <= n; i++) {
            dp[i] = dp[i & (i - 1)] + 1;
        }
        return dp;
    }
}
// Time: O(n), Space: O(n)

Solution 3: Sum of Two Integers (LC 371)

class Solution {
    public int getSum(int a, int b) {
        while (b != 0) {
            int carry = (a & b) << 1;
            a = a ^ b;
            b = carry;
        }
        return a;
    }
}
// Time: O(32) = O(1), Space: O(1)

Solution 4: Subsets Using Bitmask (LC 78)

class Solution {
    public List<List<Integer>> subsets(int[] nums) {
        int n = nums.length;
        List<List<Integer>> result = new ArrayList<>();
        for (int mask = 0; mask < (1 << n); mask++) {
            List<Integer> subset = new ArrayList<>();
            for (int j = 0; j < n; j++) {
                if ((mask & (1 << j)) != 0) {
                    subset.add(nums[j]);
                }
            }
            result.add(subset);
        }
        return result;
    }
}
// Time: O(2^n * n), Space: O(2^n * n)

Section 10: Quick Revision Card

Bit Tricks Cheat Sheet

Check k-th bit:       (n >> k) & 1
Set k-th bit:         n | (1 << k)
Clear k-th bit:       n & ~(1 << k)
Toggle k-th bit:      n ^ (1 << k)
Lowest set bit:       n & (-n)
Clear lowest set bit: n & (n - 1)
Power of 2:           n > 0 && (n & (n-1)) == 0
All 1s mask (k bits): (1 << k) - 1
Swap a, b:            a ^= b; b ^= a; a ^= b;

XOR Properties

a ^ a = 0           Self-cancellation
a ^ 0 = a           Identity
a ^ b = b ^ a       Commutative
(a^b)^c = a^(b^c)   Associative
a ^ (-1) = ~a       Flip all bits

Pattern Decision Flowchart

Problem says...
├─ "appears twice except one" ──────────→ XOR all (Pattern 1)
├─ "appears three times except one" ────→ Bit count mod 3 (Pattern 1)
├─ "two unique among pairs" ────────────→ XOR + split by bit (Pattern 1)
├─ "generate all subsets" with n ≤ 20 ──→ Bitmask enumeration (Pattern 2)
├─ "count 1 bits" / "hamming" ──────────→ Brian Kernighan (Pattern 3)
├─ "power of 2" or "power of 4" ───────→ n & (n-1) check (Pattern 4)
├─ "without +/-" operators ─────────────→ XOR + AND carry (Pattern 5)
├─ "reverse bits" / "32-bit integer" ───→ Bit-by-bit loop (Pattern 5)
├─ "maximum XOR" ───────────────────────→ Binary Trie (Pattern 6)
├─ "AND of range" ──────────────────────→ Common prefix shift (Pattern 6)
└─ "state is subset, n ≤ 20" ──────────→ Bitmask DP

Common Mistakes

1. Using n > 0 instead of n != 0 in loop — fails for negative numbers
2. Missing parentheses: if (n & 1 == 1) is wrong — use if ((n & 1) == 1)
3. 1 << 31 gives MIN_VALUE, not 231 — use 1L << 31
4. Using >> instead of >>> for unsigned shift — infinite loop risk
5. 1 << 32 == 1 in Java — shift amounts are mod 32
6. Math.abs(MIN_VALUE) overflows — always handle as special case
7. n & (n-1) for n=0 — check n > 0 first for power-of-2

String Patterns & Tricks

Comprehensive DSA Notes for SDE 2 Interview Prep

AspectDetail
FrequencyVery High — strings appear in ~50%+ of interview rounds
CompaniesAmazon, Flipkart, Google, Microsoft, Goldman Sachs, Uber
Key InsightString problems span many topics. The skill is recognizing which underlying pattern applies.
This Topic CoversPatterns unique to strings: anagrams, palindromes, encoding/decoding, parentheses, transformation
Why strings matter for SDE 2: They test attention to detail, API knowledge, and communication. The problem sounds simple but has depth.

Section 1: Topic Overview

Section 2: Core Concepts

StringBuilder vs String — The Immutability Trap

Critical: String concatenation in a loop is O(n²). Always use StringBuilder. 
// BAD — O(n²): each + creates new String, copies all previous chars
String result = "";
for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) result += chars[i];

// GOOD — O(n) amortized
StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) sb.append(chars[i]);
String result = sb.toString();

Character Frequency Arrays vs HashMap

int[] freq = new int[26];  // lowercase only — fastest, O(1) space
for (char c : s.toCharArray()) freq[c - 'a']++;

int[] freq = new int[128]; // ASCII — includes digits/symbols

Map<Character, Integer> freq = new HashMap<>(); // Unicode / unknown charset
for (char c : s.toCharArray()) freq.merge(c, 1, Integer::sum);
Rule of thumb: int[26] when problem says “lowercase letters”, int[128] for ASCII, HashMap for Unicode.
Hack: toCharArray() once, work on array, convert back.
Repeated charAt(i) calls add overhead. For problems with swaps or character manipulation, convert once: char[] arr = s.toCharArray();, do all work on the array, then new String(arr). Especially matters in problems like reverse words, sort characters, or decode strings.

String Comparison & Substring

  • ALWAYS use .equals() not == (which compares references)
  • compareTo() for lexicographic ordering
  • substring() is O(k) in modern Java — use indices in loops

Essential String Operations

s.charAt(i)              // O(1)    s.length()              // O(1)
s.substring(i, j)        // O(j-i)  s.toCharArray()         // O(n)
s.indexOf("pat")         // O(n*m)  String.valueOf(num)     // int→String
Integer.parseInt(s)      // String→int (throws on invalid)
Character.isLetterOrDigit(c)  // check alphanumeric
Character.toLowerCase(c)      // convert case
Arrays.sort(charArray)        // O(n log n)
new String(charArray)         // char[]→String
sb.reverse()                  // O(n)    sb.deleteCharAt(i) // O(n)

Section 3: Pattern Recognition Framework

Pattern 1: Anagram / Permutation Grouping

Problems: Valid Anagram (LC 242), Group Anagrams (LC 49), Find All Anagrams (LC 438)

How to Arrive at This

Ask yourself: “Does this problem care about the frequency of characters but NOT their order?”
If rearranging characters doesn’t change the answer, you’re dealing with an anagram pattern.

The key insight is finding a canonical representation — a way to represent all anagrams identically:
  • Sorted key: Arrays.sort(s.toCharArray()) → O(k log k). Best for short strings.
  • Frequency key: Count each letter, build "1#0#0#...#1#0#" → O(k). Best for long strings.
// Frequency key — all anagrams produce the same key
int[] count = new int[26];
for (char c : s.toCharArray()) count[c - 'a']++;
StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
for (int i = 0; i < 26; i++) sb.append(count[i]).append('#');
String key = sb.toString();

LC 438 (Find All Anagrams) uses frequency array with sliding window — cross-ref Topic 3.

Pattern 2: Palindrome Construction & Validation

Problems: Valid Palindrome (LC 125), Valid Palindrome II (LC 680), Longest Palindromic Substring (LC 5), Palindrome Partitioning (LC 131), Shortest Palindrome (LC 214)

How to Arrive at This

Ask: “Does this problem involve reading the string forward and backward?”

Three sub-patterns:
  1. Validation — is it a palindrome? → two pointers from ends
  2. Finding — find the longest palindromic substring → expand around center
  3. Construction — partition into palindromes or build one → backtracking or DP
The expand around center technique is the most important one to master.

Valid Palindrome II — “Delete At Most One” (LC 680)

On mismatch in two-pointer check, try skip left OR skip right. Both options, O(n) total.

public boolean validPalindrome(String s) {
    int lo = 0, hi = s.length() - 1;
    while (lo < hi) {
        if (s.charAt(lo) != s.charAt(hi))
            return isPalin(s, lo + 1, hi) || isPalin(s, lo, hi - 1);
        lo++; hi--;
    }
    return true;
}
private boolean isPalin(String s, int lo, int hi) {
    while (lo < hi) if (s.charAt(lo++) != s.charAt(hi--)) return false;
    return true;
}

Expand Around Center — The Go-To Palindrome Technique

Every palindrome has a center. There are 2n − 1 possible centers (n single-char + n-1 gap centers). Expand outward from each. O(n²) time, O(1) space.

Manacher’s Algorithm: Finds longest palindromic substring in O(n). Almost never asked — mention it exists, implement expand-around-center.
Pattern 3: String Encoding / Decoding / Compression

Problems: String Compression (LC 443), Count and Say (LC 38), Decode String (LC 394), Encode and Decode Strings (LC 271)

How to Arrive at This

Ask: “Am I converting between two representations of the same data?”

If there’s nesting (like 3[a2[c]]), you need a stack.
If it’s flat (run-length encoding), a simple scan suffices.

Encode/Decode Strings (LC 271) — Length Prefix

// Encode: "hello","world" → "5#hello5#world"
public String encode(List<String> strs) {
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    for (String s : strs) sb.append(s.length()).append('#').append(s);
    return sb.toString();
}

// Decode: parse length, extract that many characters
public List<String> decode(String s) {
    List<String> result = new ArrayList<>();
    int i = 0;
    while (i < s.length()) {
        int hashIdx = s.indexOf('#', i);
        int len = Integer.parseInt(s.substring(i, hashIdx));
        result.add(s.substring(hashIdx + 1, hashIdx + 1 + len));
        i = hashIdx + 1 + len;
    }
    return result;
}
Why length-prefix? It handles ANY characters, including # and digits. Delimiter-based encoding breaks when the delimiter appears in the data.
Pattern 4: Parentheses & Bracket Problems

Problems: Valid Parentheses (LC 20), Generate Parentheses (LC 22), Min Remove to Make Valid (LC 1249), Longest Valid Parentheses (LC 32)

How to Arrive at This

Ask: “Does this problem involve matching opening and closing symbols?”

Hierarchy of parentheses problems:
  1. Validation — is this valid? → stack or counter
  2. Generation — generate all valid sequences → backtracking with open/close counts
  3. Fix it — minimum removals → stack for indices or two-pass counting
  4. Longest valid — find longest valid substring → stack with indices
The balance counter technique (increment on (, decrement on )) is the foundation.

Balance Counter Technique

int openCount = 0, closeCount = 0;
for (char c : s.toCharArray()) {
    if (c == '(') openCount++;
    else if (c == ')') {
        if (openCount > 0) openCount--;  // matched
        else closeCount++;                // unmatched close
    }
}
// Total removals needed = openCount + closeCount

Generate Parentheses (LC 22)

Add ( if open < n. Add ) only if close < open (ensures validity). Cross-ref Backtracking (Topic 8).

Longest Valid Parentheses (LC 32)

Push -1 as base. On (, push index. On ), pop; if stack empty push current index as new base, else maxLen = max(maxLen, i - stack.peek()). Stack stores indices, not characters.

Pattern 5: String Transformation & Matching

Problems: Is Subsequence (LC 392), Implement strStr (LC 28), String to Integer atoi (LC 8), Multiply Strings (LC 43), Integer to English Words (LC 273)

How to Arrive at This

Ask: “Am I transforming one string representation into another, or simulating an operation character by character?”

These problems are about careful implementation rather than clever algorithms. The key is handling edge cases: overflow, leading zeros, whitespace, invalid input.

State machine thinking helps for complex parsing (like atoi).

Is Subsequence (LC 392)

Two pointers: advance both on match, advance only in longer string on mismatch. O(n) time, O(1) space.

atoi (LC 8) — Overflow Trick

Check result > (MAX_VALUE - digit) / 10 BEFORE multiplying — avoids overflow in the check itself.

Multiply Strings (LC 43)

Key insight: Digit at num1[i] * num2[j] contributes to positions i+j (tens) and i+j+1 (ones) in the result array.
Pattern 6: Advanced String Patterns

Problems: Repeated Substring Pattern (LC 459), Reorganize String (LC 767), Shortest Palindrome (LC 214)

How to Arrive at This

Less common but appear in harder rounds:
  • “Can I use a mathematical property?” → Repeated substring, string hashing
  • “Can I rearrange characters optimally?” → Greedy + heap
  • “Can I reduce to a known algorithm?” → Light KMP/hash approach
Most have elegant tricks that avoid heavy algorithms.

Repeated Substring Pattern — Concatenation Trick

// LC 459: Check if s = repeat of a substring
// "abcabc" → true ("abc"×2), "aba" → false
public boolean repeatedSubstringPattern(String s) {
    String doubled = s + s;
    return doubled.substring(1, doubled.length() - 1).contains(s);
}
Why does this work? If s = p repeated k times, then s+s has p repeated 2k times. Removing first and last chars breaks original boundaries, but s still appears in the middle.

Reorganize String (LC 767)

Impossible if any char freq > (n+1)/2. Use max heap, always pick two most frequent chars alternately.

Polynomial Rolling Hash (When Asked for O(n) Matching)

// hash("abc") = a*p² + b*p + c (mod M)
// Slide: remove leftmost, shift, add rightmost. Verify with .equals()
long BASE = 31, MOD = 1_000_000_007L;
long hash = 0, power = 1;
for (int i = 0; i < m; i++) {
    hash = (hash * BASE + text.charAt(i)) % MOD;
    if (i > 0) power = (power * BASE) % MOD;
}

Section 4: Code Templates

Template 1: Frequency Array Comparison (Anagram Check)

public boolean isAnagram(String s, String t) {
    if (s.length() != t.length()) return false;
    int[] freq = new int[26];
    for (int i = 0; i < s.length(); i++) {
        freq[s.charAt(i) - 'a']++;
        freq[t.charAt(i) - 'a']--;
    }
    for (int f : freq) if (f != 0) return false;
    return true;
}

Template 2: Expand Around Center (Palindrome)

// O(n²) time, O(1) space. Check both odd (i,i) and even (i,i+1) centers.
private int expand(String s, int left, int right) {
    while (left >= 0 && right < s.length()
           && s.charAt(left) == s.charAt(right)) { left--; right++; }
    return right - left - 1;
}

Template 3: Stack-Based Decode / Parse

// Two stacks: numStack (repeat counts) + strStack (accumulated strings)
// On digit: build number. On '[': push state, reset. On ']': pop, repeat, combine.
// See LC 394 full solution in Section 9.

Template 4: Two-Pointer String Validation

public boolean isPalindrome(String s) {
    int left = 0, right = s.length() - 1;
    while (left < right) {
        while (left < right && !Character.isLetterOrDigit(s.charAt(left))) left++;
        while (left < right && !Character.isLetterOrDigit(s.charAt(right))) right--;
        if (Character.toLowerCase(s.charAt(left)) !=
            Character.toLowerCase(s.charAt(right))) return false;
        left++; right--;
    }
    return true;
}

Template 5: Character Frequency with PriorityQueue (Reorganize)

// Rearrange so no adjacent chars are same. O(n log 26) = O(n)
// Impossible check: any freq > (n+1)/2 → return ""
int[] freq = new int[26];
for (char c : s.toCharArray()) freq[c - 'a']++;
PriorityQueue<int[]> pq = new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> b[1] - a[1]);
for (int i = 0; i < 26; i++) if (freq[i] > 0) pq.offer(new int[]{i, freq[i]});
// Poll two at a time, append, decrement, re-offer

Template 6: Basic Polynomial Rolling Hash

long BASE = 31, MOD = 1_000_000_007L;
long hash = 0, power = 1;
for (int i = 0; i < m; i++) {
    hash = (hash * BASE + text.charAt(i)) % MOD;
    if (i > 0) power = (power * BASE) % MOD;
}
// Slide: hash = ((hash - text.charAt(i)*power%MOD + MOD)%MOD * BASE + newChar) % MOD

Section 5: Complexity Analysis

PatternTimeSpaceNotes
Anagram check (freq array)O(n)O(1)Single pass with int[26]
Group Anagrams (sort key)O(n · k log k)O(n · k)n strings, max length k
Group Anagrams (freq key)O(n · k)O(n · k)Better for long strings
Palindrome validationO(n)O(1)Two pointers
Longest palindromic substrO(n²)O(1)Expand around center
Palindrome partitioningO(n · 2n)O(n)Backtracking, exponential
String compression (RLE)O(n)O(1)In-place with write pointer
Decode String (stack)O(output len)O(depth)Output can be exponential
Valid ParenthesesO(n)O(n)Stack
Generate ParenthesesO(4n/√n)O(n)Catalan number
Min Remove to Make ValidO(n)O(n)Stack + set
Longest Valid ParenthesesO(n)O(n)Stack
Is SubsequenceO(n)O(1)Two pointers
Multiply StringsO(n · m)O(n + m)n, m = input lengths
Rolling hash matchingO(n) avgO(m)O(nm) worst on collision

Common Complexity Traps

Watch out:
  • String + in loop → O(n²), use StringBuilder
  • substring() → O(k), creates new string
  • String.contains() → O(n·m) naive search
  • HashMap with String keys → O(k) for hashing, not O(1)

Section 6: Edge Cases & Gotchas

#Edge CaseWatch For
1Empty string ""Check s == null || s.isEmpty() first
2Single character "a"Palindrome, self-anagram, no pairs
3All same chars "aaaa"Reorganize impossible if len > 1
4Case sensitivityAsk interviewer. Use Character.toLowerCase()
5Leading/trailing spacestrim() for atoi, trim().split("\\s+") for words
6Special charactersCharacter.isLetterOrDigit() to skip
7Unicodeint[26] won’t work — use HashMap
8Integer overflow (atoi)Check BEFORE multiply: result > (MAX-digit)/10
9String immutabilityNever result += char in loop
10Null checksUse "literal".equals(s) to avoid NPE
Gotcha — Comparing Frequency Arrays:
freq1 == freq2 is WRONG (reference equality). Use Arrays.equals(freq1, freq2).
StringBuilder vs StringBuffer: StringBuilder is NOT thread-safe but faster. Use StringBuilder in interviews. StringBuffer is thread-safe but rarely needed.

Section 7: Interview Tips

Questions to Ask FIRST

  1. “What is the character set?” → determines int[26] vs int[128] vs HashMap
  2. “Can the string be empty?” → upfront null/empty check
  3. “Are there spaces or special characters?” → parsing logic
  4. “Is the input always valid?” → error handling
  5. “What to return for edge cases?” → empty result vs specific value

Implementation Tips

  • StringBuilder for building results (mention immutability to show Java knowledge)
  • char[] when modifying in place (compression, reversal)
  • toCharArray() for iteration (slightly faster than charAt())
  • Declare frequency approach upfront: “lowercase letters, so I’ll use int[26]”

Common Follow-Up Responses

Follow-UpStrategy
“String is 10GB?”Stream processing, rolling hash, constant-space
“Doesn’t fit in memory?”External sort, MapReduce
“Can you make it faster?”Manacher’s O(n) for palindrome, rolling hash for matching
“Follow-up queries?”Precompute: trie, palindrome table, cached freq maps

Full Solutions

Solution 1: Group Anagrams (LC 49)

class Solution {
    public List<List<String>> groupAnagrams(String[] strs) {
        Map<String, List<String>> map = new HashMap<>();
        for (String s : strs) {
            int[] count = new int[26];
            for (char c : s.toCharArray()) count[c - 'a']++;
            StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
            for (int i = 0; i < 26; i++) sb.append(count[i]).append('#');
            String key = sb.toString();
            map.computeIfAbsent(key, k -> new ArrayList<>()).add(s);
        }
        return new ArrayList<>(map.values());
    }
}
// Time: O(n * k), Space: O(n * k)
// Why freq key over sort: O(k) vs O(k log k) per string

Solution 2: Longest Palindromic Substring (LC 5)

class Solution {
    public String longestPalindrome(String s) {
        if (s == null || s.length() < 1) return "";
        int start = 0, maxLen = 0;
        for (int i = 0; i < s.length(); i++) {
            int len1 = expandAroundCenter(s, i, i);      // odd
            int len2 = expandAroundCenter(s, i, i + 1);  // even
            int len = Math.max(len1, len2);
            if (len > maxLen) {
                maxLen = len;
                start = i - (len - 1) / 2;
            }
        }
        return s.substring(start, start + maxLen);
    }

    private int expandAroundCenter(String s, int left, int right) {
        while (left >= 0 && right < s.length()
               && s.charAt(left) == s.charAt(right)) { left--; right++; }
        return right - left - 1;
    }
}
// Time: O(n²), Space: O(1)

Solution 3: Decode String (LC 394)

class Solution {
    public String decodeString(String s) {
        Deque<StringBuilder> strStack = new ArrayDeque<>();
        Deque<Integer> numStack = new ArrayDeque<>();
        StringBuilder current = new StringBuilder();
        int num = 0;
        for (char c : s.toCharArray()) {
            if (Character.isDigit(c)) {
                num = num * 10 + (c - '0');
            } else if (c == '[') {
                numStack.push(num); strStack.push(current);
                current = new StringBuilder(); num = 0;
            } else if (c == ']') {
                int repeat = numStack.pop();
                StringBuilder prev = strStack.pop();
                for (int i = 0; i < repeat; i++) prev.append(current);
                current = prev;
            } else {
                current.append(c);
            }
        }
        return current.toString();
    }
}
// Time: O(output length), Space: O(nesting depth)
// Trace "3[a2[c]]": push(3,"")→push(2,"a")→pop→"acc"→pop→"accaccacc"

Solution 4: Min Remove to Make Valid Parentheses (LC 1249)

class Solution {
    public String minRemoveToMakeValid(String s) {
        Set<Integer> toRemove = new HashSet<>();
        Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
        for (int i = 0; i < s.length(); i++) {
            if (s.charAt(i) == '(') stack.push(i);
            else if (s.charAt(i) == ')') {
                if (stack.isEmpty()) toRemove.add(i);  // unmatched ')'
                else stack.pop();                       // matched
            }
        }
        while (!stack.isEmpty()) toRemove.add(stack.pop()); // unmatched '('
        StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
        for (int i = 0; i < s.length(); i++)
            if (!toRemove.contains(i)) sb.append(s.charAt(i));
        return sb.toString();
    }
}
// Time: O(n), Space: O(n). Stack stores INDICES, not characters.

Solution 5: Multiply Strings (LC 43)

class Solution {
    public String multiply(String num1, String num2) {
        int m = num1.length(), n = num2.length();
        int[] result = new int[m + n];
        for (int i = m - 1; i >= 0; i--) {
            for (int j = n - 1; j >= 0; j--) {
                int product = (num1.charAt(i) - '0') * (num2.charAt(j) - '0');
                int sum = product + result[i + j + 1];
                result[i + j + 1] = sum % 10;
                result[i + j] += sum / 10;
            }
        }
        StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
        for (int d : result) if (sb.length() > 0 || d != 0) sb.append(d);
        return sb.length() == 0 ? "0" : sb.toString();
    }
}
// Time: O(m*n), Space: O(m+n)
// Key: num1[i]*num2[j] → result[i+j] (tens) and result[i+j+1] (ones)

Section 10: Quick Revision Card

String Tricks Cheat Sheet

StringBuilder > String+  (always, for loops)
int[26]       > HashMap  (when lowercase only)
char[]        > String   (when modifying in place)
.equals()     > ==       (always, for comparison)
toCharArray() > charAt() (slightly faster iteration)

Frequency Array Template (Memorize)

int[] freq = new int[26];
for (char c : s.toCharArray()) freq[c - 'a']++;
// Compare two strings: increment for s, decrement for t, check all zeros

Palindrome Approaches Comparison

ApproachTimeSpaceWhen to Use
Two pointers (validate)O(n)O(1)Check if palindrome
Expand around centerO(n²)O(1)Find longest palindromic substring
DP tableO(n²)O(n²)Need palindrome info for all substrings
Manacher’sO(n)O(n)Almost never in interviews

“Which Pattern?” — 5-Second Detector

Keyword in Problem → Pattern
├─ "anagram", "permutation", "rearrange" → Frequency array (this topic)
├─ "palindrome" → Two pointers / expand center (this topic)
├─ "decode", "encode", "compress" → Stack (nested) or scan (flat)
├─ "parentheses", "brackets", "valid" → Stack / counter (this topic)
├─ "subsequence" → Two pointers or DP (Topic 13)
├─ "substring" (contiguous) → Sliding window (Topic 3)
├─ "prefix", "dictionary", "autocomplete" → Trie (Topic 16)
├─ "multiply", "add" (as strings) → Digit-by-digit simulation
└─ "largest/smallest after rearrange" → Greedy + sorting/heap

Common Mistakes Checklist

1. Using String + in loop instead of StringBuilder
2. == instead of .equals() for comparison
3. Off-by-one in substring(start, end) — end is exclusive
4. Multi-digit numbers in decode (e.g., “12[a]”)
5. Integer overflow in atoi / multiply
6. Leading zeros in multiply strings
7. freq1 == freq2 (reference equality) instead of Arrays.equals()
01 / 10

SOLID Principles — Interview Notes (Java)

0. Building Intuition

SOLID principles are the foundation of good OOP design. Every LLD interview implicitly tests whether you follow these. You don't need to name them — but your design must reflect them.

Interview Reality: Interviewers rarely ask "explain SOLID." Instead, they judge your design. If your Parking Lot class handles payment, display, and vehicle tracking — that's an SRP violation they'll notice.
When to think about SOLID:
• Adding a new feature requires changing 5 classes? → OCP violation
• A class has 10+ methods doing unrelated things? → SRP violation
• Subclass throws UnsupportedOperationException? → LSP violation
• Class depends on a fat interface it barely uses? → ISP violation
• High-level module directly instantiates low-level class? → DIP violation

1. Single Responsibility Principle (SRP)

Rule: A class should have only one reason to change.

Analogy: A chef cooks. A waiter serves. A cashier bills. Don't make one person do all three.

Violation

class Employee {
    void calculateSalary() { /* payroll logic */ }
    void saveToDatabase() { /* DB logic */ }
    void generateReport() { /* PDF logic */ }
}

Fixed

class Employee { String name; double salary; }
class SalaryCalculator { double calculate(Employee e) { ... } }
class EmployeeRepository { void save(Employee e) { ... } }
class ReportGenerator { void generate(Employee e) { ... } }
SRP Quick Test: Describe what a class does. If you use "AND", it probably does too much.

2. Open/Closed Principle (OCP)

Rule: Open for extension, closed for modification.

Analogy: A power strip lets you plug in new devices without rewiring the house.

Violation

class DiscountCalculator {
    double calculate(String type, double price) {
        if (type.equals("REGULAR")) return price * 0.1;
        if (type.equals("PREMIUM")) return price * 0.2;
        // Adding VIP? Must modify this class!
        return 0;
    }
}

Fixed — Strategy Pattern

interface DiscountStrategy {
    double calculate(double price);
}
class RegularDiscount implements DiscountStrategy {
    public double calculate(double price) { return price * 0.1; }
}
class PremiumDiscount implements DiscountStrategy {
    public double calculate(double price) { return price * 0.2; }
}
// Adding VIP? Just add a new class. No existing code changes.
class VipDiscount implements DiscountStrategy {
    public double calculate(double price) { return price * 0.3; }
}

3. Liskov Substitution Principle (LSP)

Rule: Subclass must be substitutable for its parent without breaking behavior.

Analogy: If I order a "vehicle" for transport, a car works, a bicycle works — but a toy car doesn't.

Classic Violation — Square extends Rectangle

class Rectangle {
    int width, height;
    void setWidth(int w) { width = w; }
    void setHeight(int h) { height = h; }
    int area() { return width * height; }
}
class Square extends Rectangle {
    void setWidth(int w) { width = w; height = w; } // breaks parent contract!
    void setHeight(int h) { width = h; height = h; }
}
// rect.setWidth(5); rect.setHeight(3); rect.area() → expects 15, gets 9 for Square!

Fixed

interface Shape { int area(); }
class Rectangle implements Shape {
    int width, height;
    public int area() { return width * height; }
}
class Square implements Shape {
    int side;
    public int area() { return side * side; }
}
LSP Red Flag: If a subclass throws UnsupportedOperationException for a parent method, it's an LSP violation. Example: Bird.fly() works but Penguin.fly() throws — bad design.

4. Interface Segregation Principle (ISP)

Rule: No client should be forced to depend on methods it doesn't use.

Violation

interface Worker {
    void code(); void test(); void design(); void managePeople();
}
class Junior implements Worker {
    public void code() { /* ok */ }
    public void test() { /* ok */ }
    public void design() { /* forced empty */ }
    public void managePeople() { /* forced empty */ }
}

Fixed

interface Coder { void code(); }
interface Tester { void test(); }
interface Designer { void design(); }
interface Manager { void managePeople(); }

class Junior implements Coder, Tester { ... }
class TechLead implements Coder, Designer, Manager { ... }

5. Dependency Inversion Principle (DIP)

Rule: High-level modules should depend on abstractions, not concrete classes.

Violation

class NotificationService {
    private EmailSender emailSender = new EmailSender(); // tightly coupled!
    void notify(String msg) { emailSender.send(msg); }
}

Fixed

interface MessageSender { void send(String msg); }
class EmailSender implements MessageSender { ... }
class SmsSender implements MessageSender { ... }

class NotificationService {
    private MessageSender sender; // depends on abstraction
    NotificationService(MessageSender sender) { this.sender = sender; }
    void notify(String msg) { sender.send(msg); }
}

6. Quick Revision

S — One class, one job
O — Add new behavior without changing old code
L — Subtypes must honor parent's contract
I — Small focused interfaces > fat interfaces
D — Depend on interfaces, not concrete classes
02 / 10

Creational Design Patterns — Interview Notes (Java)

0. When to Use What

Creational Pattern Decision Tree:
• Exactly one instance globally? → Singleton
• Complex object with many optional params? → Builder
• Don't know which class until runtime? → Factory Method
• Need families of related objects? → Abstract Factory
• Creating object is expensive, clone instead? → Prototype

1. Singleton

Intent: Ensure only one instance exists globally.

Use when: DB connection pool, Config manager, Logger, Cache

Thread-Safe (Bill Pugh — Recommended)

public class DatabasePool {
    private DatabasePool() {}
    private static class Holder {
        private static final DatabasePool INSTANCE = new DatabasePool();
    }
    public static DatabasePool getInstance() {
        return Holder.INSTANCE; // lazy, thread-safe by JVM class loading
    }
}
Interview Tip: Always mention thread safety. Enum singleton: enum DB { INSTANCE; } — simplest and serialization-safe.

2. Factory Method

Intent: Let subclasses decide which class to instantiate.

interface Notification { void send(String message); }
class EmailNotification implements Notification { ... }
class SmsNotification implements Notification { ... }
class PushNotification implements Notification { ... }

class NotificationFactory {
    static Notification create(String type) {
        return switch (type) {
            case "EMAIL" -> new EmailNotification();
            case "SMS"   -> new SmsNotification();
            case "PUSH"  -> new PushNotification();
            default -> throw new IllegalArgumentException("Unknown: " + type);
        };
    }
}

3. Abstract Factory

Intent: Create families of related objects without specifying concrete classes.

interface Button { void render(); }
interface Checkbox { void render(); }

interface UIFactory {
    Button createButton();
    Checkbox createCheckbox();
}
class WindowsFactory implements UIFactory {
    public Button createButton() { return new WindowsButton(); }
    public Checkbox createCheckbox() { return new WindowsCheckbox(); }
}
class MacFactory implements UIFactory {
    public Button createButton() { return new MacButton(); }
    public Checkbox createCheckbox() { return new MacCheckbox(); }
}

4. Builder

Intent: Construct complex objects step by step.

Use when: 4+ constructor params, many optional fields, immutable objects

class User {
    private final String name, email;
    private final int age;
    private final String phone;

    private User(Builder b) {
        this.name = b.name; this.email = b.email;
        this.age = b.age; this.phone = b.phone;
    }

    static class Builder {
        private final String name, email; // required
        private int age;
        private String phone;

        Builder(String name, String email) { this.name = name; this.email = email; }
        Builder age(int age) { this.age = age; return this; }
        Builder phone(String phone) { this.phone = phone; return this; }
        User build() { return new User(this); }
    }
}
// Usage: new User.Builder("Tanvi", "t@g.com").age(25).build();

5. Prototype

Intent: Create new objects by cloning an existing instance.

abstract class Shape implements Cloneable {
    int x, y; String color;
    public abstract Shape clone();
}
class Circle extends Shape {
    int radius;
    Circle(Circle src) { this.x = src.x; this.y = src.y; this.radius = src.radius; }
    public Shape clone() { return new Circle(this); }
}

6. Common Mistakes

Singleton abuse: Don't make everything a singleton. It's a global variable — hard to test.
Builder overkill: Don't use Builder for 2-field classes.
Factory without need: If you only create one type, a factory is overhead.
Singleton → One instance (DB pool, Config) | Factory → Runtime type selection | Abstract Factory → Family of objects | Builder → Complex object, step by step | Prototype → Clone expensive objects
03 / 10

Structural Design Patterns — Interview Notes (Java)

0. When to Use What

• Incompatible interface? → Adapter
• Add behavior dynamically? → Decorator
• Simplify complex subsystem? → Facade
• Control access (lazy load, auth, cache)? → Proxy
• Tree structure, leaf + composite same? → Composite
• Decouple abstraction from implementation? → Bridge

1. Adapter

Intent: Convert one interface into another that clients expect.

Real-world: Arrays.asList(), InputStreamReader

interface MediaPlayer { void play(String file); }
class VlcPlayer { void playVlc(String file) { ... } }

class VlcAdapter implements MediaPlayer {
    private VlcPlayer vlc = new VlcPlayer();
    public void play(String file) { vlc.playVlc(file); }
}

2. Decorator

Intent: Add responsibilities dynamically by wrapping objects.

Analogy: Base coffee + milk + sugar. Each wraps the previous.

interface Coffee { double cost(); String description(); }

class BasicCoffee implements Coffee {
    public double cost() { return 50; }
    public String description() { return "Basic Coffee"; }
}

abstract class CoffeeDecorator implements Coffee {
    protected Coffee wrapped;
    CoffeeDecorator(Coffee c) { this.wrapped = c; }
}

class MilkDecorator extends CoffeeDecorator {
    MilkDecorator(Coffee c) { super(c); }
    public double cost() { return wrapped.cost() + 15; }
    public String description() { return wrapped.description() + " + Milk"; }
}

// new SugarDecorator(new MilkDecorator(new BasicCoffee())).cost() → 70
Decorator vs Inheritance: Inheritance = static. Decorator = dynamic. n decorators → 2^n combos without subclass explosion.

3. Facade

Intent: Simple interface to a complex subsystem.

class OrderFacade {
    private Inventory inv = new Inventory();
    private Payment pay = new Payment();
    private Shipping ship = new Shipping();

    String placeOrder(String item, double price) {
        if (!inv.check(item)) return "Out of stock";
        if (!pay.charge(price)) return "Payment failed";
        return "Shipped: " + ship.ship(item);
    }
}

4. Proxy

Intent: Control access — lazy loading, caching, access control, logging.

interface Image { void display(); }

class RealImage implements Image {
    RealImage(String f) { loadFromDisk(f); } // expensive
    public void display() { ... }
}

class ProxyImage implements Image {
    private String file;
    private RealImage real;
    ProxyImage(String f) { this.file = f; }
    public void display() {
        if (real == null) real = new RealImage(file); // lazy load
        real.display();
    }
}

5. Composite

Intent: Tree structures where leaf and composite are treated uniformly.

interface FileSystemItem { String getName(); int getSize(); }

class File implements FileSystemItem {
    String name; int size;
    public String getName() { return name; }
    public int getSize() { return size; }
}

class Folder implements FileSystemItem {
    String name;
    List<FileSystemItem> children = new ArrayList<>();
    void add(FileSystemItem item) { children.add(item); }
    public String getName() { return name; }
    public int getSize() { return children.stream().mapToInt(FileSystemItem::getSize).sum(); }
}

6. Bridge

Intent: Decouple abstraction from implementation so both vary independently.

interface Color { String fill(); }
class Red implements Color { public String fill() { return "Red"; } }

abstract class Shape {
    protected Color color;
    Shape(Color c) { color = c; }
    abstract void draw();
}
class Circle extends Shape {
    Circle(Color c) { super(c); }
    void draw() { System.out.println("Circle in " + color.fill()); }
}
// Any Shape + any Color without class explosion

7. Common Mistakes

Adapter vs Decorator: Adapter changes interface. Decorator keeps same interface, adds behavior.
Facade ≠ God class: Facade delegates, doesn't contain business logic.
04 / 10

Behavioral Design Patterns — Interview Notes (Java)

0. When to Use What

• Switch algorithm at runtime? → Strategy
• Behavior changes with internal state? → State
• Multiple objects react to changes? → Observer
• Undo/redo, queue operations? → Command
• Common skeleton, subclasses fill steps? → Template Method
• Pass request along a chain? → Chain of Responsibility

1. Strategy

Intent: Family of interchangeable algorithms.

#1 most used pattern in LLD interviews — pricing, notifications, payment, scheduling.

interface SortStrategy { void sort(int[] arr); }
class QuickSort implements SortStrategy { ... }
class BubbleSort implements SortStrategy { ... }

class Sorter {
    private SortStrategy strategy;
    void setStrategy(SortStrategy s) { strategy = s; }
    void sort(int[] arr) { strategy.sort(arr); }
}

2. Observer

Intent: One-to-many dependency — when one changes, all dependents notified.

interface Subscriber { void update(String message); }

class NotificationService {
    private List<Subscriber> subs = new ArrayList<>();
    void subscribe(Subscriber s) { subs.add(s); }
    void unsubscribe(Subscriber s) { subs.remove(s); }
    void notifyAll(String msg) { subs.forEach(s -> s.update(msg)); }
}

class EmailAlert implements Subscriber {
    public void update(String msg) { System.out.println("Email: " + msg); }
}

3. Command

Intent: Encapsulate request as object — enables undo, queuing, logging.

interface Command { void execute(); void undo(); }

class LightOnCommand implements Command {
    private Light light;
    public void execute() { light.on(); }
    public void undo() { light.off(); }
}

class RemoteControl {
    private Stack<Command> history = new Stack<>();
    void press(Command cmd) { cmd.execute(); history.push(cmd); }
    void undoLast() { if (!history.isEmpty()) history.pop().undo(); }
}

4. Template Method

Intent: Skeleton algorithm in base class, subclasses override specific steps.

abstract class DataParser {
    final void parse(String file) {  // template method
        openFile(file);
        extractData();  // abstract — subclass fills
        validateData();
        closeFile();
    }
    abstract void extractData();
    void validateData() { /* default */ }
}
class CsvParser extends DataParser {
    void extractData() { System.out.println("Parsing CSV"); }
}

5. State

Intent: Object behavior changes when internal state changes.

interface VendingState {
    void insertCoin(VendingMachine vm);
    void dispense(VendingMachine vm);
}
class IdleState implements VendingState {
    public void insertCoin(VendingMachine vm) {
        System.out.println("Coin inserted");
        vm.setState(new HasMoneyState());
    }
    public void dispense(VendingMachine vm) { System.out.println("Insert coin first"); }
}
class HasMoneyState implements VendingState {
    public void insertCoin(VendingMachine vm) { System.out.println("Already has coin"); }
    public void dispense(VendingMachine vm) {
        System.out.println("Dispensing...");
        vm.setState(new IdleState());
    }
}
State vs Strategy: Both swap behavior via interface. But State transitions happen internally. Strategy is chosen externally by the client.

6. Chain of Responsibility

Intent: Pass request along a chain until a handler processes it.

Use when: Middleware, logging levels, approval workflows

abstract class Logger {
    protected Logger next;
    void setNext(Logger n) { next = n; }
    void log(int level, String msg) {
        if (canHandle(level)) write(msg);
        else if (next != null) next.log(level, msg);
    }
    abstract boolean canHandle(int level);
    abstract void write(String msg);
}

7. Quick Revision

Strategy → Swap algorithms (pricing, sorting)
Observer → Pub-sub (notifications, events)
Command → Undo/redo, task queue
Template → Same skeleton, different steps
State → Behavior changes with state (vending machine)
Chain → Pass until handled (middleware)
05 / 10

UML & Class Diagrams — Interview Notes

0. Why UML in Interviews

In LLD interviews, you draw class diagrams on a whiteboard. You don't need formal UML — just clear boxes with class names, attributes, methods, and relationships.

What interviewers care about:
1. Right classes identified? 2. Correct relationships (has-a vs is-a)? 3. Is it extensible?

1. Relationships

Association (uses / knows about)

class Teacher { List<Student> students; }
class Student { Teacher advisor; }
// Both exist independently. Arrow: ——→

Aggregation (has-a, weak)

class Department { List<Employee> employees; }
// Department deleted → Employees still exist. Arrow: ◇——→

Composition (has-a, strong)

class House { List<Room> rooms; }
// House deleted → Rooms deleted too. Arrow: ◆——→
Aggregation vs Composition: If parent destroyed, does child survive? Yes = Aggregation. No = Composition.
• Car ◆→ Engine (dies with car) = Composition
• Library ◇→ Book (survives) = Aggregation

Inheritance (is-a)

class Car extends Vehicle { }  // Arrow: ——▷ (hollow triangle)

Dependency (uses temporarily)

class OrderService {
    void process(PaymentGateway pg) { pg.charge(); }
    // Uses but doesn't own. Arrow: - - -→ (dashed)
}

2. Class Box Format

┌──────────────────────────┐
│       ClassName          │
├──────────────────────────┤
│ - privateField: Type     │  (- private, + public, # protected)
├──────────────────────────┤
│ + method(): ReturnType   │
└──────────────────────────┘

3. How to Draw in Interviews

5-Step Process:
1. Identify nouns from requirements → candidate classes
2. Identify verbs → candidate methods
3. Determine relationships — is-a? has-a? uses?
4. Add cardinality — 1:1, 1:N, M:N
5. Identify interfaces — what should be extensible?

4. Multiplicity Notation

1     → exactly one     0..1  → optional
*     → zero or more    1..*  → one or more

5. Common Mistakes

Everything connects to everything: Minimize dependencies.
Missing interfaces: If multiple implementations possible, use interface.
Forgetting cardinality: Always mark 1:1, 1:N, M:N.
