Parking Lot Design — Interview Notes (Java)

1. Requirements

Functional

  • Multiple floors with different spot types (Compact, Regular, Large)
  • Different vehicle types (Bike, Car, Truck)
  • Issue ticket on entry, calculate fee on exit
  • Track availability per floor and spot type

Non-Functional

  • Thread-safe concurrent entry/exit
  • Extensible for new vehicle types and pricing

2. Class Diagram

ParkingLot (Singleton)
  ├── List<ParkingFloor>        (composition)
  │     └── List<ParkingSpot>   (composition)
  ├── PricingStrategy            (strategy pattern)
  └── EntryPanel / ExitPanel

Vehicle (abstract) ←── Car, Bike, Truck
Ticket { vehicle, spot, entryTime }
Payment { ticket, amount }

3. Core Code

enum VehicleType { BIKE, CAR, TRUCK }
enum SpotType { COMPACT, REGULAR, LARGE }

class ParkingSpot {
    int id; SpotType type; boolean isAvailable = true; Vehicle parkedVehicle;

    synchronized boolean park(Vehicle v) {
        if (!isAvailable) return false;
        parkedVehicle = v; isAvailable = false; return true;
    }
    synchronized void unpark() { parkedVehicle = null; isAvailable = true; }
}

class ParkingFloor {
    int floorNumber; List<ParkingSpot> spots;

    ParkingSpot findSpot(VehicleType vType) {
        SpotType needed = switch(vType) {
            case BIKE -> SpotType.COMPACT;
            case CAR -> SpotType.REGULAR;
            case TRUCK -> SpotType.LARGE;
        };
        return spots.stream().filter(s -> s.isAvailable && s.type == needed)
            .findFirst().orElse(null);
    }
}

class ParkingLot {
    private static ParkingLot instance;
    List<ParkingFloor> floors;
    PricingStrategy pricing;

    static synchronized ParkingLot getInstance() {
        if (instance == null) instance = new ParkingLot();
        return instance;
    }

    Ticket entry(Vehicle v) {
        for (ParkingFloor f : floors) {
            ParkingSpot spot = f.findSpot(v.type);
            if (spot != null && spot.park(v))
                return new Ticket(UUID.randomUUID().toString(), v, spot, LocalDateTime.now());
        }
        throw new RuntimeException("Parking full");
    }

    double exit(Ticket t) {
        t.spot.unpark();
        long hours = ChronoUnit.HOURS.between(t.entryTime, LocalDateTime.now());
        return pricing.calculate(t.vehicle.type, hours);
    }
}

4. Design Patterns Used

• Singleton — ParkingLot
• Strategy — PricingStrategy (hourly, flat, dynamic)
• Factory — Vehicle creation by type
• Observer — Notify display boards on availability change

5. Extension Points

• Electric vehicle → SpotType.ELECTRIC, EVSpot with charger
• Handicapped parking → Priority in findSpot
• Multiple payment methods → PaymentStrategy interface
• Reservation → ReservationService with scheduled locking

6. Common Mistakes

God class: Don't put payment + display + spot management all in ParkingLot.
No thread safety: Concurrent vehicles can get same spot. Use synchronized on park().
Hardcoded pricing: Use Strategy pattern for pricing flexibility.
07 / 10

Elevator System — Interview Notes (Java)

1. Requirements

  • N floors, M elevators
  • Users press UP/DOWN on floor, select destination inside
  • Efficient scheduling — minimize wait and travel time
  • Handle concurrent requests from multiple floors

2. Key Classes

ElevatorSystem (controller)
  ├── List<Elevator>
  └── SchedulingStrategy (interface)

Elevator { id, currentFloor, direction, destinations (TreeSet) }
Request { sourceFloor, direction }
Direction { UP, DOWN, IDLE }

3. Core Code

class Elevator {
    int id, currentFloor = 0;
    Direction direction = Direction.IDLE;
    TreeSet<Integer> upStops = new TreeSet<>();
    TreeSet<Integer> downStops = new TreeSet<>(Collections.reverseOrder());

    void addDestination(int floor) {
        if (floor > currentFloor) upStops.add(floor);
        else if (floor < currentFloor) downStops.add(floor);
    }

    void move() {
        if (direction == Direction.UP && !upStops.isEmpty())
            currentFloor = upStops.pollFirst();
        else if (direction == Direction.DOWN && !downStops.isEmpty())
            currentFloor = downStops.pollFirst();
        else if (!upStops.isEmpty()) direction = Direction.UP;
        else if (!downStops.isEmpty()) direction = Direction.DOWN;
        else direction = Direction.IDLE;
    }
}

LOOK Scheduling Strategy

interface SchedulingStrategy {
    Elevator selectElevator(List<Elevator> elevators, Request request);
}

class LookStrategy implements SchedulingStrategy {
    public Elevator selectElevator(List<Elevator> elevators, Request req) {
        return elevators.stream().min(Comparator.comparingInt(e -> cost(e, req))).orElse(null);
    }
    private int cost(Elevator e, Request req) {
        // Same direction and on the way → low cost
        if (e.direction == req.direction) {
            if (req.direction == Direction.UP && e.currentFloor <= req.floor)
                return req.floor - e.currentFloor;
            if (req.direction == Direction.DOWN && e.currentFloor >= req.floor)
                return e.currentFloor - req.floor;
        }
        return 1000 + Math.abs(e.currentFloor - req.floor); // high cost if needs reverse
    }
}

4. Design Patterns

• Strategy — Scheduling algorithm (LOOK, SCAN, Shortest Seek)
• State — Elevator states (Moving, Stopped, DoorOpen, Maintenance)
• Observer — Floor displays observe elevator positions
• Command — Button presses as command objects

5. Common Mistakes

Single elevator assumption: Design for N elevators from start.
No scheduling algorithm: FCFS is terrible. Mention LOOK/SCAN.
Ignoring direction: Elevator going UP shouldn't take DOWN request on higher floor.
08 / 10

BookMyShow — Interview Notes (Java)

1. Requirements

Functional

  • Search movies by city, theatre, date
  • View available shows and seat layout
  • Select seats, book tickets, cancel with refund
  • No double booking (critical)

Non-Functional

  • Strong consistency for seat booking
  • Temporary seat lock during payment (5 min TTL)

2. Key Classes

City → Theatre → Screen → Show → ShowSeat
Movie    Booking ← User
           ↓
        Payment
class Movie { String id, title; int duration; }
class Screen { int id; List<Seat> seats; }  // physical seats
class Seat { int row, number; SeatType type; }  // REGULAR, PREMIUM, VIP
class Show { String id; Movie movie; Screen screen; LocalDateTime time; Map<Seat, ShowSeat> seatStatus; }
class ShowSeat { Seat seat; SeatStatus status; LocalDateTime lockExpiry; double price; }

3. Booking with Seat Locking (Critical Section)

class BookingService {
    synchronized Booking bookSeats(User user, Show show, List<Seat> seats) {
        // 1. Check all available
        for (Seat s : seats) {
            ShowSeat ss = show.seatStatus.get(s);
            if (ss.status != SeatStatus.AVAILABLE)
                throw new SeatUnavailableException("Seat " + s.number + " taken");
        }
        // 2. Lock for 5 min
        for (Seat s : seats) {
            ShowSeat ss = show.seatStatus.get(s);
            ss.status = SeatStatus.LOCKED;
            ss.lockExpiry = LocalDateTime.now().plusMinutes(5);
        }
        // 3. Create pending booking
        return new Booking(user, show, seats, BookingStatus.PENDING);
    }

    void confirmBooking(Booking b, Payment p) {
        if (p.isSuccess()) {
            b.status = BookingStatus.CONFIRMED;
            b.seats.forEach(s -> b.show.seatStatus.get(s).status = SeatStatus.BOOKED);
        } else {
            releaseSeats(b);
        }
    }
}

4. The Seat Locking Problem

This is THE interview question. Two users selecting same seat simultaneously.

Option 1: Pessimistic Locking — Lock seat row in DB. Simple but blocks others.
Option 2: Optimistic Locking — Version column. UPDATE WHERE version=X. Conflict if 0 rows.
Option 3: Temporary Lock (TTL) — Lock 5 min for payment. Auto-release on timeout.

Best answer: Temporary lock with TTL + optimistic locking fallback.

5. Design Patterns

• Strategy — PricingStrategy (weekday, weekend, holiday)
• Observer — Notify when locked seats become available
• State — Booking states (Pending → Confirmed → Cancelled)
• Factory — PaymentFactory for different methods

6. Common Mistakes

No concurrency handling: #1 thing interviewers test. Mention seat locking or fail.
Mixing Show and Screen: Screen = physical. Show = movie screening on a screen at a time.
No ShowSeat: Seats belong to Screen, but availability is per Show. Need ShowSeat mapping.
09 / 10

Splitwise — Interview Notes (Java)

1. Requirements

  • Add expense shared among group members
  • Split types: Equal, Exact, Percentage
  • Track who owes whom
  • Simplify debts (minimize transactions)

2. Key Classes

User, Group { List<User> members }
Expense { paidBy, amount, splits }
Split (abstract) ←── EqualSplit, ExactSplit, PercentSplit
BalanceSheet { Map<userId, Map<userId, Double>> }

3. Core Code — Balance Management

class BalanceSheet {
    Map<String, Map<String, Double>> balances = new HashMap<>();

    void addExpense(Expense expense) {
        for (Split split : expense.splits) {
            if (split.user.id.equals(expense.paidBy.id)) continue;
            // split.user owes expense.paidBy
            updateBalance(split.user.id, expense.paidBy.id, split.amount);
        }
    }

    private void updateBalance(String ower, String owee, double amount) {
        double reverse = getBalance(owee, ower);
        if (reverse > 0) {
            if (reverse >= amount) {
                setBalance(owee, ower, reverse - amount);
                return;
            }
            setBalance(owee, ower, 0);
            amount -= reverse;
        }
        setBalance(ower, owee, getBalance(ower, owee) + amount);
    }
}

4. Debt Simplification

Key interview question: minimize number of transactions

1. Calculate net balance per person (total owed - total owes)
2. Positive = creditor, Negative = debtor
3. Match largest creditor with largest debtor, settle, repeat 
List<String> simplify(Map<String, Double> netBalances) {
    PriorityQueue<Map.Entry<String,Double>> creditors = new PriorityQueue<>(
        (a,b) -> Double.compare(b.getValue(), a.getValue()));
    PriorityQueue<Map.Entry<String,Double>> debtors = new PriorityQueue<>(
        Comparator.comparingDouble(Map.Entry::getValue));

    netBalances.entrySet().forEach(e -> {
        if (e.getValue() > 0) creditors.add(e);
        else if (e.getValue() < 0) debtors.add(e);
    });

    List<String> txns = new ArrayList<>();
    while (!creditors.isEmpty() && !debtors.isEmpty()) {
        var cr = creditors.poll(); var db = debtors.poll();
        double settled = Math.min(cr.getValue(), -db.getValue());
        txns.add(db.getKey() + " pays " + cr.getKey() + " ₹" + settled);
        // Add back remainder if not fully settled
    }
    return txns; // 5 people: max 4 transactions instead of 10
}

5. Design Patterns

• Strategy — SplitStrategy (Equal, Exact, Percent)
• Observer — Notify users when expense added
• Factory — Create different split types

6. Common Mistakes

Floating point: Use BigDecimal for money in production. Mention in interview.
Not handling reverse debts: A owes B ₹100, B owes A ₹60 → net A owes B ₹40.
Forgetting simplification: Without it, 5 people need up to 10 transactions. With it: max 4.
10 / 10

Rate Limiter — Interview Notes (Java)

1. Requirements

  • Limit requests per user/IP/API key within a time window
  • Return 429 when limit exceeded
  • Low latency, thread-safe, accurate

2. Algorithm Decision

• Simple fixed window? → Fixed Window Counter
• Smoother limiting? → Sliding Window
• Allow small bursts + steady rate? → Token Bucket
• Strict constant rate? → Leaky Bucket

3. Token Bucket (Most Common)

class TokenBucket {
    private final int maxTokens;
    private final int refillRate;
    private double tokens;
    private long lastRefillTime;

    TokenBucket(int max, int rate) {
        maxTokens = max; refillRate = rate;
        tokens = max; lastRefillTime = System.nanoTime();
    }

    synchronized boolean allowRequest() {
        refill();
        if (tokens >= 1) { tokens--; return true; }
        return false;
    }

    private void refill() {
        long now = System.nanoTime();
        double elapsed = (now - lastRefillTime) / 1_000_000_000.0;
        tokens = Math.min(maxTokens, tokens + elapsed * refillRate);
        lastRefillTime = now;
    }
}

4. Sliding Window Counter

class SlidingWindowCounter {
    private final int limit;
    private final long windowMs;
    private final Map<String, LinkedList<Long>> log = new ConcurrentHashMap<>();

    boolean allowRequest(String userId) {
        long now = System.currentTimeMillis();
        log.putIfAbsent(userId, new LinkedList<>());
        LinkedList<Long> ts = log.get(userId);
        synchronized (ts) {
            while (!ts.isEmpty() && now - ts.peek() > windowMs) ts.poll();
            if (ts.size() < limit) { ts.add(now); return true; }
            return false;
        }
    }
}

5. Algorithm Comparison

AlgorithmMemoryAccuracyBurst
Fixed WindowO(1)Low2x at edges
Sliding LogO(n)HighNo burst
Sliding CounterO(1)MediumMinimal
Token BucketO(1)HighControlled
Leaky BucketO(n)HighNo burst

6. Interview Tips

• Where: API Gateway level (before service)
• Distributed: Redis for multi-server, in-memory for single
• Headers: Return X-RateLimit-Remaining and X-RateLimit-Reset
• Graceful: Return cached data instead of hard 429

7. Common Mistakes

Fixed window edge: 100 req at 11:59:59 + 100 at 12:00:01 = 200 in 2s. Sliding window fixes this.
Not thread-safe: Check-then-increment is a race condition.
Memory leak: Sliding log needs cleanup of expired timestamps.
01 / 10

System Design Building Blocks — Notes

1. Load Balancers

What: Distributes incoming traffic across multiple servers.

Algorithms:
• Round Robin — simple, equal distribution
• Weighted Round Robin — more traffic to stronger servers
• Least Connections — send to server with fewest active connections
• IP Hash — same client always hits same server (session affinity)

Layer 4 (TCP) — fast, routes by IP/port. Layer 7 (HTTP) — smart, routes by URL/headers/cookies.

Interview tip: Always mention LB in your architecture. If asked "what if LB fails?" → Active-passive LB pair with health checks.

2. Caching

Why: Reduce DB load, decrease latency. Most reads hit cache instead of DB.

Cache Strategies

StrategyHowWhen
Cache-AsideApp checks cache → miss → read DB → write cacheGeneral purpose, most common
Write-ThroughWrite to cache AND DB simultaneouslyWhen consistency matters
Write-BehindWrite cache, async write DB laterHigh write throughput
Read-ThroughCache fetches from DB on missCache as primary interface
Redis vs Memcached:
• Redis: Rich data types (strings, lists, sets, sorted sets), persistence, pub/sub, Lua scripting
• Memcached: Simple key-value, multi-threaded, slightly faster for simple cases
Default to Redis unless you need pure simple caching.

3. Message Queues

Why: Decouple services, handle async processing, absorb traffic spikes.

KafkaRabbitMQSQS
ModelPub-sub logMessage brokerManaged queue
ThroughputVery high (millions/s)ModerateModerate
OrderingPer partitionPer queueBest effort (FIFO available)
Best forEvent streaming, logsTask queues, RPCSimple async, AWS-native

4. CDN (Content Delivery Network)

Serves static assets (images, CSS, JS) from edge servers close to users.

Push CDN: You upload content. Pull CDN: CDN fetches from origin on first request, then caches.

5. CAP Theorem

In a distributed system, you can only guarantee 2 of 3:
Consistency — every read gets the most recent write
Availability — every request gets a response
Partition Tolerance — system works despite network splits

P is mandatory (network failures happen). So you choose:
• CP — Consistent but may reject requests (banks, inventory)
• AP — Available but may serve stale data (social media, DNS)

6. Consistent Hashing

Problem: Adding/removing servers in a hash-based system redistributes ALL keys.

Solution: Arrange servers on a virtual ring. Each key maps to the next server clockwise. Adding/removing a server only affects neighboring keys.

Virtual nodes: Each server gets multiple positions on the ring for better distribution.

7. Database Sharding

Sharding Strategies:
• Range-based: Users A-M → Shard 1, N-Z → Shard 2. Simple but uneven (hot spots).
• Hash-based: hash(userId) % numShards. Even distribution.
• Directory-based: Lookup table maps key → shard. Flexible but lookup overhead.

8. Replication

Master-Slave: Writes → master. Reads → slaves. Simple, read-scalable. Slave lag possible.

Master-Master: Both accept writes. Complex conflict resolution. Use for multi-region.

9. API Gateway

Single entry point for all client requests. Handles: rate limiting, authentication, routing, response aggregation, SSL termination.

10. Microservices vs Monolith

MonolithMicroservices
DeployOne unitIndependent services
ScaleScale everythingScale per service
ComplexitySimple at startComplex (networking, observability)
Best forSmall teams, early stageLarge teams, high scale
Interview default: Start with monolith, evolve to microservices. Don't say microservices for a startup.

11. Quick Reference Cheat Sheet

Read-heavy? → Cache (Redis) + Read replicas + CDN
Write-heavy? → Message queue + Write-behind cache + Sharding
Real-time? → WebSockets + Pub-sub (Kafka/Redis)
Search? → Elasticsearch
File storage? → S3/Blob storage + CDN
Need ordering? → Kafka with partition key
Need transactions? → SQL (PostgreSQL)
02 / 10

Database Design — System Design Notes

1. SQL vs NoSQL Decision

Choose SQL (PostgreSQL/MySQL) when:
• Need ACID transactions (payments, banking)
• Complex joins across tables
• Data has clear relationships and schema
• Strong consistency required

Choose NoSQL when:
• MongoDB — flexible schema, document-based (user profiles, catalogs)
• Cassandra — massive write throughput, time-series (IoT, logs)
• Redis — in-memory, sub-ms latency (cache, sessions, leaderboards)
• DynamoDB — serverless, auto-scaling (AWS-native apps)

2. Indexing

B-Tree Index (Default)

Balanced tree. O(log n) lookups. Best for range queries, sorting, and exact match.

Hash Index

O(1) lookups. Best for exact match only. No range queries.

When to add indexes:
• Columns in WHERE, JOIN, ORDER BY clauses
• Foreign keys
• Columns with high cardinality (many unique values)
Don't index: Small tables, columns with few unique values, tables with heavy writes

Composite Index

CREATE INDEX idx_user_city ON users(city, created_at);
-- Works for: WHERE city = 'Mumbai'
-- Works for: WHERE city = 'Mumbai' AND created_at > '2024-01-01'
-- Does NOT work for: WHERE created_at > '2024-01-01' (leftmost prefix rule)

3. Normalization vs Denormalization

NormalizedDenormalized
RedundancyNoneDuplicated data
WritesFast (update one place)Slow (update many places)
ReadsSlow (joins)Fast (pre-joined)
Use whenWrite-heavy, consistencyRead-heavy, performance
Interview approach: Start normalized. Denormalize only for proven read bottlenecks. Mention the trade-off.

4. Sharding Strategies

// Hash-based (most common)
shard_id = hash(user_id) % num_shards

// Range-based
Shard 1: user_id 1-1M
Shard 2: user_id 1M-2M

// Directory-based (lookup service)
lookup_table[user_id] → shard_3
Sharding challenges:
• Cross-shard joins are expensive → denormalize or use application-level joins
• Hot shards → use consistent hashing with virtual nodes
• Resharding → need data migration strategy (double-write period)

5. Replication

Synchronous: Master waits for slave ACK. Strong consistency, higher latency.

Asynchronous: Master doesn't wait. Fast but may lose data on master crash.

Semi-synchronous: Wait for at least 1 slave ACK. Balance of both.

6. ACID vs BASE

ACID (SQL): Atomicity, Consistency, Isolation, Durability — strong guarantees
BASE (NoSQL): Basically Available, Soft state, Eventually consistent — relaxed for scale

Payment system: ACID. Social media feed: BASE is fine.

7. Database per Service (Microservices)

Each microservice owns its database. No direct DB access across services — only via APIs.

Distributed transactions: Saga pattern (sequence of local transactions with compensating actions).

03 / 10

URL Shortener (TinyURL) — System Design Notes

1. Requirements & Estimation

Functional

  • Given a long URL, generate a short URL
  • Redirect short URL to original
  • Optional: custom aliases, expiration, analytics

Back-of-Envelope

Write: 100M URLs/month ≈ 40 URLs/sec
Read: 10:1 ratio → 400 redirects/sec
Storage: 100M × 12 months × 5 years × 500 bytes ≈ 3 TB
Short URL length: base62, 7 chars → 62^7 ≈ 3.5 trillion URLs (enough)

2. High-Level Architecture

Client → API Gateway → LB → App Servers → DB (NoSQL)
                                  ↓
                          Cache (Redis)
                                  ↓
                          Counter Service / ID Generator

3. Key Design Decision: Short URL Generation

This is THE interview question for URL shortener.

Option 1: Hash (MD5/SHA256) + Base62
hash("long_url") → take first 7 chars of base62(hash)
Pro: No coordination needed. Con: Collisions possible → need DB check.

Option 2: Counter-based (Auto-increment ID)
DB auto-increment → base62(id) = short URL
Pro: No collisions. Con: Predictable, single point of failure.

Option 3: Distributed ID (Snowflake / Range-based)
Each server gets a range (1-1M, 1M-2M). Convert to base62.
Pro: No collision, no coordination. Con: Gaps in IDs.

Best answer: Range-based counter (like Zookeeper assigns ranges to servers). Mention trade-offs. 
// Base62 encoding
String encode(long id) {
    String chars = "0123456789abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyzABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZ";
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    while (id > 0) {
        sb.append(chars.charAt((int)(id % 62)));
        id /= 62;
    }
    return sb.reverse().toString();
}

4. Database Design

Table: urls
| short_url (PK) | long_url | created_at | expires_at | user_id |

NoSQL (DynamoDB/Cassandra) preferred:
- Simple key-value lookup
- No joins needed
- High read throughput

5. Redirect Flow (Read Path)

1. Client hits short.url/abc123
2. Check Redis cache → if found, redirect (301/302)
3. If cache miss → query DB → cache it → redirect
4. 301 (permanent) for SEO, 302 (temporary) if analytics needed
301 vs 302: 301 = browser caches redirect (less server load, no analytics). 302 = browser always hits server (enables click tracking). Choose 302 if analytics required.

6. Scaling

  • Cache: 80/20 rule — 20% of URLs get 80% of traffic. Cache hot URLs in Redis.
  • DB: Shard by short_url hash. Each shard handles its range.
  • ID Generation: Zookeeper assigns ID ranges to servers to avoid coordination.

7. Quick Revision

• Generate: base62(counter_id) or hash-based
• Store: NoSQL (key-value), shard by short_url
• Redirect: Cache (Redis) → DB fallback
• Scale: Cache hot URLs, range-based ID, DB sharding
• Key trade-off: 301 vs 302 redirect
04 / 10

Twitter / News Feed — System Design Notes

1. Requirements & Estimation

Functional

  • Post tweets (text, images, videos)
  • Follow/unfollow users
  • Home timeline (feed of tweets from people you follow)
  • Search tweets

Estimation

DAU: 300M, Avg follows: 200
Tweets/day: 500M
Timeline reads: 300M × 5 refreshes = 1.5B reads/day ≈ 17K QPS

2. The Key Design Decision: Fan-out

This is THE interview question. Push vs Pull for feed generation.

Fan-out on Write (Push):
When user posts → immediately write to all followers' timelines (pre-computed).
✅ Fast reads (timeline already built). ❌ Expensive for celebrity (10M followers = 10M writes).

Fan-out on Read (Pull):
When user opens feed → fetch latest from all followed users, merge, rank.
✅ No write amplification. ❌ Slow reads (query N users, merge).

Hybrid (Twitter's actual approach):
Push for normal users (<10K followers). Pull for celebrities. Merge at read time.

3. Architecture

Post Tweet → Tweet Service → Write DB + Kafka
                                        ↓
                               Fan-out Service → Write to follower timelines (Redis)

Read Feed → Timeline Service → Read from Redis (pre-computed)
                               + Merge celebrity tweets (on-the-fly)
                               + Rank/Filter → Return feed

4. Database Design

// SQL
tweets: { id, user_id, content, media_url, created_at }
follows: { follower_id, following_id, created_at }
timeline: { user_id, tweet_id, created_at }  // pre-computed, in Redis

// Tweet storage: SQL for tweets, Redis for timelines
// Media: S3 + CDN

5. Feed Ranking

Chronological is simplest. For algorithmic feed:

  • Relevance score = engagement signals (likes, retweets, replies)
  • Recency decay (newer tweets score higher)
  • User affinity (how often you interact with the author)

6. Scaling

• Timeline cache: Redis sorted sets (tweet_id as score = timestamp)
• Tweet DB: Shard by tweet_id (hash-based)
• Fan-out: Kafka consumers, process async
• Celebrity problem: Hybrid push/pull
• Search: Elasticsearch for full-text search
• Media: S3 + CDN for images/videos
05 / 10

WhatsApp / Chat System — System Design Notes

1. Requirements & Estimation

Functional

  • 1:1 messaging and group chats
  • Sent/delivered/read receipts
  • Online/offline status
  • Media sharing (images, files)
  • Message history and sync across devices

Estimation

DAU: 500M, Messages/day: 50B
QPS: 50B / 86400 ≈ 600K messages/sec
Storage: 50B × 100 bytes = 5TB/day

2. The Key Decision: WebSocket

Why WebSocket over HTTP?
Chat needs real-time, bidirectional communication. HTTP polling = wasteful. Long polling = better but connection overhead.

WebSocket: Persistent connection. Server can push messages instantly. One connection handles both send and receive. This is the right choice.

3. Architecture

Client ←WebSocket→ Chat Server ←→ Message Queue (Kafka)
                        ↓                    ↓
                  Session Service       Message Service → DB
                  (which server has      (store + retrieve)
                   which user?)

Offline? → Push Notification Service (FCM/APNs)

4. Message Flow (1:1)

1. User A sends message to User B
2. Chat server receives via WebSocket
3. Store message in DB (Cassandra) with status "sent"
4. Check: Is User B online? (Session Service / Redis)
5a. Online → Find B's chat server → forward via internal messaging → deliver via WebSocket
5b. Offline → Send push notification (FCM/APNs) → message waits in DB
6. When B receives → ACK back → update status to "delivered"
7. When B reads → ACK back → update to "read"

5. Group Chat

Small groups (<256): Fan-out on write. Send message to each member's chat server.

Large groups / channels: Fan-out on read. Members pull messages when they connect.

6. Database Design

// Messages (Cassandra — high write throughput, time-series friendly)
messages: { chat_id, message_id (TimeUUID), sender_id, content, type, created_at }
// Partition key: chat_id. Clustering: message_id (sorted by time)

// User sessions (Redis)
online_users: { user_id → chat_server_id }

// Conversations
conversations: { user_id, chat_id, last_message_id, unread_count }

7. Scaling

• Chat servers: Stateful (maintain WebSocket). Scale with consistent hashing.
• Session routing: Redis tracks user → server mapping
• Messages: Cassandra (partitioned by chat_id). Handles 600K writes/sec.
• Media: Upload to S3, send URL in message
• Presence: Heartbeat every 30s via WebSocket. Absent = offline after 60s.

8. Common Mistakes

Using HTTP for messaging: Must use WebSocket or SSE for real-time.
Storing messages in SQL: Chat is write-heavy + time-series. Cassandra or similar is better.
Ignoring offline delivery: Must store and forward. Push notifications for offline users.
06 / 10

Uber / Ride Sharing — System Design Notes

1. Requirements & Estimation

Functional

  • Rider requests a ride (pickup location, destination)
  • Match with nearest available driver
  • Real-time location tracking
  • ETA calculation, fare estimation
  • Trip history and payments

Estimation

Active drivers: 1M sending location every 3 sec
Location updates: 1M / 3 ≈ 333K writes/sec
Ride requests: 1M rides/day ≈ 12 rides/sec

2. The Key Decision: Location Indexing

How to efficiently find nearest drivers?

Option 1: Geohash — Encode lat/lng into a string. Nearby locations share prefix. Store in Redis as sorted set by geohash. Pro: Simple. Con: Edge cases at geohash boundaries.

Option 2: Quadtree — Recursively divide 2D space into 4 quadrants. Leaf nodes contain drivers. Pro: Adaptive density. Con: Complex to update.

Option 3: Google S2 / H3 — Hierarchical spatial indexing. Pro: No edge issues. Con: Complexity.

Best answer: Geohash with Redis (simple, fast) + query neighboring cells for boundary handling.

3. Architecture

Rider App → API Gateway → Ride Service → Matching Service
                                              ↓
Driver App ←WebSocket→ Location Service → Geospatial Index (Redis)
                              ↓
                     Location DB (time-series)

Trip Service → Payment Service
     ↓
  Trip DB

4. Matching Flow

1. Rider requests ride at (lat, lng)
2. Compute geohash of pickup location
3. Query Redis: find drivers in same + neighboring geohash cells
4. Filter: available, within radius, correct vehicle type
5. Rank by distance + ETA
6. Send ride request to top driver
7. Driver accepts/rejects → if reject, try next driver
8. Match confirmed → start trip tracking

5. Real-Time Location Updates

// Driver sends location every 3 seconds via WebSocket
1. Location Service receives (driver_id, lat, lng, timestamp)
2. Update Redis geospatial index: GEOADD drivers lng lat driver_id
3. Batch write to Cassandra (trip history, analytics)
4. Push to rider's app via WebSocket (for live tracking)

6. ETA and Routing

Precompute road graph. Use Dijkstra/A* for shortest path. Cache popular routes. Factor in real-time traffic data.

7. Scaling

• Location writes: 333K/sec → Redis (in-memory, geospatial commands)
• Trip data: Cassandra (time-series)
• Matching: Shard by city/region. Each region has its own matching service.
• Surge pricing: Track demand/supply ratio per geohash cell. Adjust dynamically.
07 / 10

Notification System — System Design Notes

1. Requirements

Functional

  • Support multiple channels: Push (iOS/Android), SMS, Email
  • Template-based notifications
  • User preferences (opt-in/opt-out per channel)
  • Scheduled and triggered notifications
  • Rate limiting (don't spam users)

Estimation

10M notifications/day → ~115/sec average
Peaks: 10x → 1150/sec (Black Friday, breaking news)

2. Architecture

Trigger (Service/Scheduler/API)
    ↓
Notification Service → Validate → Check preferences → Rate limit
    ↓
Message Queue (Kafka) → one topic per channel
    ↓
Workers:  Push Worker → FCM/APNs
          SMS Worker  → Twilio
          Email Worker → SendGrid/SES

3. Key Design Decisions

Why message queue is critical:
1. Decoupling: Notification service doesn't wait for delivery
2. Buffering: Handle traffic spikes (Black Friday)
3. Retry: If FCM is down, messages wait in queue
4. Priority: OTP notifications should skip the queue (high-priority topic)

Deduplication

Same event can trigger multiple times. Use idempotency key (event_id) + Redis set to track sent notifications.

Rate Limiting

Max N notifications per user per hour. Token bucket per user per channel.

4. Database Design

notifications: { id, user_id, channel, template_id, content, status, created_at }
user_preferences: { user_id, channel, enabled, quiet_hours_start, quiet_hours_end }
templates: { id, channel, subject_template, body_template }

5. Reliability

• At-least-once delivery: Queue + retry on failure
• Deduplication: Idempotency key prevents duplicates
• DLQ: Dead letter queue for permanently failed notifications
• Monitoring: Track delivery rate, latency, failure rate per channel

6. Scaling

  • Separate queues per channel (push, SMS, email) — scale independently
  • High-priority queue for OTP/security alerts
  • Batch processing for email campaigns
08 / 10

Instagram — System Design Notes

1. Requirements & Estimation

Functional

  • Upload photos/videos with captions
  • Follow users, see their posts in feed
  • Like, comment on posts
  • Stories (24h expiry)
  • Explore/search

Estimation

DAU: 500M, Photos/day: 100M
Average photo: 200KB → 100M × 200KB = 20TB/day (media storage)
Read:Write ratio: 100:1 (read-heavy)

2. Architecture

Client → API Gateway → LB
    ↓
Post Service → Object Store (S3) + CDN
Feed Service → Timeline Cache (Redis)
Social Graph → Follow/Follower DB
Engagement → Like/Comment Service
Search → Elasticsearch

3. Photo Upload Flow

1. Client uploads image → Upload Service
2. Generate multiple resolutions (thumbnail, medium, full)
3. Store originals + resized in S3
4. CDN caches popular images at edge
5. Store metadata in DB: { post_id, user_id, s3_url, caption, created_at }
6. Trigger fan-out to followers' timelines (async via Kafka)

4. Feed Generation

Same as Twitter — hybrid fan-out.

  • Normal users (<10K followers): Push to followers' Redis timelines on post
  • Celebrities: Pull at read time, merge with pre-computed feed
  • Ranking: ML model considers recency, engagement, relationship strength

5. Stories

Stories are different from feed:
• 24h TTL → use Redis with EXPIRE or Cassandra with TTL
• Must show ALL stories from followed users (no ranking)
• Ring UI → fetch story metadata first, load media on tap
• Separate from main feed pipeline

6. Database Design

// Posts (PostgreSQL, sharded by user_id)
posts: { id, user_id, media_urls[], caption, created_at }

// Social graph (PostgreSQL or graph DB)
follows: { follower_id, following_id }

// Engagement (Cassandra — write-heavy)
likes: { post_id, user_id, created_at }
comments: { post_id, comment_id, user_id, text, created_at }

// Media: S3 + CDN

7. Scaling

• Media: S3 (infinite scale) + CDN (80%+ cache hit rate)
• Feed: Redis for timeline cache. Shard by user_id.
• DB: Shard posts by user_id, likes by post_id
• Like counts: Counter cache in Redis, async update to DB
• Search: Elasticsearch for users, hashtags, locations
09 / 10

Rate Limiter at Scale — System Design Notes

1. Requirements

  • Limit requests per user/IP/API key across distributed servers
  • Sub-millisecond latency (sits in request path)
  • Highly available (if limiter goes down, allow traffic — fail open)
  • Configurable rules per API endpoint

2. Where to Place It

• Client-side: Easy to bypass, unreliable
• Server-side middleware: Each server has its own counter → inconsistent in distributed setup
• API Gateway: Centralized, consistent. Best for most cases.
• Dedicated service: When different APIs need different limits

3. Distributed Rate Limiting with Redis

The key challenge: Multiple servers need to share rate limit state.

Solution: Centralized counter in Redis. All servers check/increment the same counter.
Race condition: GET + check + INCR is not atomic. Use Redis Lua script for atomicity. 
-- Redis Lua script (atomic)
local key = KEYS[1]
local limit = tonumber(ARGV[1])
local window = tonumber(ARGV[2])

local current = redis.call('GET', key)
if current and tonumber(current) >= limit then
    return 0  -- rejected
end
redis.call('INCR', key)
if not current then
    redis.call('EXPIRE', key, window)
end
return 1  -- allowed

4. Algorithms at Scale

Sliding Window Counter (Recommended)

// Weighted count across current and previous window
currentCount × (remaining% of current window)
+ previousCount × (elapsed% of current window)

Example: limit 100/min, at 15s into current minute:
previous window had 80 requests
current window has 20 requests
weighted = 20 × (45/60) + 80 × (15/60) = 15 + 20 = 35 → allowed

Token Bucket with Redis

// Store: { tokens: float, last_refill: timestamp }
// On request: refill based on elapsed time, then decrement
// Atomic via Lua script

5. Architecture

Client → API Gateway → Rate Limiter Middleware → Service
                              ↓
                     Rules Config (DB/Config Service)
                              ↓
                     Redis Cluster (counters)

Response headers:
  X-RateLimit-Limit: 100
  X-RateLimit-Remaining: 73
  X-RateLimit-Reset: 1620000060

6. Rate Limiting Rules

// Rules stored in config DB
{
  "api": "/api/messages",
  "limit": 5,
  "window_seconds": 1,
  "key": "user_id"   // or "ip", "api_key"
}

// Different limits per tier
Free: 100 req/min    Pro: 1000 req/min    Enterprise: 10000 req/min

7. Scaling & Edge Cases

• Redis failure: Fail open (allow traffic). Better to serve than to block everyone.
• Multi-region: Sync counters across regions or accept slight inaccuracy
• Hot keys: Popular API key overwhelms one Redis node → local rate limiting + distributed fallback
• Clock sync: Use Redis server time, not application server time
Race condition: GET then INCR in separate calls → concurrent requests slip through. Always use atomic operations (Lua script or Redis MULTI).

Memory: With millions of users, each needs a counter. Use TTL to auto-expire. Sliding window log is too memory-heavy at scale.
10 / 10

Distributed Cache — System Design Notes

1. Requirements

  • Sub-millisecond read/write latency
  • High throughput (millions of ops/sec)
  • Horizontal scalability (add nodes)
  • High availability (node failure shouldn't cause downtime)
  • Eviction policy for memory management

2. Architecture

Client → Cache Client Library (consistent hashing) → Cache Node 1
                                                   → Cache Node 2
                                                   → Cache Node N

Each node: In-memory hash table + eviction + replication

3. Key Design Decisions

Data Distribution: Consistent Hashing

Why consistent hashing?
Simple modular hashing: hash(key) % N → adding/removing a node redistributes ALL keys.
Consistent hashing: nodes on a virtual ring. Only K/N keys remapped when a node is added/removed.
Virtual nodes: Each physical node gets 100-200 positions on ring for even distribution.

Eviction Policies

PolicyHowBest for
LRURemove least recently usedGeneral purpose (most common)
LFURemove least frequently usedWhen some keys are always hot
TTLExpire after time limitSession data, tokens
RandomRemove random keyWhen all keys equally likely

Cache-Aside Pattern (Most Common)

// Read
value = cache.get(key);
if (value == null) {           // cache miss
    value = db.query(key);     // read from DB
    cache.set(key, value, TTL); // populate cache
}
return value;

// Write
db.update(key, value);
cache.delete(key);  // invalidate, not update (avoids stale data race condition)
Why delete on write instead of update?
If two concurrent writes happen: Write A sets cache, Write B sets DB, Write B sets cache, Write A sets DB → cache has B's value but DB has A's value. Delete + let next read repopulate avoids this.

4. Cache Consistency Problems

Cache Stampede (Thundering Herd)

Popular key expires → 1000 requests simultaneously hit DB.

Solutions:

  • Locking: Only one request fetches from DB, others wait
  • Early expiry: Refresh cache before TTL expires (background refresh)
  • Probabilistic expiry: Each request has a small chance of refreshing early

Cache Penetration

Querying non-existent keys → always misses cache → hits DB.

Solution: Cache null results with short TTL. Or use Bloom filter to check if key exists.

Cache Avalanche

Many keys expire at the same time → massive DB load.

Solution: Add random jitter to TTL. TTL = base + random(0, 300)

5. Replication

Master-Slave: Writes to master, reads from slaves. Slave takes over on master failure.

Redis Sentinel: Monitors master, auto-failover to slave.

Redis Cluster: Auto-sharding across nodes, each shard has replicas.

6. Quick Revision

• Distribution: Consistent hashing with virtual nodes
• Pattern: Cache-aside (read-through, delete on write)
• Eviction: LRU (default), TTL for sessions
• Stampede: Locking or background refresh
• Penetration: Cache nulls or Bloom filter
• Avalanche: TTL jitter
• HA: Redis Sentinel (failover) or Redis Cluster (sharding + replication)
Update cache on write: Delete instead. Update causes race conditions.
No TTL: All cached data should have TTL to prevent stale data.
Cache everything: Only cache hot data. 80/20 rule — 20% of keys serve 80% of requests.
